# Paddlewhispers

*Working unified manuscript, assembled from manuscript/chapters/ by paddle.ps1 after each pass. The complete first draft is preserved at archive/first-draft-2026-06-23.md.*


# Chapter 1 - The Old Landing

Joel reached Will’s house a little after nine, late by the morning Mike had imagined and early by the morning the rest of them would actually live through.

Will’s truck sat in the driveway with the tailgate down and two canoes already on sawhorses in the grass. Packs, paddles, dry bags, fishing rods, a food barrel, and three milk crates of loose gear covered the concrete. Mike was standing over a roll-top dry bag with both hands on his hips, looking at it as if it had lied to him.

“That’s not fitting,” Mike said.

“It fits,” Will said.

“It fits in the sense that a couch fits in a stairwell.”

“You’re folding it wrong.”

“I’m not folding a dry bag.”

“You’re compressing it emotionally.”

Mike looked up. “Do you hear yourself?”

“Constantly,” Will said. “It’s a burden.”

Joel parked along the curb and shut off his old Acura. For a moment he stayed where he was, hands resting on the wheel. The morning had the clean September look that always made him mistrust the forecast. Blue sky, dry pavement, a small bite under the sun. St. Louis had heat banked in the sidewalks by now, but under Will’s maples the air still felt usable. A strap buckle ticked against the truck rack. A paddle knocked once under the cap, hollow and neat. Then the driveway went back to voices.

He had driven in from Madison the day before and slept badly on Will’s couch, half under a dog blanket Will insisted was clean. Mara had texted while he was crossing Illinois: drive safe, and don’t let them make you reasonable. A minute later: send me one decent tree picture so I can pretend this is scenic. He had sent back gas-station coffee in a paper cup and promised better material. After that the phone had gone quiet. Caterer, guest list, venue balance, the little domestic arithmetic of the house they had bought before they were fully used to being people who owned gutters and a sump pump: all of it waited in Madison with her.

Here, the others had started the old argument before coffee. They could argue over rope, fuel, departure time, whether a cooler counted as a food-management system, whether a seven-day route meant six dinners or seven, whether a man approaching forty should still own a foam sleeping pad from college.

This was how the trip began. Not at the water. Not even on the road. It began in somebody’s driveway with too much gear and too many opinions, four men making order by touching every object once and arguing over half of them. Pack the right things. Leave the wrong ones. Tie a clean knot over the visible mess. If the knot held, they called it order.

Mara would have made one list and taped it to a cabinet. She would have put a square beside each name and item, then crossed things off with a satisfaction too small to call joy and too visible to miss. Joel had watched her do it for the wedding seating chart two nights before he left, barefoot in the kitchen, hair pinned badly off her neck, one hand in a bag of pretzels while she moved entire family histories across a screen. When she caught him watching, she had tipped one pretzel into his mouth without looking away from the chart. He had loved her most in moments like that, when competence was not an argument but a form of care.

Before he left Madison, he had paid the venue balance from the edge of their bed and written the confirmation number on a pad beside the sink. He had stood with his pack by the back door and asked if she wanted him to stay through the florist appointment after all. Mara had looked at him over the mug in both hands and said, “Go be damp with your men. I’ll save you a centerpiece crisis.”

So he went. Not because he wanted out. He wanted the house after the bad plumbing, the wedding after the deposits and tastings, the ordinary future past all the paperwork. What he did not know how to want without flinching was the constant saying of it. The trip offered a week with fewer nouns. Water. Pack. Fire. Rain. Food. Sleep.

He had still left her with the list.

Joel got out.

Will turned from the truck. “Madison has arrived.”

Joel looked at the gear spread across the driveway. “Apparently without enough opinions.”

“We have extras,” Will said.

Mike pulled once more at the dry bag and got it cinched. “You bring the camp shoes?”

“Yes.”

“For a canoe trip?”

“For camp.”

“You know what shoes work at camp? The ones already on your feet.”

Joel lifted his pack from the trunk. “You can tell me that again when your socks smell like a moldy sponge.”

Will made his little dry cough, almost a laugh and almost a defect in the throat. Joel had known that cough since they were boys in church together, back when Will used to draw spaceships and circuit diagrams on the bulletin during sermons. Neither of them went anymore. Joel had lost the habit so completely that walking into a church now felt like walking onto the set of something he used to believe was real.

Will still had the old reflex of making a system out of whatever frightened him. As a boy it had been rockets, batteries, rainwater, hymn numbers. Lately it was knots, route options, lure weights, water filters, the small exact subjects that could be held in the hand after the job was gone. He had described the layoff as a restructuring, then a market thing, then finally as bullshit, which was the version Joel believed. The trip had been planned before that, but afterward it took on a second use. Nobody said so. Saying it would make them responsible for what it could not fix.

Mike had met Will later, in middle school. They had gone through high school together, old enough in each other’s lives to know the bad haircuts, the first cars, the first stupid jobs, the things men remembered instead of saying they loved each other. Joel knew Mike through Will and through years of trips. That was different. It was sturdy, but it had edges. Mike made himself necessary and then resented the need.

A car horn sounded from the street. Not one of theirs. A rideshare sedan stopped behind Joel’s Acura, and Alex got out with a big duffel, a backpacking pack, and the expression of a man entering a room he expected to improve.

“Gentlemen,” Alex said. “Try not to look so relieved.”

Mike looked at the bags. “That all of it?”

“For a week in the woods? Sadly, yes.”

“You fly with bear spray?”

“I look stupid to you?”

Mike said nothing.

“I bought it here,” Alex said. “Jesus.”

Alex lived in Seattle and had for a long time, long enough that he had become the group’s western correspondent on rain, coffee, ferries, and the moral superiority of mountains. But he had grown up in Alaska, and he carried that fact differently than the Seattle part. Seattle was posture and complaint. Alaska was something he mentioned less often, usually when cold or distance or bears came up and he wanted everyone to understand that he had been formed by bigger things than Missouri men with canoes.

He was loud in person. Not just volume. Presence. He made the driveway feel like an audience had gathered.

Will looked at the duffel. “You packed food?”

“I packed food I can eat.”

“That means what?” Mike asked.

“It means I have standards.”

“It means he brought crackers,” Joel said.

Alex pointed at him. “And this is why Joel’s engaged. Cruel but attentive.”

“Mostly available,” Joel said.

Alex laughed, a hard bark of sound that bounced off Will’s garage door. The neighbor’s dog answered from behind a fence, then thought better of it.

Mike bent over the duffel and unzipped it before Alex could stop him.

“Hey.”

“Relax.”

“Quit mothering me.”

Mike pulled out a bundle of tortillas, oatmeal packets, two jars of peanut butter, instant rice, protein bars, and a plastic bag of what looked like plain noodles. He held up the noodles.

“What is this?”

“Food.”

“For prisoners?”

Alex took it back. “I have a sensitive palate.”

Will coughed into his coffee. “That’s a generous name for it.”

“I’m branching out.”

“You brought unseasoned noodles.”

Alex turned to Joel. “Jesus, listen to this guy.”

“Branching is a process.”

Joel smiled despite himself and went to his trunk for the rest of his gear. He had packed neatly enough, but not obsessively. Will and Mike did the planning. They made the lists, argued routes, watched water levels, compared portage reports, and decided which old map was trustworthy enough to carry and which old map was trustworthy enough to mock. Joel asked practical questions, then showed up with what he was told to bring plus what he knew he would want when wet and tired. He was competent on the water, more than casual, less than Will and Mike, but he only got one real trip a year now if he was lucky. Work, code releases, travel from Madison, wedding machinery, house things, the ordinary undertow. He liked the trip because it made a week into one line: move north, keep the gear dry, get everyone back.

Will and Mike went several times some years: small rivers, shoulder-season overnights, route tests, gear shakedowns that sounded to Joel like recreation disguised as logistics. Mike sold and resold canoes and took it personally when anyone used the wrong tie-down. Will fished every piece of water that looked like it might tolerate him.

Alex backpacked mountains. He knew cold nights, wet feet, long climbs, and bad food. Canoes he treated with respect and mild suspicion. He was usually put up front where he could provide power, commentary, and fewer steering decisions.

They started loading Will’s truck.

It was the practical vehicle: crew cab, bed cap, roof rack, enough rust to prove loyalty but not enough to question the frame. The canoes would ride on top. The packs and paddles would go under the cap. Mike’s ancient Toyota Highlander would be staged at the take-out later, a faded gray thing with a rear hatch that sometimes opened only if you insulted it first. Mike loved it because it was paid for and hated it because it kept needing things.

Mike had brought the route into being by talking as if it already existed under them. He had found the access roads, worked the shuttle backward, chosen the lakes and portages that made the week feel remote without sounding irresponsible. Will had challenged every assumption and then improved half of them. Between them, confidence and correction had built a plan. Joel trusted plans that survived argument more than plans that arrived clean.

“No, not there,” Mike said.

Will stood in the bed of the truck with a food barrel between his boots. “You cannot say ‘not there’ to every place. Eventually objects occupy space.”

“The barrel goes forward.”

“If it goes forward we have to unload half the truck to get lunch.”

“We’re not eating lunch out of the barrel.”

“We are if you pack lunch in the barrel.”

“I’m not packing lunch in the barrel.”

“You packed lunch in the barrel last year.”

“That was because Alex kept sitting on the tortillas.”

Alex held up both hands. “They were warm and flexible. You’re welcome.”

Joel lifted a paddle bundle and slid it under the cap. The blades knocked softly together, wood and carbon and aluminum making separate notes. Will had three rods rigged in tubes, plus a tackle bag he kept moving as if it might grow legs.

Behind him the food barrel rocked against the truck bed and its black latch snapped once, a small plastic click under the larger argument.

“You bringing all of those?” Joel asked.

“Yes.”

“You can only use one at a time.”

“That’s a failure of the human body, not my system.”

Mike pointed at the rods. “They better not be in the way.”

“They are spiritually central to the trip.”

“You fish constantly,” Alex said. “It’s like a medical condition.”

“It regulates me.”

“That I believe.”

By ten-thirty they had loaded enough to reveal what would not fit. The cooler was reduced. Alex surrendered a camp chair after accusing everyone of anti-comfort extremism, then carried the rejected cooler back to the garage without being asked. Mike tried to remove Joel’s extra fleece and was denied. Will argued for a second tarp on grounds of weather, group size, and general human fragility.

Mike said, “Forecast is clean.”

Will said, “Forecasts are confessions of uncertainty.”

“That sounds smart and means nothing.”

“It means bring the tarp.”

Joel said, “Bring the tarp.”

Mike looked at him. “Et tu, Madison?”

“Especially Madison.”

The second tarp went in.

They tied down the canoes with the solemn irritation of men who had done it badly before. Mike took charge of the straps. Will corrected his twist. Mike corrected Will’s correction. Alex tried to help, then mainly stood in the wrong place. Joel held the canoe steady at the rear rack while Mike fed the webbing over the hull.

The buckle clicked against the roof bar.

“Careful,” Mike said to Alex without looking.

“I’m standing still.”

“Do it more carefully.”

“Quit mothering me.”

Joel tightened his side until the canoe settled into the foam block. The hull was scuffed green with a pale scar near the front. Mike had bought it from a dentist, fixed a cracked seat hanger, used it for two seasons, then decided it was too good to sell and too heavy to love.

Will pulled the strap flat. “Half twist or it hums.”

“That’s not how that works,” Mike said.

“It is exactly how that works.”

Alex looked at Joel. “Are they always married?”

“They’ve had a long engagement.”

“Unlike you,” Alex said. “Actual engagement. Brave man.”

“Someone had to test the institution.”

“Report back from the front.”

Mike cinched the rear strap. “Marriage is people pretending property law is romance.”

Will said, “And there’s Mike, smiling at the children.”

“I’m not wrong.”

“You’re not always useful either.”

That was the tone of it. Mike and Will could move from joke to argument without changing volume. Both of them had done relationships that ended in different weather. Will’s usually lasted three to nine months, long enough for habits to form and short enough for him to describe them later as “educational.” A few had lasted longer and left him quieter afterward. Mike’s came less regularly, sometimes with shared leases or dogs or storage units, and badly enough that his friends learned not to ask until he made the first joke.

Alex kept his own life closer, or emptier. Joel could never tell which. There were women in stories sometimes, but rarely names that returned.

Joel was engaged, which in the group made him both suspect and useful. The others treated it like a foreign war he had volunteered for. He let the jokes pass because answering made the subject larger, and because some part of him liked having one future the group could not vote on.

That future had Mara in it, and a kitchen calendar with held dates crossed out and rewritten, and a house list that kept growing in the margins. It had children as a question they had not answered and a guest room they kept pretending was only a guest room. The trip had none of that density yet. It was still clean on paper. Seven days, six nights. A blue line through old green, two vehicles at either end, men he knew by sound before sight.

At the front rack, Alex stopped joking long enough to put one hand under the canoe while Mike worked the strap through the buckle. He had bad boat habits but good hands. When the hull shifted, he caught the weight and held it without making a performance of the save.

“Now?” he asked.

“Now,” Mike said, and pulled the webbing tight.

Joel saw the small exchange and filed it with the other things about Alex that were easy to lose under the noise.

They finished loading close to noon.

Mike looked at the truck. “We should have left an hour ago.”

Will closed the tailgate. “We said eleven.”

“You said eleven because you knew it meant noon.”

“I understand the group.”

Alex climbed into the back seat with his backpacking pack between his knees. “Wake me when Missouri stops being flat.”

“We’re going north,” Will said. “Missouri has already stopped.”

“Culturally.”

Joel took the other rear seat. Mike rode up front with Will so they could argue over navigation and because neither trusted the other enough to surrender it completely. The truck smelled like dust, old coffee, fishing line, and the faint rubber smell of dry bags warming under the cap.

They pulled away from Will’s house with the canoes tight above them and the remaining day already smaller than planned.

The drive north passed in stages. Suburbs gave way to fields, then to towns built around gas stations and bars with meat raffles advertised on the signs. They stopped for fuel, ice, and the kind of lunch eaten standing beside a truck because sitting would imply delay.

At the first stop, Mike walked around the truck, touched every strap, tugged the rear line, and gave the settled canoe one last flat-palmed slap. Worrying was checking before it had a task. Mike was better once his hands could do something with it.

“We’re fine,” he said.

Will looked at him. “Because you touched it or because physics continues to exist?”

“Both.”

Alex came out of the gas station with coffee, crackers, and a banana. “You guys have any idea how hard it is to find food with no personality?”

“For you?” Joel said. “Or the food?”

Alex pointed the banana at him. “There he is. Very dry. Very Midwest.”

“I live in Wisconsin.”

“That’s advanced Midwest.”

Will came out with a bag of sour candy, jerky, and two electrolyte drinks.

Mike stared at the candy. “You are forty years old.”

“This is for morale.”

“Your morale has corn syrup in it.”

They ate beside the pumps. Trucks came and went. The canoes rocked slightly when a semi passed too close. One strap made a low hum in the wind and Will reached up to flatten it.

“Told you,” he said.

Mike said, “I’m not giving you that.”

“You don’t have to give it. Reality already did.”

At the edge of the lot Mike unfolded one of the maps on the hood. Will stood beside him with a pen. Joel drank bad coffee and watched the two of them bend over the paper. The route was a line of blue and green and little black warnings. South Access at the bottom. A chain of narrow lakes, a river section, several portages, a larger lake, a constricted channel, another portage. North Road Bridge at the top, where Mike’s Highlander would wait if the old thing agreed to start again after a week alone.

On paper the trip had manners. The portage lines were short enough to look polite. The lakes lay flat and obedient inside their shorelines. The old road crossings were thin black marks, not mud, alder, wind, or daylight. Joel knew better than to trust a map as a picture, but he still trusted it as an agreement. Someone had gone first. Someone had found enough order to name the trouble.

“First day is easy if we get on the water early enough,” Mike said.

“We are already not doing that tomorrow if we keep stopping,” Will said.

“We’ll sleep near the forest road. Get up at seven. Out by eight.”

Alex laughed.

Mike looked up. “What?”

“You said get up at seven like that creates a legal obligation.”

“It creates a plan.”

“Plans are how men like you experience hope.”

Will tapped the map. “The first real issue is here. If wind’s wrong on the second lake, we lose time.”

“Forecast says southwest, light.”

“Forecast says a thing. Weather does a thing.”

Mike cleared his throat. It was a small preparatory sound, almost nothing, but Joel had heard it before estimates, road choices, and decisions other men were expected to accept as settled. “It opens up after the first tight stretch. We’re fine.”

Joel saw Will’s mouth move around an answer and then close on it. Will did not always let arguments go. When he did, Joel noticed. He had been doing that more since the layoff: saving some corrections for later, as if he had learned that being right did not count unless someone still paid him to be useful.

Alex leaned over the map from the wrong side. “Where’s the cabin?”

Mike’s finger moved to a small square back from the water. “Map says there’s a cabin. Old maintenance cabin. Probably nothing.”

“Cabin,” Alex said. “Now we’re talking.”

“It’s not part of the route.”

“You say that like routes don’t enjoy surprises.”

“They don’t,” Mike said.

Will said, “That cabin could be gone.”

“It could be.”

“Burned, collapsed, stripped, taken apart by weather.”

“Probably.”

“Then why are we looking at it?”

“Because it’s on the map.”

Joel looked at the square. It was printed with the same confidence as the lakes and roads, but it felt different. Human marks aged badly in wild country. A lake could move at the edges, a channel could choke with beaver work, but it remained itself. Cabins became rumors. Roads became brush. Names outlived things.

He did not have a reason to dislike that, and reason was the tool he trusted. Still, his eye kept returning to the square after Mike folded the paper.

Mike folded the map hard. “We’re exactly where we ought to be.”

“There it is,” Alex said.

“What?”

“Your campaign slogan.”

Mike opened the truck door. “Get in.”

They drove until the light began to go sideways through the trees.

The motel was north of the last useful town, set along a two-lane road with a bar across from it and a ditch full of dark water behind it. The office had postcards in a rack, a bowl of mints, and a woman behind the desk who looked at the canoes without interest. Men with canoes were not rare here. Men with too much confidence probably were not rare anywhere.

In the parking lot Mike held his phone up toward the road, then toward the black line of trees behind the rooms. One bar appeared, vanished, returned as a lie. He put the phone away before anyone could make it a conversation.

They took two rooms because the rate was cheap enough after Will argued about cash and Mike made a face at the number. Even so, Mike said he might sleep in the truck.

“No,” Will said.

“What?”

“You’re not sleeping in the truck to save forty dollars.”

“I sleep fine in the truck.”

“You sleep like a toolbox in a dryer.”

“Then you won’t hear me through the wall.”

Alex said, “Let the man commune with his Highlander.”

“The Highlander is at the take-out tomorrow,” Will said.

“Then he can start saying goodbye tonight.”

Mike took the room key from Will. “I said might.”

They carried in the gear that mattered: maps, food, electronics, paddles, rods, sleeping bags, anything irritating or expensive to lose. The canoes stayed on Will’s truck under the parking lot light. Mike checked the straps again. Will checked the rods. Alex disappeared into the bathroom and came out saying the towels had the texture of legal documents.

Dinner was across the road.

The bar had a mounted bear near the entrance with dust on its muzzle and one cloudy glass eye. Alex stood beside it and asked Joel to take his picture. Joel did, badly.

“You made me look short,” Alex said.

“You are standing beside a bear.”

“Grew up with bigger.”

“In Alaska,” Will said, with the tone of a man beginning a familiar bit.

Alex put a hand to his chest. “I don’t bring it up.”

“You bring it up every time an animal is mentioned.”

“That’s because you people think deer are wildlife.”

Mike took a table in back where they could see the truck through the window. They ordered burgers, fries, fish, beer, coffee for Will because he claimed beer made him sleepy in a way that wasted the first one. Alex studied the menu with suspicion.

“Do you think the chili is aggressive?” he asked.

Mike stared at him.

“I’m asking a serious question.”

“Order chicken strips,” Will said.

“I’m not a child.”

“You packed plain noodles.”

“Chicken strips,” Alex told the waitress.

The talk loosened with food. Old trips came out. Bad landings. Broken tent poles. A river in Arkansas where Mike had sworn the gravel bar was “right around the bend” for six bends until Will threatened to drown him in ankle-deep water. The time Alex dumped a canoe getting out on the wrong side and argued that the shore had moved. The time Joel forgot a spoon and ate oatmeal with a tent stake for three mornings.

“I still say that worked,” Joel said.

“It worked in the way prison solutions work,” Will said.

“Efficient and morally clarifying.”

Alex laughed, loud enough to turn heads at the bar. He did not notice, or did not mind. “God, I missed this.”

For once nobody made a joke right away.

Then Mike said, “You missed an audience.”

Alex pointed a fry at him. “And you missed having a soul, but nobody brings that up.”

“I bring it up,” Will said.

“You do,” Mike said. “Constantly.”

Joel drank his beer and let them work. He did not feel outside the group exactly. He felt adjacent to its louder mechanisms. Will and Mike built the plan. Alex built the weather around it. Joel listened, carried, steered when needed, said no when no needed saying, and took his share of the weight. Most years that was enough. This year he was less certain, though not in any way he could have defended. The jokes still landed. The old stories still opened. The pauses lasted longer.

After dinner they walked back under a sky that had cleared into hard northern stars. The air was colder now. The ditch behind the motel smelled of mud and wet leaves. Mosquitoes gathered under the lights and vanished in the darker spaces between them.

Mike stopped at Will’s truck and put one hand on the rear strap.

“Still tight,” Will said.

Mike pulled it anyway.

Alex said, “Quit mothering the canoe.”

“The canoe appreciates me.”

“The canoe is planning to leave you.”

“Everything leaves Mike eventually,” Will said.

It came out lightly, but it did not land that way. Mike’s face changed only a little. Enough for Joel to see it. Will saw it too and looked down at the gravel.

Alex, for once, did not fill the space.

Joel said, “We staging the Highlander before or after breakfast?”

Mike let go of the strap. “After. If we say seven, we’ll leave at eight.”

“There’s growth,” Will said.

“Don’t ruin it.”

They sorted the morning load under the motel overhang. The Highlander would go first thing to the North Road Bridge with Mike and Will in it. Joel and Alex would follow in Will’s truck. They would leave the Highlander at the take-out and all come back to South Access in the truck with the boats. It was cleaner than trying to move everything twice and worse than having a shuttle, which made it a normal plan.

No satellite messenger. No emergency beacon. Will mentioned it once, not as panic but as inventory.

“We still not bringing one?” he asked.

Mike shook his head. “Didn’t buy one between lunch and now.”

“Could rent one.”

“From who, the bear?”

Will looked at Joel.

Joel had almost rented one in Madison. He had stood at the outdoor shop’s glass case, reading the laminated rate card, then heard the argument before it happened: Mike on cost, Will on signal coverage, Alex on capitalism monetizing fear. He had left with stove fuel and a new lighter.

Now he shrugged. “We’ve done without.”

That was true and not an argument, but it passed for one.

“We have two vehicles, paper maps, downloaded maps, first aid, spray, and four functioning adults,” Mike said.

Alex raised his hand. “Three and a half.”

“Fine,” Mike said. “Three and Alex.”

Alex bowed.

Will nodded, not satisfied but finished. “I’m just saying.”

“I know,” Mike said.

Joel let the subject close with the others. It was easy to do because the absence had no weight yet. It was not like forgetting a paddle or a stove. There was nothing to point to, no empty hook in the truck bed, no missing case on the motel floor. It was a blank place in the plan, and blank places stayed invisible until something needed to occupy them.

Besides, they had done versions of this before. They had been wet before, late before, turned around before, stupid before, and the world had let them come home with better stories than sense.

The rooms went quiet by eleven, late for the plan and early for them. Joel shared with Will. Alex and Mike took the other room, though Mike left once more to check the truck and came back smelling of cold air.

Will sat on the edge of the bed tying a leader onto a lure by the weak yellow light of the lamp.

“You’re doing that now?” Joel asked.

“If I do it in the morning, Mike will stare at me.”

“He’ll stare anyway.”

“True.”

Joel lay back on the bed without taking off his socks yet. The wall hummed faintly with the next room’s television. Mike and Alex were talking, their words blurred by plaster. Alex’s voice rose, then Mike’s cut across it. Then Alex laughed, that hard bark again, alive and demanding.

Will trimmed the line with nail clippers. The little piece fell somewhere onto the bedspread.

“Find that,” Joel said.

“I will.”

“You won’t.”

“I might.”

“You’ll hook yourself in the ass at two in the morning.”

“That’s not how that works.”

Joel closed his eyes.

Mike knocked once and opened the door before either of them answered. He had Will’s rod tubes under one arm.

“These were wedged against the cap hinge,” he said. “You want to snap a guide before we see water?”

Will took them from him. “Thank you for your service.”

“I hate your rods,” Mike said.

“That’s why they know you care.”

Mike looked at Joel, as if Joel might provide a better species of man to travel with, then shut the door with a soft click.

Outside, a truck went by on the road and faded north. The canoes rested on Will’s truck under the security light. The Highlander sat beside it, ancient and square and waiting to be abandoned at the far end of the route. In the morning they would put a vehicle in the future and then drive back toward the beginning. It was practical. It was normal.

Through the wall, Alex laughed once more. The sound came through plaster changed but still his: hard, bright, wanting an answer. After that came Mike’s lower voice, indistinct, then the click of a door latch somewhere down the row, then the building settling into its cheap nighttime silence. The sounds were ordinary because their sources were still there.

Joel listened until he could attach each sound to a body or object: road, wall, latch, men in the next room, the cheap building cooling around them.

Then he slept.


# Chapter 2 - Two Vehicles

They said seven and left at eight.

That was not failure. It was the trip meeting the men who had planned it. At seven, Mike knocked on Joel and Will's door with the flat of his hand and said, "We're burning daylight," though the sun was still low over the motel roof and the parking lot held more shadow than morning. At seven-fifteen, Will was in one sock, tying a leader onto a spinner because he had dreamed of a broken knot and woken offended by his own subconscious. At seven-thirty, Alex stood outside with wet hair and a motel towel around his shoulders, eating crackers from a sleeve and claiming this counted as breakfast.

At seven-forty, Mike decided he would not sleep in the Highlander after all, which meant he had to move two things from the back seat that had been there since spring: a folded camp chair with a torn arm and a cardboard box full of canoe seats, cracked yokes, two old PFDs, a bent roof-rack foot, and a bilge sponge black at the edges.

"Why do you own garbage professionally?" Alex asked.

Mike set the box on the pavement. "That's inventory."

"That sponge has seen death."

"That sponge has value."

"To mold."

Will came out carrying the food barrel. "If that box is coming with us, I'm staying here."

"It's not coming."

"It has been in your car since May."

"Then it knows how to wait."

Joel carried his pack out last and set it beside Will's truck. The morning was cool and bright. Dew stood on the canoes in dull beads. The motel's security light had clicked off, and the place looked worse in honest day: tape on one corner of the office window, cigarette butts flattened near the ice machine, a plastic chair turned upside down beside Room 4. The ditch behind the building held a skin of fog over black water. Mosquitoes floated in the shadow under the eaves, slow from cold but alive enough.

His phone buzzed once before he pocketed it. Mara had written: Water today?

Joel looked at the canoes on the truck and the Highlander with its open hatch and the men sorting themselves into the morning they had promised one another. Not yet, he typed. Shuttle first. On the water late morning. Love you.

The message hung for three seconds before it sent. There was nothing dramatic about that. Then the small delivered sign appeared, and his hand had reached hers by a method both of them still understood.

He pictured Mara seeing it in the kitchen in Madison, one shoulder tipped against the counter, coffee going cold while she answered an email she would claim was simple and then make complicated because simple things deserved to be done right. He liked that about her and had been leaving it to her too often. Then Mike shut the Highlander's hatch, Will asked where lunch had gone, and the morning took the thought out of Joel's hands.

Will's truck looked competent under the two canoes. Long bed, cap, roof rack, gear tucked tight beneath. Mike's Highlander sat beside it, faded gray and square, its rear hatch lifted halfway like a tired jaw. One taillight had red tape over a crack. The bumper was pocked with old rust and stickers from outfitters that may or may not still exist.

The plan was simple before it had to touch weather, fatigue, or human nature. Mike would drive the Highlander to North Road Bridge, the intended take-out. Will would follow in the truck with the boats and most of the gear. They would leave the Highlander at the bridge, put everyone into Will's truck, drive back south and east along the forest roads to the old landing, then launch.

Will and Mike had worked it out over three phone calls, two maps, and one argument about whether the logging road between the bridge and the landing was faster than the paved loop. The paved loop had won after Will found a road report from six years earlier and Mike called it "probably still true enough."

Joel had listened to the summary and said it sounded fine. That had been his role. Fine was not nothing. It meant he had not found the loose strap in it.

Their competence made the blank harder to see. Two vehicles. Paper maps. Downloaded maps. Men who knew enough to argue about road surfaces, spare paddles, water levels, and whether lunch should be reachable without unloading a truck. The missing satellite messenger had no shape among those things. No case. No weight. No empty slot where a man could point and say, there.

Mike stood beside the Highlander with his phone in one hand, looking at the screen.

"Service?" Will asked.

"One bar."

"That seems generous."

"It comes and goes up here."

Alex leaned against the truck and yawned. "I love watching men stare at rectangles in wilderness-adjacent locations."

"We're not in the wilderness," Mike said. "We're at a motel with vending machines."

"Exactly. Transitional habitat."

Mike lifted the phone a little, as if height might improve the truth. "Nothing now."

"We know there won't be much," Will said.

"I'm checking."

"Great. It's checked."

Mike pocketed the phone with visible reluctance. "When something goes wrong, everybody wants the guy who checked."

"When something goes wrong," Alex said, "I want the guy who brought crackers."

"You brought crackers for yourself."

"I'm not a socialist."

They ate what passed for breakfast standing in the parking lot. Muffins from Will's kitchen, bananas bought the day before, instant coffee from the motel-room machine that tasted faintly of plastic and old mineral deposits. Alex ate crackers and peanut butter with the seriousness of a man performing a medical routine. Will drank coffee and rearranged the lunch bag twice, then opened it again to prove to himself he had not forgotten anything he could still name. Mike drank from his dented thermos and walked around both vehicles, touching straps, tires, hatches, locks.

Joel watched him check the Highlander's oil even though they had checked it in St. Louis.

"You worried about it?" Joel asked.

"I'm always worried about it."

"That sounds healthy."

"It's old enough to vote and dumb enough to do it wrong."

Alex looked over. "Is this the vehicle that saves us at the end?"

"It's the vehicle that is waiting at the end," Mike said.

"That was not a yes."

"It starts."

"Again, not a yes."

Mike shut the hood. "We're fine."

He said it without looking at anyone, as if speaking to the engine.

The words did what he wanted them to do. They closed the subject. Joel had seen him do it with bad weather, bad roads, bad money, women who were gone, canoes he could not afford to keep and could not afford to sell. We're fine meant talking had reached the edge of Mike's patience with talking.

Will made his dry cough into his coffee. "Define fine."

"Starts. Runs. Has tires."

"That is a child's understanding of a car."

"Then children are practical."

Mike moved to the rear hatch and pulled out a stained wool blanket. He folded it once and tucked it into the Highlander with more care than he had shown the box of parts.

Alex noticed. "Sentimental blanket?"

"Emergency blanket."

"It has plaid. That's emotion."

"It's warm."

"Warmth is an emotion."

Mike gave him a look and shut the hatch. This time it caught on the first try, a flat metal clack that carried across the empty lot and came back from the office wall thinner than it had left.

At eight they were on the road. Mike led in the Highlander. Will followed in the truck, the canoes riding above them, Joel in the passenger seat, Alex in back with his long legs angled around a cooler and a pack he had refused to move because he said it created a useful barrier between him and responsibility.

The town ended quickly. A bait shop. A church. Two houses with junked snowmobiles beside the garages. Then trees. The road narrowed and rolled north through spruce, jack pine, and birch. The sun came and went through the windshield. The day had the clear feel of good weather before it had been used.

Will drove with one hand low on the wheel, relaxed until he saw a pothole, then personally offended. A paper map lay folded beside him and the downloaded route sat on a small GPS unit clipped near the dash. He did not look at his phone. Will trusted phones for weather at home and distrusted them everywhere else.

"You think he checked the spare?" Will asked.

"On the Highlander?" Joel said.

"Yeah."

"Probably."

"That means no."

Alex leaned forward between the seats. "Can we not begin the day imagining wheel failures?"

Will said, "We can imagine them now or experience them later."

"You two grew up together?" Alex asked Joel.

"Church."

"That explains the optimism."

"It explains my posture," Will said.

Joel watched the Highlander ahead of them. It bounced hard over a patched section, recovered, and kept going. Mike drove like he expected the road to get worse and wanted to prove he had known it first.

They reached North Road Bridge a little after nine.

It was not much of a bridge. Concrete slab, low rails, tannin water moving under it between alder banks. A gravel pullout opened on the far side. There was a post with no sign, two beer cans in the weeds, and a place where someone had cut a sapling and left the pale stump angled toward the road. The bridge made the river smaller and harder. Water went into the dark under it and came out with a different voice.

On the rail, initials had been scratched into the concrete and softened by weather until they looked less written than worn. A strip of orange tape hung from a branch below the road, old enough to have faded toward white. Human signs were everywhere if Joel looked for them, and none of them said much. Somebody had stood here. Somebody had meant something by the mark. The place had kept the mark and lost the message.

Mike pulled the Highlander into the gravel and parked it facing out. Will stopped the truck beside him and left it running.

For a few seconds nobody spoke.

Joel got out and stretched his back. The morning was quieter here than at the motel. No vending-machine hum. No door latches. No road traffic after their own engines died. Under the concrete, the current thickened and released, thickened and released. The river moved steadily, brown and cold-looking, a narrow piece of the route appearing before the route itself. Fog clung low upstream in torn bands.

This was the future. That was what made it feel wrong. They had not put a canoe in water yet, and already here was the endpoint, waiting with its gravel and weeds and blank signpost. They were leaving a vehicle inside a day they had not earned, then asking the day to hold still.

Alex walked to the bridge rail and looked down. "This is where we come out?"

"If everything goes according to plan," Will said.

"Never say that."

"It's literally the plan."

"Say something more outdoorsy. 'If the river permits.'"

Mike came around the Highlander with a small yellow dry pouch and a short loop of cord. "The river doesn't care."

He had already taken the Highlander key off his ring. Joel watched him slide it into the pouch, fold the plastic around it, and press the seal shut with his thumbs. Then he rolled the top three tight turns and clipped the pouch inside the left side of his PFD, which lay on the truck seat waiting for water. The clip made a small hard tick. Mike tugged it once. Then again. The key was no longer part of the car. It belonged to Mike now, and to the route.

"See?" Alex said. "That was almost good."

Mike locked the Highlander, then unlocked it, opened the rear hatch, and checked inside again.

Will said, "What are you looking for?"

"Nothing."

"You found it?"

Mike shut the hatch. It did not latch. He opened it and slammed it harder. This time it caught, a flat metallic sound that jumped under the bridge and came back once.

Alex winced. "She likes it rough."

"She likes decisive closure," Mike said.

Joel walked to the edge of the pullout. The river here was too narrow for their canoes to finish dramatically. If the plan held, they would come out tired, dirty, probably sick of one another, sliding through alder and road noise after seven days of water and portages. Then there would be the Highlander. A machine made ugly by usefulness. The key in Mike's dry pouch. Dry clothes, maybe. A cooler if they had thought of one. Bad suspension. Old vinyl seats. The ordinary world, close enough to smell.

Mike lifted his phone again.

Will saw him. "Still?"

"One bar."

"Congratulations."

"Enough maybe for a text."

"To who?" Alex asked.

Mike did not answer. He turned his shoulder a little from the group, typed something, waited, then frowned.

Joel said, "It go?"

"No."

"Who were you texting?"

"Just checking."

Will leaned against the truck. "You keep saying checking when you mean worrying."

"Worrying gets a task, it becomes checking."

"That's bleakly accurate."

Mike stared at the phone another second, then shoved it into his pocket. "No service at the take-out. We knew that."

"We knew spotty," Will said.

"No service is a species of spotty."

Alex laughed, and the sound went under the bridge and came back thinner. Joel heard the first bark, then a second version under concrete, lower and farther away. It was not wrong. It was only separated from him, shaved down by water and stone until it belonged for a second to the bridge instead of the man who made it. Alex looked pleased with himself, as if he had done that on purpose.

"Nice acoustics," he said.

Will walked down to the water and crouched, already looking for fish. "There's current here."

"That is often a feature of rivers," Mike said.

"I mean more than I expected."

"It's the take-out. We don't care until next week."

Will touched the water with two fingers, then wiped them on his pants. "There is a difference between a route ending at a bridge and a route delivering you neatly to a bridge."

"Great," Alex said. "We have entered the theology portion."

Joel looked upstream. Alder leaned over the banks. Beyond it, spruce closed the gaps. The fog had the flat look of breath held close to the ground. A raven called from somewhere behind them. Its voice was rough and ordinary and made all four men look up anyway.

Mike checked his watch. Before he spoke, he cleared his throat, a small preparatory scrape Joel knew from maps spread on hoods and decisions other people were meant to accept. "Let's go. We're losing the morning."

They left the Highlander in the gravel, backed Will's truck around, and drove away.

For a while the staged vehicle sat in the cab with them as a fact no one needed to discuss. It was the first object they had placed ahead of themselves and trusted to wait. Joel looked back once through the rear window and saw the Highlander shrinking between the canoe hulls. Then the road bent, and it was gone.

Alex said, "I hope it doesn't get lonely."

Will said, "It has Mike's old sponge."

"Companionship comes in many forms."

Mike had given Will directions for the return to South Access before they left the bridge, but Will still glanced at the GPS and the paper map when the road forked. He and Mike had argued the fork the night before. Joel remembered enough to know one way was shorter by distance and worse by surface. They took the longer road.

The farther south they went, the rougher the country became. Not higher exactly, but less arranged. Granite showed through the roadside. The ditches deepened. The trees leaned closer. Old logging spurs appeared and vanished, each marked by a gap and two ruts disappearing into brush. Human work here did not disappear all at once. It thinned, rotted, went seasonal, became something the trees allowed.

Alex watched through the window. "This reminds me of roads outside Fairbanks, except with more deciduous optimism."

"Deciduous optimism?" Joel said.

"Leaves. Seasonal delusion."

Will nodded. "That one I like."

"Thank you."

"Don't make a habit of it."

The road opened briefly near a gravel pit. Mike pointed out an old fire-road sign so rusted its number had become suggestion.

"That's the old maintenance road?" Joel asked.

"One of them," Mike said. "Not ours."

Will glanced at the map on his lap. "There are three old roads on the west side. Two are probably gone."

"Probably," Mike said.

"And the cabin mark is off the middle one?"

"Map says there's a cabin off the middle trace."

Alex leaned forward. "Cabin discourse returns."

"Not part of the route," Mike said.

"I feel like you keep saying that because you know I'll become emotionally attached."

"You're not getting a cabin."

"You don't know what the river permits."

Mike looked at him in the mirror. "The river permits wet feet and mosquitoes."

The last road to South Access was barely two lanes and then not really one. Branches touched the truck cap. The canoes creaked against the racks. A strap made a short, low hum, then stopped when Will slowed for a hole full of black water. Dry bags shifted under the cap and buckles clicked together once, dull and plastic.

Mike, now in the back seat beside Alex, leaned forward. "Left here."

"I know," Will said.

"You almost missed it."

"I did not almost miss it. I approached it with suspense."

"That's not a driving technique."

Alex said, "It is in Seattle."

"You drive a Civic," Mike said.

"I drive an old Civic. There's heritage."

"You flew here."

"The plane also had heritage. And a crying baby."

The old landing appeared without ceremony, which was how most important places arrived.

The forest road widened into a muddy clearing sloped toward dark water. A weathered signboard stood at an angle near the brush. Whatever notices had once been pinned there were gone or reduced to pale rectangles under rusted staples. There was a fire ring full of wet ash, a length of old rope in the weeds, and two boot tracks hardened in mud from some earlier party. The lake lay narrow and black-green below the landing, held between spruce and low shelves of granite. Morning fog still lingered over the far shore though the sun had lifted above the trees.

No other vehicles were there.

Joel checked his phone once before he put it away for good. No bars. Mara's message sat above his answer, already from another weather. There was no new reply, and he was glad for that in the small cowardly way a man could be glad not to owe one more sentence before stepping out of reach.

Will parked facing out, because Mike told him to before he could do it himself. For a moment nobody moved. The truck engine ticked. Somewhere near the signboard, a mosquito whined against the glass.

Then Mike opened his door. "All right."

The practical work took them.

Packs came out first. Food barrel. Rod tubes. Paddles. PFDs. Rain gear. The spare tarp. Alex's personal bag of edible beige things, which Mike placed in the wrong pile on purpose and Alex retrieved with dignity. Will checked his tackle bag and then checked it again. Mike took the map case from the front seat and set it on the hood.

Joel stood at the back of the truck and looked over the spread. The gear had been handled so many times already that it seemed less like preparation than rehearsal. Everything had a category. Things that needed to stay dry. Things that could get wet. Things needed at lunch. Things needed only if something went wrong. Things they had brought because last year someone had said it would be fine without them.

Things not brought had no category. That was the trick. No empty place on the mud for the satellite messenger or beacon. No missing weight in a pack. Only the fact of it, still small enough that nobody had to pick it up.

Will stood over the lunch bag, counting with his lips.

"You doing inventory or prayer?" Alex asked.

"Those are historically related."

"Church boy."

"Former," Will said.

Joel bent to retie a sandal strap and did not look at him. The word former hung between them in the mild morning, too small to become a subject and too familiar to be nothing. Former church boys were good at knowing which words could open a room.

Mike and Will argued over boat loading.

"You and I should not be in the same canoe all day," Will said.

"Because of trim?" Alex asked.

"Because of criminal law."

Mike said, "Joel and I in back makes sense if we split up."

"You and Joel both in back of different boats," Will said. "That's what I'm saying."

"Alex up front with Joel," Mike said. "You up front with me."

Alex raised his hand. "Least skilled guy in front. Tradition honors me."

"You're not least skilled," Will said.

Alex looked touched.

"You're least skilled in a canoe," Will added.

"There it is."

Joel took a PFD from the pile. "I'm fine with Alex up front."

"Joel likes a challenge," Alex said.

"Joel likes being able to see the challenge."

Mike pointed at Will. "You fish from the front if we get time."

"I fish from wherever God places me."

"You don't believe in God."

"I believe in structure."

"Then get in the front."

The canoes came down one at a time. Mike and Joel took the green one first. Will loosened the front strap while Mike handled the rear. Alex stood ready, then stood in the wrong place, then moved when everyone told him to. The buckle clicked against the roof rack.

The hull shifted.

"Wait," Joel said. "Just wait."

Mike stopped. "What?"

"Block's crooked."

Joel reached up and set the foam block straight under the gunwale before they slid the canoe down. It was a small thing, barely a problem. But if they had pulled hard it would have dropped the wrong way against the door frame, scraped paint, maybe cracked nothing, maybe cracked something, maybe only given Mike a grievance for the first three miles.

"All right," Joel said.

They lowered the canoe and carried it toward the landing. The hull was cold and damp in Joel's hand. Mud sucked at the soles of his hiking sandals as they reached the water. The lake moved softly against stone, dark and almost without wave.

Alex watched them set it down. "Ninety-dollar mistake avoided."

Mike said, "It would not have been ninety dollars."

"You don't know that. Spiritually."

Alex put one hand on the hull while Mike turned away for a strap end. It was a small steadying touch, almost accidental, and the canoe stopped rocking. He was loud about useless things and quiet about useful ones often enough that Joel kept revising him.

The second canoe came down easier. They laid both near the water and began loading. Heavy packs low. Food barrel near the middle of Mike and Will's boat because they argued over front and back weight until Joel suggested middle and both accepted it as if they had invented compromise. Rods along the side where Will could reach them if they stopped. Rain gear near the top. First-aid kit in the red bag. Spare paddle lashed. Bear spray clipped where a hand could find it, not where Alex could bury it in a duffel.

"No," Mike said, moving the spray.

"I had it," Alex said.

"You had it clipped to the grab loop of a bag that will be under a pack."

"Still technically had it."

"Quit making me mother you."

"Never."

Will snapped the food barrel latch shut. The sound was clean and small in the clearing.

Mike looked at the map one more time, though the first leg was not complicated. Narrow lake, north shore, left into the channel after the rock point, short portage. He traced it with his finger. Will looked with him, not because he needed to but because not looking would concede something.

"It opens up after this," Mike said, tapping the tight blue thread of water. "Once we're past the first squeeze."

"That sounds like a thing the squeeze says," Alex said.

Mike did not look up. "The squeeze doesn't get a vote."

Joel looked past Mike's finger to the lake. On paper the first water was a shape that could be mastered by folding. In front of them it had no border except shore and no explanation except what the trees allowed them to see. A map did not lie. It told the truth it was made to tell. Trouble started when men asked it for promises.

The bugs thickened as the sun warmed the brush. Mosquitoes came from the shaded ditch. Blackflies found wrists and the backs of ears. Alex slapped his neck.

"Jesus. They filed paperwork."

"Could be worse," Mike said.

Will looked up. "That's going on your tombstone."

"It will still be true."

Alex put on his PFD and tugged at the straps. "How tight is too tight?"

"If you can breathe, you're fine," Mike said.

"That's your whole safety talk?"

"You want a pamphlet?"

Joel stepped closer and pulled the lower strap on Alex's vest until it sat right.

Alex looked down. "See? Gentle instruction. Joel understands leadership."

Joel said, "I understand I'm sitting behind you and don't want to watch you fall out."

"Quit mothering me."

"Then stop needing it."

Will made the cough-laugh again and turned away to hide it badly.

Mike checked his own PFD, then the little yellow pouch clipped inside the left side of it. He pressed the roll once, hard, and tucked it flat.

"Key still there?" Will asked.

"Key's here."

"Good."

"This is why I check."

Will held up both hands. "I said good."

"You said it with a tone."

"My face has resting tone."

The truck had to be made into the thing they would return to only by mistake. That was the quiet part of the shuttle. They stripped it of what mattered. Joel checked the cab for maps, wallets, phone batteries, sunglasses, loose snacks, one of Will's rod tips that had slid under the seat. Mike checked under the cap as if gear might breed in shadow. Will locked the doors, unlocked them, opened the rear door, took out a forgotten lighter, then locked them again.

No one said goodbye to the truck. That would have been ridiculous. Still, they found reasons to touch it once more.

"Key," Mike said.

Will held up a small blue dry pouch. The truck key was inside, along with a folded twenty and a card with his insurance information. He rolled it shut and pushed it deep into his pack, then clipped the pack's top buckle over it.

"If we need the truck," Alex said, "we need Will."

"That's generally been my position," Will said.

By ten-twenty the truck was locked, the key sealed in Will's pack, and the boats sat half in water. The Highlander key rode in Mike's pouch, clipped down and checked twice. Mike stood at the edge of the landing with the map case under one arm and his paddle in the other hand. The old signboard leaned behind him. The road they had come in on already looked less like an exit than a cut the trees had begun closing.

Mike looked at the boats, the packs, the men. He cleared his throat again.

"We're exactly where we ought to be," he said.

Alex groaned. "You're going to have that printed on something."

"Maybe the group shirt."

"Bland colors only. Sensitive palate."

Will stepped into the front of Mike's canoe first, careful but not graceful, one hand on each side. The boat rocked and steadied. He placed his rod where he could see it.

Mike got in back and settled with one small shift that made the canoe behave. He did not talk while doing it. His body knew the correction before his mouth could improve it.

Joel held the second canoe steady while Alex stepped into the front.

"Middle," Joel said.

"I know."

"Low."

"I know."

"You're standing high."

"I am built high."

"Sit down."

Alex sat. The canoe rocked once, then settled. Joel stepped into the back, placed his feet, and lowered himself onto the seat. Damp cold came through his pants. He took the paddle and set the blade flat on the water.

For a moment they floated half a canoe length from shore while Mike adjusted something in his boat and Will freed a lure that had already found a strap.

Joel looked back.

Will's truck sat alone in the muddy clearing. The old landing showed their boot prints and the two grooves where the canoes had slid. The signboard had nothing to tell them. The road disappeared into spruce and alder so quickly that it seemed improbable a truck had come through it at all. Already the place was turning from departure into evidence.

A paddle knocked once against Will's gunwale.

"Sorry," Will said.

"Try water," Mike said.

"I was checking acoustic properties."

Alex dipped his paddle too hard and splashed Joel's boot.

"Testing splash properties."

Joel pushed them off with one stroke. Gravel whispered under the hull and then was gone. The water deepened from brown to black-green beneath them. A loon called somewhere ahead, long and hollow, widening over the narrow lake until the sound seemed to belong to the water more than the bird.

No one spoke for a few minutes.

This was always the best part. The first silence after planning and argument and straps. Paddle in. Pull. Lift. Drip. The canoe sliding forward with a grace men did not deserve and could not keep long. Spruce leaned over the shore. Granite showed in pale shelves at the waterline. Fog lifted in torn strips, and the sun turned the upper branches gold while the trunks stayed black.

Joel felt Alex's first few strokes, strong and uneven in the front. Will and Mike moved ahead, their canoe tracking cleaner. Will turned his head once toward a ring on the surface near shore, already thinking fish. Mike corrected their angle with two quiet strokes from the back.

Joel matched them and let the old rhythm find his shoulders.

Behind them, the landing slipped out of sight. The truck vanished first, then the signboard, then the road. The last thing Joel saw was the pale mud where they had stood arranging gear as if arrangement could hold. For now each sound still had its body: Alex's uneven stroke, Will's lure ticking against a rod guide, Mike's quiet correction from the back of the other canoe.

Then the trees closed the gap, and there was only water ahead.


# Chapter 3 - First Water

The first lake was narrow enough that it never let them forget shore.

Spruce leaned over the right side, black at the waterline and green where the sun struck the higher limbs. To the left, granite shelves came down in pale steps, some dry and white, some dark with old water marks. Small cedars had found cracks in the rock and grown there crookedly, as if the place had allowed them but had not made room. Fog lifted in torn strips ahead of the canoes and came apart when the sun reached it. Each cleared patch showed another bend, another short reach of black-green water, another piece of the route that looked obvious only after they were inside it.

Joel paddled from the back and let Alex set whatever rhythm he could manage in front.

Alex was strong. That helped. He was also uneven, which did not. He would paddle hard for thirty strokes, shoulders and back doing more than skill required, then look off toward a bird or snag or some imagined thing in the trees and let the blade rest on the gunwale until the canoe began to yaw. Joel corrected without saying much. A quiet draw. A little push on the left. A stroke held longer than it needed. The boat came back.

That was the arrangement outside the canoe too. Alex made weather in front of him, and Joel trimmed from behind. He had been doing some version of it for years: letting louder men fill the air, then managing consequence after it arrived. Mara had called it useful once, standing in the half-painted second bedroom with a tape gun hanging from her wrist, and evasive the next week when he let her answer the venue email because she was better at sounding certain. She had not said it cruelly. That was worse. She wanted him beside her in the life they were making, not trailing it with a quiet corrective stroke. Both times she had been right enough to irritate him. He liked Alex in pieces and resented him in waves.

After ten minutes Alex glanced over his shoulder.

"You're doing something back there."

"I am."

"Feels judgmental."

"That's steering."

"Same vibe."

"Paddle on the left."

Alex switched sides with a small flourish and dipped the blade too far forward, splashing his own knee.

"Was that wrong?"

"It moved us."

"Spoken like a man protecting morale."

Ahead of them, Mike and Will moved cleaner. Mike was in back, compact and economical, doing less than he seemed to. Will sat up front with a rod laid along the side of the canoe and kept turning his head toward shadows near shore. He paddled well when he remembered paddling was the point. Every patch of downed wood, every dark pocket under alder, every ring on the surface took part of his attention.

Mike's voice came over the water. "Will."

"I see it."

"You see what?"

"Nothing. That's the problem."

"Paddle."

"I am paddling."

"You're browsing."

Alex smiled. "Fishing is just aquatic browsing."

Will did not turn. "That's not terrible."

"Don't encourage him," Mike said.

The sound carried strangely on the lake. Not wrong. Just widened. Mike's words lay flat over the water. Will's answer came back thinner from the granite, scraped down by distance. The men were all visible, which made the distortion harmless. Joel could look at a mouth and give the voice back to it. Fog and rock and cold morning air could take a sound apart and hand it back changed.

He had heard his own paddle drip behind him before. He had heard ducks sound like men coughing. The woods were not quiet. They made quiet men look busier than they were.

They kept to the right side because Mike had said the first portage would be easier to find from that shore. The lake ran long and crooked, more like a widened river than open water. Twice they passed narrow cuts that looked like routes until they drew close and saw deadfall and shallow weeds. A beaver lodge rose on the left bank, sticks piled high and silvered at the tips. The water around it held a scum of chewed bark. A row of peeled sticks floated near the entrance like something sorted by small hands.

Alex pointed with his paddle.

"That occupied?"

Will said, "Probably."

"Do beavers attack?"

Mike said, "If you annoy them."

Alex looked back at Joel. "Why is that always the answer?"

"It's the honest answer to most animal questions."

Alex dipped his blade again. "I grew up around animals that could kill you. Beavers feel insulting."

"Beavers can ruin your day," Will said.

"Lots of things can ruin my day. Plain oatmeal. Small talk. Men named Todd."

Mike looked over his shoulder. "Paddle."

Alex paddled.

The lake narrowed further. The water changed from black-green to brown where a creek entered through grass and alder. Mud smell came through the cold, rich and sour. A kingfisher rattled from a dead branch and crossed in front of them, blue-gray and quick, sounding offended by their presence.

Joel watched the bird go. For one clean second he wanted nothing from the trip except this: water under the boat, a bird's harsh little engine of a voice, the old stupid relief of being required by a task.

"You coming or writing a poem?" Alex asked.

Joel dipped his paddle. "Waiting for you to remember yours."

"See, that's the wounded quiet-guy thing I'm talking about."

"Paddle."

Alex laughed and paddled. The canoe straightened.

For the first hour Joel felt the old pleasure return in the muscles before it reached the mind. The pull of the blade. The little shift of weight in the hips. The corrected angle. The canoe sliding as if effort were being translated into something cleaner than effort. In Madison, his days had become too square: rooms, screens, calendar blocks, errands fitted between calls, wedding decisions that seemed small until there were fifty of them. He wanted the house. He wanted Mara's shoes by the door, her coffee cooling on the counter, a child's future they had not yet named aloud. Wanting it did not tell him where the boxes went.

Here the work was dumb and whole. Push water. Move north. Avoid rock. Keep gear dry. Watch weather. Eat when possible. That was why he came. Or part of why. The trip let the old group exist in the tense it understood. Present work. Present weather. Present jokes. A man could become useful without saying what ordinary life had asked him to decide.

Mike raised his paddle ahead and pointed left.

Will said something Joel could not hear. Mike pointed again, sharper this time. Then he cleared his throat, small and official even across the water.

"Portage is after the bend," Mike called. "Stay right until the dead pine. It opens up after this first tight stretch."

Will turned partway. "Define opens up."

"Bigger water."

"That's a description, not a definition."

"It's water. It does not need a glossary."

Alex turned. "What's the verdict, back there?"

"Portage soon."

"Like portage portage?"

"No. Decorative kind."

"I'm asking because backpacking made me emotionally prepared for carrying things, but not boats."

"You'll be fine."

"I love when people lie gently."

They followed Mike and Will toward a low opening in the shore that did not look like a portage until they were almost on it. A strip of mud, two rocks placed by human hands, alder pressed back enough to suggest a path. There was an old blaze on a spruce trunk, nearly healed over. The mark had not disappeared, but the tree had been working on it for years, drawing bark over the wound without caring what men needed it to mean. The landing smelled of muck and wet leaves.

Mike got out first in calf-deep water and held the canoe. Will stepped into the shallows, slipped once in his hiking sandals, recovered, and swore with unnecessary precision.

"Rock," he said.

Mike looked down. "Yes."

"Under silt."

"Where rocks live."

Joel brought their canoe in beside them. Alex reached for a branch and missed it. The canoe swung sideways.

"Left," Joel said.

"My left or boat left?"

"Your actual left."

"Good. I only brought the one."

Joel planted his paddle and held them steady. Alex got out with one foot in water and one on rock, paused in a shape no man could hold long, then committed to the water with a splash.

"Dry feet lasted ninety-three minutes," he said.

Will looked over. "That's optimistic."

"Emotionally dry."

The first landing took their voices and lowered them. Alder crowded the shore, and the lake behind them was already partly hidden by leaves. Mud took their footprints and filled them with shining brown water. A few dragonflies moved low over the surface, bright-bodied and mechanical.

They unloaded enough for the portage. Mike did not want to double back more than necessary. Will did not want to overload the boats on the trail and crack something. This produced ten minutes of argument beside the water while mosquitoes found them in the alder shade.

"We single portage if we're not stupid about it," Mike said.

"We double portage if we're not stupid about it," Will said.

Alex slapped at his wrist. "Those sound suspiciously similar."

Joel lifted one pack and weighed it by feel. "How long is it?"

"Quarter mile," Mike said.

Will made a small dry cough into his shoulder.

Mike turned. "What?"

"Old report said closer to half."

"Old report included whining."

"You can't correct distance with attitude."

"Watch me."

Joel looked at the path. It rose almost immediately from the landing, muddy at first, then root-bound under spruce. He could see ten yards before it bent and disappeared. Quarter mile, half mile. Both were short if the footing was good. Both were long under a canoe.

"Let's not make the first portage heroic," he said.

Mike gave him a look that asked whether he was taking Will's side. Joel ignored it and pulled his pack from the canoe.

They decided on a modified double portage, which meant everyone got to act as if compromise had occurred. First trip: heavy packs, rods, paddles, map case, the food barrel carried between Mike and Alex on a strap because neither trusted the other with it alone. Second trip: canoes and whatever had been left to punish them for optimism.

The food barrel latch snapped when Mike checked it, one clean plastic click under the alder.

"Still closed?" Will asked.

"It was closed before," Mike said.

"That is often when things stop being closed."

"Stop helping."

Alex slipped one end of the barrel strap over his shoulder. "I want the record to show I am carrying group food and therefore group morale."

Mike took the other end. "It's mostly tortillas and oats."

"Exactly. Fragile morale."

The trail was worse than it had looked and better than it could have been. Mud at the start, then roots, then a rise over granite where the moss had been scraped by other sandals and boots and never quite recovered. Spruce branches brushed their shoulders. A few birches leaned pale and thin among the darker trees. The air inside the portage held less wind and more insect. Sound shortened there. It did not travel so much as stop in the brush.

The trail was narrow enough that the order of men became a kind of statement. Mike in front because he had claimed the route. Alex close behind because the food barrel forced him there. Will then Joel, old church boys in middle age, one talking too much and one answering less than he meant. Behind them the lake went on being itself, out of sight and unbothered.

Will walked ahead of Joel with two rods in one hand and a pack that looked too high on his shoulders.

"You good?" Joel asked.

"I'm calibrated."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the answer before the answer."

Up ahead, Mike said, "Step left here."

Alex said, "Your left or my left?"

"Trail left."

"That's not a thing."

"It is if you understand trails."

"I understand mountains. Trails there have dignity."

"These have water."

"Water has no dignity."

The food barrel knocked against a tree with a hollow plastic thud.

Mike said, "Watch it."

Alex said, "The tree attacked me."

"Tree had right of way."

Joel walked with his head slightly down, watching footing. Mud took the tread of his hiking sandals and gave back a sucking sound. His heel scraped once on stone, a dry little slip under the wet noise. The pack straps bit into the muscle above his collarbones. Sweat started under his fleece despite the cool morning. His body had not yet accepted the trip. It was still negotiating.

Halfway through the portage, the trail crossed an open patch of rock. Sun came down clean there. Joel stopped and shifted the pack to ease one shoulder. Through black trunks he could see a narrow piece of the lake behind them, bright where the fog had burned off. The canoes were hidden by alder at the landing. The water looked empty in the way water looked empty when a man was too far from it to know.

A knock came from behind him.

Once.

Hard against hollow. A small sound, ordinary and exact enough to make his body answer before his mind did.

Joel turned his head.

There was no wind strong enough to move anything heavy. The men ahead were still ahead, Mike's voice low and irritated, Alex answering with too much brightness. Will was just in front of Joel, close enough that Joel could see sweat darkening the back of his shirt and the two rod tips trembling beside his shoulder.

Will stopped ahead of him. "What?"

Joel listened. Mike and Alex kept moving ahead, still arguing about whether rice was a side dish or a failure of imagination. Their voices came from where their bodies were. The knock had not.

"You hear that?"

Will held still. His face changed into the attentive blank he used when listening for a small mechanical fault or a fish taking line. The woods gave them insects, breath, the soft movement of water too far behind them to see.

"Hear what?" Will asked.

Joel looked back through the trees. The lake flashed between spruce trunks. No canoe showed. No person. No movement except water moving around what he could not see.

"Nothing," Joel said.

Will waited a second longer. "Branch maybe."

"Probably."

"You sure?"

Joel smiled faintly. "That's your job."

"My job is to introduce uncertainty, not resolve it."

"Then you're doing great."

They moved on.

He expected the sound to come again. It did not. One sound was not a pattern. In the woods, a single knock had more possible bodies than a man could count, and most of them were boring.

At the far end, the portage dropped to a second landing, better than the first. Flat rock sloped into a narrower piece of water that ran north between alder and low marsh. The lake they had crossed was behind them now. Ahead, the route tightened into something that might be called a creek if no one wanted to sound grand.

Mike and Alex set the food barrel down. Alex bent with his hands on his knees.

"Quarter mile, my ass."

Will came in behind Joel and looked at Mike.

Mike said, "It was a quarter mile emotionally."

"That's not how distance works."

Alex pointed at Will without looking up. "There it is."

They drank water and went back for the boats.

The return trip felt shorter because their bodies knew the path and nothing heavy sat on their backs. The sound of their own steps changed after they had walked a trail once. First pass, every root was a question. Second pass, the body had made a rough map. Step here. Duck there. Mud on the left. Rock on the right. Branch at eye level if you stopped paying attention.

At the first landing, the two canoes lay where they had left them, half-hidden in alder and shadow. Water lapped softly against one hull. A painter rope tapped once against the front of it, light and quick. Then water shifted the boat a thumb's width, and the rope touched again.

Joel looked at it longer than necessary. There was an answer, or near enough. Rope. Water. Boat shifting. A small hard tap, light enough to seem more important than it was. Refusing it would have felt theatrical, and Joel did not like theatrical men unless they were someone else's problem.

Mike took the first canoe with Will. They lifted it, rolled it up, and set the center yoke on Will's shoulders. Will adjusted under the weight and took a few testing steps.

"You want me to take it?" Mike asked.

"No."

"You sure?"

"Yes."

"You're walking crooked."

"I have a canoe on my head."

Mike reached up anyway and moved one loose strap so it would not swing into Will's face.

"Better?" he asked.

Will stood still under the boat, surprised into honesty. "Yeah."

"Try not to die before lunch."

"That's the warmth I came for."

Alex watched them go. "That looks bad."

"It is," Joel said.

"Do we all pretend it's spiritual?"

"Mostly."

Joel and Alex carried the second canoe. Alex wanted to try the yoke and lasted thirty yards before admitting that backpacking had not prepared him for carrying a boat upside down while blind to the sky and personally hated by every branch on the route. Joel took over without comment.

Alex did not make a production of surrendering it, which Joel appreciated despite himself. He simply stopped, braced the canoe with both hands, and said, "This is stupid in a way I respect." Then he let Joel step under the yoke as if they had planned the exchange. That was the other thing about Alex. He could demand the room and still know when to hand over weight.

Under the canoe, the world became a strip of ground and feet. Joel saw mud, roots, Alex's sandals ahead when Alex carried the paddles, Mike's heels beyond him when the trail straightened. The canoe amplified some sounds and removed others. His breath came back at him. Brush scratched the hull inches from his ears. Once, a branch dragged along the canoe's side with a dry fingernail rasp. A man under a boat became mostly hearing and ankles.

He thought of the knock again and was annoyed with himself.

There were four of them. Two canoes. Paddles. Ropes. Trees. Water. Any wilderness trip produced a hundred hollow sounds before lunch. The mind kept the ones that arrived while it was facing the wrong way, then pretended keeping them was judgment.

At the open rock, Alex stopped ahead and looked back.

"You alive?"

Joel shifted under the yoke. "Keep moving."

"That's not a yes."

"It's canoe for yes."

Alex waited until the trail widened by a foot, then reached up and lifted the front of the canoe over a leaning branch before it could scrape Joel's face.

"See?" Alex said. "I provide value."

"Put that on your resume."

"I will. Under wilderness leadership."

"Under branch awareness."

"Specialized field."

At the far landing they set the boats down and loaded again. This took longer than unloading because the order now mattered. Will's fishing rods had to be accessible without becoming spears. Alex's food could not be buried under the first-aid kit because he claimed low blood sugar made him philosophical. Rain gear near the top. Map case where Mike could reach it from the back. Joel's day bag behind his seat. Spare paddle along the left side. Every object went where the next hour expected it to be.

Mike crouched by his canoe and pressed the Highlander key pouch inside the left side of his PFD, as if the key might have become wet through suspicion alone. Joel saw the gesture and looked away. The key belonged to another day, another shore. Thinking of it here thinned the present route, as if the whole week were a line stretched between two locked vehicles.

They pushed off into the narrow water just before noon.

The route after the portage was not much wider than two canoes. Alder leaned over both sides, and in places the branches met overhead. The water moved slowly enough that they still had to paddle. It was darker here, stained by leaves and old beaver work. The bottom showed in the shallows as a wavering brown skin over mud and stone.

The channel changed the scale of the day. On the lake, each man's voice had room to flatten and travel. Here every sound had edges. A paddle drip ticked near the ear. A branch scraped a pack and seemed to happen inside the boat. Their small jokes lost distance and became breath, close and human, under the alder roof.
