Joel could hear Alex swallow water from his bottle, Will's reel shift against the thwart, Mike take in air before deciding not to speak.

Will had his rod in his lap within five minutes.

Mike said, "No."

"I'm not fishing."

"You are holding a fishing rod."

"I'm preparing to fish."

"Prepare to paddle."

Will looked back over his shoulder. "There is a difference."

"Not to the boat."

Alex whispered, loudly enough for everyone, "There's trouble in the marriage."

Joel corrected their angle around a half-submerged log. Alex's strokes had grown lazy after the portage, but he admitted it without shame.

"My arms filed a complaint."

"Denied," Joel said.

"You're a harsh captain."

"I'm not a captain."

"Back guy."

"Still no."

"Rear admiral."

"Absolutely not."

The channel bent right, then left, then right again. Mike called back directions in ordinary language. "Stay left." "Come right." "Shallow middle." Will argued with some and obeyed most. Joel followed their line when it made sense and chose his own when it did not. He liked being in back because the back let him see the whole boat's mistake before it arrived, and because correction could pass for patience from there.

Once, the channel pinched so tight that both canoes had to wait while Mike and Will scraped through a slot between a drowned log and a mat of grass. The hull made a low rasp against hidden gravel. Mike leaned right, then corrected.

"Slow," he called back.

Alex looked over his shoulder. "Slow like cautious or slow like we're about to learn something?"

"Just slow."

"I hate the minimalist school of instruction."

Joel let the current take them a foot, then stopped the canoe with a brace. The branch tips ticked against Alex's sleeve. The sound was small and close. Behind them, somewhere back in the alder, water dropped from a lifted branch in a neat series of taps, counting down to nothing. Joel waited for another hollow note and disliked that he was waiting.

Alex turned once and saw Joel looking past him.

"You always this serious on water?"

"Yes."

"Good to know."

"You always this chatty?"

"Also yes."

"Good to know."

Alex smiled and faced forward. For a few minutes he paddled better.

They stopped for lunch on a low gravel bar where the channel widened under a stand of jack pine. The place was not a campsite, just a place to get out. Enough stone to stand on, enough sun to push back the bugs, enough current that the water sounded clean over the shallows.

Will had a line in the water before Mike opened the food barrel.

"I thought you were hungry," Mike said.

"I am spiritually hungry for fish."

"You catch one now, we're not cooking it."

"I didn't ask you to be involved."

"You're in my canoe."

"That's a logistical arrangement, not a moral framework."

Mike opened the food barrel. The latch snapped up, then the second one. Two small sounds, useful and exact. Alex sat on a rock and opened his private bag of bland food. He pulled out tortillas, peanut butter, and a packet of tuna he regarded with uncertainty.

"Branching out?" Joel asked.

"This is advanced work."

"That tuna has lemon pepper."

"I know. I'm not afraid."

Will cast once into the seam of current beside a downed branch. The spinner flashed and vanished. He worked it back with small motions of his wrist, face emptied of all the restless talk he used everywhere else. Fishing made him quiet in a way nothing else did. Or not quiet exactly. Directed. Joel had seen him look like that over a guitar tuner, a bad knot, a Greek verb in the church basement when they were fourteen. Some men could only rest inside a problem small enough to hold still.

Mike noticed Joel noticing.

"Only time he shuts up," Mike said.

Will did not look back. "I can hear you."

"I know."

"I choose not to respond because I am spiritually large."

"You're five-nine."

"Five-ten with purpose."

The reel ticked under Will's thumb. Line whispered through the guides. The lure hit the current with a small bright plink and vanished as if the water had closed over a coin.

Alex took a bite of tuna in a tortilla and made a face.

Mike said, "Too spicy?"

"Too narrative."

Joel laughed. He could not help it. Alex looked offended and pleased.

It irritated Joel to enjoy him that easily. Alex worked for reaction the way Will worked for precision and Mike worked for control. Sometimes the work showed. Sometimes, like now, it arrived as warmth in the middle of miles, mud, and Mike's watch. The joke did not mean much. That was part of its usefulness.

The sun held while they ate. The day had warmed enough that steam rose faintly from wet cuffs and sandal straps. Bugs worked the shaded edges but left the gravel mostly alone. A breeze moved down the channel and brought mud, pine pitch, and old water. Joel sat with his elbows on his knees and watched the current fold around a stone.

For a while nobody needed anything from anyone.

Then Mike stood. "Ten minutes."

Alex groaned. "We just sat down."

"We have miles."

"We always have miles. Miles are your love language."

Will reeled in. "He's right, unfortunately."

"Thank you," Mike said.

"I said unfortunately."

They packed lunch, refilled bottles through the filter, checked the boats, and pushed on. The food barrel latch clicked shut again. Joel noticed because the latch had become part of the day, as ordinary as paddle drip or Will's reel.

The afternoon became more physical. The channel twisted and shoaled. Twice they had to get out and walk the canoes through ankle-deep gravel. Once Alex stepped into mud up to mid-calf and came out missing a sandal for two dramatic seconds before the suction gave up with an obscene sound.

"I have been personally attacked," he said.

Will turned in the front of Mike's canoe. "By geology."

"Geology has had it in for me since Alaska."

Mike said, "Stop standing where it's black."

"The entire bottom is black."

"Then be smarter than the bottom."

Joel held their canoe steady while Alex rinsed his sandal. The water was cold enough to make Joel's fingers ache when he reached down to help clear mud from the straps. Alex looked at him, then at Mike and Will ahead, as if checking whether gratitude would be witnessed and therefore ruined.

"You don't have to."

"I know."

"Very Midwestern of you."

"I'm engaged. We covered this."

Alex laughed, but quieter than usual. "Right. Terminal condition."

For a moment the performance fell away from him. He was just a man with one bare foot on gravel, one muddy sandal in hand, calf hair slicked flat by black water. He looked younger without the noise, and more tired. Then he tightened the strap, stamped once to settle his foot, and put the face back on.

"Gentlemen," Alex said, louder, "my foot has returned from war."

"Good," Mike said. "Tell it to paddle."

By three, the channel began to widen again. The alder fell back. Ahead, through a low opening, Joel saw broader water and a strip of pale rock beyond it. Mike's canoe drifted while he checked the map case. Will used the pause to cast toward a submerged log.

Mike said, "Put the rod away."

"One cast."

"You said that six casts ago."

"Time is a construct."

"So is swimming back for your rod if I throw it."

Will reeled in and set the rod down with exaggerated tenderness.

Mike looked at Joel. "First camp should be another hour. Maybe less."

"Granite point?"

"If nobody's there."

Alex turned around. "And if somebody's there?"

"Then we keep going."

"How far?"

Mike did not answer right away.

Will did it for him. "Far enough to make us complain."

"That could be anywhere," Alex said.

Joel looked toward the widening water. The wind touched it lightly. Not enough to worry about. Enough to show direction. The sun had shifted west and softened. The day was still good, but it was no longer unused.

They paddled out of the channel and into the next lake.

It opened slowly, not all at once. First a widening, then a view past a point, then water running north in a long dark reach with granite rising on the left and low spruce on the right. Far ahead, too far to matter yet, a bird lifted from shore and crossed low over the lake. The canoes separated a little in the larger water. Mike and Will moved toward the left-hand point, and Joel let their boat run in the small wake.

Alex rested his paddle across the canoe.

"Okay," he said. "This part does not suck."

Joel let the boat glide. "Careful. Mike might hear."

"He already knows. That's what makes him insufferable."

Ahead, Mike lifted his paddle and pointed toward the left shore where the granite point pushed into the lake. Will called something back, but the wind took it. Mike pointed again.

Joel angled the canoe to follow.

Behind them, from somewhere near the channel mouth, came a single hollow knock.

Not loud. Not dramatic. Just hard against hollow, or a branch shifting in water. The same family of sound as before, if a man wanted to make families out of sounds. It was small enough to explain and placed well enough to matter.

Joel turned.

The channel behind them lay empty. Alder. Brown water. A few floating leaves turning in the slow current. The place they had just come through held still in the afternoon light. The sound had not echoed or wandered. That was what bothered him. It had arrived with a clean edge, as if the afternoon had put one knuckle to the hull and waited for him to turn.

Alex looked back too. "What was that?"

Mike and Will were ahead, their canoe moving toward the point. Will had his paddle across his lap, not in the water. Mike's blade flashed once on the right. Their boat gave him nothing he could use.

"Probably a branch," Joel said.

Alex waited, listening. For once he did not improve the silence.

The sound did not come again.

"Sure," Alex said.

He turned forward and took up his paddle.

Joel stayed looking back for one second more. The channel was already closing into distance. Sound carried over water. Branches knocked. Ropes tapped. The world had joints and hinges everywhere if you listened long enough, and most of them did not mean anything.

He dipped his paddle and brought the canoe back on line.

They went on toward the first camp with enough daylight to feel successful. Behind them, the channel made no more sound. That helped. Joel let it help while it still could.


# Chapter 4 - Granite Shelf

The granite point was empty in the way a campsite could be empty. No tent. No smoke. No voices carrying over the water. Still, the place had marks before they reached it.

Mike saw it first and lifted his paddle. The gesture was small and certain, blade angled toward the left shore where pale rock came down in shelves through jack pine and black spruce. A few red pines stood higher than the rest, long-trunked and clean-limbed, catching the last good light in their tops. Below them, the point sloped into water dark enough to double the trees.

"Camp," Mike called.

Nobody argued.

They had been paddling long enough that even Alex let the word stand by itself. The afternoon had gone soft and slanted. The lake was not wide, but the wind had found its length and pushed little black wrinkles toward the boats. The work was not hard anymore. It was only constant. Paddle, correct, watch Mike's boat, keep Alex moving, keep the front from wandering toward shore.

Joel felt the day in his shoulders. Not pain yet. A thickening. His hands had gone soft between trips and were relearning friction. The place between thumb and forefinger burned when he shifted his grip. His hiking sandals were wet, then drying, then wet again, and his feet had the swollen, gritty feel they got after a day in water and mud. At home, effort had become calendar work, a thing arranged around calls and dinners and whatever needed signing. Here it had gone back into skin.

Alex turned halfway around.

"That real camp or Mike camp?"

"What's Mike camp?"

"Any place Mike says is fine because he no longer wants to look."

Joel glanced past him toward the point. There was a break in the trees, a flat place above the rock, and a dark line where people had dragged boats before. "Looks real."

"God exists."

"You don't believe that."

"I believe in flat ground."

Ahead, Mike and Will angled in first. Will had his paddle across his lap and his fishing rod already in one hand, as if the day might otherwise forget his priorities. Mike said something Joel could not make out. Will said something back. Mike pointed to the landing with more force than the situation required.

Joel brought their canoe around the lee side of the point. The water quieted near shore. Granite showed under it in gray plates with seams of weed between them. He let the boat glide until the front touched stone.

Alex looked down. "Elegant."

"Out."

"I'm appreciating."

"Appreciate from shore."

Alex stepped carefully, one sandal on slick rock, one foot still in the canoe. He held the position for half a second too long, arms spread.

"Don't do a full performance," Joel said.

"I'm giving the landing what it deserves."

"You're giving me a problem."

Alex shifted his weight, got both feet on rock, and held the canoe while Joel stepped out. The hull bumped once against the granite. Hollow. Familiar. Nothing like the knock from the channel behind them and enough like it that Joel disliked himself for making the comparison.

He pulled the canoe up higher.

Mike was already unloading.

"Boats above the waterline," he said. "Wind shifts tonight, I don't want to hear them banging all night."

"You don't want to hear anything all night," Will said.

"Correct."

Alex dragged a pack onto the rock. "Is this where I ask if we can sit down?"

"No," Mike said.

"Good. I won't."

"You just did."

"I asked if this was where."

Joel lifted his day bag and set it near the first flat place above the landing. The campsite was better than he expected: a granite shelf with dirt pockets for tents, two good trees back from the fire ring, a pair of red pines on the water side, and a rough bench made from a log dragged against stone. Someone had left a ring of blackened rocks and a half-burned split log. A faint trail led back into spruce where previous campers had gone for privacy or firewood or both. Small limbs had been cut and stacked under a rock overhang. Old, damp, probably smoky, but still wood.

There were signs of people and not much else. A twist of blue cord tied to a branch. A square of foil half-buried in ash. Boot prints softened by rain and time. One rusted bottle cap wedged in a crack. Out here, human presence did not claim a place. It left small failures behind and asked the next men to call them proof.

Joel had always found comfort in that, foolish as it was. The ring of stones. The hacked branch. The scrap of cord another party had abandoned because it was too short to be useful and too useful, at the moment, to throw away properly. Someone had stood here cold or hungry or damp and had made these same arrangements. The place had accepted them without promising anything.

"Not bad," Will said.

Mike cleared his throat, the small dry preface he used before making a fact out of a preference. "It opens up after this."

Will turned. "What does?"

"Tomorrow. After the next narrow section. More room, better water."

"You said that about today."

"And was I wrong?"

Will looked at the lake, the rock, the trees, the reasonable camp they had reached with enough daylight to unload like civilized men. "Annoyingly, not completely."

Alex raised a finger. "Put that on his memorial shirt."

"Everyone's obsessed with my memorial shirt," Mike said.

"You make people imagine it," Will said.

They unloaded everything before doing anything comfortable. That was Mike's rule and, worse, a good one. Packs came out. Rods came out. Paddles went under the pulled-up canoes, tucked out of the way and off the grit. Mike took off his PFD last, pressed his thumb once against the small yellow pouch clipped inside the left side, then hung it over a low branch where he could see it from the fire ring. The Highlander key belonged to the far end of the route. Mike kept bringing that shore forward with his hand.

The food barrel was carried to the middle of camp and then moved because Mike said the middle of camp was where idiots put food. Will opened it anyway to get the dinner bag. The latch made its clean plastic snap, small and final. Joel put the sound with the rest of camp: hull on stone, rope on bark, zipper teeth, water moving below them. Even plastic carried.

He set his pack near the two trees he liked and looked out over the lake instead of opening it. The far shore was darkening. Sun still held in the pine tops, but the water had lost its brightness and now seemed to store the shade coming off the trees. He thought of taking a picture for Mara, proof that the trip was doing what he had claimed it would do, then left the phone sealed away. A loon called from the north, long and hollow. Another answered, or the first call came back broken by distance. The notes overlapped for a second and made the lake feel larger than it was.

Then the lake went quiet in the way water could, not silent but refusing to explain the sounds it had just carried.

"You coming or writing a poem?"

Joel turned.

Alex stood with a bundle of tent poles over one shoulder and the tent bag under his arm, smiling at his own usefulness.

"Neither if I can avoid it," Joel said.

"You looked deeply employed."

"I was waiting for you to do something correctly."

Alex handed him the tent bag. "Then your vigil ends."

They pitched the tent before the mosquitoes took full possession of camp. Mike and Alex took it. Joel and Will both had hammocks. Joel had been camping in a hammock almost exclusively the last few seasons, partly because he slept better off the ground and partly because he liked having a system that was his alone. Straps, suspension, tarp, underquilt if weather turned. Tree to tree. Line tight. Sag right. Tarp low enough to matter.

The arrangement gave him something close to privacy without requiring a conversation.

Will had brought a hammock too, mostly for lounging. He still slept in the tent unless the weather was perfect or he had talked himself into an experiment. Will liked owning options. Joel liked eliminating them.

Mike watched Joel wrap a tree strap around a red pine. "You actually sleeping in that thing?"

"Yes."

"All week?"

"That's the plan."

"Plans are how men experience hope," Alex said.

Will looked over. "That was mine."

"You left it unattended."

Joel clipped the carabiner and pulled the suspension through. The webbing made a dry scrape against bark.

At home, this kind of system annoyed Mara if he talked about it too long. She liked competence fine. She had liked the hammock too, once, on a state-park weekend when rain trapped them under its tarp and they passed a dented thermos back and forth while other campers fled to cars. They had laughed at the steam on each other's faces and at the ridiculous closeness of the rig, two adults pretending a nylon roof was a room. What she disliked was the private satisfaction he took in having one more way not to need a room, a bed, a decision made with another person. Joel had argued that a hammock was only a hammock, and even then had known he was lying by scale, not kind.

Mike shook his head. "Ground exists for a reason."

"So do rocks," Joel said. "I don't sleep on those either."

Will had his own hammock half out of its sack and was looking at the trees behind camp with a fisherman's version of commitment, which meant one eye was still on the water.

"You setting yours up?" Joel asked.

"Probably for sitting."

"You brought a chair."

"I contain multitudes."

"You contain redundant systems."

"That's preparedness."

The old Will would have made a larger case for it. He would have explained load redundancy or failure points or how most people confused comfort with utility. This Will shook the hammock once and frowned at a tangle in the suspension. Since lunch, his talk had come in brief, uneven patches.

"I keep thinking I forgot to check something," Will said.

"You did," Joel said. "Your judgment."

"No, a real thing."

Will pulled the wrong loop tight and made the knot worse. He looked at it as if it had personally changed the terms of the agreement.

Joel knew about the job because Will had told him in pieces before the trip, over texts and one late call when he had made jokes about severance until the jokes ran out. Quality control for an LED company was not a thing Joel had imagined anyone losing sleep over until Will lost it. Here, in camp, Will still had the shape of a man who knew how things were supposed to work. Filters. Knots. Rod guides. Maps. He could find the failed part in any system small enough to hold in his hands. The larger one had let him go without showing its work.

Mike and Alex argued their tent into position. The ground looked flatter than it was. Every place that seemed level had a root, a knob of rock, or a shallow runnel where rain would collect if the weather changed.

"Door facing water," Alex said.

"Door out of the wind," Mike said.

"I want a view."

"You'll be unconscious."

"I dream visually."

Mike stared at him.

Will, crouched over his hammock sack, said, "Put his side facing the water. If a bear comes, it gets him first."

Alex nodded. "Finally. Logic."

Joel fed one guyline around a trunk and tied it off. The line tightened with a small creak. Behind him, Mike zipped the tent door open, then closed it, testing it the way men tested zippers in the woods: not because they distrusted the zipper exactly, but because a zipper that failed at night became everybody's problem.

The zipper ran around the curve with a dry insect sound.

Mike looked at Alex. "Don't yank on it."

"I have used zippers before."

"Not always well."

Alex spread his hands. "Don't babysit the zipper."

Joel glanced over.

The line got the little laugh it wanted, then disappeared into the general work of camp. That was how most of Alex moved through the world: given away fast, trusting there would be more.

They staked the tent, tightened lines, and set sleeping pads inside. The pads squeaked and sighed as they unrolled. Joel shook out his hammock, clipped it, sat once to set the sag, and stood again. He pitched the tarp above it even though the sky was clear. Early September could change its mind without warning, and the first night of any trip was not the night to trust a blue sky.

Will wandered toward the water with his rod before deciding whether he was sleeping in nylon or in the air.

Mike saw him. "Water first."

"I'm at the water."

"For drinking."

Will stopped, considered whether he could argue it, and came back for the filter.

They divided chores without formally dividing them. Mike and Will handled water because Will had opinions about filtering and Mike had opinions about where the bag should hang. Joel handled dinner because he usually did. He had a dehydrator at home and had become the kind of man who could talk about sauce leather without shame. Jerky at a hundred sixty for five or six hours. Sauce lower and longer. Sweet potatoes sliced evenly or not at all.

The others made fun of him until they ate what he brought. Then they asked how much was left.

Tonight was dried tortellini with Joel's dehydrated red sauce, parmesan packets, and a little oil. Easy first-night food. No fish, no coals, no timing beyond boil, soak, simmer, stir. The more ambitious meals could wait.

Joel set the sauce pieces in a pot with water to soften. They looked like dark red bark.

Alex came over and peered in. "That food?"

"Eventually."

"It looks like something peeled off a canoe."

"You'll like it."

"You always say that."

"I'm often right."

Will called from the water, "The jerky's already open, by the way."

Joel turned. "It was sealed."

"Was."

Mike came up from shore chewing. "Good batch."

"You opened my dinner-adjacent morale system?"

"It was labeled jerky."

"That is not consent."

Alex walked to the food bag with sudden interest. "Is this the Doc's stuff?"

"Doc's-ish," Joel said. "I changed it."

Mike took another piece. "Don't change it more."

Will appeared with the gravity bag, water dripping from one elbow. "Agreed. This is one of your useful traits."

Joel looked at him. "My useful trait is meat dehydration?"

"One of them."

"I'll update the wedding vows."

Alex bit into a piece of jerky and nodded with genuine seriousness. "Okay. That's good. That has appropriate intensity."

"Food can have opinions now?" Joel said.

"Jerky is allowed. Tuna is not."

Alex went to the rock overhang and came back with an armload of the cut sticks, some damp, some dry enough to matter. He made a show of staggering under them, but he had sorted the good pieces to the top without saying so. The performance hid work. It did not cancel it.

The stove came out when the water was ready. Mike set it on a flat patch of granite near the fire ring, screwed the fuel canister in, and turned the valve. The first click did nothing. The second click did nothing. The third click gave a blue flutter of flame.

"Modern civilization," Alex said.

"Don't get attached," Mike said.

Will hung the gravity bag from a low limb. Water ticked through the filter into the clean bag, one patient drop after another. "That limb is sketchy."

"It's holding," Mike said.

"Lots of things hold until they don't."

"Put that on your own memorial shirt," Alex said.

Dinner took its time coming back to life. Joel stirred the sauce as it softened and reddened the water. The smell came up slowly: tomato, garlic, dried herbs, a faint sweetness from hours in the dehydrator. Not home exactly. A camp version of home, thinner and smokier, valuable because it had crossed water in a sealed bag.

The mosquitoes came hard at dusk.

They came out of the spruce and up from the damp places between rocks, first a few, then a whining net around ears and wrists. The air had been comfortable and then it was not. Everyone put on long sleeves. Will found a head net and wore it like a man proving a point. Alex mocked him until one mosquito got him on the eyelid.

"Head net me," Alex said.

Will looked at him through mesh. "No."

"I apologize to the technology."

"Too late."

Joel poured the sauce over the tortellini and stirred. The pot got passed around with bowls. Mike ate first because he had cooked the noodles and because he was holding the spoon when it became food. Alex approached his bowl with caution and then ate quickly, which was the highest compliment he gave anything with visible seasoning.

"Too much?" Will asked.

Alex shook his head. "No. This has discipline."

"Food review accepted," Joel said.

Mike scraped his bowl. "Make more next time."

"I made enough."

"You made enough for the men you imagined us to be."

Will said, "We also have tuna and Triscuits if you want to ruin your own evening."

"Lunch food stays lunch food," Mike said.

Alex took another piece of jerky. "Unless society collapses."

"Day one," Joel said.

"Plenty of time."

They cleaned bowls with hot water and scraps of tortilla because Will claimed that was efficient and Mike claimed it was disgusting until he did it too. The stove cooled on granite. Someone set a mug down beside Joel and it made a small ceramic tick, bright against the lower water sounds.

"Careful," Joel said. "That one's mine."

Alex glanced at it. "I know. It looks judgmental."

"Everything I own can't be judgmental."

"I have evidence."

After dinner, Will went back to the shore with his rod. Nobody told him not to. Everyone knew by now that Will would fish if there was water, light, and enough motor function left in his arms.

The lake darkened by degrees. Across the water, the opposite shore went from green to black. The first stars appeared between the pine tops before the sky was fully night. The chill came up from the stone. It entered through damp sandal straps and wet cuffs and the places where sweat had dried into salt.

Will cast from the edge of the granite shelf. The lure made a small whip in the air and a softer sound when it touched water. He reeled slowly, patiently, his head net pushed back now that the bugs had thinned in the open breeze.

Alex sat near the fire ring with his fleece zipped to his chin. "You actually enjoy that?"

"Yes."

"You know there are fish at stores."

"They're not surprised to see you."

"That's the appeal?"

Will cast again. "Part of it."

Mike had built a small fire because the ring existed, the wood was there, and the evening had dropped cold enough to justify flame. It was not much. Thumb-thick sticks, pine curls, a few split pieces from the dry stack. The hatchet bit twice into one stubborn piece before Mike gave up and fed it half-burned ends from the old ring. The fire took with a low crackle, then settled.

Alex came alive in firelight.

He could not help it. The day's fatigue left him, or else the fire gave him a place to spend it. He told the story of a backpacking trip in the Cascades where a friend had packed six pounds of cheese and no rain jacket. He told it too long and too well, with voices, pauses, and a whole middle section about the cheese sweating through a stuff sack. Nobody cut him off. Everyone was tired, the fire was warm, and Will, despite himself, kept asking questions.

"Six pounds?" Will said.

"Minimum."

"That's not physically plausible."

"It was a brick. A dairy ingot."

"What kind of cheese?"

"Orange."

"That's not a kind."

"It was by day three."

Joel watched Alex hold them there. Even Mike listened, though he pretended not to by worrying the fire with a stick. Alex had a talent for making annoyance indistinguishable from attention. He required a room, a landing, a fire ring. He made you give it to him, and sometimes the gift became real after you had surrendered it.

Mike sat back against a log. "People don't know how to be uncomfortable anymore."

The line went into the small space around the fire and stayed there.

Will looked at him. "People who pack six pounds of cheese know discomfort intimately."

"That's not what I mean."

"I know what you mean."

"You don't."

"I do. You mean everybody's soft except whoever happens to be talking."

Mike's jaw moved once. "I mean people want the story without the part where things suck."

Alex opened his mouth, then closed it. For once, the story had been taken from him and used for something heavier.

Joel fed a stick into the fire and let the end catch. The remark was not about cheese. It was about the emails required to arrange four men in one place. Jobs. Partners. Houses. Knees. Money. The little negotiations by which adulthood took a man back one weekend at a time. It was also about Mike, whose life still fit in other people's basements, spare rooms, and borrowed driveways when the teardrop camper was cold. Hardness gave need a better posture.

Will said, quieter, "There's plenty of discomfort."

Mike snorted. "Sure."

The word found Will more directly than Mike seemed to intend, or exactly as directly. Will looked at the fire. In the orange light the hollows under his eyes showed darker. Joel thought of the late call again, Will laughing too hard about being between opportunities, about LinkedIn turning everyone into a motivational hostage, about knowing every failed diode on a board no one wanted anymore. The fire made his face look briefly older, then briefly like the boy Joel remembered from church basements and bad coffee, correcting adults under his breath because nobody else had noticed the thing that was wrong.

Joel waited for Will to answer. Will did not.

The fire settled, and one piece of pine clicked as sap heated inside it.

Alex leaned forward before the silence hardened.

"Speaking as a man who voluntarily packed powdered eggs after the Incident," he said, "I have known the valley of suffering."

Will coughed a laugh.

"We all agreed never again," Joel said.

"They were not eggs," Mike said.

"They were egg-adjacent," Will said.

"They were yellow punishment."

Alex nodded gravely. "We learned. We grew. We became oatmeal men."

"Pancakes tomorrow if morale requires civilization," Will said.

Mike looked at him. "Pancakes take time."

"Joy takes time."

"Breakfast bars take thirty seconds."

"Despair also takes thirty seconds."

Joel smiled and looked out toward the water.

The canoes were pulled high above shore, turned slightly on the slope so rain would not sit in them if weather came. The paddles lay underneath, blades tucked out of the open, shafts parallel in the shadows. One painter rope hung loose and touched the hull when the breeze moved it. Tap. Pause. Tap. Ordinary enough to vanish unless a man had already begun keeping track.

Will came back from the shore without a fish and pretended not to care.

"Nothing?" Mike said.

"I had one follow."

"Of course you did."

"It's a real phenomenon."

"So is lying."

Will set the rod against a tree and took his mug from the rock. Ceramic on granite. A bright little sound. He drank and winced. "Cold."

"Drink while it's hot," Mike said.

"I can't drink it while it's hot if I'm fishing."

"That sounds like a you problem."

"You can't just reassign physics to character flaws."

"I can do whatever I want in camp."

Alex stood and stretched. His joints popped. "I'm going to offend a tree. Nobody make it literature."

Joel looked up. The line was aimed at him, so he let it miss.

"Take a headlamp," Mike said.

"It's ten feet."

"Take a headlamp."

Alex patted his pockets, found the lamp, and put it on without turning it on. "Quit mothering me."

He walked to the edge of camp, past the tent and toward the darker line of spruce. His sandals scraped once on rock, then went quiet on duff. A moment later his headlamp clicked on, throwing a white band through low branches.

From the trees came his laugh. Not loud. More private than usual. Maybe he had nearly tripped. Maybe he had amused himself. Then the light shifted, and he said something they could not make out.

Will looked toward him. "You fall in?"

"No," Alex called. "I'm communing."

"With what?" Mike asked.

"None of your business."

"Don't wander."

"Quit-"

"We heard it," Will said.

Alex laughed again. The laugh came through spruce this time, broken by trunks and needles, not by motel wall or bridge concrete or bar noise. It sounded like him and less like him for the way the woods handled it. A familiar thing could survive distance and still come back thinned.

Joel turned his mug in his hands.

The lake gave a soft slap against the rocks below camp. Somewhere far off, a loon called once. Then again, too soon or only seeming too soon because the first note had not fully left Joel's ear. The second call bent thinner and lower, not wrong, only badly returned.

Will stopped with his mug halfway to his mouth.

Mike said, "Loon."

"I know," Will said.

Nobody said anything else.

Alex came back a minute later, headlamp still on, beam hitting their faces until Mike told him to point it down. The ordinary world returned with him: fleece, sandals, bad peanut-butter breath, the need to brush teeth and secure food and keep sparks out of nylon. His body in the light gave the laugh back its owner.

They cleaned camp.

The food barrel was latched and carried back from the tent and hammocks, not far enough to satisfy a ranger and far enough to satisfy tired men. Mike wedged it between two rocks where it could not roll. Will wanted it farther downwind. Joel helped move it once and then stopped helping the argument. The latch clicked again when Mike checked it.

Dry bags went under the tent vestibule or under Joel's hammock tarp. Paddles stayed stacked under the canoes. The stove cooled on granite. Someone's mug tipped and rolled until Joel caught it with his foot. Toothpaste spit flashed pale on the edge of the firelight and vanished into dirt.

The fire burned low. Mike poured a little water over one edge, then stopped because there was still heat enough to sit with. Steam rose and carried the smell of wet ash. The night pressed in around the small ring of men and gear.

When Joel finally went to his hammock, the zipper on his bug net sounded louder than it had in daylight. He slid in carefully, felt the fabric take his weight, and lay still while the hammock found its shape around him. The tarp above him was a low dark roof. Beyond its edge he could see a strip of sky between pine branches.

Will came over and stood near his own half-hung hammock, looking between it and the tent.

"You actually committing?" Joel asked.

Will looked at the trees. Then at the tent. Then at the lake, where a ring spread and vanished. "No."

"Coward."

"Strategist."

Will gathered his quilt and went to the tent. Mike, without looking up from the fire, said, "Left side's flatter."

Will paused with one hand on the zipper. "That an order?"

"It's a fact. Do what you want with it."

Will ducked inside, bringing cold air and the smell of lake water when he opened the door. Mike and Alex were still talking in low voices near the fire. Mike said something about tomorrow's first narrow section. Alex answered with a wordless sound of disbelief.

Will whispered from inside the tent, "He's going to push early."

"Mike?" Joel said.

"No, the loon."

Joel smiled in the dark.

"He wants to make the big lake before wind," Will said.

"That bad?"

"No. Maybe. It's fine if we get there clean."

"Fine according to you or fine according to Mike?"

Will was quiet a moment. "Exactly."

Joel listened. Fire ticking. Water against rock. The faint scrape of Mike's log shifting under weight. Alex's voice, muffled by nylon and distance, telling the end of something he had already told once.

Then silence came down, not all at once, but by layers.

Will stopped talking. Mike and Alex put out the fire. Their tent zipper opened and closed. A headlamp beam crossed Joel's tarp and disappeared. The food barrel latch clicked once more, or Joel thought it did, though it could have been a buckle on Mike's pack. Mike's PFD hung dark on its branch, the yellow pouch invisible now but still fixed in Joel's mind because he had seen Mike touch it.

He lay awake long enough for the first night to become itself. His body did not yet trust the hammock. His shoulder wanted to be elsewhere. His feet stayed cold in the damp socks he had meant to change earlier and had not. From the tent came Mike's heavier breathing, then Alex turning on a sleeping pad, then Will's small dry catch every few breaths. Each sound had a source. That was the comfort. A body, a zipper, a buckle, a rope, a hull. Joel could return a sound to its object and let it go.

He woke later without knowing why.

The hammock held him in a shallow curve. His shoulders ached. His feet were still cold. For a moment Joel did not move.

Outside, the lake was quiet. The camp had the dense stillness of first night, when every object was still where men had put it and had not yet become familiar enough to ignore.

From the shoreline below camp came a soft knock.

One sound. Hollow. Wood or hull against stone. Low enough to belong to the canoes, the water below them, or a branch the lake had been worrying against rock all evening.

Joel opened his eyes fully.

He waited.

Nothing followed. No voice. No footstep. No second knock. Only the lake moving lightly below the granite and wind in the top of one pine.

He could not see the shoreline from the hammock. The tarp edge cut off the canoes and the darker slope below them, so every explanation had to be assembled from memory. He pictured the boats pulled high, angled on rock, paddles tucked beneath them, painter rope loose enough to touch. Wind could do it. Cooling hull could do it. Water lifting a branch could do it. There were a dozen ways for the night to make one ordinary sound, and one reliable way for a tired man to lose the rest of the night.

From the tent, Will breathed in and out. The dry catch came again, and then the cleaner breath after it.

Joel rolled slightly in the hammock, careful not to shift too hard and make the straps talk, and closed his eyes again. He did not sit up. If he sat up, the sound became an event. If he stayed still, it could remain weather, rope, branch.

The camp did not answer. Below it, the lake kept working lightly at the stone.

After a while, Joel slept.


# Chapter 5 - Beaver Work

Joel woke to the sound of Will's zipper.

Not the tent zipper at first. Smaller. A bag, probably. Then the tent door itself, opened by someone who believed slowness made less sound. It did not. The teeth came apart one by one in the gray morning, dry and careful and loud.

Joel opened his eyes.

The hammock held him in its shallow curve. Above him, the tarp was beaded with dew. Beyond its edge the sky had gone pale over the lake, not sunrise exactly but the idea of it. Fog lay low on the water, a gray skin that made the opposite shore appear and disappear by inches. The air was cold enough that he had pulled his beanie over his ears sometime in the night without remembering doing it. His nose was cold. His feet were warm inside the quilt until he moved them and found the damp in his sandal straps beside the hammock.

Will came out of the tent bent over and half-zipped into a fleece. He stood there for a moment, looking at the lake like a man who had expected it to do something overnight and was checking the result.

"You alive?" Joel said.

Will flinched and looked toward the hammock. "Jesus."

"Good morning."

"You look dead in that thing."

"That's the appeal."

Will rubbed his face. His hair stood up on one side. "I thought about sleeping in mine."

"You thought bravely."

"I made a weather-based decision."

"You slept in the tent."

"I made a tent-based decision."

The fire ring was gray and cold. The tent had not moved except for one side panel sagging where Alex had probably pressed into it. The canoes lay above the shoreline, dark with dew, paddles tucked underneath where they had left them. Joel looked there first without meaning to. The night's knock had not become important. It had only waited for daylight to give it a body.

A loose painter rope touched a hull once in the faint breeze.

Tap.

Then nothing.

Joel pushed the bug net aside and leaned out far enough to see the landing clearly. No paddle had shifted. No hull had slid. No branch had lodged under the stone lip. The shoreline gave him the same objects in the same positions, which was all daylight could do.

He stayed there with one cold hand on the hammock edge while the rope touched the hull again, softer this time, almost private.

A rope tapping a hull was a rope tapping a hull. He had been here long enough, in this kind of morning, to know how many objects could speak once when air moved through camp and then say nothing useful after.

Will had already seen the rods.

"They're wet," he said.

"Everything's wet."

"Not everything is a precision instrument."

"Your rod is a stick with jewelry."

Will looked offended but too tired to answer well. "That's not even worth correcting."

Mike's voice came from the tent. "If you two are talking about fishing before coffee, I'm leaving you here."

Alex groaned from inside. "Is the wilderness always this administrative?"

Joel swung his feet out of the hammock and sat up. Cold came through his base layer and made him move faster. He pulled on his fleece, then his wet hiking sandals, grimacing as the straps took hold of his feet. He had camp shoes buried somewhere but did not want to open the pack yet. The first step on granite was sharp and cold.

Morning camp took shape without ceremony. Water first. Stove next. Breakfast by negotiation.

Will wanted pancakes because he had remembered them into existence the night before. Mike wanted bars and coffee because he wanted miles. Alex said he supported pancakes in principle but not if the batter had texture. Joel found instant oats near the top of the food bag and held them up.

"This is the compromise."

"That's not compromise," Will said. "That's surrender with hot water."

"Brown sugar."

"Apology sugar."

Mike was already filling the pot. "Oats."

Alex sat up in the tent with his hair crushed flat on one side. "Do oats have seasoning?"

"They have whatever you put in them," Joel said.

"So no."

"Peanut butter."

Alex considered that. "That's a condiment with ambition."

"Oats," Mike said again, because repetition was how he ended meetings.

They boiled water and made coffee with Folgers packets. The packets always seemed like a bad idea at home and a good idea in camp. Tear, pour, stir, drink something hot enough to correct the body. Joel ate oats from his mug and watched fog pull away from the lake in low pieces, like smoke from a fire already out.

The far shore came in and out behind it. Pine, then nothing. Granite, then blank gray. Sound arrived cleanly through the damp: Will's spoon against his mug, Mike folding the map case open, Alex coughing once into his sleeve, the stove valve ticking as it cooled. Each noise came with an object attached.

At home, morning meant machines doing small repeatable work: coffee grinder, furnace, phone alarm, the garage door juddering open under Mara's hand. Here the work was older and less negotiable. Fire, water, food, route. His body liked the reduction before his mind had time to mistrust it. A man could be useful by boiling water. A task could end because the pot was empty or the bag was packed.

Mike checked the map while standing. He did not like sitting before the route was decided, even if the route had been decided months ago.

"We want to be moving by eight," he said.

Will looked at the sky. "That means nine."

"It means eight."

"Map agrees with eight?" Alex said.

Mike tapped the plastic case with one finger. "Map doesn't care."

Will leaned close enough to see the blue line under the cloudy plastic. "Map also doesn't show beavers."

"It shows water," Mike said.

"That's my point."

"Your point is always that maps have betrayed you personally."

Will's dry cough came once, more irritation than illness. "I'm just saying a line can be right and still not tell you what kind of day you're about to have."

Joel understood the irritation because it was not only about the map. Will wanted the missing difficulty named before it arrived. He wanted a system honest enough to include its own failures in the legend: mud, pullovers, morale loss, time eaten by things with teeth. A man could lose a job to a larger system and still come to the woods expecting paper to account for water.

Alex, still inside the tent, said, "It means someone will be looking for his headlamp at eight-ten."

"I know where my headlamp is," Mike said.

Nobody said anything.

Mike looked up. "I do."

Joel sipped coffee. "Then we're ahead of schedule spiritually."

They broke camp in cold stages. Sleeping bags stuffed. Pads rolled. Tent shaken, fly wet enough to be annoying but not soaked. Joel took down his hammock last because he liked how quickly his whole bedroom became a fist-sized bundle of fabric and straps. Will packed his unused hammock with the expression of a man putting away a tool he had not deserved.

The food barrel latch snapped closed. The sound was ordinary enough that no one looked up. Mike tested the latch with two fingers, then set his palm flat on the lid as if sealing a small argument. The stove cooled, clicked once as he handled it, and went into its bag. Paddles came out from under the canoes. Wet blades, grit on the shafts. Will wiped his rod guides with a cloth and pretended no one noticed.

"Fishing while we move?" Alex asked.

"In spirit," Will said.

"No," Mike said.

"I said in spirit."

"I heard the part you didn't say."

"You often do, and usually badly."

They loaded the boats. The morning light grew cleaner but not warmer. Mosquitoes were thin near shore, slow enough that they seemed confused by their own hunger. The lake held the canoes without wave. Mike pressed his thumb against the small yellow pouch clipped inside the left side of his PFD, where the Highlander key lived, then checked the front strap on the food barrel as if both objects belonged to the same future. Road and dinner. Ignition and calories.

Whatever had knocked in the night had left no sign. No shifted hull. No loose paddle. No branch caught on the rocks. Joel checked the landing while Mike tightened straps and Will repacked his rod case. Checking was easier than wondering. His body settled only after his eyes had done the work.

By a little after eight-thirty they pushed off.

The first part of the morning was easy water. The lake stretched north in a long reach, then narrowed around a low point where black spruce crowded the shore. Mike and Will led. Joel and Alex followed. Alex paddled better after breakfast than he had near the end of the day before. Maybe sleep helped. Maybe cold made complaint less efficient than work.

They passed the point and entered a channel not much wider than the one from yesterday, but lower and flatter. Alder came down to the water on both sides. Grass leaned out over mud. The water lost the clean dark of lake depth and turned brown and slow. It smelled of wet bark and old leaves, the sour richness of things busy rotting into something useful. Every bend seemed to have been made for an animal's body rather than a canoe: low, close, shoulder-height in the brush, water deep enough to float them until it was not.

"Here we go," Mike called back.

"Here we go good or here we go bad?" Alex said.

"Beaver work."

Will turned his head. "Plural?"

"Could be."

"Great."

Alex looked back at Joel. "Define beaver work."

Joel said, "Sometimes you get out and drag."

"That's work for us."

"Correct."

"Misleading name."

"They did their part."

Alex looked at the low banks sliding past, the cut stems bright as bone near the waterline. "I grew up around animals that could kill you. Beavers feel insulting."

"Beavers can ruin your day," Will said.

"Lots of things can ruin my day. Plain oatmeal. Small talk. Men named Todd."

Mike looked over his shoulder. "Paddle."

Alex paddled.

That was what made Joel's irritation unreliable. Alex made every instruction into a small public trial, but he did the work. He complained, turned discomfort into theater, and put his blade in the water with the rest of them. The performance was weather. Under it, something kept taking weight.

The channel kept narrowing. The alders leaned until their upper branches crossed over the water and made a low, jointed roof. Water dripped from lifted paddles and fell loud in the covered places. The blades clipped drowned stems. Once, Joel's blade struck something under the surface that gave back a hollow wooden note, and Alex looked at him.

"Rock," Joel said.

"Rocks don't sound like doors."

"Submerged branch, then."

"Better."

Ahead, Will said something to Mike that Joel could not catch. Mike answered with one hand lifted off the paddle, palm down, the universal sign for enough. Will turned forward again. He had his rod across his lap in a way that pretended not to be ready.

The first dam came twenty minutes later. It crossed the channel in a low brown ridge of sticks and mud, water shining over the top in thin sheets. On one side a narrow chute had been opened by previous travelers and half-closed again by the animals. Boot prints had softened on the muddy lip. A cut alder end showed pale where someone had tried to improve the gap with a saw or hatchet and had not improved it enough. The argument looked current.

"This on the map?" Alex asked.

Will gave the kind of laugh that had no pleasure in it. "No. The map people neglected to consult the beavers."

"It's normal," Mike said.

"Normal is not the same as fast."

"Nobody promised fast."

Joel looked at the dam, at the water seeping through it in several patient places. The map had promised passage by leaving this out.

Mike guided his canoe toward it, stepped out in knee-deep water, and held the boat while Will got out and pulled from the front.

"Looks simple," Alex said.

"Don't say that," Joel said.

"Why?"

"Because the river hears confidence."

Alex smiled. "That sounds like something from your Wisconsin cult."

"I'm from St. Louis."

"Madison changed you."

They came in on the right. Joel stepped out first. Cold water went up over his sandal straps and around his calves. The mud under the surface accepted one foot, then the other, and did not want to give them back. It was not the clean mud of a trail puddle. It had leaf rot in it, trapped gas, broken sticks that pressed without warning against the soft place below the ankle. Alex got out and immediately sank deeper.

"Oh, come on."

"Don't fight it straight up," Joel said. "Rock your foot."

"I am negotiating with it."

"You're losing."

The canoe slid halfway onto the dam, then stopped. Joel pushed from the back. Alex pulled from the front. The hull rasped over wet sticks. Something under it cracked. Water ran around their legs with more force than it had seemed to have from the boat, all that brown quiet narrowing itself over the dam and becoming current again. Behind them, Mike's boat bumped the dam once, a hollow aluminum note that traveled along the low banks and came back thinner.

"Hold up," Alex said.

Joel stopped.

Alex bent and freed the painter rope from a branch woven into the dam. He held it up with theatrical patience. "See? Useful."

"I didn't say otherwise."

"You were thinking it."

"I was thinking many things."

Mike, already on the far side, called, "You coming?"

Alex looked back at him. "We're preserving infrastructure."

"You're standing in beaver trash."

Will said, "Technically impressive beaver trash."

"That's your review of everything in nature," Mike said.

They got the boat over. It dropped into the water beyond with a hollow slap and a little roll that sent water against Joel's thighs. Alex made a sound between a laugh and a curse.

"Refreshing," he said.

Joel steadied the canoe and let the mud take back the footprints they had made. It closed slowly, dark water filling the shape of their legs. The dam stayed where it was.

The second dam was worse.

The channel bent left and disappeared behind alder. The dam sat just beyond the bend, higher, newer, still wet with the look of recent labor. Water backed up before it in a shallow pond full of drowned grass. A lodge rose on the left, sticks piled against a bank where fresh cuts showed pale. The chew marks were clean and angled, each sapling taken down by a mouth that knew its work.

There was nothing mysterious about it. That almost made it worse. No malice, no warning, no intention toward them at all. Just another animal improving its house, and in doing so making their route harder. The wilderness did not need to hate them. It only needed to be busy with something else.

Will said, "Somebody's active."

Mike said, "Beavers usually are."

"I mean recent."

"Are we filing a complaint?"

Alex lifted his paddle. "I support labor."

The pull-over took longer. They unloaded a few bags because the boats were catching on the crest. Food barrel out. Day bags out. Rods handed forward like fragile diplomatic gifts. The barrel was heavier than it had been at breakfast, or seemed heavier because now it had to be lifted from an unstable boat while mud held Joel by the calves. Mike took it first, set it on a mat of sticks above the water, and looked offended when it leaned.

"Don't let it roll," he said.

"I had planned to release it into the wild," Will said.

"It's the food."

"I understand the food barrel concept."

Alex took one rod and held it away from himself.

"This thing makes me feel irresponsible."

"It should," Will said.

Joel pushed and slipped. One foot went sideways between two submerged branches. For a second the mud held him and the current leaned against his knees. He caught the gunwale with one hand and steadied.

"You good?" Will called.

"Yeah."

"Don't break Madison," Alex said.

"Madison bends," Joel said.

Mike was on top of the dam, pulling his canoe by the front line. "Lift, don't drag."

"Buy lighter boats," Joel said.

Mike looked over his shoulder. "That line's less funny when you're wrong."

"It wasn't meant to be funny."

"It never is."

They got the boats over one by one. By the end, everyone was wet to the knee or higher. Alex had mud up one thigh. Will had a leech on his ankle, which he noticed only after he had already described leech behavior in too much detail.

"No, no, no," he said, more annoyed than scared. "Absolutely not."

Mike looked down. "It's tiny."

"It's attached."

"That's how they work."

Will flicked at it with a fingernail and missed. "That's not how I want it to work."

Alex crouched beside him. "Allow me. I grew up with real parasites."

"You grew up in Alaska, not a medieval swamp."

Alex pinched the leech off and held it up for half a second before flinging it into the grass. "Nature defeated."

Will stared at his ankle. "I hate that I needed you."

"You'll heal."

Alex stood and handed Will's rod back carefully, grip first, without making a production of that part. The gesture was almost formal, the kind of care he kept hidden under bits. Then he noticed Joel noticing and widened his eyes.

"Don't make it weird," he said.

"I wasn't."

"You were warming up."

They moved again.

The morning warmed. The chill lifted from their clothes but left them damp. The sun came through breaks in the alder and lit the water in copper patches. Every few bends the channel opened into a marshy pool where lily pads held yellowing edges and dragonflies stitched the air low over the surface. A beaver slapped somewhere ahead once, flat and hard, and the sound crossed the channel like a board dropped on water. A few seconds later a lower knock came from behind them, more wood than water. Joel looked back. Only the bend, the grass, the disturbed brown line of their passage. He could assign it if he wanted: hull cooling, branch settling, the dam behind them changing under its own weight. Will looked too, then faced forward before Mike could tell him to. The route tightened and made them work.

Mike did not say they were behind. He did not have to. He checked the map case more often and put it away more quickly each time, as if the blue line might change if anyone looked at it too long. When Will asked once whether the next lake was the long one or the before-the-long-one, Mike said, "After this," and kept paddling.

The route was changing them by small degrees. It was too early for drama, and the day was still bright enough to make seriousness feel theatrical, but the first-day jokes had less gloss on them. Mike's instructions got shorter. Will still talked, but more of it came between breaths. Alex performed, forgot to perform, then remembered when silence lasted too long.

The water gave them a clear stretch, then a snag. A little current, then mud. A turn that looked open until the boat entered and the alder took both sides of the canoe. A blue line could be true and still leave out the truth. It did not say how many times a man would step into brown water and feel his foot disappear.

At a shallow gravel bar, Mike stopped to check the map. Will cast once while standing in ankle-deep water, because his body had been waiting for the chance before his mind approved it.

Mike said, "Really?"

"One cast."

"You said that yesterday."

"And I have grown as a person."

The lure landed near a sunken branch with a small plip. Something moved in the shadow. Will's whole body changed.

"Fish?" Alex said.

"Maybe."

"Useful fish or emotionally symbolic fish?"

Will reeled slowly. "All fish are both."

The lure came back clean.

Mike gave him five seconds of silence. "Done?"

Will clipped the lure to a guide. "Done."

"Miracles happen," Mike said.

They ate lunch out of the boats because the ground was mostly mud and grass. Tuna packets, Triscuits, trail mix, jerky already diminished by theft and democracy. Alex examined a salmon packet as if it might argue with him.

"Just eat the crackers," Mike said.

"I'm branching."

"You branch like a dead tree."

Joel handed him a strip of jerky. "Here. Safe food."

Alex took it. "Your usefulness continues."

Will said, "Don't praise him too much. He'll dehydrate a whole cow."

"I've considered it," Joel said.

"Of course you have."

The channel after lunch became less forgiving. Beaver cuts narrowed the turns. Drowned branches reached up from underwater, invisible until a hull scraped them. Mike called back directions in clipped pieces. Left. Stay right. Shallow. Watch the snag. Joel relayed what mattered to Alex when Alex was looking at the wrong thing.

The work made a kind of language: blade drip, hull scrape, breath, boot pulled from mud, branch released and whipping back. A man could listen to it and know where the others were without seeing them. Mike's canoe ahead by the harder knocks. Will's rod tip ticking once against aluminum. Alex's paddle coming out too high and scattering water beside Joel's knee. Before anything was wrong, this was trust. Joel only knew where the other canoe was by sound alone.

Then a branch cracked in the alder to their left.

Not loud. A clean snap, followed by brush moving low and fast, not through the tops but under them. It had a body in it, or suggested one well enough that Joel's own body answered first: shoulders tightening, paddle stopped half out of the water, breath held because breath was suddenly another sound.

Alex stopped paddling mid-stroke.

The canoe turned slightly.

Water fell from his blade in three loud drops.

"Hold up," he said.

Joel put his paddle in to correct. Ahead, Mike's boat kept moving three more strokes before Will turned.

"You guys hear that?"

Mike looked toward the alder. "Animal."

It sounded like an answer. It was only a category.

"What kind of animal?" Alex asked.

"The kind in bushes."

"That narrows it."

Will held his paddle across his knees and listened. The brush moved once more, farther back. Leaves shook. A thin stem kept trembling after the rest went still, a visible piece of an invisible animal. Then nothing.

No splash followed. No grunt. No second branch. The channel kept moving under them with small brown sounds, and the alder bank offered concealment without the courtesy of shape. The canoes drifted a few feet while they listened, enough that Joel had to correct again or let the current set them sideways into grass.

"Beaver?" Joel said.

"Could be," Mike said.

"Could be bear," Alex said, but lightly.

Mike turned his head just enough. "Don't start."

"I'm not starting. I'm identifying risk factors."

"That's Will's job," Joel said.

Will, still listening, said, "Bear would probably sound heavier."

"Probably," Mike said. "Everybody paddle."

The word everybody did its work. They moved.

Joel looked once into the alder as they passed. He saw leaves, mud, the dark arch of roots where the bank had undercut, a few fresh chips on a cut sapling. No animal. No shape. Sight gave him facts, but not enough of them.

No reason to remember it except the way Alex had stopped before anyone else did, shoulders tightening under the straps of his PFD, paddle held over water so no drop fell.

A person could grow up in Alaska and still become alert to brush. Maybe especially then. Maybe larger country gave you practice hearing the small thing before the large thing chose to show itself.

The channel opened after that, though not into easy water. It widened into a marshy run where the current slowed and the wind found them from the side. The sky had changed while they worked the beaver dams. It was still clear overhead, but a smear of cloud sat low in the west. The light had gone flatter. Far ahead, a wider lake showed between low banks.

Mike checked the map without stopping, one hand on the paddle, the case open against his knees.

"Bigger water after this," he said.

"How big?" Alex asked.

"Big enough."

Will made a sound.

Mike said, "What?"

"Nothing."

"Say it."

"I'm just saying, 'big enough' is not a measurement."

"It's not the problem lake."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the lake before the problem lake."

Alex turned around. "Do the lakes know which one is the problem lake?"

Joel said, "Mike tells them."

Mike ignored that. "We get across this, short ugly bit, then camp."

"Second camp?" Alex said.

"No. Lunch two-point-five."

"Don't name things."

"I'll name whatever I survive."

They paddled into the wider water.

The wind was mild but steady, enough to worry the surface and press Alex's lazy strokes into consequence. Joel had to correct more often. The canoe showed him every small failure in the front. Mike and Will angled left toward a low shore where the map showed the next narrow exit. Will kept looking back toward the marsh.

Joel saw it and did not ask.

The branch crack was gone. The brush had stopped moving. Nothing followed them but small waves and water dripping from lifted paddles. A drip could sound like a step if a man needed one. Joel did not.

Still, for a while no one joked.

That did not last. It never did.

Alex finally said, "For the record, if a beaver kills me, lie."

Will turned in the front of Mike's canoe. "About which part?"

"All of it."

"Say bear?"

"Say moose. Say I fought a moose for the honor of the group."

Mike said, "I'm saying you fell in mud and got unionized."

Alex laughed, and because the water was wider now the laugh carried out from him and came back from nowhere in particular. It skipped once against the low shore and thinned into the marsh behind them.

Joel paddled and watched the lake ahead. He could feel the day going long. Not late yet, but moving that way. First Camp was behind them. Second Camp was somewhere past work they had not done.

Mike had rounded the route down. Maybe not by much. Maybe by the exact amount competent men rounded discomfort down when selling it to themselves first and others second. Joel knew the habit from gentler rooms: a house repair, a budget, a wedding date that could wait one more week because nobody had said otherwise out loud. The scale changed. The habit held.

They reached the far side later than Mike wanted and earlier than worry required. The exit was a gap in alder, half-hidden by grass. Water moved through it in a narrow brown pull, just enough current to show the way.

Will lifted his rod and looked at the widening water behind them.

Mike said, "No."

"I didn't say anything."

"You thought it loudly."

"There could be pike."

"There could be time."

Alex looked toward the alder gap. "Does it open up after this?"

Mike gave him a hard look.

Alex smiled. "Asking for morale."

Mike cleared his throat. The sound was small, almost lost under the wind and water.

"After the next stretch," he said. "Just keep moving."

Will glanced at Joel. Not alarmed. Just registering the phrase the way he registered all of Mike's route talk.

Joel dipped his paddle and followed the first canoe into the gap.

The alder closed around them. The light narrowed. Water slid under the boats with a dark, steady pull, and from somewhere ahead came the faint sound of it dropping over stones. Not danger, not in any form a man could name from the back of a canoe. Only current. Only rock. The route kept going after men had described it too easily.


# Chapter 6 - The Rough Carry

The alder gap did open, but not into relief.

It let them out into a run of water broken by stone. The current gathered itself in narrow places and slipped fast over gravel, then loosened into brown pools where foam collected against grass. The boats scraped twice before anyone could pretend the line was clean. Mike raised a hand from the back of his canoe and pointed them left, then right, then left again, reading the channel one word at a time.

The beaver work had left all of them slow. Mud dried on their calves and cracked when they shifted. Joel could feel grit under the straps of his sandals. In the front of his canoe, Alex sat with his paddle across his knees for half a breath too long, watching the tongue of current narrow between two stones.

"Left of that rock," Joel said.

"I see the rock."

"Good. Now miss it."

Alex dug hard on the right. Too hard. The canoe answered at once, nose swinging more than Joel wanted. He corrected from the back, blade deep, and the hull slid over gravel with a sound like a long zipper pulled under water.

"That was controlled," Alex said.

"By me."

"Group project."

Ahead, Will looked back from Mike's canoe. "If this is what opens up, I have notes."

"No one is accepting notes," Mike said.

The current carried the first canoe around a low grass point. For a second only Will's red PFD showed between alder stems, moving without the rest of him. Then Mike's paddle flashed and the whole canoe came into view again, ordinary and exactly where it should be.

That was what bends did. A boat went out of sight and returned. Still, the channel had begun to loosen sound from source. A hull scrape arrived from ahead after the hull that made it had cleared the gravel. Mike's paddle knocked once against a submerged stem and the note came back duller from the bank. Will said something too low to catch, and the alder returned only the shape of it. Nothing was wrong. Water carried sound. Brush broke it apart.

Beyond the run, the water widened.

The lake did not look large on the map. Nothing about it had looked large when they were drinking coffee at Will's kitchen table three weeks earlier, Mike tracing the route with one thick finger while Will corrected scale and Alex asked whether there would be fish he could morally refuse to eat. On paper, this lake had been one more pale shape between black lines. In front of them it took the wind cleanly from the west and turned it into long dark strips.

Mike let his canoe drift just short of the opening. Will had the map case braced across his knees, one hand on it as if paper could blow away even through plastic.

Mike cleared his throat.

It was a small sound, almost nothing, but Joel had learned to hear the decision inside it. Mike made that sound before he merged into traffic, before he told a waitress the bill was wrong, before he moved a canoe against other people's judgment. The throat cleared. Then the world received the plan.

"We cross close to shore," Mike said. "No hero lines."

"I was saving my hero line for dinner," Alex said.

No one gave him much. The wind had already reached into the channel and found the front of Joel's canoe.

Will looked down at the map. "Camp is after the portage on the north side."

"Correct."

"How bad is the portage?"

"It exists."

"That is not a measurement."

"It's a portage."

"Also not a measurement."

Mike gave him a flat look over one shoulder. "Do you want me to say it's charming?"

"I want you to say if it's a half mile of polite dirt or a mile of murder roots."

"Those are not standardized units," Alex said.

"Thank you, Alex."

"I'm pro-science."

Mike pointed with his paddle. "We go now. We don't sit in the wind talking about vocabulary."

That was reasonable. It was also Mike. Those two facts often arrived together in a way that made arguing feel indulgent. Joel set his paddle low and said, "Ready?"

Alex rolled his shoulders. His sleeves were still damp from the beaver work, and there was a mud line dried along one forearm like a cuff. "Seattle prepared me emotionally for this."

"Seattle prepared you to complain in layers."

"Exactly. Emotional layering."

"Paddle."

"Yes, Dad."

Joel did not answer that, partly because the wind took the canoe then and partly because fatherhood, even as a joke, belonged to a room he had not entered. He and Mara had talked about children in the half-specific way people used when they wanted a future but did not want to trap it. Maybe. Someday. If work held. If the small back room could be made into something other than storage and postponement. She had stood there with a tape measure in one hand and contractor mail under the other, then touched the cold metal end to his chest and told him to stop looking like a man asked to approve municipal debt. Joel had laughed. He had said there was time. He had been saying that about many things.

Here there was no room for the future to be that grammatical. There was only the next stroke, the next gust, Alex in front of him dipping too hard on one side, the canoe wanting to turn its face to the wind.

"Left a little," Joel said.

"My left or boat left?"

"The boat's left is your left."

"That's comforting in a limited way."

"Just a little."

Alex adjusted. He was better when there was something to fight. In easy water he let the paddle wander and turned his head toward every remark he wanted to make. In wind he became more useful, almost solemn. He leaned into the strokes. His shoulders worked under the PFD. Spray came over the front and darkened his sleeves again, making the morning's mud soften and run.

Ahead, Mike and Will angled along the lee side of the shore where the waves had less distance to build. Even there the lake slapped at the hulls. The spruce on the western shore bent all one way. Clouds had thickened in that direction, not storm clouds yet, just low gray freight moving over the trees.

"How much farther?" Alex asked.

"Across," Joel said.

"Great. Precise."

"Do you want a number?"

"Would it help?"

"No."

"Then I want a better lie."

Joel smiled despite himself. "Fifteen minutes."

"See? That feels like care."

It took longer than fifteen. It took the kind of time that made the body stop counting and begin bargaining in small pieces. Get to the leaning pine. Get to the rock with the white streak. Get past the opening where the wind came harder. Joel corrected and corrected again. Alex's paddle rose too high when he tired, scattering water back into Joel's lap.

The waves were not large enough to frighten anyone with sense. That made them worse in a smaller, meaner way. They were only large enough to keep correcting the boat, only cold enough to sting when spray came over the side, only steady enough to remind Joel that a lake did not have to become dangerous all at once. It could spend them by ounces.

"Lower," Joel said.

"I know."

"Then lower knowingly."

"You have a very managerial tone for a passenger."

"I'm in the back."

"Emotionally a passenger."

Mike called something from ahead. The wind tore the first half away.

"What?" Will called back, though Mike was three feet behind him.

"Stay in. Don't cut across."

"I wasn't cutting across."

"I know what your boat looks like when you're thinking about it."

Will said something that looked from a distance like a correction but did not carry. His hand moved in the air, two fingers and a little chop. Then he bent back to paddling.

A few strokes later, after Mike's canoe had gone behind a standing dead pine on the point, the same command came thinly off the shore.

Stay in.

Not a voice, Joel told himself. Not even words, really. Wind in spruce. Mike's last call snagged and thrown back by the bank, thinned until it sounded as if the shore had kept only the useful part. The lake was shaped for tricks: rock face, water, trees bent in one direction, all of it taking a simple sound and shaving the source from it. Joel looked ahead until he had Mike's shoulders in the back of the canoe, Will's cap low over the map case, both bodies attached to the sound after the fact. Sight did not explain the echo. It only gave him permission to keep paddling.

"What?" Alex said.

"Nothing."

"You keep hearing nothing."

"Paddle lower."

"That is a separate issue."

For a while the two canoes moved with the same ugly rhythm. Dip. Pull. Correct. Drift. Dip again. The wind flattened the small talk before it could become talk, and that helped. Joel could hear his own breath and Alex's breath, not in sync, not far from it. Once Alex missed a stroke and said, quietly enough that only Joel heard, "Come on," not to the lake or to Joel but to himself.

Joel liked him most in those unadvertised seconds. Then Alex looked over his shoulder and said, "You enjoying my suffering back there?"

"Academically."

"That feels like a yes."

The lake had no interest in their argument. It let them move, resisted them evenly, accepted the blades and gave back distance in stubborn increments. Joel had always liked that about water when he trusted it: the absence of persuasion. It took shape from wind, rock, depth, and pressure. It did not become kinder because someone had planned well.

Close to shore, the water turned the color of tea over stone. The landing appeared and disappeared behind the lift of the canoe's front: alder, dark notch, low rock slick with wave wash. Mike raised his paddle once and pointed, though everyone could see there was nowhere else to go.

The far shore came closer. Alder overhung a dark notch where the portage landing should be. The waves were smaller there but messier, coming off the rock and crossing themselves. Mike brought his canoe in first and badly. The front scraped sideways on slick stone. Will stepped out too soon, planted one sandal, and sat down hard in two inches of water with a slap that made Alex bark out a laugh before he could stop himself.

"Grace," Mike said.

"Physics," Will said, sitting there with both hands braced behind him.

Alex lifted his paddle like a microphone. "Ladies and gentlemen, the aquatic dignity of Will."

"Don't," Will said.

Alex did it anyway, because of course he did. He put one hand to his chest and announced to the trees, "A man. A map. A complete loss to surface conditions."

"Alex," Joel said.

"What? This is how legends happen."

Will pushed himself up, wet from hip to calf on one side. He checked the map case before he checked himself, saw it had stayed sealed, and then looked angry that this was the sort of man he was. "If you make that a story, I will put a leech in your food."

"My food has standards."

"Your food is peanut butter noodles."

"Exactly. Standards."

Joel steered toward the same sloping rock, already irritated, already preparing to tell Alex to get out cleanly and hold the boat instead of turning the landing into a stage. The irritation was ready before Alex had earned it. Alex had made himself easy to judge, and Joel had taken the convenience.

"Front out," Joel said.

Alex put the paddle down, caught the painter line, and stepped into the water without flourish. His first foot slipped, but he recovered with a hard little hop and planted himself against the boat. "Hold up."

Joel held.

Alex bent, got both hands on the line, and took the canoe's weight before the next wave could turn it sideways. He did not look back to see if anyone had noticed. Joel stepped out, water cold over his ankles, and together they dragged the canoe high enough that it stopped knocking.

Will came back for his pack, trying to act dry enough to preserve authority. One of the shoulder straps had twisted and cinched wrong, the buckle buried under the load.

"Here," Alex said.

"I have it."

"You have it badly."

"That's different from needing help."

"Everything is different from everything if you talk long enough. Turn around."

Will stood there a second, suspicious on principle. Then he turned. Alex loosened the strap, fed it flat, tugged it through the buckle, and thumped the pack once with the heel of his hand. The plastic clicked cleanly into place.

"There. Try not to drown in the forest."

Will rolled his shoulders. The pack sat better. "That was almost useful."

"I contain multitudes."

"You contain crackers."

"Also multitudes of crackers."

Joel looked away before Alex could catch him looking. He had wanted the clean pleasure of annoyance and had been handed evidence against it.
