That was Alex's worst habit, maybe: not the performing, not the needling, but the way some small honest usefulness would appear inside it and make judgment feel cheap.

Will did not thank him again, but when he lifted the pack he lifted it as if trusting the adjustment. That was the kind of gratitude their group allowed: an argument dropped before it could become one, a correction swallowed, a hand not slapped away the second time.

The portage began behind the alder as a low track through wet grass. For twenty yards it seemed almost reasonable. Then the ground rose into spruce and rock, and the trail changed its mind.

They unloaded in heat trapped under trees. The wind that had bullied them across the lake did not enter the portage except as a high sound in the spruce tops. Down on the trail the air was close and damp. Mosquitoes found the sweat under collars and behind ears. Packs came out. Rods were lashed awkwardly. Paddles were collected and redistributed. Mike stood over the gear and assigned loads with the rough confidence of a man moving familiar pieces.

The landing gave them no place to be graceful. One canoe rocked half in water while Joel held it. The other lay at a bad angle on wet rock, back end still knocking when a wave found it. Bags had to be passed hand to hand, not set down, because everything below the first line of grass was mud or slick stone. Will kept saying, "Careful, careful," which helped no one except Will.

Mike checked his watch and then the sky. He did not say what the watch told him.

"Double portage," Will said.

"We can single it if we don't baby it," Mike said.

"We have two canoes, four packs, food barrel, loose crap, rods, paddles, and your antique map collection."

"We can single it."

"We can injure someone doing that."

"The trail's not that long."

Will stared at him. "Based on?"

"Based on me looking at the map."

"That's not a trail report."

"It is literally a map."

"Maps are documents of optimism."

"You're a document of something."

Alex lifted the food barrel an inch, set it down, and made a face. "This barrel has been eating at night."

"You're carrying paddles," Mike said.

"I feel seen."

Joel looked at the load and then at the trail. The canoes had to go. The packs had to go. The only question was how many times the same strip of mud and root would receive them. He had no desire to prove anything under a bad load. He also had no desire to spend an hour ferrying gear while daylight thinned. The day had already begun to lean away from them.

"We do the canoes and packs first," Joel said. "Come back for barrel and loose stuff if it sucks."

Mike glanced at him. Joel had entered the route argument, which was not his usual place.

"It won't suck," Mike said.

"Then the second trip will be scenic."

Alex pointed at Joel. "Madison with the wisdom of compromise."

"Don't reward him," Will said. "He'll form a committee."

Mike looked from the trail to the gear. His jaw worked once. Then he nodded. "Fine. Canoes and main packs. Quick."

This was how they accepted compromise. They pretended it had been the plan.

Mike took his canoe first. He bent under it, found the yoke, and lifted in one practiced motion that made the hull rise over him like a dull green roof. Will shouldered his pack and gathered rods. Joel took the second canoe. The weight settled across his shoulders, familiar and still unpleasant. A canoe was light until it was not. The first fifty steps always lied. Then the yoke found the same tender place on both shoulders and began teaching.

Under the hull the world became private and loud. Drops that had clung to the gunwales ran down and fell near Joel's ears. A trapped mosquito whined against the aluminum, furious at the sky it could see and could not reach. His own breath came back at him.

Alex took a pack and the bundle of paddles. "Look at me," he said. "A support class."

"Less talking," Mike called from under his canoe.

"More supporting."

"Walk."

The trail climbed.

At first Joel could see only the pale mud between his sandals, the black roots shining through it, the swinging tips of Alex's paddles ahead. Then the canoe shifted and lowered his world to a thin green strip at his feet. The underside of the hull held the sound close: his breath, the knock of a thwart against a branch, the creak of pack straps from the men ahead, mosquitoes whining in the trapped air beneath the canoe. The forest became fragments. Root. Stone. Mud. Alex's heel slipping. Will's rod tube tapping his pack. Mike's voice somewhere farther ahead.

"Step left. Rock's loose."

The warning came back down the line. Will repeated it. Alex said, "Left rock bad," which was not the same warning but did enough.

Joel stepped left.

The trail did not improve. It went up because some older route had decided that up was better than swamp, then crossed exposed granite where many feet had polished the lichen slick. Blowdown forced them into the brush twice. The first tree could be stepped over with care. The second lay at an angle across the trail, too high to step over cleanly under a canoe and too low to duck under without scraping the hull.

Mike, ahead, handled it with profanity and a sideways crouch. Will talked himself through it in short technical pieces.

"Angle. Don't put weight on that root. That's not stable. No, no, this is stupid."

"You need help?" Mike said.

"I need civil engineering."

Alex waited on the far side and looked back at Joel. "You good?"

"Not elegantly."

"Brand consistency."

Joel lowered one end of the canoe enough to see. The hull scraped bark. He backed up, shifted right, tried again. The yoke bit into one shoulder. His sandal slid in mud and caught. For a second the canoe wanted to twist him off the trail. Alex stepped in and got one hand on the front thwart.

"Hold up."

"I am."

"No, hold up with intention."

"Say something useful."

"Tilt right."

Joel tilted right. Alex lifted the nose just enough. The canoe cleared the blowdown and came through with a hollow clack against a branch that made something in the brush go still.

Not silent. Still.

Joel stood on the far side, breathing hard. The canoe pressed his shoulders. A fly landed on his cheek and walked there. Somewhere beyond the blowdown, the brush held the shape of their passage and then lost it. One alder stem kept trembling after everything else had stopped.

"You're welcome," Alex said, but softly, as if the stillness had reached him too.

Joel shifted the yoke. "Thank you."

Alex blinked. "Wow. Full sentence."

"Don't waste it."

From behind him came Will's cough.

Small. Dry. Almost a laugh but not a laugh. The sound Will made when a correction had jammed itself in his throat and wanted out.

Joel stopped.

He knew immediately what it was. He also knew where Will was: ahead of him, beyond Alex and the blown-down tree, bent under his pack beside Mike. Joel had seen him there seconds earlier.

The cough had come from behind.

Joel's body answered before his mind could make a theory. He turned as much as the canoe allowed and saw the trail dropping away behind them through spruce trunks and alder, empty except for wet roots and the place where their sandals had torn the mud. No person. No red PFD. No bright pack. The lake was hidden below the rise, though wind came up from it in pieces.

"What?" Alex said.

He was twenty feet ahead now, one hand still on the paddles, looking not frightened but inconvenienced by Joel's pause.

"Nothing."

"That's a bad answer."

"Bug."

"A bug coughed?"

Joel looked at him.

Alex lifted both hands as much as the paddles allowed. "I'm just saying, suspicious bugs out here."

Ahead, Will coughed.

Real. Visible. He had stopped beside Mike and bent his head, one fist near his mouth. The same sound, or close enough that the difference had no place to live. Then Will called, "You guys coming?"

Mike said, "Keep moving."

Alex looked at Joel. "See? Answered."

Joel started walking.

There were explanations, and because there were explanations Joel used them. Sound bent under a canoe. Sound bounced off rock. Will might have coughed ahead and the hull might have held it, then thrown it behind him. A bird might make a dry click almost like a cough. Another group might be somewhere on the route, though they had seen no boats and heard no voices. He had seen Will cough. He made that fact heavier than the first sound.

He did not tell Alex what he had checked for: Will's red PFD, Mike's shoulders, the empty trail behind him. He was not ready for the act of checking to need an audience.

He told himself all this while the trail climbed.

The second half of the portage was worse because it looked better. The grade eased, but the tread vanished into wet moss and black mud. Where the trail crossed low places, old sticks had been thrown down by previous travelers, making a narrow corduroy that rolled underfoot. Pack straps creaked. Someone's buckle clicked over and over, regular as a clock. The canoe shifted with each step and made Joel's neck ache. Sweat ran down his spine and cooled when the wind found gaps between the trees.

At a small rise they stopped.

Not because Mike called a rest. Because everyone reached the same private limit at almost the same time and pretended the clearing had requested them. The trees opened enough to show water through black trunks on the other side, another lake or a long widening of the channel, dark under the west wind. The trail continued down through spruce toward it.

Joel lowered the canoe carefully, first to his thighs, then to the ground. The release of weight felt less like relief than proof of damage. His shoulders pulsed where the yoke had been. He stretched his fingers. They had gone numb around nothing.

Will dropped his pack and immediately checked his rod tips.

"If you say one thing about fishing," Mike said, "I will break that rod and make you carry both pieces."

"I wasn't going to say anything."

"You were looking at the water."

"Looking is not fishing."

"For you it is foreplay."

Alex made a wounded sound. "Gentlemen, I'm trying to recover spiritually."

"Recover quieter," Mike said.

Mike had the older map out before he drank water. Not the newer waterproof printout with clean labels and confidence, but the folded sheet he liked because it had more contour lines, more little marks, more of the suggestion that somebody had once known the interior intimately enough to draw it. It was soft at the folds despite the map case. He put one thumb against their route and another farther inland. His hands steadied when the map came out. That was one of the things Joel trusted in him and distrusted in him at the same time.

Joel watched his thumb more than the map. The nail was flattened and pale from years of tools, boats, work Mike complained about and then did well. It pinned the paper as if the paper wanted to get away.

Will leaned over. "We're here?"

"Here." Mike tapped.

"Or here."

"No. Here."

"Those are not as different as your tone implies."

"They are if you know how to read a map."

"Jesus, listen to this guy," Alex said, but tiredly, with less theater in it than usual.

Joel drank from his bottle. The water tasted faintly of plastic and iodine from some old trip, though the bottle had been washed many times since. He watched Mike's thumb move to a tiny square inland beyond the next camp, off a thin broken line that left the water and bent north through blank green.

Before Mike spoke, he touched the small yellow pouch clipped inside the left side of his PFD, a quick check as unconscious as a man patting his wallet before leaving a restaurant. The Highlander key was in there, sealed away with all the ordinary rescue of pavement and ignition. Then his hand returned to the map, and the future became paper again.

"Old maintenance cabin," Alex said. "Is that a cabin cabin or a map lie?"

Mike did not look up. "Map says there's a cabin."

The phrase came out with the flat sturdiness Mike gave to things he wanted settled. Map says. Ink as witness. Paper doing more work than paper should.

Will gave his dry half laugh, which became a cough at the end. "Map says there was a cabin when somebody cared enough to print this."

"Still a cabin."

"Could be a square of moss."

"Could be useful if weather comes in."

"Useful how? Does it have a phone? A road? A concierge?"

"It may be on an old maintenance trace."

"May be is doing a lot of unpaid labor."

Mike folded the map back just enough to keep Will from breathing on it. "If the wind stacks up tomorrow, or somebody twists an ankle, we know there is a feature inland."

"A feature," Will said.

"Yes."

"That's different from a plan."

"It is not the plan. It is information."

Joel looked from the printed square to the forest around them. Human marks did not feel permanent here. They felt like requests. A cabin was a promise people made to themselves about shelter and return. Then the roof went soft. The trail vanished. A tree fell through a wall. The map kept the square because paper aged more slowly than wood.

He almost said that. Alex would call it a poem. Will would correct the historical assumptions. Mike would say a cabin was a cabin until proven otherwise. So Joel drank water instead.

Alex crouched beside the packs, elbows on his knees. For once he did not fill the pause. His face had gone slack with fatigue, and the absence of performance made him look older, not old exactly but closer to the age they all were when no one was reflecting them back. He noticed Joel looking and immediately raised his eyebrows.

"You coming or writing a poem?"

"Drinking water."

"Poem about water."

"Very short poem."

"Let's hear it."

"No."

"Needs work."

Mike capped his bottle. "Five minutes."

"That was not five minutes," Will said.

"It will have been."

They laughed a little, because the wording was stupid and because stopping too long would let their bodies understand what had been done to them. Joel checked his phone out of habit and found nothing. No service, no surprise. The battery was still high because airplane mode had made the device into a clock and camera and dead little mirror. He saw his own face darkly in the glass: sweat, stubble, one mosquito smear near his jaw. Behind his reflection, spruce needles and sky. He put the phone away before the blankness could become another fact.

The second trip back for the remaining gear was not scenic.

They left the canoes and two packs at the rise, marked by nothing because there was no one else to take them, and went back down for the food barrel, the smaller bags, the paddles Alex had grown tired of carrying, and the loose indignities of travel. Without the canoe Joel could see too much. The trail looked worse in full view. Every root seemed placed to prove intention was not required for harm. Mud held the shape of their first passage, heel marks filling with black water. At the blowdown, bark showed fresh scratches from the hulls.

Coming down empty-handed should have felt easier. It did not. Their bodies had already spent the easy part. Knees wobbled on the descent. Wet sandal straps rubbed the same raw places. The heat held low between trunks, close enough that each breath seemed used before it entered the lungs. Above them the wind kept moving in a freer country, visible only as spruce tops leaning and righting themselves.

Halfway down, something cracked off to the right.

All four men stopped. Not dramatically. Just the same small halt, bodies arriving at caution before conversation.

The crack was followed by a brushy movement low in the spruce and alder, quick enough to be an animal and brief enough to be nothing. Joel found the three other men before he let himself study the brush: Mike with one hand still near the barrel strap, Will half-turned, Alex gone still with his mouth closed.

Will whispered, "You guys hear that?"

"Yes," Mike said.

"That's the second one today."

"Second what?"

"Animal in brush."

"We are in a forest."

"I'm aware of the setting."

Alex looked toward the sound, no smile on his face. The joking Alaska part of him had gone quiet. The actual Alaska, or whatever part of him had learned to listen before naming, stood in its place. He did not make the brush into a story. He waited for it to give him one.

"Probably not large," he said.

"Probably," Mike said.

"I don't love that word."

"You don't have to love it. You have to pick up the barrel."

The brush did not move again. Joel watched the place until leaves became only leaves. Hare. Grouse. Squirrel. Wind arriving late at ground level. A branch settling after their passage. The mind wanted an actor. The woods supplied motion and withheld the rest.

"Could have been us," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"From before," Joel said. "Branch settling after the canoes."

"Sure," Will said, too quickly.

Mike picked up the food barrel. "Good. Solved."

No one believed solved. They believed in moving because standing there made belief unnecessary.

They retrieved the gear.

On the climb back, the food barrel took turns punishing each of them. Mike carried it first and made no complaint, which made the load look lighter than it was. Will took it next and immediately began describing improved harness designs no one had asked for. Alex carried it after that and accused the group of packing "emotional ballast." Joel carried it the last stretch, one hand on the barrel strap, the other on a paddle bundle, the weight knocking against his thigh until a bruise began to announce itself.

The barrel latch clicked once against the buckle when he shifted it higher, the same clean plastic sound Alex had made fixing Will's strap.

He knew what it was. He was holding it. Still, the small hard sound made him look down. He saw the latch, the buckle, his own hand whitening around the strap. The source was present and still he had to confirm it.

At the rise, they loaded again. Nobody said lunch. Lunch had happened in the boats, in pieces, hours ago. Nobody said second camp as if naming it would make it closer. The afternoon had moved from warm to heavy. The light under the trees had flattened, and the brightness over the water beyond the trail had taken on the dull pewter look that came before evening.

The descent to the next water was steep in short sections, then muddy where springs crossed the trail. Mike went first with a pack. Will followed with rods and one loose bag held away from his body like it contained evidence. Alex took the paddles and the smaller dry bag. Joel lifted the canoe again.

His shoulders objected before the yoke settled. He made a sound through his nose and stood.

"You good?" Alex asked.

"Yes."

"That was a married yes."

"I'm not married yet."

"Practicing."

Joel adjusted his grip. "Walk."

Alex walked.

The trail narrowed between black spruce. Roots came across it in hard ribs. Once Joel's sandal slid and he had to take three quick steps under the canoe to keep from going down. The hull clipped a sapling and sprang it back. Somewhere ahead Mike called, "Rock on the right."

Will repeated, "Rock on the right."

Alex said, "Rock on the right, but spiritually."

The phrase came down the line and thinned out. Joel stepped left to avoid the rock, then right to avoid a mud hole. He could hear water ahead now: not lake chop, not the wind-crossed slap from the larger water, but a narrow sound, channel water moving through stones or over a lip. Second Camp was supposed to be past that water, near a constricted channel or marsh edge. Sparse sites, Mike had said. Good enough sites, Mike had also said, which meant sites that required agreement from people too tired to object.

The sound of water ahead should have helped. Water meant the end of the portage. It meant the weight would come off, even briefly. But after the wrong cough, the line between ahead and behind felt less clean. Ahead was still ahead. Behind was still behind. He knew this. Knowing it did not stop him from checking both.

The trail leveled. Light showed between trunks. Joel smelled mud before he saw water.

Then, behind him, from uptrail, came a soft click.

Not a branch. Not quite.

A buckle, maybe. A pack strap settling. One hard plastic tooth touching another. All the packs were ahead of him now, or supposed to be.

Joel turned his head and the canoe turned with him, awkward and slow. The trail behind was empty. It rose between spruce trunks, already dark in the spaces where the afternoon could not get in. He stood still, listening. His breath filled the hull. His heartbeat moved in his ears. There was only wind high in the trees and the small water ahead.

"Joel?" Alex called from below.

He faced forward again. "Coming."

"Or writing a poem?"

The old joke irritated him less this time. Maybe because Alex sounded tired. Maybe because the question, repeated at a distance on a narrow trail, made the space behind Joel feel briefly inhabited by all the ordinary things that could have made that click: a loose strap, a branch springing back, their own passage settling after them. Each answer was reasonable. None of them stayed answered.

"Neither," Joel said. "I'm suffering plainly."

Alex grinned up at him from the lower trail. "That's the brand."

He waited there until Joel reached him, one hand on the sapling that marked the last bad step. He did not offer help because offering would make Joel refuse. He only held the sapling out of the way so the canoe could pass.

"You're becoming considerate," Joel said.

"Take it back."

"Withdrawn."

"Thank you."

They reached the water late.

The put-in was a bad one, mud and angled grass with a black channel sliding past. The water beyond was not much of a lake, more a widening where the channel gathered itself before squeezing between alder again. Across it, past a ragged spit of spruce, a small break in the trees suggested the campsite. Or suggested a place where a campsite might be forced by need.

Mike stood with one hand on his hip, looking at the sky as if the sky had failed to meet posted expectations. Will set the rods down with exaggerated care. Alex dropped the paddles and sat on a wet log without checking it first. No one mocked him. That was how tired they were.

"We still crossing?" Will asked.

Mike looked at the distance, the wind, the darkening water. He cleared his throat, but this time no plan came out right away.

That pause did more than any admission would have done.

"It's right there," Mike said at last.

"Right there in daylight," Will said.

"We have daylight."

"We have the concept of daylight."

Alex leaned back on his hands. "I vote for any plan that ends with me horizontal before I become a labor issue."

Mike ignored him and looked at Joel. Joel disliked being pulled into the authority of a decision Mike still wanted to own.

"We can make that," Joel said. "If that's the camp."

"It's the camp," Mike said.

Will picked up the map case, then let it hang at his side. He was too tired to open it.

Alex looked across the water at the break in the trees. He had mud on one cheek now, a gray streak where he had wiped at a mosquito and made himself look younger and worse. "That's a campsite?"

"It's a site," Will said.

"Feels like a legal distinction."

"It is absolutely a legal distinction."

Mike bent for a pack. "Boats."

They loaded without ceremony. The crossing was short and ugly. Wind came down the channel in gusts and pushed them sideways. The canoes rode low with damp gear and tired men. Alex paddled hard in bursts, then stopped too long, then corrected when Joel told him to. Mike and Will led across, their boat dark against the dull water. The ragged spit of spruce slid past on their left. A raven called once from somewhere inland, harsh and flat.

Halfway across, a hollow knock came from behind them.

Joel looked back. Their wake loosened in the wind. The far shore they had left was already a dark line, alder and spruce and mud. No boat. No paddle. Nothing moving except water against grass.

The sound had the body of a paddle touching a gunwale. Not hard. Not a signal. The kind of accidental knock that had followed them since the put-in, ordinary enough to disappear if a person had not become tired enough to count ordinary things. Joel found their paddles by sight: Alex's blade in front, his own blade dripping behind, Mike's and Will's rising together ahead.

"What?" Alex said, without turning.

"Paddle," Joel said.

Alex paddled.

The knock did not repeat. That helped less than it should have.

They came in at the far side one at a time. Mike and Will first, then Joel and Alex, noses of the canoes sliding into mud under overhanging alder. The landing smelled of wet leaves, old ash, and marsh. Mosquitoes lifted from the grass as if the ground had exhaled them. Back from the water, through spruce trunks and brush, Joel could see a small opening and the gray stones of a fire ring.

Second Camp.

No one said thank God. They were not that kind of men, or not where the others could hear. They pulled the boats higher and set packs in a line above the mud. The day had taken longer than Mike had promised and longer than Joel had privately allowed. It had not broken anything yet. It had only spent them down and placed them here, at a camp with poor sightlines and close brush and water talking quietly behind it.

The fire ring had been used recently enough to hold blackened foil, but not recently enough to mean company. Two cut log ends lay half-rotten at the edge of the opening. Beyond them the brush came close, alder and young spruce knitted tight enough that anything ten yards out would be sound before it was shape. The channel behind them kept moving, narrow and dark, taking the late light in broken pieces.

Alex stepped into the opening and turned once, assessing it with theatrical injury and real fatigue.

"Rustic," he said.

Nobody answered. Even Alex let the word die where it was.

Mike stood at the edge of the opening, breathing through his nose.

For once, he did not immediately tell them it was fine.

Then the wind moved in the spruce tops, and the mosquitoes found their faces, and work became the only available answer.


# Chapter 7 - Second Camp

Second Camp was not a campsite so much as a place where other people had once lost the argument with distance and light.

The landing was mud under grass. Mike's canoe nosed in first and stopped as if the ground had taken it by the mouth. He stepped out, sank to the top of one sandal, and said, "This works," with no space between the evidence and the verdict.

Will, still kneeling in the front, looked at the bank. Alder leaned over it in wet green ropes. Black spruce crowded the higher ground. A narrow path went up through flattened grass toward a dim opening where someone had made a fire ring out of dark stones. The path was human in the smallest possible way: grass pressed down, one cut root gone gray at the end, a twist of old foil showing in the ash like a fish scale.

"For what?" Will said.

"For sleeping."

"For malaria research."

"Wrong continent," Alex said from Joel's canoe.

Will pointed at him without turning. "Thank you. I feel corrected and still bitten."

Mosquitoes came up from the grass as if the landing had been saving them. They found wrists, necks, the soft places behind ears. Joel slapped one on the back of his hand and left a red comma there. His shoulders still held the shape of the portage yoke. His thighs felt used up in a way that belonged less to pain than to debt. The day had spent them and then asked them to call this shelter.

Behind the boats, the narrow channel moved blackly between alder stems. It was not wide, but it sounded wider than it was. Water slid against grass, then clicked around stones somewhere out of sight. The sound came and went as the wind moved in the spruce tops. Now close. Now farther away. Now behind him, though the channel had not moved and Joel could still see the strip of water where it belonged.

Joel looked back at the water.

"You're doing it again," Alex said.

"Doing what?"

"The haunted real estate appraisal."

"I'm admiring the lot."

"Needs a detached garage."

"Needs less marsh."

"Marsh is a feature," Mike said. He was pulling his canoe higher by the painter line, heels digging black cuts into the mud.

"Marsh is where features go to die," Will said.

They unloaded because unloading was what came next. That was the mercy of trips like this: the body could still be given orders. Pull the boat higher. Put packs above the wet line. Take the rods away from where they could be stepped on. Find the stove bag. Find the filter. Find a place to sleep. The work did not require anyone to decide whether the site was bad. It only required hands.

They made a boundary out of labor. Canoes above the mud. Paddles together. Packs in one line, not because the line was useful yet but because a line made the site look chosen. The arrangement had the poor authority of evidence placed for a photograph: boats, packs, men, fire ring. A claim made with objects because nothing else would hold it.

Mike walked the edge of the opening once with his head down, reading roots and slope and old use. Joel saw him see what everyone saw: the fire ring too close to brush, the tent ground lumpy, the channel hidden after the first dark bend, no clean place for a man to stand and look outward.

Joel tied the canoes to a spruce root though they were already well above the water. He laid the paddles where he could see them from the fire ring, blades crossed, handles in. It was not a rule. It was only an arrangement. On the granite shelf the night before, the boats had looked like boats wherever they lay. Here they looked like objects that might be needed quickly. If they knocked, he wanted the sight of them to accuse the sound.

"This is what there is," Mike said.

"A ringing endorsement," Will said.

"You want to paddle back in the dark?"

Will looked toward the channel. The west wind had begun to move down it in short, colder pushes. "I want reality to improve its offerings."

"File a complaint."

The flat ground above the landing was smaller than it had looked from the water. One tent would fit near the fire ring if its corners were staked into roots and old ash. The second could be forced between two spruce where the ground rose and fell in slow damp lumps. Joel's hammock needed two trees far enough apart and not already claimed by brush. There were jack pines at the back of camp, but their trunks were narrow, and one of them bent when he put his weight into the strap.

Joel checked his phone while Mike and Will argued over the tent footprint. He did it for the time and because the hand went to the pocket by itself. No service. He had not expected bars here, but the words still made the camp smaller. At First Camp he had almost sent Mara a picture of the fire on granite, then decided the signal was not worth standing on a rock for. He had imagined her seeing it at the kitchen counter in Madison, between a contractor email and the wedding spreadsheet, proof that the trip was doing what he had said it would do. She would have sent back something dry and exact: Proof of life accepted. Did you remember sunscreen? Care disguised as cross-examination. Joel answered that disguise more easily than need. Now there was only the dead screen reflection: spruce needles, his own cheek dark with stubble, the pale bite swelling on the back of his hand. Home remained intact because it was unreachable.

He put the phone away, set his weight into the jack pine again, and watched it bend.

"What?" Alex said, coming up behind him with a dry bag under one arm.

"I don't love this tree."

Alex put a palm on the trunk and gave it a shake. The pine flexed and returned. "Tree says it has concerns about you too."

"It bends."

"People bend. Trees bend. Men in their forties bend and make sounds they used to mock."

"I'm not sleeping under your philosophy."

"It's your funeral hammock."

Joel looked at him.

Alex lifted both hands. "Bad phrasing. Withdrawn."

He said it lightly, but he did withdraw it. Alex could push until someone looked at him with actual resistance, then step back without making a speech about decency. Joel looped the strap higher on the trunk and tested it again. The pine bent, but not as much. It would hold or it would not. The day had reduced standards.

Mike set his tent body down near the fire ring. "Will, get the food up before dark."

Will stood with the map case still strapped to the outside of his pack. "I love when you say my name and an unpleasant task in the same breath."

"You want to sleep with the food?"

"Depends what we're calling food."

"Everything Alex won't eat."

"So most of it."

Alex had opened the blue food barrel and was looking into it with the grave expression of a man reading medical results. "There is a pouch in here called Moroccan vegetable medley."

"That's mine," Will said.

"I assumed. It has the moral loneliness of your cooking."

"It has spices."

"Spices are how suspicious men hide texture."

Mike took the barrel lid from him and snapped it back on. The latch made a small hard click, bright in the close camp.

Joel heard it once from Mike's hand and then once again from behind the fire ring.

Not again. Not the same sound. Probably not. A twig, maybe. A strap buckle under a pack. A branch shifting after the wind passed above it.

He turned.

The spruce behind camp stood too close for looking. Their lower branches were dead and gray, crossed in layers. Beyond them, alder and shadow. No road. No line of sight. No reason for the sound to have gone there except that in this tight place sound had stopped behaving like a thing with an owner.

He did not yet have a rule. Only the impulse to put his eyes on the object that had made the noise and keep them there until the world agreed.

"You good?" Will asked.

Joel faced camp again. "Yeah."

"That was a weird yeah."

"Tired yeah."

"There are subcategories."

"Go hang food."

"Authoritarian yeah," Will said, but he shouldered the rope bag.

The food tree problem took fifteen minutes and made all of them worse. The branches near camp were either too low, too dead, too close to trunks, or too snarled with smaller growth to throw a line cleanly. Will selected a spruce with one living arm reaching out over the brush and began performing a system of knots and rocks and whispered correction. The first throw hit the trunk and dropped straight down. The second caught a dead branch and brought down a rain of brown needles. The third sailed over the right limb but carried the rope into alder, where it tangled hard enough that the line seemed to belong to the brush now.

"Technique," Mike said.

"Physics," Will said.

Alex sat on a pack and slapped mosquitoes off his calves. "I support Will's right to fail publicly."

"You could help," Mike said.

"I am helping from a seated position."

Joel took the rope from Will without asking and worked it loose from the alder. His fingers had gone clumsy from fatigue. The line was damp and gritty. Alex held the alder stems apart with one arm and made a show of looking bored while thorns scratched his wrist. He did not mention the scratches. That was another kind of performance, but a quieter one.

When Joel threw, he underthrew, and the rock bag swung back into his shin.

Alex barked a laugh. It came out hard and delighted, then stopped when no one joined.

"Sorry," he said. "That was objectively funny."

"It hit me," Joel said.

"That's part of the objectivity."

Mike took the rope. "Watch."

His first throw cleared the limb. He did not smile. He only handed the free end to Will as if the world had briefly remembered hierarchy.

Before he stepped back he touched the left side of his PFD, a quick check at the yellow pouch clipped there. The motion was almost hidden by the strap across his chest. Key, Joel thought, and then disliked that the thought made the camp seem smaller: a vehicle waiting somewhere beyond days of water, metal and glass reduced to a dry pouch at Mike's ribs.

"Beautiful," Will said. "I hope the branch snaps."

"Tie it."

Will tied it. The barrel went up awkwardly, swinging once into the trunk with a hollow plastic thump. The sound moved through camp and came back softer from the channel.

Joel looked toward the water. The channel held evening in pieces: black water, green alder, a pale strip of sky caught between leaves. The canoes had been pulled high. The paddles lay crossed near the packs. Nothing there had answered anything.

The barrel hung lower than anyone would have liked. It was off the ground and away from the tents, but not by much, and the branch sagged under the weight with a green patience Joel did not trust. A determined bear could reach it, probably. A lazy bear might still take offense. The thought gave the unease a known shape, so Joel kept it. Bear was a word with weight, tracks, teeth, habits. It was not comfort, but it belonged to the world.

"Good enough," Mike said.

"That's two words doing a lot of work," Will said.

"The bears can fill out an evaluation."

Alex looked up at the blue barrel rocking in the dim. "I hope they write legibly. I have notes."

They put the tents where they would fit. Mike and Will took the one near the fire ring after three minutes of argument about slope, roots, and whether condensation was a solvable problem or a moral condition. Alex claimed the lumpy second site with the casual martyrdom of a man who expected witnesses.

"I get the bad ground," he said.

"You volunteered by standing on it," Mike said.

"Set up your tent."

"History will note my sacrifice."

The tent poles clicked together too neatly. Nylon slid over brush. The little domes looked temporary even before they were staked, two pale claims in a place that did not care whether claims were made. Alex's zipper snagged twice before it closed, then opened again because he had trapped a sleeve in the door. The second zipper pull made a thin metallic stutter that seemed to come from the front of the tent and then from behind it. Alex swore at the fabric, not the sound, and Joel let himself do the same.

Will coughed into his sleeve. A dry little cough, half laugh, half tick. "You are one mosquito away from becoming unbearable."

"I passed unbearable at the last portage. This is transcendence."

Joel clipped his hammock suspension and pulled the fabric from its sack. The nylon made its soft sliding sound, intimate and domestic in the darkening trees. He found himself wanting to set up carefully, to do one task well enough that the day could be answered. The first strap was too low. He moved it. The second tree flexed. He moved that too and lost the angle. Mosquitoes landed on his forehead. Sweat and repellent had made a bitter paste at the edge of his lips.

At First Camp, the water had been visible from everywhere. It had made the site feel held open. Here the camp was a room with no walls and too many doors. Brush pressed in on three sides. The channel, barely visible through alder, seemed less like an edge than a way in. Joel could turn once and lose the canoes, turn again and lose Alex's tent, turn a third time and find the food barrel hanging lower than memory wanted it. Nothing vanished. The camp only refused to fit inside one look.

He pulled the hammock tight and sat in it. The pine bent, complained without sound, and held. He imagined waking in the night to the tree easing over by inches, lowering him into brush while everyone else slept. The image was ridiculous and practical at once. He got up and tightened the strap again.

"Not dead," Alex said from across camp.

"Your concern is moving."

"Mostly curiosity."

"Also moving."

Mike broke deadfall for a small fire. He used the hatchet sparingly, more for pressure than chopping, setting one sandal on the wood and snapping what he could by hand. The hatchet made a flat knock each time it bit. Once, after a knock, another knock came from the channel side. The second was smaller, like a paddle touching a gunwale.

Will, who was kneeling over the stove, stopped with one hand on the fuel canister. "You guys hear that?"

"Hear what?" Mike said.

"Knock."

"Me."

"No. After you."

"Echo."

"Hatchets don't echo off marsh."

"That's not how that works."

Mike gave him a look. "Please explain acoustics to the forest."

Will opened his mouth, reconsidered, and closed it. The restraint looked painful.

Alex leaned toward Joel. "He's growing."

"He'll relapse," Joel said.

"I'm right here," Will said.

"See?"

The stove took three clicks before the flame caught. Each click sounded too neat under the trees. Blue fire trembled beneath the pot, then steadied. Joel filled bottles at the channel while Alex held the filter hose and commented on the smell.

"This shoreline smells like a wet dog had a legal dispute."

"That's just marsh," Joel said.

"Marsh needs help."

Joel balanced one foot on a root and lowered the intake away from the mud he had stirred. The channel looked still until he watched it closely. Then he could see the current pulling bits of grass and foam under the alder. A white moth lifted from the bank and vanished into spruce shade. Mosquitoes whined in his ears. Somewhere downstream a frog made one low sound and stopped.

Alex crouched beside him, quiet for almost half a minute.

"You ever miss it?" Alex said.

"Marsh?"

"No. This." He moved his hand vaguely, taking in water, bugs, twilight, the stupid labor of staying alive in comfort-adjacent ways. "The annual misery project."

Joel looked at him. Alex's face had lost the firelight performance because they were not at the fire. In the last of the open sky his eyes looked tired and ordinary.

"Sometimes while I'm doing it, no," Joel said.

"That's an honest Yelp review."

"Afterward, yes."

Alex nodded. "Yeah."

The filter gave its small patient ticking as water moved into the bottle. Joel waited for Alex to turn the answer into a joke. He did not.

"I keep telling people it's good," Alex said. "Then I get here and remember it's mostly chores with scenery."

"That's accurate."

"But if I say that at home, nobody comes."

Joel screwed the cap onto the bottle. "Maybe that's the system working."

Alex smiled, but the look passed quickly. "Yeah. Maybe."

He took the full bottle from Joel and set it with the others instead of handing it off like a prop. For that small moment he looked less like a man collecting a story and more like a man afraid the story would stop being available if no one kept showing up for it.

Then Mike called, "Water?"

Alex raised his voice. "We're discussing brand identity."

"Bring the water."

"Authority is so needy."

The moment closed. Maybe it had never opened much. Joel handed him a full bottle and took the next empty one. The small exchange left him irritated in a different way. He had wanted Alex to keep joking so his irritation could remain clean. Instead Alex had, for a second, stepped out of the role Joel had assigned him. It made Joel feel ungenerous, which was worse than feeling annoyed.

Dinner was rice, chicken packets, Will's vegetable pouch, and the peanut butter noodles Alex made with the solemnity of a private religion. Steam lifted from the pots and held the smell of salt, garlic powder, fuel, and wet wool. They ate sitting on packs, rocks, and exposed roots around the fire. No one bothered with plates longer than necessary. The sun went behind the trees before it went down, and the camp entered an early, local dark.

Mike set his bear spray upright beside his pack before he sat to eat. It was an ordinary gesture, almost fussy, the same way he kept the map case zipped and the canoe painters tied off where he could see them. The orange canister stood in the firelight like a tool waiting for a task no one wanted to name.

Will tried to defend his vegetable pouch.

"It's not bad," he said.

"It's warm compost," Mike said.

"You didn't even try it."

"I saw it."

"Sight is not taste."

"Sight is how taste warns you."

Alex stirred his noodles. "I respect Will's commitment to making dinner feel like a graduate seminar."

"Your dinner is peanut butter and starch."

"Exactly. It knows what it is."

"It knows nothing. It's paste."

"A humble paste."

Joel ate what was handed to him and let their voices move around the fire. He was too tired to contribute much. The old rhythm still worked, even here, which helped and did not help. They had made the same rough circle around kitchen islands, motel beds, picnic tables, granite shelves. Now they made it in a dark little camp where the brush stood close enough to take the heat off their backs.

Cold began under the sweat in Joel's shirt. It came first along his spine, then through the damp cuffs at his wrists. He put on a fleece without standing. The fabric stuck at his shoulder where bug spray had dried tacky on his skin.

Mike checked his phone once, holding it low as if embarrassed by the gesture. The screen lit his face blue. He turned slightly from the fire and waited.

"Anything?" Will said.

"No."

"Because phones famously love marsh basins."

"I know why."

"I'm just saying."

"Then don't."

Mike put the phone away. Joel noticed that he did not make a joke about it. Earlier, at the take-out, Mike had checked service with annoyance, as if towers were another thing the world owed him for planning correctly. Here he checked like a man touching a pocket where a key should be. The absence was expected and still became information.

He touched the left side of his PFD again before he sat, confirming the little yellow pouch without looking down. The Highlander key was still there. That should have been reassuring. Instead it made the route feel like a long sentence with the verb hidden at the end.

"We're fine," Mike said.

No one had asked. That made the sentence heavier than it needed to be.

Will scraped his spoon around his pot. "We're tired. That's different."

"Fine and tired overlap."

"Lots of bad things overlap."

"Jesus, listen to this guy," Alex said, but gently. "One questionable dinner and he's writing a systems paper on doom."

Will pointed the spoon at him. "Doom has inputs."

"Doom has vegetables, apparently."

Joel laughed. It surprised him. Not because the joke deserved it, but because the day had left some last small piece of him available to be moved. Alex caught the laugh and smiled into his noodles without looking up. Joel saw the need in it and disliked himself for naming it. Why shouldn't a man want proof he had reached the people he had come this far to be with?

Dark thickened. The fire stayed small. The wood was damp enough to smoke but dry enough to burn reluctantly, making little red tunnels in itself. Sparks rose and vanished under the spruce boughs. Beyond the ring of light, the brush took every outline and kept it.

Alex told the thunderstorm story because no one had enough energy to stop him.

"Different trip," he said, pointing his spoon at Mike. "Different river. Same prophet of weather. This guy is standing there in his little hat like he's filed paperwork with the atmosphere."

"I said it was moving north," Mike said.

"You said it with force. That's the important part. You said, and I quote, 'That's moving north.'"

"It was moving north."

"It hit us in the face from the west."

"Storm systems rotate."

Will sat forward. "No, no, no, don't give him rotation. He will live there forever."

"Weather has multiple directions," Mike said.

Alex slapped his knee. "Weather had your number. Weather looked at your map and said, 'I don't work for this man.'"

"I got us off the water."

"After the sky turned green."

"Greenish."

Will laughed and coughed into his sleeve. Joel laughed too, because he remembered parts of it: Mike shouting over rain, Alex holding a tarp like a doomed sail, Will insisting lightning distance was being calculated incorrectly by everyone but him. Joel had been younger then by only a few years, but the memory had the false distance of another life. The men in it had stronger knees and less money worry in their faces. Or maybe memory always edited the body kindly until grief or fear restored the costs.

"Best part," Alex said, warming now, "is Mike yelling, 'It's just wind,' while a branch comes down right behind him."

"Small branch," Mike said.

"Tree attempted murder."

"Tree missed."

"And we've let you keep navigating."

The fire snapped. Not loud. A resin pocket, maybe. At the same moment something knocked once from the channel.

The talk stopped cleanly enough that the absence of it became its own sound.

Joel looked toward the water. The canoes were pulled high on the bank, noses angled away from the channel, painter lines tied off around a spruce root. They were not touching. No wave reached them. No paddle lay where water could lift it.

Mosquitoes whined. The stove, cooling, ticked once. Somewhere behind the camp the food barrel creaked faintly on its rope.

"Boat settling," Mike said.

"Boat is on shore," Will said.

"Then shore settling."

"Shore is famously restless."

"Branch," Joel said.

He said it because it was plausible and because plausible things needed saying first. Branch. Animal. Rope. Water. Echo. Camp gear cooling. The world still had a large vocabulary of ordinary causes.

Alex looked at him across the fire. "Madison with the emotional support branch."

"Take what you can get."

"I will."

Will picked up his headlamp and angled it toward the landing. The beam showed alder leaves, mud, the side of Joel's canoe, grass flattened by their feet. Nothing moved. Will kept the light there too long.

"Don't burn your battery staring at shrubbery," Mike said.

"Shrubbery made a noise."

"Everything made a noise."

"That's less comforting than you think."

Mike poked the fire with a short stick. "Eat. Clean up. Sleep."

"The old sequence," Alex said. "Civilization in three commands."

They cleaned up slowly. Spoons were licked, then rinsed. Pots were wiped with a scrap of paper towel that disintegrated before anyone wanted it to. Food trash went into the odor bag. Will lowered the barrel just enough to add the night's waste and leftover packets, then hauled it up again with small irritated grunts. The branch bowed, lifted, bowed again. The rope rasped over bark.

The latch clicked under his hand.

From the brush behind Joel came a second click.

He froze with one hand on the pot.

This time Will heard it too. Joel saw him turn, not toward the sound exactly, but toward Joel turning toward it.

"What?" Mike said.

No one answered fast enough.

Alex filled the gap. "If it's a bear, tell it we have Moroccan vegetable medley."

"Bears have standards," Will said, but his voice had changed.

Mike stood. "Enough."

He took his headlamp and walked to the back of camp. Not far, only to the first screen of spruce. He shone the light between trunks, then left, then right. The beam caught old needles, root, a dead branch polished by old weather. It caught two pale moths and made them into white flecks. It caught nothing with eyes. Joel kept the canoes in the corner of his vision while Mike searched the trees, as if two confirmations could be held at once by force.

Joel watched the light instead of the dark beyond it. That was the more useful thing, he told himself. The beam showed what could be known. Everything outside it could remain unsorted for another minute.

"It's camp noise," Mike said.

"Camp noise from outside camp," Will said.

"We're in the woods. Outside camp starts everywhere."

Mike came back and sat down harder than he needed to. The orange bear-spray canister stood beside his pack, untouched and upright, its label bright in the firelight.

Joel listened past the fire. That was the mistake, if it was a mistake. The more he listened, the more the dark supplied: brush sounds, water sounds, the high movement of wind. Every ordinary noise auditioned. He could hear Alex breathing through his nose as he tied off his tent guyline. He could hear Will's spoon scrape once, though dinner was finished. He could hear Mike swallow. He could hear his own pulse inside the tender places where the pack straps had ridden. Listening made a second camp around the first one, larger and less certain.

First Camp had been open enough to let sound leave. Here sound went into the brush and came back handled.

Joel thought of the old maintenance cabin Mike had pointed to on the map, the tiny square inland, the promise that something human had once stood in all this and been useful. A cabin on a map, a fire ring in a bad camp, old foil in ash: marks people made against being swallowed by distance. The marks lasted longer than the reasons for them. Then other people came along and mistook them for permission.

"You okay?" Alex asked.

Joel realized he had been staring at the fire ring.

"Yeah."

"Another subcategory?"

"Long day yeah."

"That's the premium tier."

"Costs extra."

"Everything does."

The line landed flatter than Alex likely meant it to. Mike looked at the fire. Will looked away. Money was not a clean subject with Mike around, or work around Will. The old friendships were full of doors like that: ordinary sentences that opened onto rooms no one wanted to enter at night.

Alex seemed to notice the drop and disliked it. "Okay," he said, pushing himself up. "I'm taking a piss. Nobody panic."

"Stay close," Mike said.

Alex spread his arms. "See? Panic already."

"Close," Mike said.

"Quit mothering me."

"Headlamp," Joel said.

"I have one."

"On your head?"

Alex touched his forehead, found nothing, and stood there for half a second with his mouth open.

Will made a small delighted sound. "Documented."

"This is harassment," Alex said.

"This is care," Joel said.

"Care is harassment with better marketing."

He came back for the lamp from the top of his pack. The strap had twisted. He untangled it with more force than required and settled it on his forehead. When he clicked it on, the beam jumped across Joel's face, then Will's, then Mike's sandals, then the brush.

"Spray," Mike said.

Alex looked down at himself. "For pissing?"

"For walking out of camp."

"I am not walking out of camp. I am visiting the suburbs."

"Spray."

Alex held Mike's stare for one beat, then made a show of sighing. "The suburbs are dangerous."

His bear spray was in the side pocket of his pack. He clipped the canister to his belt with exaggerated care, then patted it twice. The plastic buckle made a small, tidy click. Joel did not like that the sound made him look toward the food barrel first.

"Don't piss on anything we're eating," Will said.

"I make no promises."

"Or the food rope."

"Now you're limiting me artistically."

"Alex," Mike said.

"Close. I heard the sermon."

He walked toward the channel side instead of the back brush, stepping through the uneven edge where grass met alder. He did not go far. Ten yards, maybe less. His headlamp marked him in pieces: white on leaves, white on his shoulder, white on the mud at his feet. Then alder broke the beam and made it appear and disappear as he moved. The distance was small enough to be insulting later. A man could still talk from there. A man could still be seen, until one stem and then another took him apart.

Joel watched longer than he needed to.

"He's fine," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"What?"

"Nothing."

"He's right there."

"I know."

Mike seemed ready to say more, then did not. The restraint had the feel of fatigue rather than tact. He went back to the fire and used the same stick to push the ends of two small logs inward. Sparks rose and died. Will lowered himself onto a pack, then immediately stood again because something under it had shifted. He swore softly and adjusted the straps.

His headlamp had been on his forehead for the last check of the landing. Now he pulled it down to save the battery, then forgot what he had done with it and let the strap hang loose around his neck.

From beyond the alder came the small ordinary sound of a man pissing in the woods. It should have made the camp feel normal. Instead, because they all heard it and pretended not to, it made the boundary of camp more exact. Here were the men by the fire, not looking. There was Alex past the last agreed-upon edge, also pretending he had not gone beyond anything.

"Tomorrow," Will said, because he could not leave silence alone, "we need to get moving early if the wind's still west."

Mike exhaled through his nose. "It'll lay down overnight."

"Maybe."

"It will."

"Weather has multiple directions," Alex called from the dark.

"Shut up and finish," Mike called back.

Alex laughed, not the full bark, but a shorter version made private by distance. The alder caught it and thinned it. For a second, after the laugh ended, Joel thought he heard the shape of it continue beyond Alex, farther down the channel.

Not the laugh itself. The afterimage of it, as if the channel had kept the shape and let the man go.

He looked toward the water.

"What now?" Will said.

"Nothing."

"That's becoming a genre."

Joel almost told him. Then he did not. If he said it, the sound would have to sit with them by the fire.

The fire settled lower. Wind moved in the spruce tops but did not come down to the ground. The camp smelled of smoke, damp nylon, bug spray, old ash, marsh water, sweat cooling in synthetic shirts. Joel rubbed one thumb along the edge of his spoon and felt a nick in the metal he had never noticed before. Small damage, discovered by touch because there was too little light to see.

Alex's headlamp turned toward the channel.

The motion was small, but Joel caught it because he was already watching the breaks of light through the alder. The beam had been pointed down. Then it lifted and fixed on something farther out, past the place Alex should have needed to look.

"Alex?" Joel said.

His voice came out lower than he intended.

Alex did not answer him. The beam stayed still. Then it moved right, slowly, following.

From the channel edge came a small sound.

Not a knock this time. Not the barrel latch. Not water. A branch touched another branch and stopped. It was the kind of sound a person made when he was trying not to make one, except no person had any business there and Alex was already the person at the edge.

Will stood partway up. "You guys hear that?"

Mike turned his head. "Alex."

The headlamp swung back toward camp for a second. Joel saw the white oval of Alex's face through leaves, amused and irritated, alive with the old impatience, already forming the answer that would bring the moment back into the old shape.

"What?" Alex said.


# Chapter 8 - Nobody Panic

For a second the camp held its shape.

Mike by the fire with the stick still in his hand. Will half risen from the pack, one knee bent, his headlamp strap loose around his neck. He had taken it off to save battery and forgotten he had done so. Joel standing with his spoon in his hand, the nicked edge pressed into the pad of his thumb. Beyond the alder, Alex's headlamp made a small white wound in the brush.

Alex had just said, "What?"

It had come back in his ordinary voice, irritated more than afraid, the voice he used when someone interrupted a story or questioned a minor competence. The word should have ended the moment. It should have been followed by a joke, a complaint, a report of a branch or his own stupidity. Alex could restore ordinary weather around himself. A bad landing became a bit. A missed throw became evidence of conspiracy. The group had relied on that more than Joel wanted to admit.

But the headlamp did not turn fully back toward camp.

It held low and slightly right, aimed into the dark beyond the place where Alex stood. The light touched alder leaves, moved over them, and stopped on nothing Joel could see. The beam looked like fact until it failed to find a body.

A sound came from beyond him. Not a word, not quite. A wet little click in the throat of the channel, like water catching under a root and letting go. Joel held still, waiting for it to become water again.

"Alex?" Joel said.

His voice went into the little camp and nothing answered.

Then Alex said, "Hold up."

Not loud. Not theatrical. The way he had said it at the beaver dams when mud kept one sandal, the way he had said it on the rough portage when a pack strap caught on blowdown. Practical. Annoyed. Expecting the world to wait because he had found a small problem inside it.

Joel could not tell whether Alex had said it to them or to whatever had made him turn.

Mike stood all at once. "Come back in."

Alex did not answer.

"Alex," Mike said, sharper.

Will fumbled at his headlamp. The plastic housing clicked against his collarbone. "What is it?"

"Shut up," Mike said.

"I'm asking what--"

"Shut up and get your light."

Joel had not moved. He saw that before he felt it. His body remained in camp while his attention had gone through the alder and stopped beside Alex. The spoon was still in his hand. He set it on the nearest pack too carefully, as if a louder movement might answer for him.

Alex's beam jerked left.

"What the hell," Alex said.

He took one step farther from camp. Not a run. Not panic. The small offended step of a man going to prove the thing was nothing before anyone could make a rule about it.

Brush moved.

At first the sound was small enough to stay ordinary. A branch scraping another branch. A foot in wet grass. Something pushing through alder with less care than a person and more care than wind. Then the brush gave way in a heavy rush, not from one point but along a short line of darkness. Leaves flashed silver in Alex's lamp. Stems bent, snapped back, bent again. For half a blink the beam caught weight: a low dark change in the leaves, or only the leaves folding over one another. No face. No eyes. By the time the light steadied, there was no animal in it.

Alex shouted once. Not a word. A raw, insulted burst of air.

Mike grabbed for the bear spray.

The canister had been set upright beside his pack after dinner, part of the normal theater of caution. At First Camp it had seemed like an object that proved they were responsible. Here it looked small in Mike's fist. He tore the safety free with his thumb and started toward the channel side.

"Back," he said.

No one went back.

Joel moved because Mike moved. Later he would not know whether he had decided to follow or had been pulled by the sight of another man acting. He took two steps, then stopped and turned for something, anything. His hand closed around the nearest paddle, the bent-shaft one Alex had been using all day because he liked the look of it and because Will said it made no difference for his form.

"Joel," Will said.

"Light," Joel said.

Will had his own lamp on now. The beam shook over the fire, Mike's shoulder, Joel's paddle blade, the wet path to the channel. "Alex!"

"Don't yell," Mike snapped.

"Alex!"

"Will."

The second shout came from beyond the alder.

Alex this time. Or the shape of him under pressure. It started as a word and broke before it reached them. Something hit brush hard. A body hit mud. There was a wet thump, then a tearing sound too brief and physical for Joel's mind to keep. Alder whipped back and slapped itself. The headlamp beam spun upward: leaves from below, a hard piece of sky, the white undersides of leaves still moving after the violence had passed.

Joel ran.

He struck the first screen of alder shoulder-first. Branches raked his face and arms. His sandal sank past the sole into black muck. The paddle caught across two stems and almost tore from his hand. Mike was ahead of him by two body lengths, broad back hunched, bear spray out, lamp beam jumping. Will came behind making a continuous broken sound, not quite Alex's name anymore, not quite words.

The brush made every distance dishonest. Ten yards became a room with no door. Mike's light opened one narrow piece of it at a time: green leaves turned white, black water between roots, mud polished by movement, a broken stem bright at the fresh split. Joel's own light found Mike's elbow, then lost it. Something crashed to their right. Or behind them. Or across the channel. The alder broke the sound and gave it back from everywhere.

"Stop," Mike said.

Joel stopped because the command had found some old trail in him. Will did not. He pushed into Joel's back and nearly knocked him down.

"Where is he?"

"Quiet."

"Where is he, Mike?"

"Quiet."

Something breathed beyond the light.

That was the only word Joel had for it, and even as he thought it he distrusted it. It might have been wind moving through wet leaves. It might have been Alex. It might have been his own breath made huge by the cup of his hands around the paddle shaft. It came once, low and close, with a wet catch at the end.

Mike raised the spray.

"Don't," Joel said, too late.

The canister made a hard choking hiss. Orange fog hit the alder and opened in the beam, immediate and chemical, a cloud with weight. It drove back into them as soon as it left the nozzle. Joel inhaled and his throat closed. Fire ran into his eyes. He turned away coughing, blind, spit hanging from his mouth. Will cursed and stumbled into him. Mike kept spraying for another second, then swore hard enough to tear his voice.

"Jesus," Will said. "Jesus, Jesus."

"Move left," Mike said.

"I can't see."

"Then blink."

"That's not--"

"Left."

They moved because standing in one place was worse. Joel wiped at his eyes with the heel of his hand and made the burning worse. His lamp beam became water and color. The alder stems doubled, then slid back into one another. His lungs wanted air and rejected what came.

The smell of spray covered everything for a few seconds. Then another smell came through under it.

Mud. Split green wood. Marsh water. Blood.

Mike stopped so abruptly Joel almost ran into him.

"No," Will said.

He said it once, softly, before he had seen enough. The word had arrived ahead of understanding.

Alex lay ten yards beyond the first screen of alder, in a place that had not looked like a place from camp. There was a narrow shelf of mud between roots, then black water. The alder grew thick around it, trunks no wider than broom handles but multiplied into a wall. Something had cleared a gap through them. Stems were bent flat. Leaves floated in the water with their pale undersides up.

Joel's mind refused Alex at first. It accepted only objects.

One hiking sandal, heel-down in mud. A calf streaked with black muck. A hand open as if asking for something small. The headlamp strap twisted under his cheek, cracked at one corner, its light pointing sideways into the water. A torn sleeve. An orange canister half-hidden in grass near his hip, belt clip turned in the weeds, safety still bright, trigger clean. Blood on alder leaves, too dark under the lamps. A wet red place where the body had stopped being only Alex.

"No, no, no," Will said.

Now the words had weight. They came out of him as if something was being unloaded too fast.

Mike put out one arm and blocked him.

"Don't touch him."

"He's--"

"Don't touch him."

"We have to--"

"Back up."

Will tried to go around him. Mike caught him by the front of his PFD and shoved him hard enough that Will slipped in the mud and caught himself on a branch.

"What is wrong with you?" Will said.

"Do not touch him."

"He's right there."

"And whatever did it might also be right there."

The sentence entered the brush and gave the dark a shape. Joel lifted the paddle with both hands. He did not know what he intended to do with it. Hit a bear. Hit the air. Give his hands a task.

His eyes kept returning to Alex's hand. Mud darkened the lines of the palm. One fingernail was torn back. The hand looked separate from the violence because it was still shaped like Alex: broad, careless, capable, the hand that had slapped mosquitoes off his calf and held the filter hose badly and stirred peanut butter noodles with religious seriousness. Joel had spent whole stretches of the day annoyed by the man attached to it. Now the hand lay open and all of Joel's measurements became obscene.

The woods were very quiet.

Not empty. Quiet. There was water moving under the alder. There were mosquitoes, somehow still alive in the spray and blood, whining thinly around their faces. There were the small internal noises of three men trying to breathe through panic. But the larger woods had withdrawn from them. No frog. No night bird. No branch settling after a disturbance.

A small wet sound came from beside Alex's cheek. Will jerked toward it. Joel's light found only current drawing a ribbon of blood under a root.

"Alex," Will said.

Joel almost told him not to say the name. He did not know why. It would have been cruel and useless. He also knew, in some part below reason, that names went farther here than they should.

Mike swept his light past the body and into the brush beyond it. "Hey!"

"Don't," Joel said.

Mike ignored him. "Hey! Get out!"

His voice cracked on the second word. Not fear exactly. Force applied to something that would not take shape.

No bear moved in the beam.

The light showed broken alder, mud gouged into shelves, black water where the channel curled close, a dead spruce branch with old gray lichen on it. It showed one dark smear low on a trunk. It showed something that might have been a print near the waterline and then, when Joel looked longer, only a collapsed place in mud. It showed wet roots scraped pale where bark had come off. It showed no eyes, no retreat, no body large enough to carry the whole explanation.

Then, to their left, brush ticked once.

All three lights went there.

Nothing.

Mike sprayed again, a short burst this time, reckless and furious. The cloud opened and fell. Joel turned his face away before it reached him, but it caught the side of his neck and made the skin burn.

"Stop wasting it," Will said.

Mike rounded on him. "You want to save it?"

"I want to see."

"See what?"

"Anything."

"It's a bear."

The word sounded prepared. Not because Mike had known, but because it was the only box large enough and close enough to put the scene inside. Bear. The route had bears. They had bear spray. They had hung food badly in a bad camp near water and brush. A bear could be reported. A bear belonged to the world.

Joel looked again at Alex's body and tried to make the word fit over it.

It fit in places. Violence. Strength. Blood. Mud. The smashed alder. The speed of it. The plain fact that large animals could do more damage to a human body than humans liked to remember.

It did not fit everywhere.

The food barrel was back at camp, still hanging. Alex had been standing. His spray had not been fired. There had been no bawl, no roar, no warning huff, no heavy retreat after Mike came at it with lights. Nothing had gone for the barrel or the trash or the cooking pots. The ground near Alex showed confusion but not the certainty of a large animal leaving. Maybe mud did not keep prints. Maybe alder hid movement. Maybe bears did not follow the little script men carried for them. Maybe Joel knew too little to know what he was seeing. Fear was not expertise.

He did not say the other thing, because it had no useful place in a bear attack: Alex had turned before the rush. Alex had answered something.

"Bear," Mike said again.

Will heard the repetition and flinched from it. "Where is it?"

"Gone."

"How do you know?"

"Because we're standing here."

"That's not how that works."

"Will."

"No, don't Will me. Where did it go? It was just--it was just right here."

Mike lifted the canister toward the dark. "It ran."

"We didn't hear it run."

"We heard everything."

"No. We heard brush. We heard--"

"Stop."

Will's mouth stayed open. His face had gone pale and shiny under the headlamp. The lamp tilted down and lit his own hands, which were shaking hard enough that the beam trembled over the mud at his feet.

Joel stepped closer to Alex.

He did it slowly, not out of courage but because his mind needed one more fact and hated him for needing it. Mud sucked at his sandal. He kept the paddle between himself and the brush as if the shape of defense mattered. Mike made a warning sound but did not stop him.

Alex's face was turned partly into the mud.

Joel saw enough to know he should not try to see more. One eye closed. Cheek streaked. Mouth open as if he were about to object to the inconvenience. Mud had filled the small crease beside his nose. The headlamp had slipped under him and lit the water, where blood moved in a thin ribbon and separated into the black current. The sight felt private. Joel backed up one step.

He remembered Alex at the channel earlier, crouched beside him while the filter ticked into the bottle.

You ever miss it?

This.

The annual misery project.

Joel had given him half an honest answer. Afterward, yes. He had not said the smaller, meaner truth: that he missed the men more reliably from Madison, when irritation had time to edit itself into affection and Alex became a story told at a reasonable volume. Being with them was harder than loving them from a distance. The trip needed planning, mileage, vacation time, weather windows, stored gear, staged keys, and the old deliberate lie that if they repeated the shape often enough, nothing essential had changed.

Now Alex was ten yards outside camp, and every unsaid thing had become permanent.

"We need to do something," Will said.

Mike rubbed at his eyes with his free wrist. "We are doing something."

"No, we're standing here."

"We're making sure it's gone."

"And then what?"

Mike did not answer.

The question went through Joel with a cold practical edge. Then what. Carry Alex? Where? Through alder, mud, two days of route behind them, harder water ahead, no service, no beacon. Leave him? Stay beside him? Sit in this brush until morning with whatever had done it still close enough to hear them breathe?

Will said, "We can't just--"

"I know," Mike said.

"Do you?"

Mike turned his light on Will, not quite at his face. "I know."

There was no comfort in the words. There was only the beginning of an argument too large for the dark.

Something splashed softly at the waterline.

Joel's body answered before thought. The paddle came up. Mike swung his light. Will made a strangled noise and stepped back into Joel's shoulder.

The beam found water moving around a broken branch. A leaf turned over in the current. A bubble rose and broke.

Nothing else.

"Back to camp," Mike said.

"No," Will said.

"Yes."

"We can't leave him."

"We're not leaving him."

"What do you call this?"

"Surviving the next eight hours."

Will stared at him. "Listen to yourself."

"I am."

"Mike--"

"If that bear is still inside fifty yards, you standing here saying his name is not helping."

The cruelty of it worked because it was practical. Will shut his mouth. His chest rose and fell fast. He looked at Joel as if Joel might have another category available, some third thing between abandonment and suicide.

Joel did not.

"We mark where he is," Joel said. His voice came out thin. He cleared his throat and hated the sound. "We go back. We keep lights out except for checks. We stay together. At first light we come back."

Will shook his head once, hard. "At first light he's still dead."

No one answered.

Mike crouched without turning his back fully to the brush and picked up Alex's headlamp by the strap. Joel almost told him to leave it. Then he saw the cracked light shining stupidly into the water and understood that Mike needed one small task that could be completed. Mike switched it off. Darkness took back the water immediately.

"Don't take his stuff," Will said.

"It's a light."

"It's his."

"Will."

"Don't say my name like that."

Mike held the lamp out to Joel, who took it because someone had to. The plastic had split near the battery case. It was slick. He did not look at what made it slick. He clipped it to the front of his own PFD, then unclipped it again and carried it in his fist, away from his body.

They backed out of the alder.

No one wanted to turn first. They made an ugly sideways retreat, lights pointing into too many places, feet slipping in mud. Branches caught on straps and sleeves. Once Will stumbled, and Mike grabbed his arm, and Will yanked away as if the help itself accused him. Joel kept the paddle across his body and listened for something following. Every scrape they made invited another scrape. Every branch they bent sprang back with a small wet tick that sounded, for half a second, deliberate.

When they broke through the alder screen into camp, the fire had settled into red pockets under ash. The tents stood open-mouthed and thin, nylon walls holding the small warmth of men who were no longer inside them. The food barrel hung pale and round in the back of camp, absurd in its correctness. The latch faced the fire exactly as before. The canoes lay high on the bank. The pots were stacked. The water bottles stood where Joel had left them. Their camp looked abandoned by people who had intended to come back in a minute.

Will stopped at the edge of the firelight and bent over with his hands on his knees. He retched once but nothing came up.

Mike went straight to the packs. "Spray. Where's the second can?"

"Out there," Joel said. "Alex had it."

The sentence broke something in the air. He had not meant to say had.

Mike looked toward the alder.

"I'm not going back for it," Will said.

"Nobody asked you."

"I'm saying it."

"Stop saying things."

"Don't tell me to--"

"Both of you," Joel said.

It surprised them enough to work. Mike turned. Will straightened halfway. Joel stood with Alex's dead headlamp in one hand and the paddle in the other and felt ridiculous and old and utterly unqualified.

"We need the boats farther up," he said. "Then we sit where we can see the channel and the back brush. No tents."

"No tents?" Will said.

"If something comes through one, I don't want to unzip my way out."

Will almost laughed. It came out as one hard breath.

Mike nodded once. "Canoes."

Work saved them because work did not ask what it meant. They hauled the canoes another few feet from the mud line, too close to camp now but better than the channel. Joel and Mike turned one sideways near the fire ring as a poor barrier, though all of them knew it would stop nothing determined. Will gathered paddles and laid them within reach, then moved them again because he did not like the first arrangement. He made a row along a log as if order could become defense.

Mike checked the food barrel line. It was still up. The latch was closed. He yanked the rope once and the barrel swung, knocking softly against the trunk.

All three men froze.

The knock seemed to come back from the channel side, smaller.

No one spoke.

Joel could not tell whether it was echo, memory, the barrel hitting twice, or something else answering with the vocabulary of their camp. His eyes burned from spray. His throat tasted like metal and pepper. He wanted to say branch, or barrel, or nothing. He did not. Words were beginning to feel less like explanations than offerings.

Mike steadied the barrel until it stopped moving. He tied the rope off again with fingers that missed the first turn.

"It's an echo," Will said.

His voice sounded angry at the mercy he had given them.

"Yeah," Mike said.

The agreement was too fast.

They put on every layer within reach. Fleece over damp shirts. Rain jackets zipped to the throat. Hats pulled low. Joel's hands shook when he tried to work the zipper. He looked at the pull and forgot what order fingers used. Will reached over and did it for him without comment, then turned away before the kindness could become visible.

Mike tried his phone.
