He did it facing slightly away from them, as if the old embarrassment still mattered. The screen lit his face. He held it up, turned once, stepped onto a root, raised it higher. No bars. No service. The blue light showed flecks of bear spray on his cheek and the wet shine under his eyes. He put the phone away.

"Nothing," Will said.

Mike did not answer.

"Because of course nothing," Will said. "Because why would we have the one thing that solves this. A beacon. A messenger. One stupid red button."

"Will."

"No. Don't. This is exactly when people have the thing."

"We don't."

"I am aware."

"Then save it."

Will laughed once, without humor. "Save what?"

Mike sat on a pack near the fire ring and set the partly spent bear spray across his knees. "Your battery."

"Great."

"Your air."

"Good."

"Your whatever keeps you from making this worse."

Will stared at him. For a second Joel thought he would cross the small distance and hit Mike. Instead he crouched by the turned canoe and put both hands over his face. His shoulders moved once. Then again. No sound came out. That made it harder to watch than crying would have been.

Joel looked away and saw Alex's tent.

The second tent, the lumpy ground he had claimed with theatrical martyrdom, stood half zipped. One guyline had pulled loose. The door flap moved when the wind reached camp, lifting and dropping with a small nylon whisper. Inside would be his sleeping bag, his dry clothes, the pouch of bland food he had guarded from Will's spices, the paperback he had been unlikely to read, his spare socks. He had said nobody panic and left all of it arranged for morning.

Joel turned from it.

"We should get his pack," Will said, still crouched.

"No," Mike said.

"Not from out there. From the tent."

Mike looked at the tent. "In the morning."

"It's right there."

"In the morning."

"You think the bear cares about his socks?"

"I think if we start sorting a dead man's gear right now, you're going to come apart."

Will lifted his head. His face was wet and furious. "Don't make me your project."

"Then stop trying to fix the one thing we can't fix in the dark."

"Jesus Christ, Mike."

"Enough." Joel heard his own voice and recognized the thinness in it. "Enough for tonight."

They sat under the tarp because it was the only structure that let them see out. It had been pitched low against possible rain and now made them hunch. Water from old condensation or fog gathered along one seam and fell at intervals onto the dirt. Tick. Tick. Then a longer pause. Then tick again. The sound made time without moving it forward.

They killed the fire after a short argument. Mike wanted light. Joel wanted not to sit in a lit room for whatever stood outside it. Will said both options were bad in different, specific ways until Mike told him to pick one or be quiet. Will picked quiet and made it look like injury.

When the last flame died, the world became depthless.

Headlamps stayed off except for brief checks. In darkness the camp rebuilt itself out of sound. The channel spoke first: water under alder, small and black. Then the food barrel rope, tightening and easing as wind moved above. Then nylon from Alex's tent. Then someone's swallow. Then Mike's thumb, once, on the bear spray safety. The click was tiny and enormous.

Joel sat with the paddle across his lap. He had washed his hands twice from a water bottle and could still smell blood where the cracked plastic of Alex's lamp had marked his palm. Maybe it was not there. Maybe the smell had entered the whole camp. He rubbed his thumb against the seam of his pants.

Will whispered, "What if he was still--"

"No," Mike said.

"You don't know what I'm going to say."

"Yes, I do."

"What if he was still alive when we--"

"No."

"You don't know."

"Will."

"Don't say my name."

"He was gone."

The words sat between them. Joel wished Mike had not said them. He wished someone else would say they were true. He wished truth had a sound you could identify in the dark.

Will's breathing changed. "You can't know that."

Joel looked toward the place where Will was only a darker shape beside the canoe. "He was gone."

It cost him less than it cost Will to hear it. That made it worse.

For a while no one spoke.

Joel tried not to listen. The effort made him listen harder. His mind sorted sound into columns without permission: water, rope, nylon, breath, branch. Ordinary. Ordinary. Ordinary. Maybe ordinary. He thought of the motel wall, the way he had lain awake and matched each noise to a source until he could sleep. Bathroom fan. Door latch. Alex laughing through plaster. Mike's cough or Will's. It had seemed like a way to settle the world. Now it felt like practice for a test that had changed its questions.

A branch cracked beyond the channel.

Will inhaled.

Mike lifted the spray.

Joel held up one hand, though neither man could see the gesture well in the dark. He waited. The crack did not repeat. Wind moved in the spruce tops and arrived at camp a few seconds later in pieces. A dead twig dropped somewhere behind them.

"Branch," Joel whispered.

He hated himself for needing the word and hated the word for being useful.

Later, maybe minutes later or an hour, something moved near Alex's tent. Nylon touched nylon. Joel turned his lamp on for one second. The beam showed the loose flap settling, the guyline slack, no animal, no man. He turned it off before the light could travel farther and find the alder.

"Wind," Mike said.

"I know," Joel said.

Will said nothing.

The night became a series of decisions not to move. Do not check the body. Do not check the sound by the channel. Do not retrieve Alex's spray. Do not open his tent. Do not ask whether anyone else heard the small almost-voice, if it had been a voice at all, that might have been water under a root. Do not say his name more than necessary.

Joel's legs cramped. His back tightened. The cold worked upward from the ground through the soles of his sandals and into his knees. Mosquitoes found the gap between his cuff and glove. The bear spray burn faded from flame to raw skin. He pressed his tongue against the roof of his mouth until the taste of pepper changed to blood from a bitten place there.

Will began talking again near what might have been midnight.

Not loudly. Not even to them at first. He listed facts under his breath, the way he might list gear before a portage, trying to make sequence stand in for sense.

"Bad food hang. Marsh camp. Dusk movement. He stepped away. We heard brush. Spray cloud. Body ten yards out. His spray still safetied. Food untouched. Bear country. This is bear country."

Mike said, "Stop."

"I'm just saying what happened."

"You're making yourself worse."

"I am trying to keep sequence."

"Keep it in your head."

"My head is currently not a trusted environment."

Joel almost laughed. The sound rose in him and stopped at his throat, not because it was funny but because it was Will, impossibly Will, still able to make a sentence that was both precise and unbearable. He looked toward the shape of him in the dark and felt the old church-basement version of Will pass through him: the boy who had corrected adults under his breath, who had made Joel laugh during prayers, who had been impossible to silence even when silence was the point.

"Sequence doesn't save us tonight," Joel said.

Will was quiet for a few breaths. "It might tomorrow."

"Tomorrow," Mike said, with the hard relief of a man taking possession of a word. "At first light we assess. We mark the site. We pack. We leave."

"Leave which way?" Will said.

"Out."

"Thank you. Very detailed route plan."

"Not now."

"It's not a small question."

"I said not now."

Will let out one dry, broken cough. The sound went into the dark above them and broke among the spruce. For a second Joel thought it came again from behind the fire ring, thinner and late.

Joel turned his head.

Nothing.

He looked at Will. Will was still beside the canoe, one hand over his mouth as if ashamed of the cough. Mike had not moved. Maybe neither of them had heard it. Maybe there had been nothing to hear. Maybe Joel's ears, burned and strained and full of blood, had made a double out of one sound.

He said nothing.

The silence after that felt less like restraint than debt.

Near dawn the cold deepened. The black in the trees thinned by degrees without becoming light. The channel showed itself first as a dull strip. Then the turned canoe. Then the hanging food barrel. Then the three packs they had pulled close and never opened. Shapes came back into the world reluctantly, each one bringing its own accusation.

No one had slept. They had leaned, shifted, jerked awake from half-dozes, and returned to listening.

Mike stood when there was enough gray to see his hands. His face had changed in the night. It had not softened. It had set around something. He picked up the bear spray, tested the weight of it, and looked toward the alder screen.

Will remained seated. "Don't."

Mike said, "We have to."

Joel stood too. His knees made small private sounds. He took the hatchet this time instead of the paddle, then looked at it and understood there was no correct object. A hatchet was only a smaller tool in a night that had made tools ridiculous, but it had weight and an edge, and his hands accepted it.

Will looked at both of them as if they had betrayed him by becoming able to move.

"At first light," Mike said.

Joel wanted to tell him this was not light. It was only the end of dark. But the distinction belonged to a world that still cared about precision. He clipped his lamp to his head and did not turn it on.

Will picked up the map case before he followed them. No one asked why. Joel understood anyway. A map was another thing with lines on it. Another thing that promised the world could be reduced to marks and distances if a man held it hard enough.

Together, three men walked back toward the place where the fourth had become a fact they could not yet name.


# Chapter 9 - Bear Work

Morning did not make Alex easier to see. It only made the world stop helping them not see him.

There was no sunrise. The dark thinned into gray by small degrees, giving back one shape at a time: the turned canoe by the fire ring, the wet black circle of ash, the food barrel hanging too low in the back of camp. Then the alder gap, which had been only a wound in the dark and now was brush again: stems, leaves, mud, a corridor wide enough for a man to step through and not come back.

Fog sat on the channel. It did not move like weather yet. It lay low over the water, torn on alder branches, thickening where the channel bent out of sight. The camp smelled of wet ash, pepper spray, mud, blood, crushed leaves, and three men who had not slept. They had spent the night under the tarp with layers pulled over damp clothes, jerking awake whenever water fell from the seam or spruce needles ticked on nylon. Headlamp straps had cut lines into their foreheads. Their hands were stiff. Their eyes looked smaller in their faces.

Will still sat with his back against the canoe they had dragged sideways as a useless wall. He held the map case in both hands. He had held it for most of the night, maybe because it had edges, maybe because it was the only thing in camp still claiming that the world could be flattened, named, and followed.

Mike stood when there was enough light to see his own fingers.

"We have to look," he said.

Will did not move. "No."

Mike looked toward the alder. His jaw worked once. The skin under his eyes was raw from spray and no sleep. "Yes."

"You can say it in that voice all you want."

"It isn't a vote."

"It is if you want me to walk in there."

Joel stood before Mike could answer.

He did not feel brave. He felt the old pressure of a task no one wanted and no one could make disappear.

His knees hurt. The cold had stiffened them, and the night had put a private age into every joint. He picked up the hatchet from beside the packs. The orange handle was nicked and sticky with old pitch. Two nights ago Alex had held it badly while Mike told him to choke up, and Alex had said, "Quit mothering me," without changing his grip. It had been a camp tool then. A small corrective object. In Joel's hand now it became more serious and no more useful.

Will watched him. "You too?"

"We have to look at what we can," Joel said.

The sentence made Alex into work. Joel hated it. He hated that work was the only mercy left that did not turn at once into suicide or theater. Sit here. Listen. Wait for wind. Go back into the alder. Leave without looking. Every choice failed Alex. This one at least had an order.

Mike took the bear spray. There was less in it now. Joel could tell by the way Mike weighed it without meaning to, the small drop in his wrist before he straightened his hand. Mike saw Joel see it and looked away.

"Stay close," Mike said.

Will gave a dry laugh. "Good. Great. We have a plan."

Mike's head turned a fraction.

Will's mouth stayed open, but whatever he had meant to put through it did not come. He got up slowly, the map case still under one arm. He looked as if standing had become a kind of consent.

"Leave that," Mike said.

Will looked down at the plastic sleeve as if surprised to find it there. "No."

"It won't help."

"Neither did that can, apparently."

Mike took one step toward him.

Joel said, "Enough."

The word was not strong. It worked because none of them had strength for a better argument. Mike stopped. Will tucked the map case against his ribs and followed.

They went through the same gap in the alder they had made in the night. In daylight the distance was obscene. Ten yards. Less than the length of two canoes. A man could have thrown a mug from the fire ring and hit the place where Alex had died. All night Joel had made the distance larger in his head because terror needed space to justify itself. Morning took that away.

The alder kept the record badly. Branches were snapped at shoulder height and knee height. Leaves hung torn and silver-backed. Mud had dried in thumb-thick smears on stems. The black ground underfoot had been cut by sandals, knees, roots, something broader, something dragged. Bear spray had left orange dust on low leaves. In the gray light the dust looked artificial and almost clean, and the cleanness made the rest of the place worse. It was the only part of the scene that admitted a human hand.

Joel tried not to step in blood and found that there was no clean path.

Mike stopped first. Will stopped behind Joel with a hard intake of breath.

Alex lay where they had left him.

That fact had its own violence. Joel had known it would be true, but the night had given disbelief room to work. Morning closed the room. The body was still half turned toward the water, one arm bent under him, one hand open in the mud as if he had been interrupted while reaching. His shirt was torn and dark. One hiking sandal was gone. The other was still strapped on, absurdly intact, its heel packed with black muck.

Joel kept his eyes on the hand.

It was less terrible than the rest and therefore worse. Mud had dried in the creases of the palm. One fingernail was broken. The fingers were slightly curled. It was recognizably Alex's hand, not evidence, not a casualty, not the neutral thing a report would later try to make. The hand that had stirred peanut-butter noodles with solemn incompetence. The hand that had reached back without being asked to steady Joel's canoe over a beaver dam. The hand that had always seemed to be starting a bit before Alex's mouth caught up.

"No, no," Will said.

Not the panic from the night. Smaller. A correction addressed to the world.

Mike cleared his throat. The sound was raw from pepper spray. "We need to check the site."

Will stared at him. "The site."

"Will."

"No. Say it again. I want to hear how official you can make it."

"If it was a bear, we need to know if it's still close."

"If?"

Mike's face tightened. "Because you're going to make a federal case out of the word."

"I am looking at our friend dead in the mud and trying to be accurate."

"Accuracy won't move us."

"Inaccuracy got us here."

The sentence hit harder than Will seemed to expect. Mike's eyes changed. For a moment the whole route stood between them with Alex at its center: the old map, the staged Highlander, the missing beacon none of them had been embarrassed enough to insist on, Mike's certainty, Joel's willingness to let certainty do work for him. The trip had been built from small agreements that had felt like competence until they had to hold a death.

Joel stepped between the next words before they could happen.

"Tracks first," he said.

Mike looked at him as if he had forgotten Joel was there.

"Tracks," Joel said again. "Then his spray. Then we cover him. Then we decide."

"We decided," Mike said.

"No," Joel said. "We didn't."

Will turned toward him.

Joel did not look back. If he did, Will's relief might ask for more than Joel could give.

They began with the ground because the ground at least pretended to be evidence.

The mud made no simple testimony. It accepted some marks too well and refused others. Where water seeped through root holes, impressions slumped almost as soon as they formed. Where alder roots crossed under the surface, footprints split and slid. Their own night movements were everywhere: Mike's deeper heel, Will's narrow sandal tread, Joel's scraped sideways step where he had coughed blind through the spray. Alex's tracks came from camp, paused near the edge, turned slightly, then disappeared into churn.

Joel made himself slow down. He put one hand on a root and felt cold water squeeze up between his fingers. He tried to read direction from bent grass, weight from depth, animal from accident. The ground gave him pieces and refused the line between them.

He had never wanted to be the kind of man who studied the mud around a friend's body. He had wanted facts in other forms: a map folded on a tailgate, a forecast, a mileage note in Will's handwriting, a text from Mara asking for a photograph of the lake because she knew he would forget unless asked. Facts that stayed useful because nobody had died beside them. Here every fact charged admission. To know where Alex had stepped was to picture him stepping. To decide whether a mark was a paw was to stand longer where something might still be close.

Beyond Alex's shoulder was a broad mark in the mud.

It could have been a paw.

That was the worst of it. It could have been. Wide. Deep at one edge. A dragged smear where claws or roots or a slipping heel had cut through the top layer. Joel knew too little about bear tracks to argue it away. He knew enough about fear to distrust how badly he wanted one answer, and enough about Mike to distrust how badly Mike wanted the answer that could be acted on.

"There," Mike said.

He pointed with the bear spray, not touching anything.

Will leaned in. "Maybe."

"It's a print."

"Maybe."

"Don't maybe this."

"I'm saying maybe because that's the correct word when you don't know."

"It is a bear print."

"How many toes?"

Mike stared at him.

"No, answer. How many do you see? Because I see mud and panic."

"Will."

"There should be a track line. Something that heavy doesn't leave one print and vanish."

"It's all churned up."

"By us. By him. By something. Fine. But look." Will crouched lower, careful not to touch the mud. His hand hovered near the waterline where the ground should have held a large animal leaving. "This is nothing."

"Water washed it."

"In six hours?"

"Current."

"Current goes that way."

Mike turned his headlamp, still off, toward the brush as if habit could make a beam. His throat worked. "A bear came in. Alex stepped out. It hit him. It left through the channel."

"You left out the part where he said 'What.'"

No one moved.

Fog pulled through the alder behind Alex, thinned, caught again.

Joel felt the word from the night open in him. What. Alex's voice, irritated and curious, speaking to something just beyond his light. Not afraid yet. Not performing. Answering before any of them knew answer could be a category of danger.

Mike said, "He heard brush."

"He answered somebody."

"No."

"You don't get to no that."

"He answered a sound."

"A sound that sounded like somebody."

Mike's face went blank in the way that meant he was holding several uglier things behind it. "You don't know that."

"Neither do you."

Joel looked at the mud. "We heard him say it. That's what we have."

Will gave one dry little break of laughter. "Great. Very useful."

"I mean it."

"I know you mean it. That's why it's annoying."

Mike lowered the bear spray. "The more we stand here talking, the less daylight we have."

Daylight. The word had sounded generous under the tarp. Here it was already being spent. Above the trees the cloud deck moved faster than the fog below it. Wind worked in the spruce tops first, high and dry, then came down in pieces. Later it would find open water and make the map smaller than weather.

They found Alex's bear spray six feet from his body, half under leaves, safety still in place.

Mike stared at it for too long.

"He didn't get it off," Will said.

"I can see that."

"I'm saying--"

"I can see it."

Joel picked it up before either of them could turn it into another accusation. The canister was cold and wet. Mud filled the groove around the safety clip. Alex had carried it clipped to the front of his PFD because Mike had told him twice to stop putting it in the side pocket of his pack.

Quit mothering me, Alex had said the first time, in Will's driveway, grinning while changing nothing.

On the first lake, when Mike pointed at the canister with his paddle and told him again, Alex had clipped it where it belonged with great ceremony and said, Look at me growing as a person.

Joel slid the canister into his jacket pocket. The pocket sagged.

"Don't take his stuff," Will said.

"It's spray."

"I know what it is."

"Then don't make me say why."

Will looked away.

There were other objects. Alex's missing sandal lay upside down in the water weeds, where the current worried the strap. One glove had been mashed into mud. A torn corner of rain jacket hung from a broken alder stem. The cracked headlamp was back at camp in the cookpot where Joel had put it because he could not hold it and could not put it with anyone's personal gear. The inventory became an accusation. Things could be carried. A man could not.

Mike took out his phone before they covered anything.

For a second no one spoke. The phone belonged to calls, weather, photos of fish, proof that a person had stood smiling on a rock before weather and distance made the smile worth keeping. Mike held it in both hands, the way a person might hold something fragile or loaded.

"We need pictures," he said.

Will's eyes went hard. "Of him?"

"Of the place."

"Say what you mean."

Mike looked down at the screen. "Of enough."

Joel wanted to tell him no. He wanted to tell him yes. He wanted some future person with a badge or radio to have more than three shaking men and one word none of them trusted. Mike photographed the alder gap from camp-facing angles, the possible print without stepping closer, the place where Alex's spray had lain before Joel moved it, the orange pepper dust on leaves, the torn jacket caught on the stem. When he lifted the phone toward Alex himself, Will made a small flat sound.

Mike lowered it.

"Enough," Joel said.

Mike nodded once and put the phone away without checking the picture.

They did what they could for Alex.

No one called it burial. The ground was root and water and cold stone under a skin of mud. They had no shovel, no safe time, no tool equal to the task. Mike brought the gray groundsheet from Alex's tent. Will objected because it was Alex's, then stopped because every object was Alex's or no one's now. Ownership had become another thing the morning did not know how to honor.

They unfolded the sheet beside him. It snapped softly in the damp air.

"Careful," Mike said.

Will's eyes flashed. "I was planning to be casual."

"I'm saying--"

"Don't."

Joel knelt in the mud. The wet came through his pants immediately. He took one corner of the sheet. Mike took another. Will stood useless for two breaths, then crouched and took the third, his hand shaking so badly the plastic trembled.

Moving Alex even a little was impossible and necessary.

Joel closed his mind around the work. Corner here. Lift enough to pull cloth. Do not look at the torn place. Do not let the sheet catch under the arm. Do not put weight on the open hand. Mike said something under his breath that might have been a curse or a prayer. Will made a sound and bit it off. The sheet slid under part of Alex and bunched under another part and would not do what they needed. They had to try twice.

At last they covered him as well as they could.

The groundsheet made a poor shape over the body, low in one place, too high in another. Rainwater or fog already beaded on it. They gathered stones from the channel edge to hold the corners. The stones were cold and slick. Joel chose each one as if choosing correctly mattered. A flat one near the shoulder. A heavier round one near the feet. A granite chunk with lichen on the side where the wind might get under.

Will set his stone down, moved it an inch, then moved it back.

"This is insane," he said.

Mike wiped mud from his hand onto his pants. "It's what we can do."

"Don't make it sound clean."

"You have a better one?"

Will looked at the gray shape. "No."

The admission emptied him. He stepped back and put both hands on the top of his head, fingers locked in his hair. The map case hung from the crook of his elbow. Joel remembered Will at twelve in the church basement, doing the same thing over a lost youth-group scavenger hunt clue, furious that the world would not yield to close reading. Folding chairs. Watered-down lemonade. Dust and floor wax. Someone's mother telling them to use indoor voices. Will had wanted the clue to be fair. Joel had already suspected fair was a word adults used when they were tired.

Now Will stood in the alder beside a covered body and wanted the clue to be fair.

Joel turned away before the memory could make a speech out of itself.

Mike took the map case from Will without asking and opened it on a root-wide patch of ground. The plastic crackled. He pointed to their approximate position with a muddy finger.

"We go back," Will said at once.

Mike did not look up. "We don't know that yet."

"We know it."

"Back means the rough portage, the beaver mess, the wind lake from yesterday, all of it against us with three men and two boats."

"Forward means unknown."

"Forward means shorter to the Highlander."

"On paper."

"On the map."

"Those are the same only when the map isn't leaving something out."

Mike's eyes lifted. "The map is not lying."

"I didn't say lying. I said leaving things out. There's a difference."

Joel could feel both routes in his body. Back meant passing the beaver dams again, the rough portage, the narrow channel where every branch crack now had a shape behind it. Forward meant water they had not tested, wind they could already hear being built, camps they had not seen, and Mike's confidence moving ahead of evidence. Both directions required leaving Alex under a gray sheet in the alder.

"We can't carry him," Mike said.

Will's jaw set. "You don't know that."

"I do."

"He's not gear."

"That's why I'm saying it before somebody breaks himself trying to prove something."

Will stepped toward him. Mike did not step back.

Joel got between them too late to prevent the words but early enough to prevent hands.

"If we try to carry him," Joel said, "we don't get out today. Maybe not tomorrow. We need both boats moving. We need three people able to portage. We need--"

"Stop," Will said.

Joel stopped.

Will's face had gone rigid. "Don't turn him into logistics."

Joel looked at the groundsheet. The stones. The dark water licking at alder roots. "I don't know how else to get us out."

No one answered.

They marked the site. Mike tore a strip from Alex's orange stuff sack and tied it to an alder above the body, high enough to see from camp and low enough not to look like a flag. The color hurt the eye.

Joel wrote the first note in Will's little waterproof notebook because his own was packed too deep. The page took the pen cleanly.

Second Camp. Body ten yards east/channel side of camp. Covered with gray groundsheet. Orange marker on alder. Date. Approximate time.

His handwriting looked calm. The letters did not know where they were. They belonged to grocery lists, route notes, a name on a dry bag. They did not belong beside Alex.

He added the objects because objects were all he could make himself trust: Alex's spray safetied. Body minimally moved to cover. Food barrel intact at camp. No service. Three survivors.

Survivors looked wrong on the page. It made Alex into what the category excluded.

Will watched the pen move. "Write bear."

Mike looked at him.

"Go ahead," Will said. "If we're committing."

Joel held the pen above the paper. "Observed possible large animal sign."

Will laughed without humor. "You would write a clean sentence next to this."

"I know."

"Put no clear tracks."

Mike said, "Don't."

Joel wrote: no clear track line.

Mike breathed through his nose. The sound was almost a snort, almost the beginning of another argument, but it did not become one.

From the brush on the far side of the channel came a low laugh.

Not loud. Not theatrical. It did not roll through the fog. It was one bark of amusement, cut short in the old familiar way, the sound Alex made when a joke pleased him before he decided whether to make it bigger.

Will stopped breathing.

Mike's face emptied.

Joel's hand closed around the pen until the plastic bent.

Fog held over the water. The channel moved under the alder with its ordinary black patience. Somewhere upstream, a small bird clicked once, bright and real.

"Bird," Mike said.

No one asked which bird.

The laugh did not repeat.

Joel waited for his mind to reject it. Grief, exhaustion, memory, a raven, a fox, water hitting a hollow root, Will making some involuntary sound and Joel assigning it the wrong owner. Explanations were available. They were ordinary. They were also too late. The sound had already reached the part of him that knew Alex before it reached the part that needed proof.

He did not pick one up.

Will said, very softly, "That was him."

"No," Mike said.

"Mike."

"No."

"You heard it."

"I heard an animal."

"You did not."

Mike turned so fast the map case slid off the root. "Then what? Say it."

Will opened his mouth.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Not hard. Enough.

Will looked at the hand on him. His eyes were red, the skin beneath them gray. He understood, or nearly understood, before Joel did. Whatever came next would be heard. Whatever word they gave the morning would not stay theirs.

Will shut his mouth.

They stood that way for several seconds: Mike with the bear spray half raised toward fog, Will caught between speech and silence, Joel holding a pen and another man's sleeve beside a covered body.

Then, from closer, from the alder behind the groundsheet or the channel bend or nowhere Joel could place on a map, something in Alex's voice said, "Quit mothering me."

It was soft. Almost fond. Almost annoyed.

The phrase came with the exact little drop on mothering, the way Alex had said it when warnings bored him but attention pleased him. It was not a scream from beyond death. It sounded conversational, as if he were just out of sight adjusting a strap, waiting for one of them to roll his eyes.

That was the cruelty of it. The old request for reaction was still inside the words. Alex had spent the trip tugging at the group until someone looked up, sighed, laughed, corrected him. The sound kept that shape after Alex could not.

Will made a sound like his body had been struck.

Mike sprayed into the alder.

The hiss tore the morning open. Orange fog burst against wet leaves and came back in pieces. Joel pulled Will backward by the sleeve. The spray drifted low and chemical, cutting through mud and blood and spruce. Mike swept the canister left to right, too fast, spending what little remained, his arm rigid.

"Stop," Joel said.

Mike did not.

"Mike, stop."

The canister coughed and weakened. Mike lowered it. His mouth was open. His eyes streamed, though the spray had gone mostly away from him.

Nothing moved in the alder.

No body shifted under the groundsheet. No shape crossed the channel. No bear crashed away. The woods gave them wet leaves, fog, black water, and the bird's last small tick before it flew.

Will bent and vomited into the mud. There was little in him. Coffee acid, water, a string of bile. He stayed folded over afterward, one hand braced on a root, the other still holding the map case against his ribs as if it were an organ.

Mike wiped his eyes with the back of his wrist. "We leave now."

His voice was hoarse and flat.

Will spat. "Don't say bear."

"I didn't."

"You wanted to."

"I said we leave."

"Because what? That was supposed to be a bear?"

Mike took two steps toward him and stopped with the spray canister hanging useless at his side. His control had not returned. Only the outline of it. "Because staying beside him is worse."

Joel looked at the groundsheet. Fog had gathered on the plastic and begun to run in narrow lines toward the mud. One bead hung at the edge, swelled, and fell onto a leaf. The leaf dipped and came back up. The shape under the sheet did not change, and that steadiness was another kind of horror.

He wanted to say Alex's name. Not to the brush, not as answer, not as lure. Just once, properly, to the covered shape on the ground. He did not. The wanting itself frightened him. It felt like stepping toward a voice.

They went back to camp without turning their backs until the alder forced them to.

The camp in daylight looked worse than it had in darkness because daylight made its inadequacy visible. The tarp sagged with condensation. The fire ring was a black scrape full of wet ash. Alex's tent stood half open, one guyline slack, the fly torn low where some branch or body had caught it in the confusion. The turned canoe lay between the fire ring and the channel like a child's idea of a wall. Paddles were lined along a log where Will had placed them during the night, a neat row of tools that had protected nothing.

Mike went straight to the food barrel.

"Don't," Will said.

Mike stopped with his hand on the rope. "We need to know."

"We know enough."

"No. We know one thing we can't use."

He lowered the barrel carefully. The rope rasped over the branch. The sound made Joel's shoulders tighten. The barrel descended in little jerks, pale and round, until Mike caught it against his chest and set it on the ground.

The latch was not open, exactly. One tongue had not seated fully into its keeper. The red strap crossed the lid, but the metal tab sat proud by half an inch, making a small clean shadow.

Joel knew Mike had checked it in the night. He had watched him lift the latch, press it down, tie the rope, yank the line once. The barrel had knocked against the trunk, and all three of them had listened to the knock come back from the channel side. But hands failed under pressure. Latches looked closed when they were not. Memory edited itself to reduce blame or increase it.

Mike crouched and touched the tab.

"Don't touch it," Will said.

Mike gave him a look.

"Sorry," Will said. "Reflex."

Mike pressed the latch. It clicked home.

All three men looked toward the alder.

Nothing answered.

For once, the world withheld the answer they feared.

Mike opened the barrel. Oatmeal. Coffee. Noodles. Peanut butter. Rice packets. Will's spices in doubled bags. Alex's plain rice meals with his name written on duct tape in thick marker because he had claimed otherwise they would all get ideas. Everything was there. Nothing torn. No claw marks in the plastic. No food dragged through camp.

"Food didn't bring it," Will said.

Mike closed the barrel. "Food could have attracted it without being eaten."

"You hear yourself?"

"Yes."

"Do you?"

"Enough."

Joel expected Will to answer. He did not. He was looking at Alex's labeled meals through the translucent side of the barrel. His face had lost the argument and found something worse.

Packing required belief in later. Later they would need dry socks. Later they would need the stove. Later a map might matter. Later a pot should be nested correctly and fuel capped and straps tightened. The body wanted to sit down, run, or go back into the alder and remove the sheet.

They packed anyway.

They did it badly.

Joel poured too much water into the coffee grounds because his hand shook when he meant to stop. Brown liquid climbed the filter cone and spilled over the rim onto the dirt. He stood watching it run past a spruce root until he heard Alex in memory, not from brush but from yesterday evening by the stove: This is less coffee than a wet opinion. Joel had rolled his eyes then. Now the line arrived whole and useless, and he held the mug until heat hurt his fingers.

Will took down Alex's tent.

No one had asked him to. Maybe that was why he did it. He unzipped the door, then stopped with one hand on the pull. The zipper made a small dry line through the morning.

Mike turned sharply.

"It's me," Will said.

The phrase hung there. It should have been ordinary. It had become a claim that needed proof.

Will ducked into the tent and began handing things out: sleeping bag in its stuff sack, shirt, socks, paperback swollen slightly from damp, spare bland-food pouch from the mesh pocket, headnet, spare batteries, little bag of electrolyte tablets. Each object came out changed. Joel put them into Alex's pack because no other category existed.

At the bottom of the tent, Will found a small plastic bottle of hot sauce wrapped in a sock. He held it up and made a sound that was almost a laugh.

"He lied," Will said.

Mike looked over.

"Bland-food purity was a lie."

No one answered.

Will screwed the bottle tighter and put it in his own pocket with both hands.

Joel took the cracked headlamp from the cookpot. Dried blood darkened one side of the strap. Mud had gotten into the hinge. He could not decide whether to pack it with Alex's gear, keep it out as evidence, or throw it into the channel because looking at it made the morning tilt. He wrapped it in a spare bandanna and put it in the top pocket of Alex's pack.

"We take his pack?" Will said.

"Some of it," Mike said.

"What does that mean?"

"It means we can't carry everything."

"I can carry his pack."

"No."

"You don't know what I can carry."

"I know what you can carry over one portage while crying and not sleeping. I know what you can carry over six."

Will's face twisted. "Go to hell."

"Later."

It was the kind of Mike line that would once have worked. Dark, quick, badly timed. No one laughed. Mike seemed to realize this half a second after he said it. His mouth tightened, but apology was a language none of them had ready.

They divided Alex's gear into necessities, evidence, and things too personal to abandon. Even the categories trespassed.

They took the spray, fuel, batteries, first-aid odds and ends, one fleece layer, food. They kept the headlamp, wallet, phone, the note about the site, the photographs if Mike's phone survived long enough to matter, the fact of the safety clip still on the spray. They kept Alex's small notebook with mileage estimates and grocery lists. They kept the hot sauce because Will had already claimed it. The phone went uselessly into a dry bag, as if service might wake inside it if kept dry enough.

Mike photographed the camp edge once more from where the fire ring could be seen, then the low food hang, then the note under the fly. The phone made a small official sound each time. Click. Click. Click. Each one turned a piece of the morning into something flat enough to show later. None of them looked at the screen.

Then Mike held the phone up and checked service though he knew. No bars. No emergency call. The blank screen had become another way for the world to refuse them.

"Battery," Joel said.

Mike lowered the phone. "Yeah."

He turned it off.

Will watched him. "We could climb."

"What?"

"Tree. Ridge. Something. Signal sometimes--"

"There is no ridge."

"You don't know."

"Look around."

"Trees are not a topographic argument."

"We are not climbing wet spruce for one imaginary bar."

"People get emergency calls out with no displayed service."

"People also fall out of trees."

Joel said, "Wind's coming up."

That redirected them because it was outside and harder to accuse.

The spruce tops were moving now. The fog on the channel had begun to tear into strips. Beyond the narrow water, through the screen of alder, the next lake showed only as a pale hard brightness. They could not see the surface from camp, but they could hear it beginning to work: small waves touching rock, then not touching, then touching again. A steady increase. A clock with no face.

Mike spread the map on the turned canoe. He put stones on two corners. Will came despite himself. Joel stood close enough to see and far enough not to own the route.

"Back to First Camp," Will said.

"Forward to the larger lake, then the next marked camp if we can make it," Mike said.

"If we can make it is not a plan."

"It is when every plan starts with if."

"The rough portage is behind us. We know it."

"And it took four of us half the afternoon fresh."

"We were not fresh."

"We were fresher than this."

"Forward gets us to the cabin mark eventually," Will said, jabbing the map.

Mike looked at him sharply.

"What?" Will said. "That's your thing. Map says there's a cabin."

"Not today."

"Then why forward?"

"Because the take-out is forward."

"And the body is behind us," Will said.

There it was. The real geography.

Mike folded one edge of the map, then flattened it. His hands were steadier with paper than they had been with the spray. "We leave a note at camp. We mark the site. We travel. We get to the Highlander or service or people. We bring them back."

"Bring them back to what?"

Mike did not answer.

"No, say that one too," Will said. "Since we're saying practical things."

Joel looked toward the alder. The orange strip was visible from camp, a small wrong color in the brush.

"We need to move while we still can," Joel said.

Will turned on him. "You keep saying we."

"Because it's still true."

"Is it?"

The question came without heat. That made it harder.

Joel had no answer that would not sound like a poster in a ranger station. Stay together. Keep moving. Don't panic. All the good advice in the world became thin when spoken beside a dead friend.

Will looked down first.

They wrote the camp note on a torn page and sealed it in a freezer bag with a strip of orange tape on the outside. Joel wrote because Mike's hands shook when he tried and because Will refused the pen after seeing the first line.

ALEX B. BODY MARKED CHANNEL SIDE OF SECOND CAMP. COVERED WITH GRAY GROUNDSHEET. ORANGE MARKER ON ALDER. BEAR ATTACK SUSPECTED. NO SERVICE. GOING FOR HELP.

Joel wrote bear attack suspected because leaving the word out felt like cowardice and putting it in felt like fraud. Suspected was the hinge he could live with for the next hour.

He tucked the note under Alex's tent fly folded beneath a rock near the fire ring. It looked too small to do what they needed from it. It looked like a thing a person wrote to make himself less guilty.

Will read it once after it was sealed. His lips moved around the words without giving them sound. When he reached BEAR ATTACK SUSPECTED, his mouth flattened and stayed that way. He did not ask Joel to change it.

They finished packing.

Mike loaded the canoes as if load order could repair reality. He put the heaviest packs low, centered the food barrel, tied the loose painter with too much force. Once, without comment, he pressed the small yellow pouch clipped inside the left side of his PFD, feeling for the Highlander key as if the truck at North Road Bridge were a place he could touch. Will repacked the map case twice. Joel checked the stove cap, then checked it again because he could not remember doing it. They kept Alex's wallet, phone, cracked headlamp, unused spray, hot sauce, some food, and one fleece layer. They left the rest under a rock-weighted tent fly with the note and the marker visible from camp.

The division made no moral sense. It only made weight.

Before they pushed off, Will walked to the edge of the alder gap.

Mike said, "Don't."

Will stopped.

The word worked because it was not an order this time. It was a plea with bark on it.

Will stood with his back to them. "I'm not going in."

No one moved.

"I just--" He cleared his throat, and the small rough sound went into the trees. Joel flinched before he could stop himself. Will heard the flinch. His shoulders rose, then dropped. "I just need a second."

Mike opened his mouth and closed it.

Joel looked at the channel, at the fog taking itself apart, at water brightening under wind. Leaving would not be one action. It would repeat all day. Every paddle stroke would leave Alex again. Every portage would leave him. Every practical decision that kept the living alive would commit the betrayal one more time.

Will did not say Alex's name. That was what Joel noticed. He stood at the edge of the brush and gave the silence nothing it could use.

Then, from somewhere near the covered body, very soft, came the click of a food-barrel latch.

The actual food barrel sat in Mike's canoe, twenty feet behind them, strapped under the middle bar.

Mike turned. Will turned. Joel did not, for one second. He looked at the barrel in the canoe first.

Red strap. Metal latch seated. Rope tied. Still.

The click did not repeat.

Will whispered, "No."

Joel said, "Don't answer it."

The words left him before he could weigh them. They were too loud in the morning and still not loud at all.

They were also a rule, though he did not know that yet. A bad first draft of one. Do not answer what has no body. Do not let a sound turn you before your eyes have finished their work.

Mike lifted the mostly spent spray toward the alder, then lowered it. Something in his face had gone older and smaller.

"Boats," he said.

They got the canoes into the water. The hulls scraped mud and then floated free with a soft, living shift. Three men, two boats. The imbalance showed immediately. Mike would take one canoe alone with the food barrel and heavier gear. Joel and Will would take the other. No one said that Alex's absence had a seating arrangement.

Joel stepped into the back of the canoe. Will got in front with the map case between his knees. His paddle lay across his lap for a moment before he picked it up. Mike shoved off first, too hard, then corrected with two quick strokes.

For a few seconds they drifted in the narrow channel beside Second Camp.

The tarp sagged. The fire ring was black. Alex's tent site had become a flat wet patch under spruce. The orange strip in the alder moved once in the wind and stilled. The groundsheet was hidden from the waterline, which was good and not good enough.

Joel put his paddle in.

The blade entered without splash. The water took the shape of it, closed around it, and let it go.

Behind them, from camp or brush or the channel's crooked mouth, Alex gave one low bark of laughter.

Will's shoulders seized.

Mike said, without turning, "Keep paddling."

No one said bear.


# Chapter 10 - Windbound

They did not leave.

For ten minutes they believed they had. The canoes came loose from the mud. The narrow channel accepted them into its black strip and carried them away from Second Camp one boat length at a time. Behind them, the tarp, the dead fire, Alex's flattened tent site, and the orange shred in the alder began to take the first poor shape of memory. Not mercy. Distance. The camp did not get smaller in any moral way. It only moved behind leaves.

Mike paddled ahead alone, the food barrel low in the middle of his canoe, the extra weight shoved toward balance and not quite getting there. His shoulders were set too high. Will sat in front of Joel with the map case wedged under one knee and his paddle moving in stiff, angry strokes. The map case clicked softly against the thwart whenever the canoe lifted.

No one turned around when Alex laughed from camp.

No one spoke for the first bend. Joel made himself look at the back of Will's rain jacket, at the wet seam running down the hood, at the small pale place on one knuckle where Will gripped the paddle too hard. He counted strokes without meaning to. Twelve to the bend. Eight through slower water. Six where the channel widened and the current hesitated. Counting gave his mind a shape that was not the shape under the gray groundsheet.

The channel helped with the lie. It bent twice, screened the camp with alder, and made leaving feel like a process already underway. A paddle could go in, pull water, come out clean. A man could do the next small correct thing and, for two breaths, believe correct things still mattered.

Joel knew better and let the belief work anyway. He kept his paddle quiet. He kept his eyes off the bank where a torn leaf might look like orange fabric, where orange fabric might become the marker, where the marker would become Alex again. Distance was only a useful measurement.

The lake showed itself all at once.

It was not large on the map. It had been a neat blue widening between lines, a thing they would cross before choosing the next water, the next portage, the next answer. In front of them it was white at the tips and darker in the troughs, a blunt field of water shouldering east under wind. Gusts came down the length of it without obstruction. They flattened the near grass and tore spray off the farther chop. The opposite shore was visible, then blurred, then visible again, low and black under cloud.

Mike's canoe lifted, dropped, and swung sideways before he corrected.

The movement showed too much of the bottom. For one second Joel saw pale aluminum flash above dark water, saw the food barrel shift against its straps, saw Mike's left knee slide from where he had braced it. Mike corrected fast, with real skill. That was part of the danger. A worse paddler might have quit sooner. Mike was good enough to imagine good enough mattered, and Alex's empty place in the front was a weight no one had loaded correctly.

"Left shore," he called over his shoulder.

The wind took the words and scuffed them flat. Joel heard them anyway. He also heard the small catch under the certainty, the part of Mike that had to spend effort making the sentence sound like a plan.

Will did not turn. "No."

It was not argument yet. It was a fact leaving Will's mouth before he could dress it up.

Mike dug on his right and angled toward the near point. "We can hug it."

"No."

"Don't start."

Joel held their canoe steady with a backstroke and watched the wind get under the empty front of Mike's boat. That was the problem made visible: three men, two tandem canoes, one of them suddenly wrong. A tandem with one man in it did not become a solo boat because the map required it. Loaded low, it could be managed on calm water. In this wind it was a long argument with physics.

"Mike," Joel said.

Mike looked back. His face was wet from fog or spray or both. The lake behind him kept lifting.

"Not with the boats like this," Joel said.

"If we stay tight to shore--"

"That shore has blowdown all along it."

"So we don't land."

Will laughed once without humor. "Outstanding. Lake too windy to cross, shore too bad to land, and the plan is don't need either."

"Shut up and paddle."

"That's not a plan either."

A gust hit then, clean and broad. It caught Mike's canoe on the quarter and pushed the front away from shore. Mike drove his paddle down hard, corrected, overcorrected, and for one second the hull lay almost broadside to the chop. The canoe did not tip. It only showed them the idea.

Joel's hands tightened on his paddle.

In the front of their canoe Will had gone still except for the paddle shaft trembling in his hands. He had seen it too: not the capsize, but the shape of one. Mike in water. The barrel floating loose. Joel and Will trying to turn in chop. The lake taking one bad fact and making five more before anyone could speak.

Mike saw it too. He swore and let the boat fall back toward the shelter of the channel mouth.

No one called the decision. The lake called it. They backed out in pieces, Mike first because he had no choice, Joel and Will following because there was no better courage than refusing stupidity. Wind pressed at their shoulders all the way into the channel. The black water there looked sheltered only because the lake was worse.

Second Camp reappeared around the bend as if it had been waiting.

No one said they were going back. That would have made it an action and given it an owner. They let the wind push them into the channel, let the channel bend, let the canoes drift toward the mud, and only then accepted what their bodies had already done.

The tarp sagged where they had left it. The ground under spruce was darker than the surrounding needles. The orange strip in the alder moved once and then stopped. Joel did not look for the groundsheet. From the waterline it was hidden. That was good. That was unbearable.

They landed badly. Mike drove his canoe into the mud and stepped out too fast, one foot sinking to the ankle. The boat slid back. He grabbed the painter and hauled until the front rasped onto roots. Will got out before Joel told him to wait and nearly went down when his hiking sandal caught under a submerged branch.

"Careful," Joel said.

Will rounded on him. "Fantastic note."

Joel let it go.

The camp had changed because they had failed to leave it. Before, it had been the place after Alex. Now it was the place they had to enter a second time. The same tarp line, the same stove scar, the same black fire ring. They had packed it once as if packing could convert it into past tense. Now every object had to be touched again, and every touch admitted they were still inside the same hour.

They dragged the canoes higher than before. Mike tied his boat to a spruce, then tied it again to a different root, not trusting the first knot once it existed. Joel ran the painter of his and Will's canoe around a low cedar twice. His hands had lost fine feeling from wet and cold. The rope felt slick and abrasive at the same time.

"We wait an hour," Mike said.

Will stared at the lake mouth. "An hour."

"Wind can lay down."

"Wind can also not do that."

"Thank you, meteorology."

"No, no, no." Will pulled his hood back from his face as if it had insulted him. His hair stood wet against his forehead. "You don't get to do the Mike thing where the bad version is somehow not part of the plan."

Mike stopped coiling rope. "The Mike thing."

"Yes."

"Good. Great. We're naming things now."

"Somebody should."

Joel set his paddle under the canoe seat and straightened. The movement made his back flare. "We wait because we can't cross. That's the whole plan for the next hour."

Will looked at him. The anger in his face had no object sturdy enough to hold it. It moved from Mike to Joel to the channel to the alder.

"And if it doesn't lay down?" he said.

Joel looked at the lake. A line of whitecaps moved west to east like something being combed hard. "Then we make the next decision."

"That is such a meeting answer."

"It's a weather answer."

"It's an answer that gets paid by the hour."

Under other circumstances Joel might have laughed. Now he only heard the exit Will had built into the sentence, the small side door back into joking if the wound drew too much blood.

Mike lifted the map case from the canoe and wiped water from the plastic with his sleeve. "Backtracking is worse."

"You keep saying that like worse has one setting," Will said.

"Back is the beaver mess, the rough portage, the lake from yesterday against this same wind, and then another day at least to the truck. With one less person. With him behind us."

Him. Not Alex. The pronoun hung there and failed at kindness.

"Forward is unknown water with a solo boat in wind," Joel said.

Mike looked at him sharply.

Joel did not soften it. "Forward may still be right. It's not right right now."

The practical sentence steadied the air for half a breath. Then Will said, "We could wait for someone."

No one answered too quickly. The thought entered all three of them and found no food.

"Who?" Mike said at last.

"Another group. Rangers. A plane. I don't know."

"This route doesn't have scheduled patrol."

"You don't know everything this route has."

"I know enough."

Will made the dry broken sound he had made beside the body. "That might be the problem."

Mike turned on him. "You think I wanted this?"

"I think you wanted to be right."

There was no tarp over them now. The sentence arrived bare.

Mike took one step toward him and stopped. His fists were not closed, but his hands looked ready to become fists. Joel watched the old geometry reassemble: Will needling, Mike controlling, Joel between them because the alternative was worse. Alex would have made a joke by now. Not a good one. Something too loud, something that made everyone look away from the edge.

The thought of him as useful came too late.

"Phones," Joel said.

Mike blinked once.

"Check them," Joel said. "Here, lake mouth. Maybe there's something in the wind. It costs us five minutes."

"There won't be," Mike said.

"Then it costs five minutes to know."

Mike already had his hand in his PFD. Checking was easier for him than waiting. The yellow pouch with the Highlander key was clipped inside the left side, dry and hard-edged under his fingers; the phone was in the other pocket, sealed in plastic. He worked it out with wet hands and pressed the side button until the screen woke.

Will pulled his phone from the doubled dry bag in his pack after him. The motion was clumsy. His fingers fumbled the fold twice. He had put the phone away after taking pictures in the morning, and Joel could see he hated touching it now because it had become a device for proving the world was absent. Joel did not take his out. His was sealed deeper, off, saved for later in the way people saved things that could not save them.

Alex's phone was in the dry bag with his wallet and cracked headlamp. No one suggested opening it. That would have meant unsealing the little package they had made of him: evidence, property, proof of a person. It would have meant touching his screen with muddy fingers and asking the dead man's battery to do what the living men's batteries could not.

The screens lit their wet faces weakly.

No service.

Mike lifted his phone higher, then turned in a slow half circle. The gesture was so common it was almost comic, a man asking a towerless sky for one bar. He stepped onto a root, held the phone above his head, frowned at the blankness, and stepped down.

"Emergency call?" Will said.

"No."

"It can sometimes show no bars and still--"

"No emergency network."

"You're sure?"

Mike held the screen toward him. "Look."

Will looked. It did not help.

Joel glanced at the spruce tops. "Turn them off."

Mike did. Will did not, at first.

"Will."

"I know," Will said, and powered it down.

They put the phones away with more care than the objects deserved. The absence of service did not become dramatic for being confirmed. It moved from assumption to fact. No beacon. No satellite messenger. No one listening in any way that helped.

Joel remembered the planning table at Will's house, the maps weighted by mugs, the quick little silence when someone had mentioned emergency beacons and no one had wanted to sound soft or expensive or overprepared. Mike had said the route was remote but not insane. Will had said downloaded maps and paper backups were not nothing. Alex had said if they needed a helicopter for a canoe trip he wanted a refund on masculinity. Joel had smiled because the joke let the subject move on. That was how an absence got made: not by decision, exactly, but by everyone accepting the same easier next sentence.

Now the missing device had weight in Joel's imagination: a clipped orange case, a rubber-covered button, a signal going up through weather to someone with a desk and a radio. It existed perfectly because they had not brought it.

They re-made the camp they had meant to abandon.

It was work done against hope. They put the tarp back up over the same miserable center between the fire ring and the canoes. The first line went badly; Mike chose a spruce limb that sagged under tension, and when Joel pointed it out Mike said it was fine, then saw the sag and moved it without admitting anything. Will stood under the empty space where the tarp would be, holding two stakes like he had forgotten their purpose.

"Corner," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"That corner. Pull it toward the cedar."

Will obeyed. The ordinary task gave him a body again. He took three steps, wrapped the line, unwrapped it because he had crossed it wrong, wrapped it again, then tied a knot too elaborate for the job.

Mike watched the knot. "You building a suspension bridge?"

"Yes, Mike. For the tiny rescue vehicles."

Mike almost smiled. It died before reaching his mouth.

The tarp lifted at last. Wind snapped one loose edge until Joel tied it lower. It would keep the worst rain off the stove and packs if the rain came from the right direction. It made a roof over their refusal to leave.

They did not pitch the tent. No one proposed sleeping. The tent meant night, and night beside the alder was still a thing the mind refused to carry. They shook water from the fly and folded it badly, then left it under the tarp with the packs. Thin nylon would keep off rain. It would not make a voice belong to anyone.

Alex remained beyond the alder.

Joel had been trying not to frame the camp around that fact and failing. Every position was measured by it. The stove was eight yards from the gap. The boats were twenty. The food barrel, re-strapped after the morning's check, sat under Mike's canoe for the moment and would need to be hung again if they stayed. The orange marker was visible only from one angle near the shore. It moved when the wind touched it, then vanished into leaves. The covered place itself could not be seen. Not seeing it made the mind draw it, badly and over and over.

The freezer-bag note by the fire ring looked stupidly official. Orange tape. Folded page. Rock on top. It said where Alex was as if location were the problem. It said bear attack suspected as if suspected were enough fence around the truth. Wind worried one corner of the plastic. Joel turned the rock and pinned it flatter.

Will kept looking at the gap.

"Don't," Mike said.

Will's eyes stayed there. "I didn't do anything."

"Don't start doing it."

"What, looking?"

"Standing there like you're about to go in."

"I'm not."

"Good."

"Don't good me."

Mike pressed the heels of his hands into his eyes. When he took them away, he seemed smaller and angrier for having shown tiredness. "We can't spend all day at the edge."

"We can spend it in the center?" Will said. "Is the center good? Did the center put in an application?"

Joel crouched by the stove and checked the fuel bottle. He had already checked it. He checked it again because the motion delayed speech. The bottle was capped. The pump was clean enough. They had not eaten since coffee and the failed oats. Hunger had become part of the fear, a flat weak place under the ribs. He could make rice. He could make noodles. Even food had Alex in it now: labeled bland meals in the barrel, hot sauce in Will's pocket, jokes stored in their heads.

"We should eat," Joel said.

"You're hungry?" Will said.

"No."

"Then why--"

"Because we need to function."

Will opened his mouth, closed it, and looked away. Mike got the barrel without comment.

The latch made its small metal click when Mike opened it.

All three men froze.

It was the real latch. Under Mike's hand. At their feet. Joel saw the tab lift. He saw the red strap slacken. Cause and sound were joined cleanly. His body did not care at first. It heard the click and threw him back to the alder, to the impossible click by the covered body, to the food barrel sitting in Mike's canoe while another latch spoke from the brush.

Mike saw it in both of them and hated them for making him see himself.

"That was me," he said.

"We know," Joel said.

"Do you?"

Joel met his eyes. "I saw it."

That mattered. He did not know why yet. A thing could still be usable if the hand that made the sound was visible, if the metal tongue moved and the click arrived with it. A latch under Mike's hand was a latch. A latch in the alder was not yet anything.

Will lowered himself onto the turned canoe. "Great. New rule. Applaud every verified noise."

"Could do worse," Joel said.

Will looked at him, surprised. For one second the corner of his mouth moved. Then the wind hit the tarp and the moment went.

They ate because bodies required one small betrayal after another. Mike boiled water. Joel divided rice and peanut butter and a torn packet of tuna. Will refused at first, then took the mug and held it until steam fogged the lower half of his glasses. He had not cleaned them since the night before. A smear of mud crossed one lens. Joel wanted to tell him and did not. The small caretaking sentences had begun to sound like traps. Careful. Eat. Stay close. Quit mothering me.

The food was warm and wrong. Peanut butter made a greasy skin on the rice. Tuna salted the back of Joel's tongue. He chewed because chewing was work. Across from him, Mike ate too fast, then stared into the mug as if offended by the fact that it was empty. Will held his portion with both hands and breathed through his nose, once, twice, as if he could negotiate with nausea.

Will ate three bites, then put the mug down. "No, no, no."

Mike's head came up.

Will was not looking at the brush. He was looking at the food. "I can't."

"You can," Joel said.

"Don't."

Joel stopped.

Will pushed the mug away and stood too fast. The tarp line brushed his hood. He flinched as if touched by a hand.

"I need to walk," he said.

"You need to sit down," Mike said.

"I am going to walk six feet in a circle like a zoo animal. Is that inside the approved grief perimeter?"

"Every time you talk, you make it worse," Mike said.

The sentence silenced them more completely than a shout.

Mike seemed to hear what he had said only after it was out. His face changed, not into apology, but into guarded calculation. He had not named anything. That was the point. He had still stepped off the bear track. Worse required an object.

Joel looked toward the alder before he could stop himself. Nothing moved there except wet leaves. Make it worse. The phrase was as far as Mike could go. Mike preferred nouns he could manage: route, wind, bear, daylight. This had come out of him anyway.

Will stared at him. "Make what worse?"

Mike looked toward the lake. "Nothing."

"No. No, no, no. You don't get to say that and then nothing it."

"I meant sound carries."

"Bullshit."

"I meant we're all worked up and repeating ourselves and making it worse."

"Making what worse?"

Mike grabbed the mug Will had abandoned and set it harder than necessary near the stove. "The situation."

"You are physically allergic to nouns."

Joel said, "Wind first."

Will turned on him. "What?"

"Wind first," Joel said. "Whatever else is true, the wind is why we're here."

"That is not the whole reason we're here."

"No. It's the reason we can do something about."

Will laughed under his breath. "Can we?"

Joel did not answer. He went to the shore because he needed to see the lake again. The others followed, Mike because he would not let Joel be the one to assess it, Will because remaining under the tarp alone was worse.

At the channel mouth the wind had strengthened. The low cloud had dropped until it seemed snagged in the spruce tops on the far shore. The lake surface had a hard metallic look now, dark gray under white streaks, water moving in separate orders: the long push of wind down the length, the sideways chop rebounding from rock, the confused slap where both met near the point. A canoe could cross it. Men crossed worse water every year and told stories afterward. That was the dangerous truth. Not impossible. Stupid for them, with one solo boat, shock in their hands, and no spare margin.

The map offered no texture for this. Its lake was blue, flat, obedient to its banks. It did not show the quartering gust at the mouth, or Mike's empty front seat, or Will's hands going white around a paddle shaft. It did not show Alex under plastic ten yards behind camp. Paper could say distance. It could not say what distance cost.

Mike stood with his arms crossed. "It may drop near evening."

"And then what?" Will said. "We cross at dusk?"

"If it's calm enough."

"If it's calm enough at dusk after no sleep and half a meal, with a dead man ten yards behind us and something stealing his voice from the brush."

Mike's jaw tightened.

Joel kept his eyes on the water. "We don't cross in the dark."

"Nobody said dark," Mike said.

"Dusk becomes dark faster when you're wet."

"I know how light works."

Will coughed once, a small dry bark.

The same cough answered from behind them.

Not an echo. Not delayed by rocks in any natural way. It came from the camp side, left of the tarp, near the food barrel and the turned canoe. Will was standing on Joel's right at the channel mouth, bent slightly into the wind, one hand still at his throat from the first cough. The second cough had the same tight scrape, the same embarrassed finish. It was not Will's phrase yet, not his fear, not his argument. Only the little broken hinge in his throat.

Joel turned, but not fast.

The camp was empty.

Rain had not started yet, but the air had thickened. The tarp moved. The barrel sat under the canoe. The packs lay where they had left them. No shape stood by the spruce. No branch moved except with wind. There was enough distance between Will and the camp for the fact to become large.

Joel checked in the order his body chose. Will: visible. Mike: visible. Barrel: visible. Gap: not visible from here. Tarp: moving with wind. Canoes: tied. The cough had belonged to Will and had not come from Will.

"What?" Will said.

Joel looked back at him.

Will's face had already understood from Joel's expression. "What?"

Mike said, "Sound off the water."

Joel did not answer.

"It was my cough," Will said.

"You coughed," Mike said.

"Twice?"

"Echo."

"From behind us?"

"Wind does weird things."

"That's not how that works."

The phrase struck the air and seemed to remain there, available.

Joel saw Will realize it. His mouth closed hard.

Mike looked away first. "Back to camp."

"No," Will said. "No, wait."

"Back to camp."

"You heard it."

"I heard a cough."

"My cough."

"You are not the only mammal with a throat."

"Find me the other anxious ex-LED-quality-control mammal in the spruce and I will apologize."

Mike's face flashed with anger and fear together. "Do you want to stand here and feed it?"

Again the word, nearly named and still not named.

Joel said, "Back. Eyes on camp."

He did not say do not answer. He did not say voices. He did not say Alex. Still, something moved between them, crude and silent: look first, speak only if speech survived looking.

They walked the short distance with too much care. No one turned his back fully until the brush forced it. At the tarp, Mike picked up the bear spray from where he had set it beside the stove. The canister was almost spent. Its weight had become symbolic before it had become useless, and now it was both.

Will stood in the center of camp and looked at the place the cough had come from.

"Say something," he said.

Mike stared at him. "Are you out of your mind?"

"Not to it. To me. Say what you think."

"I think we are exhausted and cold and hearing things."

"No, you don't."

"I think we are going to sit tight until the wind drops."

"That is not an answer."

"It is the only answer you get."

Will turned to Joel.

Joel hated him for it a little. He hated the request in his face, the old assumption that Joel would be fair, precise, unwilling to bully the evidence into shape. They had grown up believing truth and goodness were close cousins if you chased them hard enough. Now truth had arrived without instructions.

"I heard it from camp," Joel said.

Will's face loosened with relief and tightened again with fresh fear.

Mike said nothing.

"I saw you," Joel said to Will. "You were here."

"So."

"So we know that much."

"That's it?"

"That's it."

Will laughed, almost silently. "You're killing me with the scientific method."

"I'm trying not to get us killed without it."

That ended the conversation because it had too much of the truth in it.

The hour passed. Then another. The wind did not lay down. It rose, dropped, rose again harder, as if reconsidering and becoming more committed. Low cloud came in from the west and erased the far shore for minutes at a time. The air smelled like cold metal and spruce pitch and old water. The mosquitoes vanished. The quiet without them was not peace.

They sorted gear because inaction became unbearable.

Mike repacked his canoe twice on shore, moving weight forward, then back, then forward again. He tried kneeling behind the center thwart and rocking the hull under his hands, testing balance that would not matter until water told the truth. Joel watched without comment. The old Mike would have taken advice as insult and silence as judgment. This Mike took both badly and kept working.

Will opened the map case on the canoe seat. The plastic had fogged inside. He wiped it with the sleeve of his fleece and left streaks. Joel could see the route under his hand: the blue lake they could not cross, the portage line beyond it, the next water, and farther along the old dotted trace that ran toward the maintenance cabin Mike had mentioned yesterday as if a square on paper could dry clothes, raise a radio, or prove a road still existed.

The dotted trace looked almost delicate under the clouded plastic. It crossed contour lines with the confidence of print. It passed near a little black square and a label too small to read unless a man bent close and wanted it badly. Joel had seen old human marks in woods before: blazes grown around and swallowed, logging roads gone to moss, campsite signs half-buried in bark. A mark could be true and still be dead.

"If we backtrack," Will said, not looking up, "we know every ugly piece."

"We know some of it," Joel said.

"We know more than forward."

Mike tightened a strap on his pack. "Forward gets us closer to the Highlander."

"Forward also gets us to your magic cabin, eventually."

"I didn't say magic cabin."

"Map says there's a cabin," Will said, imitating him without humor.

Mike's head came up.

"Don't," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

Joel kept his voice low. "Don't give that one away again."

The warning landed strangely. Not as command, not yet as rule. Will looked past Joel toward the alder, then down at the map. His finger lifted from the dotted trace.

Mike's mouth tightened. He hated the warning and understood it.

"The cabin isn't today," Mike said. "Not from here, not in this wind."

"Then it isn't an argument for forward today," Will said.

"The take-out is."

"On paper."

"Everything is on paper until we get there."

"That may be the most honest thing you've said."

Mike did not answer. He bent to the strap again and pulled until the webbing creaked.

Joel watched the strap go tight over the pack and thought of Mike's Highlander waiting under trees at North Road Bridge. The real key was still sealed in the yellow pouch clipped inside the left side of Mike's PFD; Joel had watched Mike touch the pouch a dozen times, including just now when the phone came out. One small tooth-cut piece of metal, dry and accounted for, useless unless they could move their bodies through every mile between here and it. The key was proof of an ending, not a path.

By midafternoon the first rain arrived. It came as a mist, so fine at first that Joel could see it only where it gathered on the black blade of his paddle. Then the drops thickened and began to tick through the spruce. The lake darkened under it. The whitecaps remained, lower but steadier, the whole surface working with a cold patience that did not care about men, maps, or names.

"We need to decide before dark," Mike said.

"We decided," Will said. "We're stuck."

"Stuck is not a decision."

"It sure has decision-like consequences."

Joel pulled his hood up. Water ran from his hair down the back of his neck. "We don't move in this."

Mike looked at the lake again, and for a moment Joel thought he would argue from pride alone. Instead he nodded once, almost invisibly.

"Fine," Mike said.

The word did not mean fine. It meant the line had been reached.

They secured the camp for weather with the bitter efficiency of men who had lost the day. Boats higher. Packs under tarp. Wet clothes separated from less wet clothes, though nothing was dry. Food barrel closed, latched, strapped, and hung again from the same bad tree because there was no better tree inside the shrinking perimeter they were willing to use. This time Joel watched the latch close. Mike saw him watching and said nothing.

The hang was poor and they all knew it. The branch was too low, the trunk too close, the barrel too near camp. Yesterday those had been flaws of convenience and fatigue. Now each flaw made room for a sound from the wrong place. The rope rasped over wet bark. The barrel rose in short, ugly jerks. Mike tied it off, then stood under it and looked up at the red strap, the metal latch, the pale plastic sides beaded with rain.

"Closed," Joel said.

Mike looked at him.

Joel pointed. "I saw it."

Will gave a weak, irritated breath. "Are we doing notarized groceries now?"

"Yes," Joel said.

No one laughed. The answer stayed useful.

Will helped until helping ran out. Then he stood under the tarp and looked toward the alder gap again.

"No," Mike said.

Will did not move.

"I'm not going in," he said.

"Good."

"I was just thinking."

"Try less."

Will turned on him, but the fight was gone from his face. What remained was worse. "What if he isn't covered right?"

Mike shut his eyes.

Joel felt the question pass through him like cold water. The groundsheet. The stones. Rain gathering on plastic. Wind getting under one loose edge. The indignity of weather. The impossibility of fixing all of it.

"He is," Joel said.

Will looked at him. "You don't know."

"I know we covered him as well as we could."

"That sentence again."

"It's the only one I have."

Rain strengthened. It hit the tarp in separate taps that became one continuous sound. The lake disappeared behind the wet screen of alder and low cloud. The camp pulled inward until it was stove, packs, knees, rope, wet sleeves, the stink of bear spray still caught in fabric, and three men trying not to listen too hard.

Daylight thinned without becoming evening in any clean way. The sky simply lost depth. The orange strip in the alder showed once through rain and then not at all. Joel understood that they would not go back to check the groundsheet. Knowing that did not make the choice decent. It only made the choice repeatable.
