Mike sat with his back against a canoe and the nearly spent bear spray across his lap. Will sat opposite him, arms around his knees, the map case under one hand. Joel stayed near the tarp edge because he could see both the channel and the food barrel from there. That was how he justified the position. Sitting deeper under the tarp made him feel trapped inside the sound of their own breathing.

"We're fine," Mike said.

He said it quietly, to the rain or to himself. The old phrase had lost its use and kept its shape. No one mocked it. No one accepted it. The words went out under the tarp anyway, a small bright thing thrown where none of them could see it land.

Joel looked at the three of them under the tarp. The sentence was dangerous because it was false, and because Mike had said it again in Mike's exact voice, and the rain had taken it.

Will's mouth opened.

Joel shook his head once.

Will closed it.

Mike saw the exchange. For a moment Joel thought he would challenge it on principle, because Mike hated any command that had not come through him. Instead he lowered his eyes to the spray canister and turned it once in his hands. The plastic made a soft wet squeak. Nothing answered.

For several minutes they listened to the rain. Some of it was only rain. Some was spruce releasing water from higher branches. Some was lake chop reaching through the channel and touching mud. Once, a strap ticked against a pack buckle until Joel reached over and stopped it with his thumb. The absence after the ticking stopped was immediate and large.

Near the food barrel, from the wrong side of the tarp, Will coughed once.

Will sat in front of Joel with both hands over his mouth.

Mike lifted his head.

Joel did not turn quickly. He turned with care, as if the speed of looking could become an answer. The barrel hung pale and wet from the branch, red strap darkened by rain, latch seated where he had seen it seat. There was no one beneath it. No figure behind the spruce. No movement except water running down bark.

The cough did not repeat.

Will lowered his hands. He had not made a sound. His eyes were wide and furious, as if the theft had offended him almost as much as it had frightened him. There was grief in that too, Joel saw. Not for Alex this time. For the smaller private parts of a person that could be taken while the person still sat breathing under the tarp.

Joel put one finger to his lips.

This time Will obeyed without argument.

Mike looked from Will to the barrel. His mouth tightened around the phrase he wanted to use. Bear, echo, wind, weird acoustics, any word with enough ordinary weight to hold the moment down. He did not say any of them. He lifted one hand instead, palm out, a small stop sign no one had taught him.

Will nodded once.

Joel kept his finger against his mouth until his arm ached. It was not a plan. It was not even a rule yet. It was only the first crude shape of one: no answer without a body, no sound without a source.

Rain came down harder. The camp they had tried to leave became shelter again, and shelter was only a shape that kept weather off while sound found its way in.


# Chapter 11 - The Second Night After

By the second night after Alex died, they had begun to know what to listen for and still did not know what it was.

No one called it the second night after. There was no useful after yet. Alex was not behind them in any way the word could hold. He was ten yards through alder and wet brush, under a gray groundsheet and stones, close enough that a man could reach him in less than a minute and far enough that no one living could make himself go.

They had failed to leave him once already. That changed the night. The first night had been shock and blood and the small animal labor of surviving until light. This one had choice in it. They had packed, launched, turned back, rehung the barrel, and built the same bad shelter under the same trees. They were here because water and wind had refused them, but also because every plan they had was still made of bodies, boats, daylight, and nerve, and all four had come up short.

Rain closed the camp. It came down without force, a cold steady rain that made everything near and indistinct. It roofed the smallest sounds and flattened the rest. The tarp became an instrument. Each drop struck, joined another, became one continuous hand on nylon. Water gathered along the low edge and released in ropes. The spruce above them held its own store and spent it later, heavier, as if another weather had been waiting in the branches.

The men sat close without comfort.

Mike kept his own bear spray across his knees, the nearly spent canister smaller in the dark, its orange cap wet and dull, its weight wrong for what they wanted from it. Alex's unused spray lay beside Mike's pack with the safety still in, too clean and practical to look at for long. Will had the map case open though there was no light to read by. He kept one hand on the plastic as if the route might move under him if he let go. Their sleeping bags stayed rolled inside damp sacks under the packs. The tent bodies and flies lay in wet bundles where they had dropped them, unused and already sour. Joel's hammock hung black between two cedars, useless except as another shape to check. No one had gone into a tent. No one had taken off wet socks. Joel had his notebook in the dry pocket of his jacket and had not taken it out.

They had agreed on watches. Then they had all stayed awake.

The agreement itself had been brief and useless. Mike had said first watch, because saying first watch sounded like command. Will had said no, no, no, we need actual rotations, and then had been unable to produce one that mattered. Joel had proposed two-hour blocks, not because two hours was sensible but because any number was better than the three of them staring into rain until morning. They named the blocks and immediately broke them. Mike would not close his eyes. Will closed his twice and opened them both times before a minute passed. Joel sat near the tarp edge, hood up, one knee braced against a pack, watching the food barrel hang in the bad spruce beyond the gray line of rain.

The barrel was still where they had put it before dark. Closed, latched, strapped, hauled into the same inadequate tree because moving farther from camp would mean moving nearer the alder gap, and none of them had said that aloud. The rope ran black against the trunk. The barrel turned a little when the wind found it, not enough to swing, only enough to remind Joel that it was there.

Beyond it, somewhere behind brush and weather and the orange strip Mike had tied that morning, Alex lay under the groundsheet.

No one had checked him before full dark.

Will had asked once. Not directly. He had said, "The wind could get under plastic," while staring at the gap, and Mike had said, "It won't," too quickly. Joel had almost said they should look from the shore angle at least, just enough to confirm the sheet had not lifted. He did not. Saying it would have made a clean task out of a dirty desire. It would have put one of them at the mouth of the alder in rain, measuring duty against fear with the covered shape just out of sight.

Joel watched Will's mouth move around the next sentence and fail. That was how the old friendship worked under strain: the worst thing approached, became visible, and everyone looked at the practical object nearest to hand. Knot. Stove. Map. Rope. An object could be handled.

Now there were no objects left to fix. There were only objects to guard. Barrel. Boats. Paddles. Packs. Map case. Bear spray. The covered place beyond the alder was not an object, but they treated it like one because the living had run out of better duties.

"If it is a bear," Will said, too softly, "it may still be nearby."

Mike's head shifted. "It is nearby because we're still here."

"That's not a reason."

"It's the only reason that matters."

"No. No, it's not."

Joel said, "Save it."

Both men looked at him. He rarely used that tone. He did not like how easily it came, how quickly fatigue found a harder voice in him and put it on without asking. The rain filled the space after it.

Will looked back at the map. "I'm not trying to be an ass."

"You don't always have to try," Mike said.

Will gave him a thin look. "Good. Nice. Productive."

"Then stop."

"Stop what? Thinking about the large animal that may or may not be a large animal? Because I have made several attempts."

Mike drew a breath, held it, let it out through his nose. Joel saw his throat move. He did not clear it. The withheld sound was visible and more intimate than the habit itself.

"We treat it like a bear until there is a better thing to do," Mike said.

"There isn't a better thing to do," Will said. "That is not the same as it being a bear."

"Will."

"There is a difference."

The phrase entered the tarp space and sat there with them. Joel thought of the first camp, Will saying it over some minor correction, a lure knot or a map distinction, one of the ways he made the world bearable by cutting it into categories. There should have been a difference. Between bear and not bear. Between echo and answer. Between friend and the sound of a friend.

Joel said, "Not tonight."

Will looked at him.

"The difference doesn't help tonight," Joel said.

Will's face tightened. He wanted to argue. It was not only habit now. It was survival, or the nearest thing he had. If he could say it correctly enough, separate cause from coincidence and animal from voice, the world might become answerable again. Joel understood the impulse and distrusted it. Will's mind was trying to build a shelter out of distinctions. Mike's was trying to build one out of orders. Joel had nothing better than looking.

Joel had known him as a boy in church basements where adults used language the same way. Prayer requests. Testimonies. Concerns. A job loss became a season. A sick child became a burden. A death became a homegoing if the room was frightened enough of death. Joel had hated that before he knew he hated it, the soft hand laid over anything sharp. Will had not hated it, or had not needed to name the hatred. He had been bored and restless and overbright, whispering wrong jokes during youth group and coughing into his sleeve when he tried not to laugh. Joel could see him at twelve, fluorescent-lit and too thin, making Joel look down at his Bible so the adults would not see his face.

They had learned early that some rooms punished the direct sentence. They had learned to speak around the thing and let tone do the work. The woods did not punish the direct sentence. The woods kept it.

A drop struck Joel's wrist and ran under his cuff. He pulled his hand back and tucked it inside his sleeve.

"What are you thinking?" Will said.

"Nothing useful."

"That's not an answer."

"It's the one I've got."

Mike shifted the bear spray on his knees. "We need less talking."

Will laughed once. No humor in it. "You first."

Mike turned his face toward him. In the dark under the tarp his eyes had no color. "I mean it."

"So do I."

Joel lifted a hand. Not high. Just enough. Both of them saw it and both of them stopped, which surprised him. He kept his hand up another second, then let it fall. He listened until the rain separated again into layers: tarp above, spruce beyond, water dripping from a paddle blade somewhere near the boats, the lake shouldering itself against the channel mouth.

Then the food barrel latch clicked.

It was a small sound. Ordinary. One of the first trip sounds. Plastic, metal, pressure released. The kind of sound a body could identify before thought arrived. Joel's skin tightened before his mind placed it.

The barrel hung outside the tarp, pale in the rain, ten yards away and too high for any man to touch without the rope.

No one moved.

The click had not come from the barrel. Or the barrel had made a sound without moving. Or something below it had made the barrel's exact sound. Joel watched the hanging shape. The strap remained tight. The lid did not lift. Rain ran down its curved side. Nothing stood beneath it.

Mike's hand closed over the bear spray.

"Don't," Joel said.

"I didn't."

"I know."

"Then don't tell me don't."

Will whispered, "It was the latch."

"I heard it," Mike said.

"The latch is up there."

"I can see that."

"Then how--"

"I can see that."

The second time was quieter and worse. Mike was not arguing the fact. He was refusing what the fact required of him.

Joel leaned forward until his rain jacket pulled tight across his shoulders. He kept his eyes on the barrel and tried to make his body understand the new order: a thing could be seen shut and heard open. A man could stand beside him and cough from camp. Cause and proof had separated.

Something moved at the edge of the tarp.

Not outside. On the tarp itself. A bead of water traveled along the seam above Mike's shoulder, gathered, fattened, and dropped onto his sleeve. Mike flinched, then hated that he had.

Will covered his mouth with both hands. It might have been to stop a laugh. It might have been to stop speech. His fingers were pale where he pressed them into his face.

"Barrel can make noises," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"Wind twists it," Mike said. "Strap settles. Plastic flexes. Temperature drops."

"Sure," Joel said.

Mike heard the flatness. "What?"

"I said sure."

"You mean bullshit."

"I mean possible."

"Possible is not agreement."

"No."

Will lowered his hands. "There is a difference."

Mike's head snapped toward him.

Will's eyes widened a little, as if he had heard the phrase only after saying it. He pressed his mouth closed.

They sat in the rain, three living men under a piece of nylon, each trying not to give the night an obvious shape to take.

The tent zipper came from the dark behind them.

It was not loud. It did not need to be. A small tooth-by-tooth rasp, rising, then stopping. A pause. Then the downward rasp, slower. Joel knew the sound from every camp he had ever slept in, knew the slight catch where wet fabric pulled and the slider dragged over grit. He also knew where the tents were. The larger tent body lay rolled under the tarp at his left, fly folded badly on top. Alex's smaller tent sat half-packed in its open stuff sack by the packs, door panel folded against itself, zipper teeth visible and still. Joel's hammock and bug net hung dark between cedars, straps slackened by rain but visible. No tent stood in the dark behind them.

The zipper moved again.

Up. Down.

Same pace. Same catch in the teeth.

Will said, "The tents are packed."

"I know," Joel said.

"No," Will said. "No, I mean right here."

"I know."

Mike stood too fast and hit his shoulder on the tarp line. Water shook down over all of them. He raised the bear spray toward the sound, though the sound had already ended.

"Show yourself," he said.

"Don't," Joel said.

Mike's jaw worked. "It's an animal."

"Then it doesn't understand English."

Mike froze with his mouth half open.

That, more than anything, made Joel afraid. Not the zipper. Not the latch. Mike had wanted to answer. The night had offered him the motion his life had practiced: challenge it, name it, push back. A bad deal, a wrong map, a man arguing from the front of a canoe, a route going ugly. Stand up. Make noise. Take command. Here command was another sound, and sound was what the dark seemed to want from them.

Mike sat back down slowly. Rain dripped from the brim of his hood onto his hands.

"I'm not going to sit here while something walks around camp," he said.

"You don't know it's walking," Joel said.

"Oh, that's comforting."

"You don't."

"So what is it doing?"

Joel kept his eyes on the tent bundles. "Making sounds."

"Christ."

Will made a soft, broken sound. "That's the first true thing anyone's said in two hours."

Mike turned on him. "Do not start."

"I am deeply past starting."

Joel put one hand on Will's sleeve. Not gripping. Just contact. Will looked down at it, then at Joel.

"What?" he said.

Joel shook his head once.

Will swallowed the next sentence. It cost him. Joel could see it in the muscles around his mouth, in the way his body needed to push language out and had nowhere to send it. He looked suddenly young and old, the boy from church and the unemployed man under a wilderness tarp.

Out in the rain, Alex laughed once.

Will bent forward as if struck in the stomach. Mike did not move. Joel's hand tightened on Will's sleeve without his meaning to.

The laugh came from beyond the barrel, near the alder but not exactly at it. Alex's bark of laughter, the hard bright start, the little scrape at the end when he had been drinking coffee or trying to get everyone to turn toward him. It did not sound far away. It did not sound close. Rain made a room of the camp and put the voice in every corner.

"Stop," Will said.

Joel's grip tightened.

The laugh came again, farther off this time, or shaped to sound farther off. Thinner. Less body in it.

"Stop," Will said, louder.

"Will."

"Stop it."

Joel grabbed his sleeve with both hands. Will tried to pull free. For one bad second Joel thought he would go, not because he believed Alex was alive but because grief does not need belief to move the body. Then Will stopped fighting. His breath came in small hard pulls, almost coughs, and he held them back until his shoulders shook.

Mike said, "Maybe somebody's out there."

Nobody believed him fully, but they all needed the sentence. A poacher. Another group. A ranger. Some human cruelty so improbable it became attractive. Men could be punished. Men could be found. Men could explain why they had a dead friend's laugh in their mouths.

"Then why Alex?" Will said.

Mike did not answer.

The rain thickened. It drummed hard enough to make talk private and impossible at once. Joel heard the lake beyond the channel as a low shove, felt it more than heard it. The water had kept them here. Wind and rain and one bad boat. No monster was needed for that. The awful part was that something intimate had entered all that indifference and learned to use its acoustics.

Mike lifted the bear spray, looked at it, and set it back across his knees.

"If we hear it again," he said, "we don't answer."

Will laughed into his hands. "Now you arrive."

"I said don't answer."

"I heard you."

"Then act like it."

Will's head came up. "What do you think I'm doing?"

"Talking."

"So are you."

"Because you keep--"

Joel said, "Enough."

It came out quieter than before. It worked better. Mike looked away. Will looked at the wet ground between his sandals. Joel could see his toes, pale and wrinkled in the straps, dirt caught under one nail. Everyone had been wet too long.

"We need rules for the dark," Joel said.

Mike's mouth tightened. "Rules."

"Don't answer voices. Don't leave camp. Don't split up. There's your rules."

"Visual confirmation," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"If one of us hears one of us," Joel said, "we look first. We confirm by sight. We don't move toward sound unless we can see the person."

Mike stared into the rain. "This is insane."

"Yes."

Will gave a short, surprised breath that almost became a laugh and did not.

Joel took the notebook from his jacket pocket. The cover had softened at the corners despite the plastic bag. He opened it under his coat, shielding the page with his body, and clicked his pen once. The sound was small but all three of them noticed it. Even the pen had become a presence in camp.

"Sorry," he said.

"Don't apologize to the pen," Will said.

For a second Joel almost smiled.

He drew four columns because columns were not prayer. They made a place for facts to stand without touching.

Alex.

Mike.

Will.

Camp.

The pen shook once before he steadied it. His hand was cold and clumsy; the page took the ink badly where damp had reached the paper. Under Alex he wrote laugh. mothering. hold up. He paused before the last one. Not a return. Not yet. Material. Joel crossed nothing out. Instead he made a small mark beside it.

Under Will he wrote cough. no no no. He hesitated, then added difference.

Under Mike he wrote we're fine. opens up. throat clear. The words looked foolish on the page, too ordinary to justify fear. They looked like notes from a bad meeting, evidence for an argument no one would believe.

Under Camp he wrote latch. zipper. paddle knock. rope tap. stove click.

Mike leaned over. "What is that?"

"What it might have," Joel said.

"You're making a ghost spreadsheet?"

"I'm making a list."

"Same thing, Madison."

The old nickname landed almost normally. Joel had been Madison since he moved there, as if relocation were a defect and the city itself had replaced whatever name they used before. Alex had made it louder. Will used it for needling. Mike used it when Joel was being, in Mike's view, too careful, too cleanly reasonable.

For half a second Joel almost laughed. The almost hurt.

"Put that down," Will said.

Joel looked at him.

"Madison," Will said. "It heard that. Put it down."

Mike stared at him. "Jesus, Will."

"No, I'm just saying."

"Stop just saying."

Will flinched, then pressed his lips together.

Joel wrote Madison under Joel, in a fifth column he had not meant to make. He did not like seeing his own column there. He wrote wait beneath it, then stopped. Whatever was outside had heard him too. It had heard all of them. The timeline mattered. Tonight it only widened the dark.

"What about if it makes a sound that isn't a voice?" Mike said.

Joel capped the pen halfway, then uncapped it. "Same rule."

"How do you visually confirm a paddle knock?"

"You don't move toward it."

"What if it's the canoe?"

"Then it will still be the canoe in daylight."

Mike looked at him, angry because the answer was good enough to obey and not good enough to relieve him.

Will said, "What if it wants us not to move? What if this is the whole point? It pins us. It makes camp unsafe. It waits."

"Then we wait better than it does," Mike said.

Will blinked.

Mike seemed almost embarrassed by the sentence. "For tonight," he said.

That was the closest thing to courage anyone had said since the rain began.

Joel wrote: night rule - no sound without sight.

The words were too neat. He made himself underline sight once.

Something knocked from the shoreline.

Hollow wood on stone. One clean knock.

No one laughed. No one breathed loudly enough for the sound to hide inside it.

The knock came from near the canoes, or near the water beyond them, or from a place the rain had invented between. Joel could see the two canoe hulls pulled high and turned partly on their sides, dark and shining. Paddles were stacked beneath one boat, blades inward, exactly where he had left them. Painter ropes lay tight. No hull moved.

Another knock.

Not louder. Not closer. The same. The kind of repeat a person might make if the first had not been answered.

Will's hand went to the map case. His fingers pressed the plastic until it crackled.

Mike whispered, "Bear won't do that."

Will turned his head slowly toward him.

Mike did not look back. He had given up the word by accident and knew it.

The knock came a third time, but wrong. Softer at the start, with a scrape after it, like a paddle shaft sliding on granite. Joel saw in memory the first portage, a paddle knocked from under a thwart, Alex cursing with too much theater, Will saying that is why you don't put them there, Mike telling him to load faster and lecture less. The sound in the rain had no memory. It only had the shape.

"Gear too," Joel said.

He had not meant to say it aloud.

Both men looked at him.

The rain made the sentence small enough that the camp might not keep it. That was what he told himself. A lie, probably. Or not a lie. He did not know how close a sound had to be to matter. He did not know whether rain protected them or delivered them in pieces.

"What does that mean?" Will said.

Joel looked at the list. "It doesn't need just voices."

Will's face changed. Until that second, even after the cough, even after Alex's laugh, the danger had arranged itself around speech. Do not answer. Do not call. Do not give it phrases. But camp itself had been talking since the first day. Buckles. Zippers. Latches. Ropes. Paddles. Stove. Nylon.

The shoreline knocked once more.

Mike lifted the bear spray and lowered it again.

"We should bring the paddles in," he said.

"No," Joel said.

"They're right there."

"No."

"If something takes a paddle--"

"Then we deal with that when we can see."

Mike stared at the black shapes under the canoe. "We may need them fast."

"We're not getting on the water tonight."

"You don't know what happens tonight."

"I know we don't chase a paddle knock in the dark."

Will whispered, "I hate that he's right."

Mike shut his eyes. His face folded inward for a second. He was exhausted. They all were, but Mike wore exhaustion as a private insult. He opened his eyes and kept his body still.

The knock did not come again.

They sat until the rain changed pitch. The hard drumming softened into a finer hiss. Water ran from the tarp edges steadily. Somewhere to Joel's left a pack strap ticked against a buckle. He reached over and stopped it with his thumb. The absence after it stopped was immediate and large.

"Good catch," Will whispered.

Joel shook his head.

Will nodded once. No praise. No more words.

Time lost its edges. Joel checked his watch under his sleeve and saw that only twenty-three minutes had passed since he last looked. Mike sat with his chin tucked, eyes open. Will shifted, winced, shifted again. His knees must have hurt. Joel's own lower back had become a hot band beneath cold clothing. His feet were numb at the toes and raw where the sandal straps rubbed. He wanted to take the sandals off, dry his feet, change socks, do any of the small maintenance that kept a body from becoming stupid. He did none of it. Bare feet in the dark felt like surrender.

"You can sleep," Joel said, barely audible.

Will shook his head.

"Close your eyes for ten."

"That's how every terrible camping story begins."

"No talking."

Will made a face but closed his eyes.

For almost a minute he stayed that way. Then, from beyond the tarp and to Joel's right, Will said, "You guys hear that?"

Will's eyes opened.

He had not spoken.

The copied voice was quiet and exact enough in its parts to be wrong as a whole. It had Will's dry edge, the little lift at the end, the old invitation to make everyone else listen with him. But it carried no breath before it, no body after. A sentence removed from a man and set in the rain.

Mike's face went slack. Only for a second. Then anger covered it.

"No," Will whispered.

Joel put one finger to his lips.

Will's eyes filled, not with tears at first but with rage. His own phrase, his own small anxious hook, had been placed outside him and used without permission. Joel understood the rage better than the fear.

The voice came again. "You guys hear that?"

Same phrase. Same direction. Too even this time. It did not adjust for the fact that no one had answered. It had only the sound of invitation.

Mike turned his head toward Joel slowly, asking without asking.

Joel pointed two fingers at his own eyes, then toward Will sitting under the tarp. Visual confirmation. Will was here. Therefore Will was not there. The conclusion was crude and lifesaving.

Will mouthed something.

Joel shook his head.

Will's mouth closed.

The copied voice did not speak again.

After a while, Mike leaned close enough that Joel could smell stale coffee and rain on his breath. "What did you write?"

Joel showed him the page.

Mike took it in with a strained, impatient glance. The columns. The small fifth column with Joel's name. The underlined rule.

"Add that," Mike whispered.

Joel wrote under Will: you guys hear that.

The pen moved too loudly. He stopped between words and listened. Nothing answered the scratch of ink on damp paper. Maybe that meant nothing. Maybe the page had no voice unless one of them gave it one. Writing took tone away. It made Will's panic into shapes, Mike's certainty into shapes, Alex's laugh into a word too small to contain him. The thought steadied Joel more than it should have.

He almost wrote the question he could feel forming about the page itself. He stopped it before the pen moved. A question could become a kind of invitation even without a voice. He did not know that. He did not know anything that cleanly. The page had made no sound except the pen, and the pen had not yet become bait.

Will watched him write. His eyes moved over the page but he did not speak. Joel saw the question in him, bright and hungry. Can it learn from this? Does it understand? Is it only recording? Joel put the notebook back in the plastic bag before Will could borrow it and turn questions into air.

Mike saw the motion and nodded once.

It was the first time all night the three of them had agreed on anything without saying it.

The rain eased near midnight, or what Joel thought was midnight. The woods grew louder when the rain softened. Water dropped from needles. The lake resumed its individual slaps against the channel bank. Somewhere far off a loon called once and then did not call again. The sound was so ordinary Joel almost hated it. A real animal in a real night, carrying on with its life in the same dark that held Alex's voice.

Will's head dipped.

Joel watched him. The dip became a nod, the nod became a jerk, and Will woke with a sharp inhale that he managed to cut off before it became a cough. He looked ashamed.

Joel touched two fingers to his own chest, then pointed at Will, then curled his hand under his cheek: I watch, you sleep.

Will shook his head.

Joel pointed harder.

Will looked at Mike. Mike, after a moment, jerked his chin once in agreement. That decided it because Mike still had enough authority to make surrender feel like compliance.

Will lay down on his side under the tarp, not in the tent, not in the hammock, just on the thin pad they had dragged near the packs. He did not take off his rain jacket. He put the map case against his chest like a child with a book. His glasses stayed on. Within minutes his breathing grew uneven, not sleep exactly but a border state his body took without permission.

Mike remained upright.

"You too," Joel whispered.

"No."

"Ten minutes."

"I said no."

Joel did not push. Mike needed to refuse something. If sleep was the thing, fine.

They listened together.

Without Will talking, Mike seemed less angry and more frighteningly alone. The rain had carved the force out of him. He sat with the spent canister, a man built for decisions in a place where every decision fed the wrong mouth. Joel could see him wanting daylight with a physical hunger. Daylight would not solve it, but it would return edges to things. Canoes. Paddles. Packs. Barrel. Map case. Bear spray. Body.

"Tomorrow," Mike whispered, "we move."

Joel nodded.

"I don't care if it's ugly."

Joel did not nod to that.

Mike saw the distinction and almost smiled. Not with humor. Recognition, maybe. "Fine. We assess and move."

Joel nodded once.

Mike looked toward the hanging food barrel. "That thing makes the latch again, I am not going out there."

"Good."

"Don't good me."

Joel looked at him. Mike looked back. For a second they were simply two wet men being petty in the dark, and the pettiness was human enough to hurt. Agreement would have been too intimate. Irritation still belonged to them.

Then Mike cleared his throat.

He stopped halfway through the sound.

Joel saw his face change.

The unfinished throat clear hung in the tarp space, small and living. Mike's own habit had caught him by surprise. He put one hand over his mouth, as if he could take it back. For several breaths nothing answered from the woods. No route instruction, no we're fine from beyond the barrel. The absence did not relieve them. It held.

Mike lowered his hand.

"I hate this," he whispered.

Joel nodded.

There was nothing useful to say to that, and because there was nothing useful, he said nothing.

Then, from the rain beyond the hanging barrel, came the first half of the same sound.

Not the whole throat clear. Not Mike's voice. Only the little taken catch at the start of it, cut off where Mike had cut himself off. It was so small that in ordinary weather it would have disappeared inside water on leaves. Here it landed with a body missing from it.

Mike did not lift the bear spray. His hand twitched and stayed on his knee.

Joel pointed at Mike. Here.

Mike nodded once, eyes fixed on the pale barrel.

Will slept for maybe twelve minutes. Then his body, treacherous with exhaustion, coughed.

Not loud. A dry catch in the throat, the familiar small bark. He woke as it left him and clapped both hands over his mouth, eyes wide behind rain-spotted lenses.

All three men listened.

The answer came from near the food barrel.

The same cough. Will's cough. Wet night around it. A little embarrassed finish, as if the body that made it wanted to apologize.

Will began to shake.

Mike raised the bear spray and kept it aimed at the dark below the barrel. Joel put his hand on Will's shoulder, hard enough to hurt if hurt would keep him here.

The cough did not repeat.

Joel leaned close to Will's ear. He did not speak. He pressed two fingers against Will's shoulder, then pointed at Will, then at the ground: you are here. Will stared at him. Joel did it again.

You are here.

Will nodded. Once. Then again.

The gesture steadied Joel too. That was the terrible simplicity. The man you could see was the man. Sound stayed sound until sight gave it a body.

Something brushed the outside of the tarp.

All three froze.

The brush came again, low along the edge behind Mike. Fabric dented inward an inch and released. Mike turned with the bear spray. Joel's mind supplied branch, wind, water weight, a rope end shaken loose. The tarp edge moved again, harder this time, then stopped.

No voice followed.

No laugh. No latch. No zipper. No knock.

That was worse in its own way. The silence after a touch was nearly a shape.

Mike crawled one knee forward, slow, keeping under the tarp. Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike looked back.

Joel shook his head.

Mike pointed at the tarp edge with two fingers, then at his own eyes.

Visual confirmation.

It was almost funny that Mike had learned the rule fast enough to turn it back on him.

Joel hesitated. The tarp edge was three feet away. Not ten yards. Not the alder. Three feet. They could look without leaving shelter. They could also give the night exactly what it wanted: a body leaning out, a face at the edge, a man responding to a sound because not knowing had become intolerable.

Will reached for the notebook.

Joel let him.

Will opened to a blank lower corner and wrote with slow block letters, the pen nearly tearing the damp page.

WAIT.

Then, after a moment, he added:

DAYLIGHT.

He pushed the notebook toward Mike.

Mike read it. His face moved through anger, insult, calculation, and finally exhaustion. He sat back.

The tarp edge did not move again.

Joel took the notebook and wrote below Will's words:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

Will read it and nodded.

Mike read it and looked away.

It was not a theory. Theories wanted more words. It was not even a full rule. It had no exception for kindness, panic, or a dead friend needing rain pulled off his face. It was a refusal written before any of them could soften it, and therefore the closest thing to mercy they had.

The night went on.

It was not dramatic after that. That was part of its cruelty. They did not spend the whole night in a sequence of voices and signs. Much of it was wet discomfort and waiting. The body made its demands without respect for terror. Joel had to piss and did not. Will's leg cramped and he worked it out silently, teeth bared. Mike's hands grew stiff around the bear spray until Joel pointed at them and Mike flexed his fingers with visible irritation. Rainwater found a path down Joel's neck. The tarp sagged, and at one point all three men pushed upward with their palms to dump a cold pool off the far edge. The water hit the ground outside with a heavy slap. Everyone listened afterward to see if the slap would be returned.

It was not.

Once, near the hour before dawn, Alex said, very softly, "Quit mothering me."

Not from the alder. Not from the body site. From the camp center, or the shore, or the rain's false room. The phrase came with less of him in it than the laugh had. The shape of Alex without Alex's heat. The old joke emptied of the face that made it survivable.

Will did not move. Mike did not lift the bear spray. Joel closed his eyes for one second and saw Alex at the motel, Alex at the first muddy pullout, Alex holding up the hot sauce like a sacrament, Alex alive enough to be irritating. Joel had resented that life when it filled the air. Now the absence used the same air and asked for an answer.

No one gave it one.

The phrase did not repeat.

Gray began slowly, not as light but as a thinning of black. The trees appeared first as darker decisions inside dark. Then the hanging food barrel took shape, wet and pale, latch closed. The canoes emerged from the shore gloom, still there. The paddles remained stacked under the nearer hull. The tent bundles and damp sleeping gear lay where they had been all night. Joel's hammock straps hung black and slack between cedars. Packs. Stove. Map case. Alex's unused spray by Mike's pack, safety still in. Mike's hand around his nearly spent canister. Will's face, unshaven and slack with the beginning of shock beyond fear.

The world looked almost accountable again.

No one said morning. No one said fine.

No one said Alex.

Joel opened the notebook one last time before they began the work of leaving. The page had buckled from damp. The columns were ugly, crowded, childish in their certainty and still the best thing they had.

Alex: laugh. mothering. hold up.

Will: cough. you guys hear that. no no no. difference.

Mike: we're fine. opens up. throat clear.

Camp: latch. zipper. paddle knock. rope tap. tarp touch.

Joel: Madison. wait.

At the bottom, in Will's block letters:

WAIT.

DAYLIGHT.

And beneath it, Joel's own:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

Mike leaned over, read the last line, and said nothing. Will read it too. His eyes stayed on the words for a long time.

Joel understood only this: something had learned the sound of their camp. It had learned the parts of them that carried without bodies. Latch and zipper and paddle. Cough and laugh. The little phrases men used because silence felt too large. It had not used the page. Not yet. Maybe not ever. Or it had learned and had not spent that learning yet. He wrote the uncertainty nowhere.

That was not hope.

It was only a fact with a hard edge, and by morning a hard edge was enough to hold.


# Chapter 12 - Bad Crossing

Morning gave them less wind and gave the fear edges.

The camp looked almost innocent in gray light. The barrel hung shut. The canoes were still on the stones. The tents sagged and steamed a little where the rain had stopped. Joel's hammock straps made dark bands around wet trunks. Daylight laid every object back in its proper place and made the night's terror look like poor reasoning, as if fear had been a failure of visibility. Then he counted anyway: barrel, canoes, paddles, packs, map case, Mike's bear spray, Alex's unused spray, notebook, men.

Alex's groundsheet showed through the alder as a flat wrong color.

No one looked at it for long.

Mike stood with both hands on his hips, staring at the water as if the lake were a man refusing an order. His hood was down. His hair had dried in hard cowlicks during whatever few minutes of sleep his body had taken without permission. His own bear spray hung from his PFD by its strap, orange and dented and nearly empty. Alex's clean canister sat upright beside Mike's pack, safety still in, the practical object no one wanted to touch until they needed it. Mike had not offered to pack either one in a dry bag.

Will sat on a rock with the map case across his knees. The plastic had clouded inside from condensation. He kept wiping it with the heel of his hand and making it worse. His glasses were spotted from the night. His face had the pinched, elderly look of a man who had spent too long cold and upright, but his fingers would not leave the map alone. They worried the folded edge, smoothed the portage line, came back to the same notch as if enough pressure might make the paper admit which shore was real.

Joel stood at the shore and watched the lake measure itself in short black lines. Each line lifted, darkened, and fell apart before it reached the stones. The wind had not stopped; it had only lost some of its argument.

It was not calm. Calm would have been a different world, and they were not in it. But the high white tearing of the day before had flattened. Wind still came from the west in uneven pushes, with spaces between them. The near shore was protected by the point, and beyond it the main water showed dark, ribbed, manageable if they were clean and quick and lucky and did not have to look anywhere except forward. Joel could already feel the fraud inside that condition. Forward was where the water was. Forward was also where the wrong sound would come from if it wanted them to believe it.

Those were too many conditions.

Mike took them as permission.

"We move now," he said.

Will did not look up from the map. "That's still ugly."

"Ugly is not closed."

"That's not a category."

"It's the only category we have."

Joel wanted to object with something better than instinct. He wanted a sentence with weight in it, a fact that could stand against Mike's need to be gone. The map showed the nearest exit forward: the lake, a narrow landing somewhere on the north shore, a short portage into the next chain, then more water toward the take-out and Mike's Highlander. Backtracking meant Alex, the beaver dams, the rough portage, First Camp, the first water, the long unspooling of everything they had already survived badly. Staying meant another day beside the covered body and another night under nylon, listening until their bodies were only places sound could enter.

He looked at the notebook page again. The pencil and ink had buckled the damp paper. Will's block letters still sat near the bottom.

WAIT.

DAYLIGHT.

Joel's own line beneath it:

NOT SOUND. SIGHT.

It had worked for the night because the night had been made of waiting. Morning was work. Morning required motion: hands on rope, boats in water, bodies separated by wind and distance. Sight was no longer a rule written under a tarp. It was something they would have to keep while the lake tried to take it from them.

Will tapped the map case. "If we miss this landing, we're committed to the north shore."

"Then don't miss it," Mike said.

"I don't steer your boat."

"No, you just narrate it afterward."

Will made a small sound, almost a laugh and almost a cough, and strangled it. All three of them heard the restraint in it. The habit stopped in his throat before the woods could take it.

Mike saw it too. His mouth tightened, then loosened. "Sorry."

Will looked up at him. Neither of them knew what to do with that.

Joel folded the notebook and put it back in the plastic bag. "We can cross the protected part and reassess from the lee of that rock hump."

"That's not an island," Mike said.

"Hence rock hump."

Will lifted the map. "Technically there is a named island."

Mike looked at him.

Will said, "Not helpful. Understood."

For a second the old rhythm tried to return. It flashed once under dark water and was gone.

They broke camp without calling tasks across the site. That alone changed everything. On every other morning of the trip, leaving had been a noise: Mike telling someone to get the barrel down, Will asking where his filter cap went, Alex laughing from the wrong side of a job, Joel answering from a pack or a stove. Now they pointed, nodded, touched objects, made small hand motions like men working beside a sickbed. If one of them had to step behind a trunk, he raised a hand first and waited until the other two had seen him go. The rule made them slow and faintly ridiculous. It also kept them together.

The silence did not make the work graceful. It made it stupid and slow. Every ordinary job had gained a second job: do the thing, and be seen doing it. Coil rope. Show hands. Reach for a buckle. Let two exhausted men register the reach before the buckle clicked. A body had to become visible before its sound could be allowed to mean anything. Their campsite had become a small country of permissions.

Mike lowered the food barrel while Joel watched the brush. The rope hissed over the branch, a normal sound so exact it made Joel's teeth touch. The barrel thudded against the trunk. Mike flinched and then angrily did not look toward the alder. Will packed the tarp with fingers that kept missing the fold. Twice he started to ask something and stopped himself with his mouth open, breath held around the unspent words. Joel handled the stove, the wet mugs, the fuel bottle, the small hard things that could be counted and made to fit.

Alex's gear had already been divided the day before, but his absence kept being reissued by objects. His spare socks in a compression sack. His hot sauce wrapped in a bandanna. The cracked headlamp in Joel's side pocket. A red carabiner Alex had used for nothing important and had still somehow needed to bring.

Will found the carabiner clipped to the outside of a pack and held it up. The little gate clicked once under his thumb.

Nobody claimed it.

Will put it in the pack anyway and shut the buckle with great care, as if roughness would be heard as disrespect or invitation. Joel watched the click happen under Will's thumb and still had to tell his body not to look toward the brush.

They did not go back to Alex's body. Not fully. Mike walked to the edge of the alder and checked the orange tape from ten feet away. Joel went with him because letting Mike go alone would break their only rule before breakfast. Will came halfway and stopped behind them, arms wrapped around himself, map case under one elbow.

The groundsheet remained under its stones. Rain had slicked the plastic down so it showed the shape beneath too clearly in some places and not enough in others. The stones looked small. Every stone looked small once it had been asked to hold a body in place.

Mike stood with his jaw working.

"We come back with people," he said.

It was the first future he had allowed that included anyone outside the three of them.

Joel nodded.

Will said, "We write that."

Mike turned. "Write what?"

"That we left. Direction. Time. Names again. If someone comes in from behind us."

"No one's coming in from behind us."

"Mike."

The name was not argument. It was exhaustion shaped into one syllable.

Mike looked away. "Fine."

Joel tore a page from the back of the notebook and wrote beneath the overhang of his jacket while rainwater fell from the spruce in slow after-weather drops. He kept it factual. Three survivors leaving Second Camp morning Day 4. Suspected fatal animal attack. Body covered and marked. Attempting north crossing toward next portage and route exit. Names. Date as best he could track it. He did not write voices. He did not write mimic. He did not write that the dead man had laughed in the rain. He did not write any sentence that would make a stranger stop reading before the useful parts.

Will watched his hand. "Put weather."

Joel added rain overnight, wind lowered but still strong.

Mike read it once, folded it into a plastic bag, and wedged it under a stone near the first note they had left for Alex. His hands were careful. He did not say anything over the body. None of them did. Silence there felt like the last decent thing they could give him and also the safest.

When they turned back, from somewhere near the waterline came a soft click.

Food barrel latch.

All three men looked at the barrel. It sat strapped in Mike's canoe, closed, visible, ten yards away in open light.

The click did not repeat.

Mike breathed through his nose. "Daylight," he said, as if the word were an accusation.

Will opened his mouth, then shut it.

Joel pointed at the barrel, then at his eyes, then made a short downward motion with his palm: seen. Done.

They went to the boats.

Three men in two canoes made every old habit wrong. They had known this since Alex died, but knowing it on shore was different from loading it into moving water. One boat wanted a second body in front. The other wanted Alex's uneven strength, his complaints, his big shoulders blocking the low spray. Instead there were packs where men should have been and men where the dead had made room. Even the boats seemed to remember wrong: one front seat empty, one back seat overburdened, both hulls asking for the weight they no longer had.

Mike took his canoe alone. Before he loaded, he touched the left pocket of his PFD without looking at it. The old gesture was so quick Joel almost missed it: the small yellow pouch, the Highlander key still there. North Road Bridge still waiting in the form of a small piece of metal sealed against Mike's ribs. He clipped Alex's unused spray beside his own nearly spent canister, then loaded the food barrel and the heaviest packs forward to keep the front down. He shoved Will's larger blue dry bag farther ahead with his foot until it wedged under the front seat. It was too much weight in the wrong place and still not enough. The empty half of the canoe made it look false and unbalanced, a boat pretending to be ready.

"You need ballast behind you too," Will said.

"I need the front down."

"You need trim."

"I know what trim is."

"I'm just saying--"

Will stopped. His own phrase had become a bad tool in his hand.

Mike softened by one degree. "I know. Hand me the tent."

Will handed it over.

Joel and Will took the other canoe. Will in front because that was the shape the boat still knew, because Joel steered, because changing one more thing seemed impossible. Joel tucked the notebook into his jacket pocket instead of the thwart bag. He wanted it on his body, where his hand could find it without looking away from water.

They pushed off without a launch joke. No one said here we go. No one said nobody panic.

The first twenty yards were almost easy.

The camp slid behind them in pieces: wet tarp line, black spruce, the pale flash of the barrel rope left looped around a branch, the alder mouth. Joel kept his head forward. He did not turn for the orange tape. He could feel Will wanting to turn. The canoe carried the want through the slight hesitation in the front stroke, a hitch so small Joel would not have noticed it on any other morning. Grief had weight now. It changed trim.

"Eyes front," Joel said.

Will's shoulders rose. He paddled.

Mike moved to their right and a little behind, correcting too often. The solo canoe yawed with every wind push. He took four hard strokes on one side, switched, dug, overcorrected, swore under his breath, then began again. He was good enough to keep moving and tired enough to make it ugly.

Joel kept their front angled toward the first black shoulder of rock across the water. It looked close when the wind paused and far when it rose. The lake had the color of old tea under a pewter sky. Low cloud pressed the far trees into a single dark line. Each paddle stroke lifted cold drops that blew back against Joel's hands and made the shaft slick inside his grip.

No one spoke for a while.

That should have helped. It did not. Silence left too much room for water.

Sound carried differently in the open. Under trees, the night had made false rooms. Here the lake took every noise, flattened it, and slid it sideways. Mike's paddle slap came from the right, then seemed to come from behind Joel's ear. Will's blade knocked the gunwale once and the small wood sound ran ahead of them over the surface. A raven called somewhere high and unseen. Even true sounds were less true once water had handled them. The lake did not imitate. It distorted, which was almost as bad, because distortion still belonged to the natural world.

Joel watched Will's back. He had learned to read the front paddler by muscle and angle: Will's left shoulder dropping too fast when he was rushing, his wrist turning out when he was tired, the small pause before he switched sides. Now he watched for something else too. Confirmation. The living Will was in front of him. The living Mike was behind and right. Alex was not anywhere the water could put him.

The first gust struck them past the lee of the point.

It did not hit like a wall. It leaned. The canoe slid sideways, then the front rose over a short dark wave and slapped down. Spray blew across Will's chest. He gasped and kept paddling. Joel dug hard on the right to bring them back. The packs shifted behind Will with a wet rubber squeak. The hull felt suddenly narrow under them, not the familiar red canoe from the first two days but a thin promise trying to hold.

"Keep the left working," Joel said.

Will nodded without looking back.

Mike fell farther behind.

"Mike," Will said.

Joel made a sharp sound with his tongue before he could stop himself.

Will flinched. "Sorry."

Joel looked back. Mike was visible, head down, shoulders pumping, his canoe angled wrong but not in immediate trouble. He lifted his paddle once, not a wave exactly. Alive. Seen. Joel returned the gesture and faced forward.

They crossed another hundred yards.

Halfway to the rock hump, from somewhere ahead and left, near a dark cut that was not the portage notch, Mike's voice called, "It opens up after this."

The sentence came clean across the water.

It had Mike's working voice, not his argument voice. The practical tone he used at the mouth of a channel, at the start of a portage, when the route looked worse than he wanted to admit and he needed everyone to believe the next bend would justify the last one. It was not loud. It did not echo. It came without paddle slap, without hull scrape, without any living body to spend breath on it. It simply arrived in front of them, already useful and already wrong.

Mike was behind them and to the right, head down, paddling.

Will stopped.

The canoe lost its rhythm at once. The front swung left. A wave slapped the front quarter and threw water over Will's knees.

"Paddle," Joel said.

Will did not move. "That was--"

"Paddle."

"Mike said--"

"Mike is behind us."

Will turned halfway, too fast. His paddle blade lifted and caught the wind. The canoe rolled under them, a quick cold tilt toward the whole lake.

"Low," Joel snapped.

Will dropped his weight and grabbed the gunwales. For one second they were not crossing a lake but holding a table with the legs cut short, everything sliding toward the low edge. Joel braced, corrected, felt the hull resist and then come back.

"Paddle," he said again, quieter because quiet worked better now.

Will paddled. His strokes were too fast and shallow. The canoe yawed. Joel had to work against him, then with him, then against the water. His forearms burned in a narrow band. Cold water spread around his heels and found the torn skin under his sandal straps. The written rule had not failed. It had become muscle work. Sight did not sit on the page anymore. It lived in Joel's neck as he looked back, in his hands as he corrected, in the ugly seconds he spent choosing between the visible man behind them and the wave lifting under the boat.

Behind them, the real Mike shouted something.

Joel did not answer. He looked over his shoulder long enough to see Mike still there, still in his own bad boat, one arm raised in a question. Joel pointed forward. Mike dropped his arm and dug in.

A small latch click traveled between the boats.

Joel's eyes went to the food barrel lashed in Mike's canoe. The lid was shut. The black strap lay tight across it. Mike was paddling, both hands on the shaft, twenty yards back in open water. No one had touched the barrel. The click was already gone.

Will heard it too. Joel saw his head tilt toward the sound and then force itself back to the line of travel.

From the far trees came Will's own voice: "You guys hear that?"

Will made a noise low in his throat.

"Don't," Joel said.

Will did not turn. His head had lowered between his shoulders.

"Will."

"That was me."

"No."

"That was me."

"The living you is in the boat," Joel said. "Paddle."

The sentence cut through. Joel saw it in the way Will's shoulders changed. Not comfort. Instruction. That was all they had. Will put the blade in and pulled. The far shore did not repeat him. It had asked the kind of question old friends answered without thinking, and they had not answered. That felt less like success than like stopping a reflex.

The rock hump did not protect them. It was too low, more of a black back just under the skin of the lake than an island. Wind broke around it and came at them in short confused slaps. Mike tried to come up on their right and lost ground. Joel saw his canoe turn broadside for two bad seconds, the food barrel lifting against its straps. Mike threw a hard draw stroke, cursed, and straightened out.

They were not going to make the planned landing.

Joel understood it before he let himself say it. The intended landing lay farther right, a north-shore notch where the map showed a short portage into the next chain. From shore it might have been obvious. From water it vanished into the repeating black teeth of spruce and rock. Every possible landing looked like a bruise. Every gap closed as they approached it. The map had made a clean interruption in the shoreline. The actual shore gave them only shadow, roots, and the same dark conifers repeating themselves until decision became guesswork.

"Right?" Will said.

"No."

"The portage is right."

"We won't make it."

"Then what are we doing?"

"Nearest gravel."

"That loses the route."

"So does swimming."

Will laughed once, not because it was funny. The laugh came out broken and stopped short. Joel hated that his mind counted it as material before it counted it as pain.

The next gust pushed them toward a low, steep shore where spruce roots gripped rock like black fingers. No landing there. Joel bore left, saw nothing, then saw a shallow fan of pale stones below a dead cedar leaning over the water. It was not a campsite. It was barely a place. It did not offer safety. It offered an end to being on the lake, and that was enough.

He pointed with the paddle.

Will saw it and nodded too many times.

"Don't jump early," Joel said.

Will nodded again.

They came in too fast.

The first stone struck the hull with a flat bang. Will threw his paddle across the gunwales and grabbed at a root. The root broke in his hand. The canoe slewed. Joel jammed his paddle down to pry them straight and felt the blade catch between stones. The shaft torqued hard. Pain ran up his wrist and into the small bones of his hand. For one stupid second he held on because losing a paddle felt like losing a hand. Then the next wave lifted the back and drove the canoe sideways.

"Let it go," Will shouted.

Joel let go.

The paddle popped free, spun once in the shallows, and drifted along the hull toward deeper water. Its blade showed and vanished with each slap of water, close enough to save and far enough to make saving stupid.

Will lunged for it.

"Don't," Joel said.

Will lunged anyway, belly over the front thwart, fingers scraping the blade. The canoe tipped toward him. Joel threw his weight back and grabbed Will's PFD with one hand.

"Will."

Will caught the shaft with two fingers, lost it, caught it again by the throat. He dragged it in and sat back hard, breathing through his mouth. His sleeve was soaked to the shoulder.

The blade had split near the edge. Not broken through, but cracked, a pale line in the dark wood.

"Nice," Joel said, because anger needed somewhere small to go.

Will stared at him. Then, absurdly, he said, "That was an expensive paddle."

"I'll invoice the lake."

The joke was poor. It still worked for half a second. Will's face twisted as if he might laugh, cry, or vomit. He did none of them. He ran one thumb along the split blade instead, because the paddle had the mercy of being damaged in a way a man could see.

Joel stepped out into thigh-deep water. Cold took the bones out of his legs. He grabbed the painter rope and hauled. Stones rolled under his sandals. The canoe resisted, then came up grinding. Will climbed out on the shore side and nearly went down, one hand still holding the cracked paddle like proof.

Mike came in badly.

He had seen their landing too late and tried to correct from too far downwind. His canoe ran in at an angle, front heavy with the barrel and packs. The front struck a submerged rock with a sound that was not the hollow knock from the night but close enough to make Joel's stomach close. The boat stopped. Mike did not. He pitched forward onto one knee, caught himself on the center thwart, and the thwart cracked with a sharp wooden pop. The food barrel lurched against its lashings and held.

"Damn it," Mike said.

The word was too small for the day.

His canoe swung broadside in the wash. Joel splashed over and grabbed the front. Will got the back rope. Together they dragged it high enough that the next wave only slapped the tail of it. Mike stayed kneeling for one second, head lowered, both hands on the cracked thwart.

"You hurt?" Joel asked.

Mike shook his head.

"Look at me."

Mike looked up, furious. Not at Joel. Not exactly. At needing to be checked like cargo.

"You hurt?"

"No."

Joel nodded. Sight before statement. Even for injury.

They unloaded in pieces because pieces were all their hands could manage. Packs first. Barrel. Tent. Map case. Spare clothes. The larger blue dry bag from the front of Mike's boat had a dark wet seam along one side. Mike saw it and swore again, softer.

"It wasn't clipped," Will said.

"It was clipped."

"The roll wasn't."

"I rolled it."

"Not enough."

Mike rounded on him. "Do you want to do this now?"

Will's face changed. "Do what? Point out reality?"

"Reality is we landed."

"Reality is something used your voice while you were twenty yards behind us."

The wind moved through the spruce above them. Nobody answered that. Answering was the old reflex, and the old reflex had nearly rolled them into the lake. It was also how they had loved each other: correction, answer, answer back.

Mike looked back across the water. The place where the voice had come from was only lake and low tree line. "I didn't say it," he said.

No one argued. The denial came from his mouth, close enough for Joel to see the wet shine on his beard, and it still did not settle the voice that had crossed the water ahead of them. Mike seemed to hear the failure of it. His face closed, not in anger this time but in a kind of stunned privacy, as if even his own voice had stopped belonging wholly to him.

Joel pulled their canoe above the stones and turned it sideways against the wind. His hands were numb. His mind felt too clear and not clear enough. He could see everything in front of him as separate facts. Two canoes. One cracked thwart. One cracked paddle. One wet larger blue dry bag. Three men. No Alex. No proper landing. No known camp. The yellow key pouch still in Mike's left PFD pocket. Mike's nearly spent spray. Alex's unused one. No one coming.

"Inventory," he said.

Mike looked at him as if the word belonged to some other trip.

"Gear first," Joel said. "Then map. Then camp."

"We should find the portage."

"With what dry hands?"

"It's north of here."

"Everything is north of something."

Will made a weak gesture with the cracked paddle. "The portage was east of the notch. We are not at the notch."

Mike rubbed both hands over his face. "I know where we are."

Will's eyes sharpened behind his wet glasses. He did not say it.

Joel said, "Maybe. We still make camp."

"It's morning."

"Emergency camp. Dry what we can. Fix what we can. Then decide."

Mike looked toward the tree line. The shore rose quickly into spruce and jack pine with no inviting space between them. Moss covered the first shelf of rock in dark soaked mats. Blowdown made a low fence ten yards inland. It was a bad place for camp, but bad had become a relative word.

From somewhere up the shore, Alex said, "Hold up."

The phrase was soft. Not a shout. Not even exactly a call. It had Alex's old impatience in it, the half-laugh he used when someone got ahead of the story he wanted to be part of. It came from beyond a screen of cedar and deadfall, the direction a person might walk if he had landed first and found something.

Will closed his eyes. His face did not change, but his body did: the smallest forward lean, a private answering motion stopped before it became visible enough to shame him.

Mike turned his head toward the sound.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike looked down at Joel's hand. His face went hot and ugly. "I know."

Joel did not let go.

"I said I know."

Joel let go slowly.

The shore held still. No brush moved. No one said hold up again.

Will sat down on a wet stone as if his legs had quit. "It's using commands now."

"It's repeating what it has," Joel said.

Mike laughed under his breath. "Oh, great. That's better."

"It matters."

"Why?"

Joel looked at the broken cedar, the bad stones, the canoes pulled high and wrong. "Because Alex already said that."

Will opened his eyes. "You don't know it's only repeating."

"I know what we've heard."

"That's not the same."

"No."

Will swallowed. "There is a difference."

He said it without irony, and no one mocked him for it.

They sorted the larger blue dry bag under the cedar. It held Will's sleeping bag, a fleece, one pair of socks, and the spare fuel canister. The canister was fine. The fleece was wet along one arm. The socks were damp but salvageable. The sleeping bag had taken water along one side and into the footbox, not soaked through but bad enough that cold would own it by night if they gave it time.

Will knelt over it with both hands spread on the fabric. His face went blank in a practical way that was almost worse than panic.

"It's synthetic," he said.

"Good," Joel said.

"That is the entire good news."

"We'll wring it and hang it."

"In what sun?"

Joel looked at the sky. Low cloud. Wind. The occasional brightening that might become nothing.

"In what we've got."

Mike inspected the canoe. The front thwart had cracked on one side near the screw, not split free. It flexed when he pushed it.

"Usable," he said.

Will looked up. "For what?"

"For a canoe."

"That's an aspiration."

Mike's mouth opened, but the fight did not come. It died somewhere between his chest and teeth. He looked old for a second, older than forty, older than anyone should look while kneeling in wet sandals on a shore with a boat he had trusted.

"We can lash it," he said.

Joel nodded. "Paracord?"

"Repair kit."

Mike dug it out himself. Small roll of cord, tape, zip ties, a multi-tool, a packet of screws that suddenly seemed both ridiculous and precious. Joel held the thwart in place while Mike wrapped cord around the cracked joint and through the seat frame. His hands were clumsy from cold, but the work steadied him. Over-under. Pull. Cinch. Knot. Test. Practical motion returned his face by degrees. The lashing was ugly and probably enough, which had become the standard for everything.

Will propped the cracked paddle against a rock and examined the blade. "This can be taped."

"Then tape it," Mike said.

"I'm going to."

"Good."

"I said I'm going to."

Mike closed his eyes. "I heard you."

They worked in strained quiet. The wind dried nothing and took heat from everything. Joel's wet shorts clung to his thighs. His sandals had trapped grit under the straps, and each step ground it into skin. Hunger came and went without resolving into appetite.

Will took out the notebook.

Joel almost told him to wait. Then he saw the shape of Will's face and did not. Will needed one task that did not require him to move toward a sound.

Will turned to the page with the columns and added in small block letters beneath Mike:

IT OPENS UP AFTER THIS - WATER - NOT MIKE

Under Will he wrote:

YOU GUYS HEAR THAT - FAR SHORE - NOT ME

He hesitated, then under Alex:

HOLD UP - SHORE - NOT ALEX

The names made the denials look childish and necessary. Not Mike. Not me. Not Alex. The page was beginning to fill with refusals, which was different from answers and maybe all they could afford. It was not explaining the world. It was keeping each sound from becoming a person too quickly.

Mike leaned in and read them. "Add the latch."

Joel glanced at him.

Mike looked annoyed by the glance. "What? It happened."

Will wrote:

LATCH - DAYLIGHT/WATER - BARREL VISIBLE

Then he stopped, pen hovering. His face had changed while he wrote. Not calmer. More exact. Joel had seen the look before when Will was sorting some trip problem no one else cared about: stove failure, wrong portage length, a fish ID, a bad knot blamed on the wrong person. Original source. Current source. Effect. The columns let him breathe. They also tempted him. A column made a pattern look like a handle, and Will had always been at his most dangerous when a problem offered him a handle.

"Should we write what it wants?"

"No," Joel said.

Will looked at him.

"We don't know," Joel said.

"It makes us move wrong."

"Maybe."

"It makes us answer."

"Maybe."

Mike said, "Dead is dead whether it wants it or not."

Joel looked toward the lake. "Exactly."

No one liked the exchange. Joel did not either. But it kept the thing smaller than a mind they could anger or trick or bargain with. The page did not need motive. The page needed what had happened and what had not. A latch with the barrel visible. Mike's old line from the wrong water. Will's question from the wrong shore. Alex's command from brush where Alex was not. Facts were thinner than theories. They also weighed less.

Will wrote at the bottom of the page:

REPETITION, NOT INTENTION?

He put a question mark after it and underlined nothing. Joel was grateful for the question mark. He did not trust any sentence about the thing that ended clean. He also saw, with a small private dread, that Will looked better after writing it. Not safer. Better. The page had given him a problem again, and a problem was almost a place to stand.

Mike unfolded the map on a dry bag and weighted one corner with the repair kit. The three of them crouched around it in the wind. Nobody put a finger down until everyone could see the hand that did it.

"We're here," Mike said.

His finger touched a point west of the intended portage notch.

Will leaned close. "Maybe."

"We're here."

"Mike."

"What?"

"Say maybe."

Mike stared at the map. His throat moved. He did not clear it.

"Maybe we're here," he said.

The concession cost him more than shouting would have. Joel watched Will recognize that and choose not to press. Mike kept his finger on the map another second, holding the uncertain place down as if it might drift if he lifted his hand.

If Mike was right, the portage was less than a mile east along the shore. Less than a mile on a map. In wind, with damaged gear, after a bad landing, with the light already leaning away, less than a mile was not a distance. It was a number pretending to be a fact.

"We can walk shore and find it," Mike said.

"Today?" Will asked.

"Before dark."

Joel looked at the trees. Shore walking here meant slick rock, roots, blowdown, little coves that forced them up and around, no line of sight to the boats, and constant sound from water on stone. It meant one man wanting to scout ahead, one man telling him not to, one man hearing something that might be another man. It meant losing the boats to find the route.

"Tomorrow," Joel said.

Mike's eyes moved to him.

"We have enough bad decisions for one day," Joel said.

Will gave a weak snort.

Mike did not. "If weather comes back, we're stuck."

"We're already stuck."

"We're one lake farther."

"And no safer."

That landed. Not as victory. There was no victory left in being right.

A loose painter rope tapped the hull of Mike's canoe in the wind.

Tap. Tap. Tap.

All three men turned. The sound had a body this time: rope against hull, the line visible, the canoe pulled high on the pale stones. Joel walked down and tied it tight anyway. A true sound could still train them wrong.

Across the lake, too far to be certain of any single tree or stone, the old camp lay hidden behind the point. Alex lay there. The distance was not large enough. It would never be large enough. Water moved between them without judgment, carrying light, carrying sound, carrying the shape of a man's voice when the wind let it.

Mike watched Joel come back up the stones. His face had closed around the decision before he spoke.

"Camp," Mike said at last.

Will lowered his head, either in relief or defeat.

They started up through the roots with the first load, two men moving while the third stayed where he could be seen. The food barrel bumped Joel's thigh. The wet sleeping bag dragged against Will's hip. Mike carried the repaired canoe partway above the wash and set it down harder than he needed to.

They had crossed one bad piece of water and lost the comfort of believing motion meant escape.

By late afternoon, that was the day's whole progress: one lake farther, wet, damaged, accounted for, and no safer than before.


# Chapter 13 - Emergency Camp

Emergency Camp had no fire ring and no mercy.

It was not a camp until they had made enough mistakes around it to call it one. Before that it was only a steep poor shore, a wet shelf of root and stone, two canoes dragged above the chop, and three men trying not to look toward the places where sound collected.

The afternoon thinned without warming. Wind came over the lake in hard panels and broke against the spruce, shaking water loose from the branches long after the rain had ended. Every gust made the tarp snap unless Joel kept the line in his hand. When he let go, the cloth slapped hard enough to make all three of them look. The line had been too short for a proper pitch, then too long after he retied it, then wrong again after Mike moved the gear under it. Nothing would take the shape they needed. Not the tarp, not the tents, not the day.

The forced shore rose too fast for comfort. Below them the lake worried the stones. Above them the ground broke into black roots, reindeer moss, and shin-high spruce that took a step of privacy from a man as soon as he climbed into it. Joel kept finding small signs of almost-camp and losing them: a level place no wider than a sleeping pad, a cedar trunk too rotten to hold a line, a gap between roots that filled with water when Mike set a boot in it. The place had no center. They had to make one by putting objects where their eyes could return.

They set the tents where roots rose highest from the mud. One tent went crooked between a jack pine and a spruce with half its stakes buried sideways under stones. The other would not pitch at all because the ground fell away under one corner, so they made it into a gear shelter, fly pulled low and patched where the fabric had abraded against a pack buckle during the landing. The silver repair tape shone in the dim light, too bright for the rest of the camp. Under it they put the map case, stove, wet blue dry bag, repair kit, spare layers, the loose pot, every object that still had to be findable by sight.

"This is ridiculous," Will said.

He said it flatly, without heat. That made it worse.

"It's shelter," Mike said.

"It is fabric losing an argument with gravity."

"Then argue quieter."

Will looked at him, and for a second Joel thought the old exchange would build itself out of habit. Will would explain load angles or water runoff. Mike would tell him to stop narrating weather. Joel would say something small enough to pass as help. Alex would make it a performance, would step back from the sagging fly and judge it like bad theater, would call them pioneers in the dumbest possible voice.

The space where Alex would have stood remained open.

Joel tightened the tarp line again. The hook at the dead cedar ticked once against bark. He saw it, wrapped the loose tail around the line, and stopped the sound.

That was the work now. A sound, then a source. The barrel visible when the latch clicked. Mike behind them when Mike's voice had come from ahead. Will in the front of the canoe while Will's own question moved out of the trees. Eyes first. Body first. Everything else after.

They had written it down and still kept forgetting. Men who had known one another for forty years had survived by answering one another. Now each answer had to pass through the body before it could be allowed into the air.

The wet sleeping bag hung from a line between two spruce trunks, lifted and dropped by the wind. It was Will's. The footbox had taken water when the dry bag failed, and the dark strip along one side did not shrink no matter how many times they wrung it. His spare socks were damp. One fleece sleeve was wet. The base layer he had counted on for night had a cold shine at the seams. Not soaked enough to abandon. Wet enough to own him after midnight. Will stood under it with both hands in his armpits, watching as if attention might dry fabric.

When he took one hand out to touch the bag, Joel saw the cut across his palm. A short clean line from the landing scramble, opened by granite and kept open by water. Will closed his fingers before anyone could make it a subject.

"Synthetic," he said again.

No one answered. They had used the good news already.

Mike knelt beside his canoe under the low tarp, redoing the repair on the cracked thwart. His PFD hung on a root beside him, left pocket zipped shut with the Highlander key still sealed inside. His own bear spray lay within reach, orange and dented and nearly spent; Alex's unused canister sat beside the repair kit, cleaner than anything else in camp. The first lashing from the shoreline had held, but he did not trust it. He had taken the repair sleeve from the kit and cut two thin strips of aluminum with the multi-tool, working the metal back and forth until it gave. Now he set the strips along the split wood, braced them with a tent stake, and wrapped cord through the seat frame with care so furious it looked almost tender.

Give Mike a cracked place and he became almost gentle. Wood, metal, cord, screw: damage with edges. Damage that let his hands answer. He could lean his whole fear into that kind of work and call it checking.

His fingers shook from cold. He pretended they did not.

Will watched too closely.

"It's a field repair," Mike said without looking up.

"I didn't say anything."

"You were breathing critically."

"There is a difference between skepticism and breathing."

Joel almost smiled. The almost hurt.

He wrote the line down later because whatever was outside might have heard it. That was what affection did now: a living man's small joke became inventory. Joel hated how quickly his mind made Will useful to the notebook while Will was still alive, cold, ten feet away, his glasses wet and his hands still hunting for tasks.

The repair held when Mike leaned on it. It creaked, but the creak came from the canoe in front of them, visible, accounted for. Mike leaned again, harder than necessary.

"Don't prove a point by breaking it," Joel said.

Mike glanced up. "That's what field repairs are for."

"For breaking?"

"For finding out before you're in the middle of the lake."
