Will's mouth opened. Joel could see the correction rise, technical and helpless. Then Will shut it again. The effort showed in his jaw.

Mike saw it too. "Say it or don't."

"I was not going to say anything."

"You were absolutely going to say something."

"Fine. I was going to say testing to failure is not the same as checking."

"Congratulations. You said it."

Will looked toward the trees as if ashamed of the sound of his own voice.

That was new, and Joel noticed it more sharply than the argument. Will had always put words into space and warmed himself by them. Now each sentence left him looking after it, frightened of where it might go.

The wind pushed through the tarp and brought lake smell with it: cold tannin water, bruised cedar, mud opened by their sandals. Joel's socks were damp inside his camp shoes. His wet shorts had dried only at the edges and remained cold at the waistband. When he bent for the pot, the muscles under his ribs fluttered once and kept fluttering. Hunger came over him in shallow waves and broke before it became appetite. Cold made the body practical before the mind caught up: hands slow, jaw tight, decisions reduced to heat, calories, dry cloth, light.

He thought of Mara's kitchen for no useful reason: the bright towel looped over the oven handle, the magnet list on the refrigerator, the way she left mugs upside down on the drying mat and he kept turning them right-side up because he claimed it helped them air. A house made by small corrections. A life with a thousand noises he had never had to prove.

The thought left as fast as it came. A spoon clicked against the pot, and all three men looked.

They cooked because they had to warm something. It took too long. The stove sputtered in the wind, blue flame flattening under the pot, then flaring when Mike moved his pack as a shield. The click of the igniter made all three of them freeze before flame took. Joel saw the stove. Mike saw the stove. Will pointed at it anyway, a small bitter ceremony.

"Sight," Will said.

"Yeah," Joel said.

"We are going to become insane in a very organized way."

"We're ahead of schedule."

Will gave one breath that might have become a laugh if it had more strength. He held it in. The held-in thing seemed to remain between them.

They ate rice from the pot because bowls were packed somewhere stupid. Mike wanted to keep food simple, no smells, no waste, no mess. Will wanted more salt and did not ask. Joel scraped the bottom with a spoon and divided the browned, stuck layer into three equal pieces. He almost made a fourth. His hand moved before thought did, spoon hovering above the pot while his mind counted a dead man's portion.

No one mentioned it.

Alex had complained about food constantly and eaten constantly. He had treated every bland meal as a personal betrayal and then gone back for more, making noises over tortillas and cheese like a man forced to survive a hostage situation. Joel had wanted to tell him to shut up at least twice a day. Now the quiet around the food was so complete it felt staged. The trip had lost one of its weather systems, and the air did not know how to move without him.

Mike sealed the pot in the kitchen bag and reached for the barrel. The latch snapped under his thumb.

Will flinched.

Mike lifted the barrel toward him. "Me."

"I know."

"I'm right here."

"I said I know."

Mike set the barrel down too hard. "Don't make me responsible for every noise."

Will's face went white in a way that had nothing to do with cold. "I am trying to keep track of reality."

"So am I."

"No, you're trying to pin it down and yell at it until it becomes a route problem."

Mike stood. The tarp was too low, and his shoulder struck it, shaking cold water onto all three of them.

Joel put a hand up. Not between them, exactly. Just up. His own fingers were gray at the knuckles.

The gesture worked because everyone wanted it to. Mike turned away first. Will sat back on his heels and pushed both hands through his hair, then winced when the cut palm dragged through wet hair. The rainwater from the tarp had spotted his glasses again. He took them off and wiped them on a shirt already too damp to help.

Joel took the first aid kit out without asking. Will gave him the hand after one look at Mike. The cut was shallow but open. Joel rinsed grit from it with filtered water, wrapped gauze across the palm, and taped it too tight before Will said anything. Then he loosened it. The white strip looked official for about ten seconds before damp began to take it.

From somewhere behind the camp, in the spruce above the root shelf, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will turned before Joel could stop him.

It was not dramatic. That was the horror of it. No shout. No scream. Just Will's body obeying an old arrangement: a friend calls, you turn. His shoulders twisted toward the dark. His head lifted. For less than a second he looked not afraid but ready, almost relieved, as if the world had made a mistake and would correct it by sending Alex down through the trees, wet and loud and too pleased with himself.

Mike grabbed Will's shoulder.

"No."

Will stopped so sharply his glasses fell from his hand and landed in the duff.

The voice came again, softer. "Hold up."

It stood behind them in the trees with Alex's impatience in it. The little lift at the end, the half-command, half-request. The exact sound of him at a beaver dam, at a landing, catching a painter rope, making everybody wait because he had found a better way or a worse joke.

Will whispered, "Alex."

Mike's fingers tightened on his shoulder. "No. It isn't."

Those words changed the camp more than the voice had.

They had all known it in pieces. Joel had written denials in the notebook. Will had said repetition and almost said more. Mike had conceded uncertainty, maybe, with the map in front of him and the water behind him. But Mike had not given up the world out loud. He had kept bear as long as he could. He had kept weather, shock, echo, grief, mishearing, any hard word with a body behind it.

Now he stood with one hand on Will's shoulder and said that their dead friend's voice was not their dead friend. The ordinary names fell away without drama. Mike did not look relieved. He looked poorer.

Will bent slowly and picked up his glasses. He did not put them on right away.

Nothing moved in the spruce.

Joel took one step sideways until he could see both men and the black line of trees beyond them. He wanted to write. The need came so strongly it embarrassed him. His hand went to the notebook in his jacket as if paper were a weapon. It was not a weapon. It was a place to put what sound had ruined.

Mike let go of Will.

"You okay?" he said.

Will looked at the ground. "That's a stupid question."

"I know."

"Then why ask it?"

"Because I don't have a better one."

Will put on his glasses. One lens was smeared dark. "No."

Mike nodded once. "Okay."

That was all the comfort they managed.

Joel opened the notebook under the tarp with his body blocking what little wind came through. The pages had swollen at the edges. Pencil still worked better than pen. He turned past the existing columns, then back again because the columns mattered. Alex. Mike. Will. Camp. Joel had added his own name last night and wished he had not.

Under Alex he wrote:

HOLD UP - BEHIND CAMP - NOT ALEX

He hesitated and added:

TWO TIMES

Will crouched beside him, keeping his bandaged palm curled against his chest. "It used the same phrase at the shore."

Joel nodded and wrote:

SHORE BEFORE CAMP

"Same voice?"

Joel did not answer. He looked at Will.

Will closed his eyes. "Right. Sorry."

Mike came over and knelt on the other side. "Put no movement."

Joel looked up.

"No brush. No steps." Mike's face was hard with the effort of being accurate. "If we're writing evidence, write evidence."

Joel wrote:

NO VISIBLE MOVEMENT

Will leaned closer. "That's not evidence of absence. That's absence of observed movement."

Mike stared at him.

Will held up both hands. "I'm not being cute."

"No one thought you were being cute."

"I'm saying we should be precise."

"Write a peer-reviewed article after we get out."

Will's face tightened. "Precision is not the enemy here."

"Talking might be."

The sentence landed harder than Mike seemed to intend. It was too close to true and too blunt to survive as an ordinary insult.

Will looked away first.

Joel wrote:

LESS TALK.

Then, because the phrase looked like command and because commands had become suspect, he crossed it out and wrote beneath:

TALK ONLY WHEN NEEDED.

Then, smaller:

BODY / SOURCE FIRST.

The page did not make any of it easier. It made the next failure harder to pretend away.

For a while the emergency camp held itself around them. Wind. Tarp. The lake below speaking in its low side-on way against the stones. Will's sleeping bag lifting and dropping. The repaired thwart creaking once as the canoe settled under its own bad fix. Each sound required a glance. Each glance used energy. Fear had become clerical.

Will took the pencil from Joel and wrote in the margin:

IT REPEATS.

Then he stopped, pencil tip pressed hard enough to darken the paper.

"Maybe not only repeats," he said.

Joel looked at him.

Will swallowed. The words wanted out of him. Joel could see them crowding behind his teeth. Will was a man built out of distinctions, and this distinction had weight.

"Maybe it can cut things together," he said, barely above breath. "Not invent. Not like us. Phrase, tone, timing. It can put what it heard where it doesn't belong. That's not understanding."

"Will," Joel said.

"No, that's the point."

"I know."

"Then let me finish."

"Don't answer it," Joel said. "Don't give it more."

Will's mouth stayed open.

The warning had not been sharp. Joel wished it had been. Sharpness would have given Will something to argue with. Instead it came out tired and almost kind, and Will received it like a hand against his chest.

Mike said, "He's right."

Will turned on him. "You don't even know what I'm saying."

"I know you're feeding it."

"That's not how this works."

"Then don't explain how it works where it can hear you."

"That's exactly the kind of sloppy thinking that gets people killed."

"People are already killed."

Silence took the sentence at once. Mike looked as if he would take it back if the woods had not already heard it.

Will stood and walked two steps out from under the tarp, then stopped because two steps was as far as the rule allowed without making everyone follow. His shoulders shook once. Not sobbing, or not quite. Cold, rage, grief, all of it using the same muscles. He kept his back to them for one breath too long, and Joel felt the new arithmetic of distance: two steps, one turn, one voice.

Joel wanted to say Alex's name. Not to answer the thing. Not to call him. Only to put the name where it belonged, with them, in the camp, among the living who had known him. But the name felt like food left out.

So he wrote it instead at the top of the column, already there, already insufficient.

Alex.

The pencil mark did not sound like him. It also could not call him back.

Dusk took the trees by degrees. First the separate needles went away. Then the branches joined. Then the spaces between trunks filled until the woods were one dark mass above camp. The lake kept a little light longer, dull strips between the lower trunks. The emergency camp shrank as the light left it, tarp and tents and men drawn tighter against the dark.

They brought the paddles fully under the tarp. Joel's cracked paddle had been taped and marked with orange cord, a warning to the hand. Will checked the tape twice and then stopped because checking became another kind of fear. Mike tied both painter ropes off again down by the canoes and climbed back up wet to the knees.

"Boats are high," he said.

Will nodded.

"Ropes tied."

Joel nodded.

"Barrel's as good as it's getting."

The barrel hung shoulder-high between two trees, a bad compromise. No clean branch. No good distance from camp. No one willing to go deeper into the spruce at night to perform proper food storage for an animal they no longer believed was the main problem. Mike had tied it anyway. Not tying it would have meant surrendering one of the last ordinary rules.

Will glanced toward it. "If a bear comes, it gets a nice convenient snack."

"If a bear comes, we can almost call that refreshing," Mike said.

No one laughed. The line deserved a laugh from some earlier version of them.

The tent zipper sounded from the gear shelter.

All three men turned.

The shelter sat five yards away, half pitched low, its patched fly silver at one corner. The zipper pull hung at the door where Will had left it, tucked under the flap after Will had pressed it there with two careful fingers. It hung still.

Then the zipper moved again.

Not the whole length. Not a theatrical opening. Just three careful teeth separating, the small dry rasp of fabric and metal. The door gap widened by an inch, maybe less. It was almost nothing. Almost nothing was enough.

They looked.

Looking was allowed.

Will made a small sound through his nose.

Joel raised one hand, palm down. Stay.

Mike's face had gone empty in the way it did when he was very angry. He took one step toward the shelter.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike did not turn. His arm hardened.

Joel shook his head.

The zipper did not move. The shelter showed no shape inside but bags, a coil of rope, the corner of a blue dry bag, the pale round lid of a pot. All visible. All ordinary. All arranged badly enough that a man could turn one wrong step into another problem.

"Wind," Mike said, without conviction.

Will whispered, "No."

Mike looked at him.

Will pointed. His hand shook. "Pull tab is tucked under the flap. Wind can't get that angle."

"Don't," Joel said.

Will's eyes flashed. "I'm identifying a mechanical constraint."

"Quietly."

Will closed his mouth.

Mike stepped back under the tarp. That was harder for him than going forward would have been. Joel could see the effort in his neck, the humiliation of retreat from a three-inch zipper sound. He did it anyway.

The gear shelter remained half open. No one fixed it.

After full dark, the paddle knock began below them.

One hollow tap from the shore.

Joel's hand went at once to the paddles at his feet. The cracked one. The spare. Will's. Mike's. He touched each shaft as if counting heads. All there.

Tap.

The sound came again from lower down, near the canoes or beyond them. Wood on wood, maybe. Paddle against gunwale. Hull settling on stone. The lake putting a small thing in motion. There were enough possible causes to build a reasonable world from, and none of them had the shape of the sound in the dark.

Mike reached for his depleted bear spray and stopped with his hand beside it.

"Boats are high," Joel said.

Mike looked at him.

"Ropes tied. Paddles here. Sight."

Tap.

Will leaned forward, elbows on knees, hands over his ears without fully covering them. "It's using camp."

Joel nodded.

"Not us. Camp."

"It heard camp too."

"Jesus."

The word was out before Will could stop it. He took his hands down and looked at the dark as if he owed it nothing else.

Mike said, "Maybe it wants us to check the boats."

"Maybe it wants us to answer," Will said.

Joel looked at the dark below the trees. Want was a dangerous word. It gave the dark a center, a face it had not earned. Cold did not want. Wind did not want. Water did not want. Human was the shape it had learned to wear.

He took the pencil and wrote:

DO NOT ASSUME WANT.

Will read it and gave a short, bitter nod. "Fine. Function."

Joel wrote:

FUNCTION: MAKE US MOVE / ANSWER / SEPARATE?

DO NOT ASSUME UNDERSTANDING.

The question mark mattered.

Mike crouched closer, rain jacket whispering. "Add old cabin."

Will looked at him sharply. "What?"

"Tomorrow."

"No."

"We can't sit here."

"And we can't just chase a square on an antique map because the lake scared you."

Mike's throat worked. He did not clear it. Joel noticed that. The swallowed habit, the authority denied its own sound.

"It isn't because I'm scared of the lake," Mike said.

Will gave him a look so exhausted it had almost no anger left. "Mike."

"The canoe is damaged. The paddle is damaged. Your bag is wet. Weather's not stable. We're off the route and we missed the portage landing. The barrel's hanging too low. We have maybe one dry sleep system between three people. Nobody is coming with a motor. Nobody knows where we are except the note by Alex, and the note says where we used to be. If we keep hugging shore looking for the portage, we spend half a day beside water where voices carry. If we cross again wrong, we lose a boat or one of us goes in."

He touched the zipped pocket on the PFD beside his knee without looking at it. Key, road, truck: the whole outside world reduced to a hard piece of metal and a line on paper.

"The old map puts a maintenance cabin inland from the next portage," Mike said. "A square and a dashed trace. Maybe there's a road."

"Maybe there's a fairy goddamn godmother."

"Map says there's a cabin."

The phrase came out smaller than when he had said it before. Not declaration. Not certainty. A man offering up the last solid object he had.

He looked at the patched canoe, the wet bag, the barrel hanging badly between two trees. "I put us on this route," he said. "I'm not saying that fixes anything. But if there's another human mark out here, I want to see it in daylight."

Joel felt Will beside him gather for the correction. Map says there was a cabin. Map says someone once paid a printer. Map says a lot of things. Joel could hear all of it, and because he could hear it he knew the woods might too.

"Tomorrow," Joel said.

Both men looked at him.

He wished he had a better reason. He wished he believed in the cabin. What he believed was narrower: they could not move at night; another full day here would make them colder and more stupid; the water had nearly killed them in daylight; the woods were no safer, but a trace would put solid ground under their feet. The cabin did not have to be rescue. It only had to be a thing they could test while there was light to turn around in.

"We look in daylight," Joel said. "All three. No scouting alone. If it is nothing, we come back."

"Come back to what?" Will asked.

Joel did not answer.

The paddle knock stopped.

That made the silence after it feel selected.

Mike rubbed both hands over his face. "We need sleep."

Will looked at the hanging bag. "That's optimistic."

"Rest, then."

"Also optimistic."

"Horizontal misery," Joel said.

Will stared at him for a second, then gave a laugh so small it barely had air in it. The laugh frightened him as soon as it left. He looked toward the trees.

No answer came.

They arranged the night by sight and exhaustion. Nobody called it a watch schedule. A schedule implied morning as a simple consequence. Mike took the first sitting position under the tarp with both canisters beside him but not in his hand. Joel would stay in the hammock at the bad angle and try to rest without closing himself away from the others. Will would take the one pitched tent, because his sleeping bag needed body heat and because arguing about fairness would only produce more sound.

Will objected anyway.

"You take it," he told Joel.

"No."

"Your hammock is garbage."

"Your bag is wet."

"That's not a moral category."

"It's a heat category."

Will looked too tired to appreciate his own kind of language coming back at him. He ducked into the tent and stopped before closing the zipper. The pull was between his fingers. All three of them watched it.

"This is me," he said.

"Seen," Joel said.

Will drew the zipper slowly, then stopped halfway and left it there. "I don't want to make the whole sound."

No one told him that half a door was stupid. Fear had its own engineering now. The gap left enough air to see him if the headlamp caught the right angle. It left enough dark to hate.

Joel climbed into the hammock. The hang was wrong, shoulder higher than hip, fabric pinching behind his knees. Cold came through the underquilt where it had been compressed against his wet clothes. Above him the tarp sagged inches from his face and shivered with each gust. He could see Mike's boots, the two orange canisters, the notebook in its plastic bag beside the stove. He could see the half-open gear shelter. He could see Will's tent wall, pale in the dark under the tarp edge, moving softly when Will shifted inside.

He could not see Alex.

That remained the basic fact. Not from camp. Not under the tarp. Not on the shore. Not behind the spruce. The dead did not get a vote because the dark could make his voice.

Joel took the notebook from the bag and wrote by headlamp under his jacket so the light stayed small.

Alex:
HOLD UP - SHORE / CAMP - NOT ALEX

Mike:
IT OPENS UP AFTER THIS - WATER - NOT MIKE

Will:
YOU GUYS HEAR THAT - FAR SHORE - NOT WILL

Camp:
LATCH - DAYLIGHT - BARREL VISIBLE
ZIPPER - GEAR SHELTER - NOT WIND?
PADDLE KNOCK - PADDLES UNDER TARP

He paused over the last column.

Joel:

The blank after his name seemed louder than the lake. He had given it less, maybe. Not nothing. Wait. Just wait. Eyes front. Paddle. Small jokes. His voice had moved over the same water, under the same trees. If sound could be kept, then parts of him were already out there without permission.

He did not write that.

On a clean page he wrote in block letters:

DO NOT ANSWER.

He looked at the sentence. A dead friend could ask. A living friend could call from out of sight. A boat could knock. A zipper could open. A bear could actually come. The rule did not choose safety. It chose which danger they would prefer.

He added beneath it:

UNLESS SEEN.

Then he crossed that out.

Too late for that. The living men could be seen and still have their voices stolen elsewhere. Seeing a man only proved where his body was. Answer the body in front of you, the gesture, what eyes could hold.

He wrote:

CONFIRM BODY / SOURCE. THEN MOVE.

Mike leaned over from his sitting place and read it upside down. His face in the headlamp was all planes and grime, eyes sunk deep.

"That's the plan?" he said.

"For tonight."

Mike looked toward Will's tent, then the trees, then the lake. "Great plan."

Joel waited.

Mike rubbed at the bridge of his nose. "I don't have a better one."

From inside the tent, Will said, "That's going in the minutes."

His voice was muffled by nylon, ordinary and exhausted and alive. Joel saw the shape of him through the tent wall, one knee raised, one elbow moving as he fought the wet sleeping bag around himself. Body and source aligned for one second, and the voice came back to him.

Then, beyond the camp, low among the spruce, Alex laughed once.

Not loud. Not the full bark. A small breath of it, as if he had heard Will and could not help himself.

Will went still inside the tent.

Mike's hand closed around the nearer spray canister.

Joel put the notebook page where both men could see it, black letters in the narrow light.

DO NOT ANSWER.

No one did.

The laugh did not repeat. The lake worked below them. The tarp ticked once, and Joel found the line with his eyes. The food barrel hung where they had left it, too low and visible between two trunks. The gear shelter's zipper remained half open. The canoes down by the shore were pale shapes turned away from the dark water. Nothing moved that could be trusted. Nothing spoke with a body.

They held their silence until it became less like courage and more like another kind of labor.

By the time Mike's head began to lower against his chest and Will's shivering settled into uneven breaths, Joel had understood the cabin for what it was. Not rescue. Not safety. A square on an old map, a human mark in a place that was steadily taking human things apart: voices, routes, jokes, certainty, the usefulness of saying a name. It was something that could be checked in daylight, all three of them together or not at all.

In the morning they would put stones on the map corners and pretend paper still knew how to tell the world what to be.


# Chapter 14 - The Old Map

By morning Mike had the old map spread on a wet rock, stones on the corners.

The rock was not flat enough. Nothing there was. Day had come without much light, and the lake had dropped in the night, or seemed to have, leaving a black line on the stones where water had held and withdrawn. The canoes lay higher than the mark, pale and cold under spruce shadow. The tarp sagged over the root shelf. Will's sleeping bag hung dark at the foot where the water still owned it. Every object they owned looked like the poorer version of a thing that had once had a clear use.

Joel stood with his arms folded against his chest and watched water gather in the creases of the map case. The plastic was clouded from old scratches. Under it the printed lakes and portages held their clean colors, blue and green and brown arranged with confidence. Little broken lines crossed land as if land had agreed to be crossed. A square sat inland from the portage they had missed the day before, small enough to be nothing, exact enough to make three exhausted men lean toward it.

Maintenance cabin, the old legend said.

The print was thin at the edges. A previous fold ran directly through the word cabin, making the letters step over a white break.

Mike had found the map before coffee. He had not slept, or had slept only in dropped pieces. His eyes were dark under his cap. The stubble along his jaw had come in gray and uneven. He held himself with the hard attention of a man who had woken before doubt could get up with him and was trying to keep it down by giving it work.

"Here," he said.

Will did not bend closer. He stood above the rock with both hands tucked under his armpits, one palm bandaged from the landing scramble the day before, where granite had taken a clean little bite out of him and given back blood. The bandage had already loosened at one edge. Damp made every repair temporary.

Joel could see the old route as Mike saw it. Emergency Camp below them on the wrong shore. The missed portage north and west along the lake, reachable if the wind stayed down, if the repaired canoe behaved, if no sound pulled them apart at the landing. The cabin inland from that portage, connected by a faint black line to something marked as an old fire road. Past that, perhaps another road, or a clearing, or a ditch where a road had been. Perhaps only the shape of an intention after use had gone out of it.

Paper kept all those versions in the same ink.

The other choices lay around the rock without needing to be pointed at. Wait here and become colder, wetter, easier to separate. Try the lake again with one cracked boat, one cracked paddle, and wind that had only paused. Work the shore toward the missed portage and let every landing, every alder mouth, every voice across water pull at them. Turn back toward Second Camp and Alex. None deserved the word plan. The cabin was only the choice that could still become fact before dark.

"We leave the boats cached," Mike said. "Take what we can carry for two days. Repair kit, first aid, filter, stove, one pot, ration bag. Sleeping bags. Tarp. Map. Compass. If the cabin has anything useful, we use it. If there's a road, we follow it. If there isn't, we come back."

"That's a lot of if," Will said.

"Everything is if."

"No." Will leaned down then, because he could not resist the map even when he hated it. "Some things are distances. Some things are contours. Some things are false hope printed in black by a guy in an office thirty years ago."

Mike's throat moved. He did not clear it.

Joel noticed the restraint because the habit had become visible by its absence. A day earlier he might not have known what Mike was stopping. Now the unsounded thing seemed to stand between them with the rest of the sounds they were trying not to give away.

"You want to paddle big water with a cracked boat and voices?" Mike asked.

Will looked toward the lake.

The lake had eased in the night but not forgiven them. Small wind marks ran over it from the middle out, darkening and smoothing by turns. The far shore had no scale. Trees lined up there in black ranks, all of them looking close until Joel imagined crossing and understood again how distance hid itself on water. Last night the voice had crossed without a canoe. Sound carried where men could not.

"No," Will said.

Mike waited.

"I don't want to walk into wet brush because of a square," Will said. "Those are different categories of stupid."

"Great. Pick a category."

Will's mouth tightened. He had slept in the least wet bag and looked worse for it, as if warmth had only given his body more room to shake. "The map says there was a cabin."

"Map says there's a cabin."

"You hear the difference?"

"I hear you making every sentence longer."

Joel crouched beside the rock. The old map had been opened and folded so often the route wore a softness like cloth. He touched the plastic above the square, then drew his finger along the faint line toward the fire road. Under the case, the line looked deliberate. On the ground it would be alder, blowdown, wet leaves, rotten corduroy maybe, old tire ruts full of black water. A human mark did not mean human presence. It meant somebody, once, had wanted the same thing they wanted now: a way through, or the comfort of believing one existed.

He thought of Mara's house list, gutters and inspection dates and the electrician's number written in her square hand on the refrigerator. Beside it, before he left, she had drawn a little box for tulip bulbs and written only if you actually want them, which had made him laugh because he did, because she knew he did, because she had found a way to make even a chore tease him for being known. Those marks belonged to a world where a list could still become a Saturday. This map belonged to that world too, or pretended to. A line. A square. A word. In Madison those things started errands. Here they could spend a life.

"If the road is gone?" Joel asked.

Mike looked at him with a flash of relief. A practical question was a handhold.

"Then we turn around before dark."

"Before dark," Will said.

"Yes."

"We've become excellent at arriving places before dark."

Mike folded his lips in, held them there, and let the line pass. That was new too. He did not have energy to punish every challenge. Or he had begun to understand that every argument became inventory for whatever listened.

Joel took the notebook from the plastic bag. Its pages had dried in warped ridges near the spine. He turned past the columns from the night before. Alex. Mike. Will. Camp. Joel. The names made a poor shelter. They had held facts for a few hours and protected no one.

On a clean page he wrote:

CABIN PLAN - DAYLIGHT ONLY.

ALL THREE.

NO SCOUTING.

NO ROUTE CHANGE FROM VOICE ONLY.

Mike watched the pencil. "Add return by dusk."

Joel wrote:

TURN BACK IF NO CABIN / NO TRACE BY MIDDAY.

Will made a small sound.

Joel looked up.

"Midday is optimistic," Will said.

"Then say it with the pencil."

Will took the notebook. He held the pencil awkwardly in his bandaged hand, then shifted it to the other and wrote in block letters:

OLD MAP MAY BE WRONG.

He pushed the notebook back.

Mike read it. "That's useful."

"It is."

"It's just pessimism with handwriting."

"No, it's a constraint."

Joel wrote beneath it:

ASSUME WRONG UNTIL SEEN.

That quieted them for a moment. The rule was not only for voices anymore. A friend's voice. A latch. A boat knock. A square on a map. None of it counted until the body or object stood where the sound or mark claimed it would be.

Joel added:

SOURCE BEFORE TRUST.

Mike looked away first. "Pack."

The word had enough command in it to organize the next hour.

They made coffee because coffee was a normal human act and because hands needed heat. The stove click made all three of them glance down before flame came. Will pointed at it with two fingers, the small ceremony from the night before repeated without speech. Seen. The blue flame flattened in the lake wind, then steadied when Joel set his pack as a shield. They drank from metal cups that tasted faintly of old rice. No one wanted food, so they ate bars and the last of a bag of trail mix by count, three shares laid on the map rock with one bright raisin rolling toward the edge.

Joel almost stopped it with Alex's name in his mouth.

He caught the raisin instead and put it on Will's pile.

Will saw. His face changed and changed back. He ate it without comment.

When he lifted the trail mix, the loose edge of his bandage caught on his cuff and peeled back. The cut across his palm opened red in the wet white gauze. Will tucked the bandage down with his thumb and kept chewing, eyes on the map rock, as if blood were only another leak no one had time to fix.

Joel took the first aid kit from the cache pile before Will could turn away. He did it without asking because asking would make a scene out of care and because Will, given a scene, would fill it.

"Hand," Joel said.

Will looked at him, then gave up the argument before starting it. The surrender was worse than complaint.

The cut was not deep enough to explain the color of Will's face. It ran across the pad below the fingers, clean at one end and ragged where grit had found it. Joel rinsed it with filtered water while Will stared over his shoulder at the lake and made no sound except one breath drawn through his teeth. Mike passed over a strip of tape without being asked. They wrapped the gauze too tight, then looser. The new white bandage looked official for about ten seconds before damp began to take it.

Packing for a possible exit was harder than packing for a route. A route allowed redundancy. You carried extra socks, extra line, an extra headlamp, a book you thought you might read on a point in evening light. Escape made every object argue for the future it believed in. The first aid kit said injury. The repair kit said return to the boats. The stove said another wet night. Food said not enough and too much. Sleeping bags said weight and hypothermia at the same time. The tarp mattered. The tent body did not. The cracked paddle might become a brace or a probe. Will's rod belonged to the life they had been in before.

Will held his rod tube for a long time, then set it under the gear shelter.

"Don't say anything," he said.

Mike lifted both hands.

"I mean it."

"I didn't say anything."

"Your face said a short essay."

"My face is conserving language."

Will stared at him, then gave one dry breath. Not a laugh. A failed start at one.

They sorted the food barrel into a small ration bag for Mike's pack and two cached side bags. Mike wanted to take more than they could carry and less than they might need. Will wanted the bear line, not because he believed in proper storage anymore but because abandoning ordinary precautions felt like inviting every kind of failure at once. Joel wanted weight low and simple. The argument had no clean winner. They took the ration bag, the line, and the barrel lid's sweet plastic smell on everything.

Mike unclipped the nearly spent bear spray from his PFD and set it beside the headlamp bag. The canister looked too light for what they had asked of it. Alex's unused spray lay beside it, orange and clean except for mud around the cap. Full, probably. Better than Mike's, probably. No one reached for it at first. Taking it made sense in the way taking a full can of spray always made sense. It also meant carrying one more object from the place where Alex had tried to save himself and had not even fired.

"We should take that," Will said.

Mike looked at the canister, then at the packs. "I know."

He picked it up. His face did not change. He checked the safety, then clipped it to the front of his PFD where his own canister had been. The clip made a hard little sound. All three men saw his hand make it and waited anyway for the woods to try it back.

"Happy?" he said.

Will stared at him.

Joel did not know whom Mike meant. Will, maybe. The page. Alex, if a man could be angry at the dead for leaving behind a tool that had not saved them. The full canister had become more than a tool. Alex's safety clip had stayed in place through the attack. It was evidence of the one second he had not been given.

Joel put Mike's near-spent canister into the cracked headlamp bag and wrote:

FULL SPRAY - MIKE PFD.

SPENT SPRAY - CACHE.

Will read it and looked as if he might object. Then he looked toward the trees, where objection had become another kind of offering, and did not.

The food barrel latch snapped when Mike closed the empty barrel over the cache odds.

All three men looked anyway.

The barrel sat in front of Mike, lid down, latch under his thumb.

The sound had come from his hands. They had seen it. Still, Will's shoulders rose to his ears.

"Mine," Mike said.

"Seen," Joel said.

Will nodded too fast.

They cached what they could not carry under spruce boughs and the low fly of the failed gear shelter. Cache was too grand a word for a pile of wet equipment hidden from weather, animals, and whatever else had no need to respect a tarp. Joel folded the tent body into it, then the rod tube, spare cookware, extra clothes too wet to justify their weight, and Alex's remaining odds and ends sealed inside the cracked headlamp bag because no one could decide who should carry them. He put Alex's hot sauce there too. It had rolled out from a side pocket when Mike shook out a pack, red plastic bright against the moss.

Will looked at it. "He would haunt us for leaving that."

No one answered. They were already being haunted by less.

Joel slipped the bottle into the bag. The cap made a tiny click against the headlamp case. He saw it. The click belonged to plastic touching plastic, not Alex. It still opened a place in him shaped like Alex's hand reaching for it.

Down by the shore, they worked on the canoes. The damaged one went first, dragged higher above the waterline with all three of them on the gunwales. The repaired thwart creaked under its own accusation. Mike flinched at the sound, then set his jaw and lifted again.

"Up here," he said.

"That's visible from the water," Will said.

"Good. We need to find it again."

"Also visible to anything with eyes."

"Have you seen eyes?"

Will looked at him. "Is that the standard now?"

Mike did clear his throat then, once, quiet and real.

Joel hated how his body reacted to it. Every muscle around his ribs tightened before thought arrived. Mike was three feet away. His mouth had made the sound. His hand was on the canoe. The sound belonged to him and still did not feel safe, because belonging had become something that could be counterfeited.

"We need them not to float away," Mike said. "We need to find them if we come back. We don't need to build a museum exhibit."

"I'm just saying."

"I know. You are always just saying."

The old rhythm rose and failed. Alex should have cut in there. He would have given Will a title, made Mike defend the stupidest possible interpretation of his own words, turned the canoe cache into a bit about survival influencers with discount hatchets. Joel could hear the shape of it without sound and hated the hearing.

They pulled spruce boughs over the hull. The green needles left resin on Joel's fingers. He tied the painter to a black spruce trunk, then retied it higher when he saw the old water mark on the bark. The knot came out ugly. He made himself redo it. Fatigue wanted him to accept almost anything. The woods had already accepted too much from them.

The second canoe they carried farther into brush and turned upside down between two downed logs. It was the better boat, and hiding it felt wrong. Leaving it visible felt worse. The paddles went under it except for the cracked one, which Mike insisted they carry in case a pole or splint became useful. Joel took it first because it was his damage and because the orange cord on the shaft made it easy to track by sight.

Mike kept his PFD on.

Will watched him buckle it over his rain jacket. "We are not walking through a lake."

"Pockets."

"You have pockets on your actual body."

"Key's in this one."

Mike touched the zip pocket on the left side of the PFD. The gesture was quick but not quick enough to hide. The Highlander key. The staged exit. North Road Bridge sitting somewhere beyond all this with Mike's old vehicle waiting beside a road that still belonged to the human world.

He had talked down the missing beacon before the trip. Not cruelly. Not even carelessly, exactly. The route was known, the vehicles were staged, the old map had backups, they had done harder trips with worse gear and come home irritated but fine. Joel had agreed by not objecting. Will had qualified and still packed. Alex had made a joke. The absence had been passed around until it belonged to all of them. Still, Mike was the one with the key in his pocket and the map in his hands, so guilt kept choosing his body to stand in.

"Put it in the notebook," Mike said.

Joel looked at him.

"If something happens," Mike said.

For a second no one moved.

That was the first time Mike had allowed the sentence to reach that shape. Not if we get delayed. Not if the cabin is nothing. If something happens. The plainness of it stripped the morning colder.

For a moment he looked as if he might say more. Beacon, maybe. Sorry, maybe. Some word that would put his hand near the missing thing and make the absence official. Instead he pressed his thumb against the PFD zipper until the fabric dented, and the missing thing remained exactly what it had been since the put-in: a decision no one had made loudly enough to own.

Joel opened the notebook and wrote:

HIGHLANDER KEY - MIKE PFD LEFT ZIP.

He showed the page to both of them.

Will said, "Maybe one of us should carry it."

Mike's hand stayed over the pocket. "No."

"That is not a plan, it's ownership."

"It's my car."

"It's our exit."

Mike turned on him. "You want to do this now?"

Will's face went hard, then tired. "No."

Joel did not say that Will was right. He did not say Mike was not wrong. The key was a practical object first: a cut piece of metal and plastic, the staged vehicle made small enough to lose. It was also Mike's claim on being the man who still held the exit. Saying that aloud would only make both things heavier. Joel wrote instead:

KEY LOCATION KNOWN.

Mike read it and nodded once, as if the page had ruled in his favor.

They took one more pass through camp. Put out what had never been a fire. Tighten the gear tarp. Tuck the food-stained barrel under spruce and fly cloth with the rest of the cache, a poor answer to bears and the only answer they were willing to spend daylight on. Mark the canoe cache on the map by sight: bent cedar, split granite face, three dead spruce in a row. Joel sketched the shape in the notebook because paper could remember without making sound.

The emergency camp looked smaller once packed. Not safer. Just reduced. The root shelf, the bad hammock trees, the stones where the stove had sat, Will's wet footprints pressed dark into the duff. By evening, if rain came, much of it would disappear. By another week the site would be disturbed moss, a few crushed ferns, and whatever they had hidden badly enough to find again.

Will stood at the edge of the shelf, looking uphill. "Where does the trace actually start?"

Mike glanced at the map, then at the slope. "Old access path. Should cut above the inlet, then bend toward the portage."

"Should."

"Yes, Will. Should. As in, not guaranteed by God."

Will's mouth opened. Joel thought he might make some church joke because old reflexes chose bad moments. Then Will closed it.

Joel was grateful and sorry at once. He and Will had grown up in rooms where men spoke certainty from pulpits and boys learned to say amen before they understood the question. Joel had spent half his adult life removing that tone from his own head. Mike had a different version of it, trail-made and brash instead of holy. Under pressure it asked for the same obedience: say the thing firmly enough and let the world be the one that was wrong.

The world did not line up. It lay wet and irregular above them.

They climbed.

The first stretch was not a trail but a direction. Spruce branches slapped water against their sleeves. The ground rose in slick shelves of granite broken by moss. Packs caught on dead limbs. Twice Mike pointed with his whole hand and the others followed because none of them had anything better. Joel kept the lake behind him when he could see it, a gray glint through trunks. When he could not, he used the slope and called that enough. Enough had become a smaller word every day.

No one spoke.

Silence made simple movement clumsy. A branch whipped back because Mike forgot to hold it. Will stopped too abruptly and Joel nearly stepped on the backs of his sandals. Joel wanted to say sorry and did not. Will lifted his hand without turning, two fingers bent back. Fine. Or stop. Or I know. They were inventing a language under duress, and like all inventions made in fear, it broke often.

After ten minutes they found the old path.

It appeared all at once and then, under looking, became almost nothing: a flattening between young spruce, grass in the center, alder leaning inward from both sides. Along one edge a rotten cut stump wore moss over the saw face. Another stood beyond it at the same height. Human repetition. Not enough to save them, but enough to be followed.

Mike stopped at the entrance and looked back.

There it was, the thing Mike wanted: proof. Not rescue. Not yet. But a mark on paper answering a mark on the ground. For a moment his face almost softened.

Will saw it too. He could have taken it from him. The correction was available. A path was not a cabin. A trace was not a road. An old cut stump was not a promise. Joel could see each sentence arranging itself behind Will's eyes, useful and cruel in equal measure.

Will looked down instead and adjusted the strap on his pack.

Mike said, quietly, "Okay."

The word did not belong to certainty. It belonged to relief.

Joel wrote in the notebook while standing:

TRACE REAL.

Then, after a pause:

NOT PROOF OF CABIN.

He showed it to Will first.

Will nodded.

He showed it to Mike.

Mike read it and looked away, but he did not argue.

At the mouth of the trace Mike held out his hand for the cracked paddle. Joel gave it to him. The exchange was practical and still felt like surrender: his broken thing becoming Mike's tool, Mike's tool becoming the point of the line.

They entered single file: Mike first, then Will, then Joel. That order was Mike's without being discussed. He wanted the front because the front gave him decisions. Will went in the middle because someone needed to watch him and because Joel, behind, could see both living men at once. Joel did not know if Mike had thought of that part. He hoped not. He wanted one good decision from the day that had not come from control.

The trace held for fifty yards. Grass dragged cold across Joel's calves above his sandals. Mud took the prints of all three men and filled them slowly with brown water. Once a raven knocked loose from a tree ahead and beat away through branches, black wings loud in the damp air. All three stopped. All three saw it. The bird went on being a bird. The relief of that was humiliating.

Mike lifted one hand without turning. Keep moving.

They did.

The woods were different away from water. At the lake every sound had space to reveal itself, even when it lied about distance. Here sound came already handled. Branches broke close but unseen. Drops fell from high needles and struck leaves at ankle height. Their own packs produced small betrayals: buckle tick, nylon brush, the cracked paddle knocking once against Mike's water bottle until Mike caught it flat with his hand. The road, if it was a road, had brought them into a narrower kind of listening.

The trace bent left around a blowdown and narrowed. Will put one hand back, palm low. Joel stopped. Mike stopped because Will had stopped, and the three of them stood in a line among alder with their breathing loud and useless.

From the brush ahead came Mike's throat-clear.

Not behind Joel. Not from the living man ten yards ahead, turned half-profile in a gap between branches. Ahead of him. Beyond him. The same small preparation of the throat before instruction, before reassurance, before a plan. It was familiar as the stove click before flame. It made a body prepare for the sentence before the mind had voted.

The living Mike did not move.

Joel saw the back of his neck go pale.

Will turned his head by inches until Joel could see one eye behind the smeared lens of his glasses. Question, fear, the old terrible wish to ask if the others had heard.

Joel shook his head once.

No.

The woods waited with them.

Then Mike's voice came from the brush beyond Mike, close enough to seem useful.

"Just keep moving."

The sentence entered the old trace and took up space there. It had Mike's impatience, his route confidence, the clipped end that turned suggestion into order. It was not loud. It did not need to be. It sounded exactly like the thing a competent man would say when the others froze too long, and for that reason it was worse than a shout.

Mike's real hand lowered slowly to his side.

Joel could see him wanting to answer himself. Anger moved through his shoulders. Shame too, maybe. There was something obscene in having your authority borrowed while you were still alive to hear it, like watching someone use your hand to point.

Will's lips parted.

Joel stepped forward and put two fingers against the back of Will's pack.

Will closed his mouth.

For a while nothing happened. Rainwater ticked from alder leaves. A mosquito worked near Joel's ear and he let it whine because swatting made sound and because some part of him now believed even irritation could become material. The false voice did not repeat. Repetition would have shown the machinery. One clean use was worse. It left the sentence standing in the path as if it still expected obedience.

Mike turned at last. His eyes moved from Will to Joel, then down to the notebook in Joel's chest pocket.

Joel took it out. His hands shook enough that the pencil point broke on the first stroke. He turned it and wrote with the splintered wood:

MIKE THROAT CLEAR - AHEAD OF MIKE.

MIKE VOICE - "JUST KEEP MOVING" - NOT MIKE.

He added:

WE SAW MIKE.

Mike read the page. His jaw worked.

Will pointed to the line, then to Mike, then held both hands apart as if measuring a distance. Maybe asking how far. Maybe saying too close. The new language failed him there. He wanted words with edges.

Joel wrote:

SOURCE AHEAD / BODY HERE.

Under it:

NO ANSWER.

Will nodded once, hard.

Mike kept looking at the sentence that denied him. Not Mike. Joel expected him to object, not because it was wrong but because seeing it written made the theft official. Instead Mike touched the PFD pocket where the key sat. The gesture was small and almost childish: the real key, the real pocket, the real body. Then he turned back toward the brush.

He did not say the thing again. He did not say anything.

He lifted one hand, palm open this time, not command exactly. Signal. Then he stepped forward. Standing still had its own dangers. The map's square was still ahead, and even a wrong human mark was still a mark they could test with their eyes. That was all they had left of trust: not belief, but inspection.

Will followed.

Joel looked once over his shoulder. Through the trees he could no longer see the lake or the canoes or the bad camp they had made on the wrong shore. Only spruce, wet alder, the path closing behind them as branches settled back into place.

He put the notebook away and followed the two living bodies he could still see.


# Chapter 15 - The Trace

The trace disappeared three times before it became a road again.

For the first twenty minutes after the false Mike had told them to keep moving, moving stopped meaning progress.

It meant bodies kept in sight, feet placed where the ground would take them, packs watched for stray sounds, every branch that snapped assigned to a branch before the sound could become a voice. Mike led with the cracked paddle in one hand and the old map under his rain jacket. Alex's unused bear spray rode on the front of his PFD, orange and clean in a way nothing else was clean. Will followed with his shoulders drawn up around his ears. Joel came last and watched both of them as if attention were a line he had to hold without letting it show.

The first loss happened so gently that Joel did not know it had happened until Mike stopped. A minute before, the old path had been grass and mud, two shallow wheel ruts holding brown water, alder leaning in from both sides. Then it was only wet brush. No ruts. No cut stumps. No straightness. The woods took back the human mark without drama, as if the mark had never been more than grass pressed briefly in one direction.

Mike lifted one hand.

Will stopped behind him. Joel stopped behind Will. The order still mattered. Mike first because Mike could read a route and wanted decisions where he could touch them. Will in the middle because he was more likely to turn toward sound. Joel last because he could see both living men, and because by then seeing had become the only mercy left.

No one spoke.

Rain from the night before still lived in the branches. It fell one drop at a time onto hoods, pack lids, the backs of their bare calves above the hiking sandals. Mosquitoes had returned with the mildness after the weather. They worked at wrists and ears and the seams where collars had loosened. Joel let one land on the back of his hand and fill itself. Slapping it would make sound. He watched the insect darken with blood and kept his hand still.

Mike crouched and pushed wet grass aside with the cracked paddle Joel had carried from camp. He used it like a staff, like a probe, like one broken thing could still be made useful if a man refused embarrassment. The orange cord on the shaft had darkened in the wet. It touched a rotted stump hidden under fern, then stopped there while Mike leaned closer.

Mike pointed.

The stump was cut flat, or had once been. Moss had rounded the saw face until it looked almost natural. Beyond it another stump stood at the same height, twenty feet off, and beyond that the ground dipped in a shallow line where water had learned to sit. Human repetition. A decision made by hands and tools, almost erased but not erased. The old road was not gone. It had only withdrawn under growth.

Mike looked back.

Will did not give him the satisfaction of a nod. He adjusted the bandage on his hand instead. The gauze had gone slack and dirty around his palm. He kept flexing the fingers, then remembering the cut and making them still. His face had the waxy look of a man running a fever or anger or both, but he stayed in the line.

Joel took the notebook from his chest pocket and wrote while the paper rested against his thigh:

TRACE LOST / FOUND BY STUMPS.

Under it he added:

ALL THREE VISIBLE.

That line had become a prayer stripped of religion. It asked nothing to intervene. It stated what still held, and because it was written it did not add another voice to the woods.

Mike read it and tapped the stump with the paddle. Once. Soft.

Seen.

Then they went on.

The trace took them uphill at an angle that made no clean sense from the map. It might have followed old grade, avoided low marsh, or bent around a place where the road had always been bad. The map showed intent, not water sitting black in a rut for thirty years, not a poplar dropped across both wheel lines and becoming soil, not spruce seedlings growing where tires had once kept the earth open. It did not show the time they had promised themselves they would still have.

Will wanted to say this. Joel could feel the want in him the way he felt heat from a stove. It came off his shoulders. It worked in his jaw. Twice he raised his good hand as if to stop Mike and begin a diagram in the air. Twice he lowered it.

The third time he failed.

"Old logging roads move," Will said.

Mike did not turn.

Will's voice came out low, almost reasonable. That made it more dangerous. "I mean, not move. Obviously. But the map can be right and wrong at the same time. It can mark a legal line, a winter route, a skid road, a thing somebody drove once and then--"

Mike stopped.

Joel watched the back of his neck. The tendon there tightened.

"Write it," Mike said.

Will blinked. "What?"

Mike turned halfway. "You have a theory, put it in the book."

"It's not a theory. It's just--"

Joel held the notebook out.

Will looked at it as if it had insulted him. Then he took it. His bandaged hand made the pencil awkward, and when he shifted the notebook a dark bead pressed through the gauze near his thumb. He used the other hand, pressing too hard.

MAP CAN BE RIGHT AND STILL NOT HELP.

He pushed the notebook against Joel's chest.

The sentence looked better than it had sounded. Writing removed tone and left a tool small enough to carry.

It was the kind of failure Will could bear better than panic: a failure with a hinge, a category, a cause he could put his thumb on. A map could be right and useless. A road could exist and not save them. If the world would give him a flaw with edges, he could still make himself useful by naming it.

Mike read it. His mouth moved once, no sound. Joel thought he might argue with the page. Instead Mike nodded, one hard dip, and turned back to the brush.

They kept moving.

The second loss of the trace came in blowdown.

A wind years old had taken a strip of spruce and laid the trunks in the same direction, root plates standing shoulder-high and black with soil. The old road ran into the mess and did not come out where it should have. Branches had dried into gray nets. New alder filled the gaps. Their packs caught on everything. Sleeping bags bulged under rain covers. The pot in Joel's side pocket knocked against his water bottle until he stopped and tightened the strap with fingers made clumsy by cold, fatigue, and the obligation to know exactly what every sound belonged to.

In ordinary time they would have complained loudly. Alex would have turned it into a bit about premium wilderness experiences, about how people paid good money to crawl under dead trees and lose shin skin. Will would have explained windthrow until someone threatened him. Mike would have said people were soft now. Joel would have let them make noise and would have been privately glad for it.

Now the work happened in fragments of breath and gesture. A point. A palm. Two fingers on a pack strap. Their old talk had filled gaps like this without anyone noticing. Without it, the gaps widened.

Mike pointed under one trunk. Will shook his head and pointed over. Joel looked at the root plate and saw a dark hollow beneath it where water had run, or where an animal had used passage, or where nothing should put a human head. He touched Will's pack and pointed over.

Over, then.

They climbed.

The bark came away wet under Joel's palms. His sandal slipped on a branch polished by rain and his shin hit hard enough to make him see white at the edges. He opened his mouth around a curse and closed it on air. The pain moved up his leg and became heat. He held still until it settled into a shape he could walk with, one more private fact he did not dare make public.

Ahead, Will made a small dry cough.

Joel saw him do it. Saw his shoulders jerk once. Saw the hand rise halfway toward his mouth and stop too late. Will turned back immediately, eyes wide behind smeared glasses.

Sorry, his face said.

No one wrote it down. There was nothing useful in punishing a cough after the body had already betrayed him.

They pushed another twenty yards through the blowdown.

Then the same cough came from ahead of Mike.

Not loud. Not theatrical. It did not echo. It was Will's cough exactly, the small irritated clearing before correction, before a sentence beginning no or actually or that is not how. The living Will stood six feet in front of Joel with one hand on a dead branch and the other pressed against his own mouth as if he could take the sound back into himself. His shoulders were shaking, but he stayed turned toward Joel.

Mike froze.

Joel froze.

The cough did not repeat. It did not need to. One exact sound, placed once, left little for the mind to argue with and much for the body to obey.

The woods resumed its ordinary noises around the absence: water ticking from needles, the faint rub of pack cloth where Joel's weight shifted, one far raven knocking once and then nothing. Ordinary sound had become a set of suspects. Each one had to show its hands.

Will looked at Joel.

Joel shook his head.

No answer.

He took the notebook out. His hands were wet enough that the page softened under his thumb.

WILL COUGH - AHEAD.

WILL SEEN.

Mike read it. His face stayed closed, but Joel saw the arithmetic behind it. If Will could be in one place and Will's voice in another, then Mike's own voice from the trace ahead of him had not been an exception. It had been instruction in a rule none of them wanted.

Will wrote beneath Joel's lines:

IT CAN USE LIVING.

Then, after a pause:

NOT JUST DEAD.

The page looked colder with that on it. Until then, part of Joel had wanted the wrongness to belong to Alex, to death, to the place where the trip had broken. The line took that comfort away.

Mike stared at the words for too long. Then he reached up and wiped rain from the bill of his cap with two fingers.

"We knew that," he said.

His voice sounded too large in the blowdown.

Will flinched. Joel did too.

Mike's jaw set as he heard himself hearing himself.

"Write," Will whispered.

It was barely a sound, but it was a sound. He knew it. Shame went through his face so quickly it would have been easy to miss in any other life.

Joel took the pencil and wrote:

LESS TALK.

He underlined it once. Not as command. As fact. The pencil groove cut through the damp top fibers of the page.

They found the trace again where the blowdown thinned. Mike found it, really. He angled left when the easier ground went right, cut around a wet hollow, and stopped beside a line of stones sunk under moss. The stones had been laid by hand. Maybe to hold a culvert, maybe to harden mud, maybe by men who had never imagined three strangers decades later reading their labor for permission to keep going.

Mike touched the stones with the paddle tip.

There was no triumph in the gesture now. Only use.

The trail rose. The woods changed by degrees. Black spruce gave way in patches to jack pine and young birch. The ground under the moss became sandier, then wet again. Grass grew in the center of the old grade. In places the ruts were clear enough that Joel could imagine tires: a pickup, a maintenance truck, a ranger's vehicle with the door hinge squealing and a man stepping out to clear brush from a sign. Imagination had become suspect too. It could give the woods shapes it had not earned.

At midday, or what Joel guessed was midday from the pale place above the clouds, they stopped beside a granite knob with enough open surface to sit without soaking through immediately. The old road passed below them in two wet suggestions, almost visible from that angle and therefore almost reassuring.

No one wanted to stop. All three needed to.

Mike checked his watch. The face was scratched and fogged at the edge. His thumb stayed on the rim too long. He checked the compass next, then the map in its cloudy case. He did it with the care of someone handling evidence after being accused. Joel could see him choosing not to say the time.

Will sat on the rock and peeled the dirty bandage from his palm. The cut had opened. Not badly, but enough. Blood made a bright line in the wet crease below his thumb. He looked at it with irritation, as if his hand were making a poor argument.

Joel crouched by him and took out the first-aid kit.

Will let him clean it. That was how tired he was.

Alcohol made Will's fingers jerk.

"Sorry," Joel said, before he could stop himself.

The word hung there.

It was small. Human. Useless. It had crossed the air and become available.

Joel felt Mike look at him.

Will looked at the ground. "Seen," he said, too softly.

Joel nodded. He wrote anyway:

JOEL SAID SORRY - TO WILL HAND.

It felt foolish to inventory kindness as if it were a dropped wrapper. It felt worse not to. The notebook had become a ledger of what they had given away.

Will read the line while Joel wrapped the new gauze. He did not smile. He touched two fingers to the clean edge of the bandage, then to the page.

Seen.

They ate bars because the body would not negotiate forever. Mike divided them by sight on the rock: three damp rectangles, two with chocolate gone gray at the edges, one oat thing that Alex would have refused with a face elaborate enough to make a meal out of it.

Joel saw the empty fourth share before anyone made it.

There was no fourth share. There had not been since Second Camp. Still, his hand paused with the wrapper half open.

Will saw him pause and looked away.

Mike chewed without looking at either of them. His beard held crumbs. He was still wearing the PFD over his rain jacket, orange shoulders dulled by mud, straps dark with wet. On land it made him look both prepared and absurd, like a man who had misunderstood the category of danger. But the pockets mattered. The Highlander key mattered. North Road Bridge mattered somewhere past water, road, time, and whatever had learned to speak with them. The vest made Mike ridiculous; the key made him necessary.

Mike's hand went to the left zip pocket.

Joel saw the gesture before Mike knew he had made it. So did Will.

Will raised his eyebrows.

Mike caught him and lowered his hand. For one second the old answer formed in his face. Some blunt defense. Some managing sentence.

He said it anyway.

"Truck doesn't help if we lose the key."

It was a reasonable sentence. It was even necessary. Spoken aloud, it became material.

The three of them listened to the woods after he said it. Even practical sentences had an afterlife now.

Nothing answered.

Joel wrote:

MIKE: TRUCK DOESN'T HELP IF WE LOSE THE KEY.

Then, because the practical mattered more than the fear:

KEY STILL MIKE PFD LEFT ZIP.

Will read it and nodded.

Mike read it too. "Good."

His voice was low. Tired. Almost grateful. Then he seemed to hear the softness in himself and closed down again.

Will was still looking at the pocket. Not accusing. Worse than accusing. Waiting.

Mike looked past him into the brush. "I know what I said about the beacon."

The sentence landed harder than the key line. It did not excuse anything. It only opened a place in the day where the absent object stood: the small box they had not carried because the route was known, because the vehicles were staged, because men who had come home before expected to come home again. Mike had not forbidden it. That would have been cleaner. He had made it sound unnecessary until unnecessary became the group decision.

Will's good hand twitched toward the notebook.

Mike shook his head once. "Don't."

Joel wrote anyway, smaller:

MIKE: BEACON.

He did not write apology. Mike had not offered one, exactly. He had put the missing object on the table between them and let it stand there without a use. It was the closest Mike had come to saying he was afraid of his own judgment.

They rested five minutes more. Maybe seven. Time had become distance, light, water, breath. Joel's watch still worked, but he had stopped trusting numbers to mean what they had meant before Alex died. A mile on a map was not a mile in alder. Noon did not promise afternoon. A cabin on paper did not promise shelter.

Will took the notebook and wrote:

DISTANCE DOESN'T MEAN TIME.

Then, under it:

TIME DOESN'T MEAN SAFE.

He stared at what he had written and gave Joel the pencil without comment.

Mike folded the map and stood.

The third loss came after the old road dipped into cedar.

It was darker there though the day had not darkened. Cedar branches held the wet close and low. The ground turned soft enough to take the whole shape of their sandals and then drink the prints back in. Each step made a quiet sucking sound. Their packs brushed both sides of the corridor. The trace, if it was still a trace, narrowed to discomfort and refusal. The air smelled of cedar rot and cold water.

Mike slowed. Then stopped.

Will almost walked into him.

Ahead, the grade split around a mound of deadfall. Left looked lower and wetter. Right looked higher and wrong. Straight was impossible. The map did not acknowledge choice at that scale. It offered a line. The ground offered a place to be mistaken.

Mike took out the compass.

Will leaned to look and stopped himself halfway. His restraint looked painful.

Joel watched Mike align the compass with the map case against his forearm. Mike was good at it. Even exhausted, even afraid, his hands knew the work. He sighted past two spruce trunks, checked the slope, checked the light, checked something in the ground Joel could not see. Competence again. The terrible comfort of it. The fact that it had not saved them did not make Joel want it less. Alex's spray tapped once against the zipper of Mike's PFD as he leaned, a small hard sound Joel saw before he could mistrust it.

Will could not stand it.

"Contour says higher," he said.

Mike closed his eyes.

Will pressed on, softer but still speaking. "I'm not being an asshole. The old road's probably staying out of that wet. If there was a culvert, it's gone. If the line bends, it bends around drainage. That's all I'm--"

"Will."

"No, I'm trying to help."

"Then help quietly."

"Quietly doesn't change the contour interval."

Mike turned.

There was anger in him, but the larger thing was shame. Joel saw it and did not want to. Mike heard every correction now as proof that he had brought them here wrong. The map, the route, the missing beacon, the cabin square, Alex under stones back at Second Camp: it had begun to gather around him. Will was not wrong, and Will was trying to help. That was part of the cruelty.

Joel put the notebook between them.

Will looked at it, then at Mike. His mouth made a hard line. He wrote:

HIGHER PROBABLY BETTER.

Mike read it.

"Probably," he said.

Will snatched the notebook back and added:

NOT CERTAIN.

Mike gave one breath through his nose. Not quite a laugh. "That's new for you."

Will's face flickered. Under other weather that might have become something. A jab returned. A little pressure released. Here it collapsed almost at once.

From the left, through the wet cedar, Mike's voice said, "It opens up after this."

All three men went still.

The living Mike stood in front of Joel, visible in profile, map case in one hand, compass in the other. His mouth was closed. His eyes had gone flat with shock.

The false voice had come from the lower, wetter option, the one Mike had not yet chosen and the one Will had almost argued them away from.

It had Mike's clipped certainty, the old impatience with hesitation, the practical shove toward motion. It did not sound malicious. That was what made Joel's legs want to obey it. It sounded, worst of all, useful. It sounded like relief arriving from the wrong ground.

Will's good hand rose. Not to his ears this time. To his mouth.

Joel stepped close enough to put two fingers against Will's pack.

Will closed his eyes.

The woods waited.

Then, from behind them, Alex laughed.

Bright, exact, wrong.

It came from the trace they had already walked, from the direction of the canoes and Emergency Camp and the body they had left farther back than that. Not the little laugh the thing had used at night, but the hard bark Joel remembered from the motel parking lot, from the truck, from Alex making himself the center of any room too quiet to resist him. It had no grief in it. The grief belonged to the men who heard it.

Will bent forward as if someone had hit him in the stomach, but he did not turn.

Mike's hands tightened around map and compass.

Joel looked behind them because not looking felt like superstition. There was only the cedar corridor and their own disturbed passage through it. Leaves still moved where packs had brushed them. Water fell from one branch to another. Nothing with Alex's face stood there. Nothing with any face.

He wrote:

MIKE VOICE LEFT / LOWER ROUTE.

ALEX LAUGH BEHIND.

ALL THREE SEEN.

NO ANSWER.

SPRAY TAP SEEN - MIKE PFD.

Will read it with a concentration almost violent. Mike read it too.

Mike pointed right. Higher.

Will nodded.

They took the higher side.

For twenty minutes the old trace was nothing but inference. Mike led them along faint grade, then lost it, then found a line of old blazes so grown over they were only scars under bark. One cedar had a rusted nail in it, head swollen with orange. Human evidence, small and mean. Joel touched it as he passed, then regretted touching anything. Rust flaked onto his fingertip. He wiped it on his pants and kept going.

The higher way did open, eventually.

No one said so. Saying it would have made the false voice right in a way none of them could bear.

The cedar thinned into mixed spruce and birch. The ground firmed. A faint roadbed reappeared, broad enough now that they could walk not quite single file. Light entered from the west in a colorless wash. The afternoon had advanced without permission. Shadows were longer. Or Joel was imagining shadows because the idea of dusk had entered him and begun to work.

Mike did not look back for credit. That made it harder to refuse him credit. He had chosen the higher ground. The false voice had chosen lower. Skill still mattered. The same confidence that had narrowed the world badly could still find a road inside brush.

They crossed a shallow ditch where a culvert had collapsed. Black water moved under broken metal. The culvert pipe was rusted through in two places, its edges thin as torn paper. Mike stopped there and looked at it with a strange expression.

"Road," he said.

The word came out before he caught it.

It was not triumph. It was need: a man asking the world to acknowledge one thing he had read correctly.

Will opened his mouth.

Joel could see the correction waiting there. This is not a road. This was a road. There is a difference. The phrases had weight. They wanted out.

Will swallowed them.

He wrote instead:

ROAD WAS REAL.

Then he handed the notebook to Joel.

Joel added:

WAS DOES NOT HELP YET.

Mike read both lines and said nothing.

They stepped over the culvert one at a time. On the far side, Joel's left sandal sank to the ankle in black muck. Cold water went under the strap and between his toes. He pulled free with a wet pop too close to the old sounds from beaver mud and portage muck and Alex swearing theatrically about losing footwear. The memory rose whole and stupid.

From the birch to Joel's right, Alex said, "You coming or writing a poem?"

Joel stopped.

The line was aimed at him. He knew it before he understood how he knew. It had Alex's teasing lift, the affectionate cruelty of being caught observing instead of moving, the exact little push he used when Joel lingered over light on water or the arrangement of camp as if attention itself were a vanity.

For an instant Joel was not on the old fire road. He was back on Day 1, with a canoe to unload and Alex turning impatience into theater, alive enough to be irritating, alive enough that Joel could resent him without consequence. That was the cruelty of the line. It returned Alex not as a ghost but as an inconvenience, the living man made whole for half a second by annoyance.

The voice came again, closer by a few yards, or seeming closer because grief moved distance.

"You coming or writing a poem?"

Will turned toward Joel, not toward the sound.

That saved him. Or helped save him. It was the first time Joel saw Will choose the living witness over the voice, and the choice cost him. It showed in the hard set of his mouth.

Mike was ahead and had not turned all the way around. He watched Joel from the corner of his eye, giving him the dignity of not making too much of it.

Joel's throat worked. It wanted Alex's name. It wanted an apology for every private irritation, every time Joel had wished the man would be quieter, every small judgment now made grotesque by absence. It wanted to make grief into language because language was what men had in place of touch when they did not know how to touch. The woods had learned their jokes. Joel would not teach it their remorse if he could help it.

He put the pencil on the page instead.

ALEX: POEM LINE - RIGHT.

TARGET JOEL.

He stopped. The pencil point pressed a dark spot into the paper.

Then he wrote:

DO NOT GIVE IT GRIEF.

That one he did not show them at first. It seemed too private and too general, a rule and a confession.

Will reached for the notebook gently, which was so unlike him that Joel let it go. He read the line. His face changed. He passed it to Mike.

Mike read it and looked back into the birch. His face did not soften, but something in his grip on the map did.

The false Alex did not speak again.

"Move," Mike said.

The word was too useful to punish. They moved.

After that, hope came into the day in small practical increments, which made it harder to distrust.

The roadbed widened by inches. Grass grew waist-high in the middle, wet seed heads dragging across their thighs. The old ruts became visible again, shallow but continuous. Here and there the brush on both sides looked cut back by some more recent force than the original road: an animal trail, storm damage, maybe men five years ago or ten. The trees no longer pressed so closely against their faces. Air moved.

Joel did not trust the relief, but his body took it anyway. His shoulders lowered. His stride lengthened. Will lifted his head. Even Mike's pace changed, not faster exactly, but cleaner. The world had begun to resemble the marks on paper, and the human mind was weak before resemblance.

They passed a rusted can half sunk in moss. Then another. A strip of black rubber curled beside a stump. Old flagging tape hung from a branch in pale pink tatters, so faded it was almost the color of skin.

Will pointed to the tape.

Mike nodded.

No one said recent. No one said not recent. The strip moved once in a draft Joel could not feel, then hung still.

The grade bent around a stand of spruce. Joel smelled old ash before he saw anything. Not smoke. Nothing warm. Just the mineral ghost of burned wood under damp rot, the way a fire ring smelled after rain.

Mike stopped so suddenly Will had to put a hand out against his pack.

Ahead, through trunks, a hard dark line cut across the vertical woods.

Roof.

For one minute the whole day changed.

Joel knew better. A roof was not help. A square was not rescue. A cabin was not a radio, a road was not a truck, and a sound was not a man. He knew all of it with the dry exactness of the notebook in his pocket. Still, the sight entered him below knowledge. His body saw a made thing in the wet and believed, briefly, in the species that had made it.

Will moved faster.

Mike lifted a hand, warning, but he moved faster too.

The trees opened onto a small clearing. Grass grew high and yellow-green around rusted cans, a collapsed sawhorse, and a pile of cut rounds gone soft with fungus.
