The cabin sat at the far side, square and deliberate and failing. Its roof sagged toward the back. One shutter still hung beside a dark window. Another window was boarded from inside with mismatched planks. The door stood open three inches, not welcoming, just not latched. No smoke. No antenna. No solar panel. No fresh axe chips at the woodpile, no recent boot line through the grass that Joel could see. Beyond the cabin, an opening in the trees suggested where the old road had once continued and did not promise that it still did.

Mike stopped at the edge of the clearing and looked back at them.

Something like vindication crossed his face before exhaustion pulled it down. He had not imagined the square. He had not led them to nothing. That mattered, even if it did not matter enough. Joel hated the part of himself that was relieved on Mike's behalf.

Will was too tired to deny him.

Joel took out the notebook.

CABIN REAL.

He paused, then added:

NOT HELP UNTIL SEEN INSIDE.

Mike read it. His mouth twisted.

"You are a joy," he said.

His voice was hoarse enough that the joke almost disappeared.

Will read the line and gave Joel a look that might have been gratitude or simple agreement. His glasses were fogged at the edges. Mud had dried in a streak along his cheek where he had wiped at a mosquito bite with a dirty wrist.

They stood listening.

The clearing held too much quiet. Not true silence. The grass ticked against itself. Water dripped from the roof at the back corner into something metal. A fly worried at the open door and made a small hard sound against wood. After the close brush, the open space felt staged. It gave sound room to arrive and three men nowhere to hide.

Mike pointed to himself, then the cabin, then to Joel and Will: stay together.

Joel shook his head and pointed all three fingers toward the door.

All three.

Mike stared at him.

Joel held the stare.

At last Mike nodded.

They crossed the clearing in a loose line, no more than arm's length apart. Grass soaked Joel's thighs. One rusted can crushed under Mike's sandal with a faint metallic complaint and all three stopped until they identified it. Seen. Can. Mike lifted his foot. The can had flattened into the mud, its orange label long since weathered to blank.

At the door, the smell strengthened: mouse droppings, wet wood, old ash, mildew, and something mineral under the rot, as if the cabin had been slowly becoming soil from the inside.

"Careful," Joel said.

Mike almost smiled. "Now we're careful?"

Nobody answered.

He pushed the door with the cracked paddle, not his hand.

The hinges gave a long groan.

The sound moved through the clearing and came back from the trees too late, thinner at the edges.

No one stepped in until the echo or answer or imitation had died.

Mike went first because he still went first. Will followed with his bandaged hand held close against his chest. Joel entered last and stopped just inside, keeping the door, the two men, and as much of the room as possible in sight.

Inside, light came through roof gaps in thin white pieces. Dust and damp had made a skin over everything. There was a table built from rough boards, two broken chairs, a rusted stove pipe tilted away from a small black stove, an empty shelf, a coil of wire too corroded to trust, a mouse nest in the corner made of paper and insulation. A black pot sat under one leak with water in it and no sign of use except gravity. On the empty shelf, a clean rectangle in the dust showed where something had once sat and been removed years ago or yesterday or by a hand no longer useful to imagine. Names had been cut into one wall by people who had believed, at least while carving, that a mark could hold them in a place.

RAY 1998.

STUMP CREW 04.

KELLY.

Three initials inside a heart, the heart cut poorly and much deeper than the letters.

Will laughed once.

Not humor. Exhaustion.

"Okay," he said. "Cabin."

The word was too large and too small.

Mike stood in the middle of the room with the map in his hand. For a moment Joel saw what he wanted: a radio on the shelf, a ranger's log, a road chart, a can of fuel, anything with current use clinging to it. Instead there was the old square made actual around them and already failing. The roof existed. The walls existed. The world had met the map only far enough to make the map's failure personal.

Outside, from the far side of the clearing, Mike's voice said, "Map says there's a cabin."


# Chapter 16 - The Cabin

No one moved toward the voice.

For a few seconds that counted as discipline. Then Joel realized none of them had breathed.

The false Mike did not repeat itself. It had come from outside the cabin, from the far side of the clearing or the old road beyond it, and then it left the sentence hanging in the room. Map says there's a cabin. The words were ordinary. They were not even wrong. The cabin was here. Mike had brought them to the thing he had promised, and the promise had failed by being true.

The sentence still worked. It named the square on the map and the roof over their heads. It did what a useful sentence was supposed to do, except it had arrived from a mouth no one could see. The usefulness was the violation.

Rainwater dripped through the roof into a black pot on the floor. One drop, then another, each one making a thin metallic note. Somewhere in the wall a mouse worked at paper. The real Mike stood with the old map half folded in both hands, looking more exposed than Joel had ever seen him. In the motel, at the put-in, on every bad landing since, he had carried certainty like gear: irritating, useful, always strapped where he could reach it. Here it hung loose on him. His shoulders had not changed, the same broad wet orange of his PFD, the same set jaw, but the room had taken the use out of the posture.

The room accepted his competence and refused the conclusion he needed.

Will had one hand against the doorframe. His bandaged palm had bled through again, not dramatically, just enough red stippling to show through the dirty gauze. He looked at Mike, then at the opening behind Joel, then at Mike again.

"It heard me say that," Mike said.

His voice was low. Not denial. Inventory.

Will nodded too fast. "Yes."

"At the gas station."

"Yes."

"Or on the second day."

"Yes."

"Stop saying yes."

Will's face hardened. The old Will would have met that tone with something sharp enough to make the others laugh and make Mike mad. This Will only looked at him with blood in his gauze and mud dried in the lines beside his mouth. "I'm agreeing with you, you miserable--"

Joel lifted a hand between them.

The argument died because the cabin was too small to hold it. It had room for three men, three packs, one failed hope, and the sound of water collecting in a pot. There was no room for the kind of argument they used to have in a kitchen or on a gravel landing, the kind that could flare, spend itself, and leave everyone still alive.

Joel took out the notebook. The pages had begun to swell at the corners. The pencil dragged over damp paper.

FALSE MIKE: MAP SAYS THERE'S A CABIN.

He paused, then added:

REAL MIKE SEEN INSIDE.

Mike read it. His eyes stayed on the last line longer than the first. Joel thought of the motel room, of Mike standing over the maps with his throat-clear and certainty, turning a wilderness into lines and timing. It was not that Mike had been useless. That would have been easier. He had read water. He had found lost trail. He had kept them moving when stopping would have become another form of collapse. His competence had brought them here, and here was the worst kind of nothing: a thing that existed.

There were walls. There was a roof with holes in it. There had been a stove and a shelf and men with knives cutting names into soft pine. The map had not lied enough to save them from trusting it. A bad plan could still have skill inside it.

Will looked around the cabin as if the room itself might now be shamed into producing a radio.

"Okay," he said. "So we search."

No one argued.

They searched because not searching would have been another kind of answer. Mike folded the map and put it in the cloudy case with unnecessary care. Joel saw his fingers not quite work on the snap. Cold, exhaustion, anger. The snap closed on the second try.

They began with the obvious places.

There was a shelf above the little stove. On it sat a rusted coffee can with no lid, three mouse-chewed packets of something that had once been instant soup, a stub of candle gnawed down to gray wax, and a spool of picture wire gone green at the core. Will tipped the can with the back of his knuckle. Mouse droppings slid out, small and black and dry. He made a face but did not speak.

The stove was a square iron thing on four short legs, its door warped open a finger's width. Joel crouched and swung it carefully with the cracked paddle. A nest had been built inside from shredded paper, pink insulation, and needles. No ashes fresher than old gray dust. No matches. No dry kindling. A spider moved from the door hinge into shadow.

Under the table they found a plastic jug collapsed in on itself, a bent fork, an oil-stiff rag, two bottle caps, and a cigarette lighter with the wheel fused in place. Mike tried it anyway. Once. Twice. The tiny scrape of flintless metal sounded obscene in the room.

Will flinched at the second scrape.

Mike saw him. "It's me."

Will held up his good hand. I know.

Joel wrote:

LIGHTER DEAD.

It looked stupidly formal on the page, as if they could report the cabin for failing.

The names cut into the wall drew Joel's eye again. RAY 1998. STUMP CREW 04. KELLY. A heart carved too deep around three initials. Men and women had stood here with knives and time, believing a mark could hold them in a place. Or not believing it, only being young enough to cut wood because the wood was there. Joel imagined them entering wet and laughing, shaking rain from jackets, setting coffee on the table, complaining about blackflies and bad pay and somebody else's chainsaw work. The cabin had held their noise once. Now it held the three of them and gave the woods four thin walls to throw sound against.

Will moved to the back corner, where a warped plywood box lay under a fallen strip of ceiling. He pulled the strip aside with his foot. The plywood had swollen and delaminated, its layers opening like pages ruined by water.

"Could be a logbook," he said.

Mike looked at him.

Will's jaw tightened. "I'm not answering anything. I'm telling you what I'm seeing."

Joel almost said that telling was answering if there was something outside to hear. He did not. There were too many rules now and too little mercy in enforcing them all.

Will pried at the box. His cut hand made him clumsy. Joel crossed the room to help, and for a moment they worked shoulder to shoulder as they had in another life: a stuck cooler latch, a tire chain, a tent pole sleeve in rain. Will smelled of wet wool, old sweat, iodine, and the sourness of fear kept too long in the body. The lid came up with a soft tearing sound.

Inside were papers, but not a logbook. Permits, maybe. Carbon forms. A map brittle with mildew and eaten through at the folds. A printed sheet of radio frequencies so old the paper had browned at the edges. Columns of numbers. Call signs. The ghost of contact.

On the wall above the box, two screws held an empty metal bracket. A pale rectangle showed where something had hung long enough to keep smoke and dust away from the boards. A wire ran down from it, cut close to the floor and green at the exposed end.

Mike touched the bracket with two fingers.

Joel saw the hope enter him and leave almost in the same motion.

A little vinyl pouch in the box held three batteries furred white at the terminals, their ends swollen and split. The pouch had once been clear. Now it was cloudy enough to make the dead batteries look underwater.

Mike took the sheet from Will.

For one second Joel saw the old current enter him. Numbers meant systems. Systems meant someone on the other end. Someone had once written this because communication had existed here: this wet square, another room, a road, a desk, an agency that had once cared enough to nail a cabin onto the map. The sheet had the shape of rescue without rescue inside it. The empty bracket had the shape too. That was worse than finding nothing; it let the room show exactly what had once been possible.

Then Mike turned the page over.

The back was blank except for mouse stains.

"No radio," Will said.

Mike did not answer.

Joel wrote:

OLD FREQ SHEET. EMPTY RADIO BRACKET. DEAD BATTERIES.

Will pointed at the map, or what remained of it. Joel laid it on the table. The paper came apart where his damp fingers touched the fold. It showed the old road in a thicker line than Mike's map did, a confident slash through contour and marsh, then a spur toward the cabin, then another line continuing north and east. The line had been printed black and clean, as if ink could keep trees from growing through it.

At the edge of the sheet, where the continuation mattered, the paper had gone soft and black. The road left the map into rot. The missing corner seemed almost deliberate until Joel made himself reject that. Water had done it. Mice had done it. Time had done it. The woods did not need intention to take away the useful part.

Will stared at it.

Joel knew what he wanted to say. The road existed here. The road might not exist there. There is a difference. Will's mouth worked around the distinction, the whole machinery of him straining toward speech because speech was how he made fear take shape.

He took the notebook instead.

ROAD ON OLD MAP CONTINUES.

Then beneath it, after a long pause:

PAPER FAILS WHERE NEEDED.

He pushed the notebook to Joel as if angry at the sentence for being true.

Mike read it. A muscle moved once in his cheek.

"We don't know it fails outside," he said.

Joel waited.

Mike heard himself. The sentence had crossed the room and become material. His hand closed around the frequency sheet until it buckled.

Will stared at the sheet in Mike's fist. "You said that about the route."

Mike looked at him.

Will's mouth opened, then shut. He had not meant to say it aloud, or he had meant it and hated the mouth that gave it away. Either way the sentence was loose now. Joel saw Mike take it. Not as insult only. As record.

Mike said, "I know."

The words were almost too quiet to be useful and too late to help.

Will looked away first, which was almost kindness.

They finished the search with the same thoroughness they had brought to every camp chore because thoroughness was what was left. Joel checked behind the stove and under the table. Will lifted the broken chair and found only mouse pellets and a bottle cap. Mike worked along the back wall, testing loose boards with the paddle, as if a maintenance crew might have hidden a working hand radio under a panel chewed by mice.

The cabin gave up nothing current.

No radio. No working batteries. No first-aid kit. No flare. No tarp worth taking. No tool but a rusted file with the handle missing. No rope that would hold weight. No food. No recent tracks in the dirt floor beyond theirs. No ash new enough to hold shape. People had once been here and left only proof of leaving.

Outside, water dripped from the roof at the back corner into something metal. The same note as the pot inside, but lower. Two drips. Two little bells. Joel watched Mike hear it and look toward the wall.

Not now, Joel thought. Don't let even water become another message.

Mike stepped to the broken window and peered through the gap where the board had shrunk away from the frame. Late afternoon light sat low in the clearing. The grass had gone a flat green, each blade holding water. The old road beyond the cabin was a darker opening between young spruce, not a path so much as the idea of one. Joel could see Mike measuring it already. Not out of optimism. Out of the same hard practical insistence that made him test a dead lighter twice.

If the road was gone, then the cabin had failed. If the road still held for any distance, the day might not yet be a waste, or at least not the kind of waste that had to be admitted while light remained. Joel understood the trap because it was not only Mike's. The room had nothing. The road might. That was enough.

"We need to see the road," Mike said.

He did not say it like hope. That made it harder to refuse. The road was not rescue yet. It was a fact still outside the room, and facts had to be checked before they could be hated.

Will laughed once.

It came out wrong. Not humor. The body making a sound because the mind had found no useful action.

Mike turned on him.

"What?"

Will rubbed his forehead with the heel of his good hand. "Nothing."

"Say it."

"No."

"You already made the noise."

"I said no."

The word sat between them. Small. Dangerous.

Joel wrote:

CHECK ROAD BY SIGHT ONLY. ALL THREE.

He held the notebook up before the argument could collect itself.

Mike read it and shook his head. "Will's hand is bleeding."

Will's face tightened. "My hand is attached."

"Someone stays here with packs."

Joel wrote over the empty lower half of the page, larger:

ALL THREE.

Mike stared at the words. "This isn't a vote."

Will said, "Good, because you're losing."

It would have been funny two days ago. Maybe even yesterday morning, if anything could have been funny then. Now the old rhythm surfaced, recognizable and useless. It could not carry them.

Mike opened his mouth, then closed it. He looked at the door, the clearing, the old road. He looked at the map case under his arm. Joel could see the argument happening in him: speed against rule, control against evidence, the contempt he had always had for committee decisions against the fact that every voice had become suspect except the ones attached to faces. Checking the road was not only stubbornness. It was the next practical question. It was also Mike trying to keep the failed cabin from becoming the final verdict on him.

At last he pointed to himself, then to the road, then held up three fingers.

All three.

They left the packs inside except for water, Alex's bear spray, the notebook, Mike's map, and the small first-aid kit. Leaving gear in the cabin felt wrong. Carrying everything felt worse. The choice had no good shape. Joel watched Mike set his pack against the wall and then touch the left zip pocket of his PFD where the yellow Highlander-key pouch sat. The gesture had become involuntary now, a little proof he could perform with his hand.

Joel wrote:

KEY STILL MIKE PFD LEFT ZIP.

Mike saw it and gave him a look.

"I know where my key is."

Joel did not answer. The page answered enough.

Outside, the clearing received them without change. No voice. No branch crack. No sudden movement at the edge. Grass dragged wet over Joel's shins. A blackfly worried at his ear, and he let it. The cabin behind them looked smaller from ten steps away, more like an abandoned object than a place. Its open door made a dark vertical shape in the wall. Their packs sat just inside, visible if he turned his head, which he did every few seconds.

Mike led, but slower than usual. Will walked in the middle with his injured hand tucked against his chest. Joel stayed close enough to touch Will's pack, close enough to see Mike's shoulder, close enough to see the cabin door behind them when the road bent only a little.

The old road north of the clearing had once been real. There was comfort in that and insult in it. Two shallow ditches ran on either side. A crown rose in the middle under moss and grass. Here and there gravel showed through, rounded and sunk deep. A rusted culvert showed one torn lip under fern where water had chewed the shoulder out. Then the young spruce began.

The road had not ended at a line. It had thinned into trees, which was worse. A clean ending could be obeyed. This asked to be argued with.

Spruce had grown in the roadbed itself, not beside it. Knee-high first, then waist-high, then taller than Joel. Poplar saplings leaned across at face level. A jack pine had fallen lengthwise along one rut and become a nursery for moss. No tire had passed here in years. No four-wheeler. No maintenance crew. No human traffic heavy enough to keep light on the ground. If there had ever been a way to drive to the cabin, it had become a memory the woods used for other work.

Mike stopped at the first wall of spruce and pushed through with the paddle. Branches bent and sprang back wet against his sleeve. Needles stuck to the orange fabric of his PFD.

"It continues," he said.

Joel looked at the trunk in the roadbed, the moss over old gravel, the darkening sky beyond the branches. Continuing was not the same as carrying them. He had begun to hate how many things could be true without being help.

"Not today," he said.

Mike turned.

Joel regretted the sound as soon as he made it, but the words were not theory. They were weather and daylight and bodies. He took the notebook and wrote:

ROAD REAL / NOT PASSABLE BEFORE DARK.

Mike read it, then looked past the page as if distance might change out of embarrassment.

"You don't know that."

Joel wrote:

YOUNG TREES IN ROAD. LIGHT DROPPING. WILL HAND. NO SCOUTING.

Will leaned in to read. "Concise."

The word was too close to a joke. Will winced after it, as if even that small shape of himself had escaped without permission.

From the spruce ahead, Mike's throat cleared.

Not words. Just the small rough sound Mike made before turning uncertainty into decision. It came from ten yards beyond the living Mike, from the road that could not be read past the young trees.

The living Mike stood beside Joel, close enough that Joel could see rain caught in his beard and a pale crescent of old sunscreen at the edge of one ear. His throat had not moved. The sound had his habit without his body.

Will's eyes went wide behind his smeared glasses.

Then, from ahead, in Mike's voice, low and practical: "Just keep moving."

The phrase entered Joel's body before thought. It was an old instruction, useful on portages, useful in rain, useful when someone wanted to sit down in a place that would only make standing harder later. It had gotten them across wind and mud and beaver work. It was one of Mike's better tools. The woods had taken the tool without learning the hand.

Mike took one step forward.

Joel caught the back strap of his PFD.

Mike swung around with real fury. His hand came up, not to strike, but hard enough that Will shifted back.

Joel held on.

He shook his head once.

Mike's face went red under the dirt. For a second Joel thought he would rip free out of pride alone. Then the false throat-clear came again from ahead. Softer this time. Almost thoughtful.

Mike heard it. Hearing himself stopped him more completely than Joel's hand had.

Joel let go.

He wrote:

FALSE MIKE AHEAD: JUST KEEP MOVING.

REAL MIKE SEEN.

DO NOT FOLLOW MIKE WITHOUT SEEING MIKE.

Mike read it. The last line looked absurd and necessary. A child's rule written for grown men: a man is not his voice.

Will added beneath it:

MIKE VOICE NOT MIKE.

He pressed the pencil so hard the point tore the damp page.

Mike read that and looked at Will with something worse than anger. Hurt, maybe. Shame with its skin off.

Will looked like he wanted to take the sentence back. The page would not give it back.

They retreated to the clearing.

No one said retreat. Mike walked first because the path was too narrow to rearrange without ceremony. Joel watched his back. The PFD made him broad and orange, dulled by mud, ridiculous on land and indispensable because of a pouch in one pocket. Beyond the PFD, beyond the old road and cabin failure, was the Highlander at North Road Bridge, ordinary and locked, windshield probably filmed with road dust, stale coffee smell trapped in the upholstery. The thought of it hurt. Help had become not a voice on a radio but a hard pouch under wet orange fabric.

At the cabin door, Will stopped.

"Packs," he said.

Joel looked in. The packs were where they had left them. Mike's blue pack slumped against the wall. Will's green one beside it. Joel's gray one near the table. They had not moved. Nothing had opened them. Nothing had dragged them into the clearing. That should have comforted him more than it did.

From inside the cabin came a dry bag buckle click.

Once.

Then again.

All three men stood at the threshold and looked at the packs.

No buckle moved. The sound had come from inside, or from under the floor, or from the back wall, or from memory using the room's stale air. Joel's eyes went to Mike's pack. A black buckle hung loose from the top roll, motionless. Will's sleeping bag stuff sack bulged under its straps. Joel's own side pouch was open where he had taken the first-aid kit out and not closed it properly.

The click came again, this time from behind the cabin.

Mike's hand went to his PFD pocket.

Joel saw the thought arrive. Gear. Key. Pouch. Something misplaced. Something critical. The whole trip had trained them to answer such sounds with action. A buckle meant a bag not sealed. A latch meant food exposed. A shift in gear meant water inside, something lost, something that would matter later.

Mike pressed the pocket flat. The hard pouch was there. He closed his hand over it through the fabric.

"It's on me," he said.

Joel wrote:

YELLOW KEY POUCH FELT THROUGH POCKET.

Will read it and nodded. He had begun to tremble, not violently, only a fine shaking in his shoulders that might have been cold or rage or the effort of swallowing every sentence that tried to rise.

Mike stepped into the cabin and grabbed his pack. Too fast.

Joel put a hand on his arm.

Mike looked down at the hand.

"Don't," Joel said.

The word came out before he could make it paper. It was small, but the cabin took it. The room received the sound as if sound were weather, as if every syllable had weight and could soak into wood.

Joel released him and wrote:

JOEL SAID DON'T - TO MIKE.

The inventory had become humiliating. They were cataloging not only mistakes now but care. Every small human reflex had to be laid out like contraband.

Mike watched him write. Some of the anger went out of his face and left only fatigue.

"I know," he said.

Then, because he knew, he added nothing.

They checked the packs by sight. Buckles closed. Roll-tops tight enough. Food not there; food had been cached with the boats except for the small ration bag in Mike's pack. Water bottles half full. Map case present. First-aid kit on the table. Alex's full bear spray with Mike. Yellow key pouch still in Mike's left PFD pocket. Alex's absence was everywhere, even here, in the way no one had a fourth pack to count, no fourth complaint about the smell, no one making the dead cabin into a bit about rustic accommodations.

Will leaned against the table and closed his eyes.

"We stay?" he asked.

It was a real question. The kind they could not avoid.

Joel looked up at the roof. Three holes showed pale sky through blackened boards and moss. One was narrow and might be covered with a pack liner. Another spread along a rotten seam above the back wall. The third was large enough that a hard rain would own the room. The floor had soft places. Mouse droppings everywhere. The door did not latch. One window was open to the size of a human shoulder if a human were determined or desperate enough to enter sideways, and whatever followed them did not need to enter by rules Joel understood.

Outside, the clearing was open enough to see movement but too open to feel protected. The woods stood around it in a dark ring. If they stayed in the cabin, they would have walls, a bad roof, and one door. If they returned toward the water, they would have distance, fading light, the trace, and the problem of traveling tired through sound.

There was no correct answer. Only the failure nearest shelter.

Mike spread the map on the table again. The page had softened under the damp air. He put one finger on the cabin square. Then he traced backward with the other hand along the maintenance trace they had taken from Emergency Camp. His fingertip shook once at the bend where the trace met the old road, and he pressed harder, flattening the paper as if the map had lifted under him.

"Back to the boats is too far before dark," he said.

Will did not say yes. He watched the map.

Mike continued, more carefully now, as if each sentence had to pass through a gate. "Road forward is not passable tonight. Cabin is bad shelter. Clearing gives sightlines. We make one night here. First light, we check ten minutes farther. If it doesn't give us anything, back to the boats."

The plan sounded like Mike because it was Mike. It also sounded like the only plan available. Joel hated that. He had wanted the cabin's failure to disqualify Mike's authority cleanly, but failure did not do that. A wrong man could still say the needed thing. A damaged map could still tell them where they stood.

Mike looked at the map a second longer, then at the door. "I talked it down."

Will's head lifted.

Mike did not look at him. "The route. The beacon thing. All of it. I didn't lie. I rounded it off until it sounded manageable."

No one answered. The confession had no use in the room. It could not raise Alex. It could not fix the radio, open the road, dry the sleeping gear, or make the thing outside less interested in their voices. It did not even make Mike less right about staying.

Joel wrote because leaving it loose felt worse:

MIKE: TALKED ROUTE DOWN.

Mike read the line. His mouth tightened, but he did not ask Joel to cross it out.

Will took the notebook.

CABIN BAD BUT BETTER THAN DARK TRACE.

He looked at Mike before adding the second line.

NO ONE OUT ALONE.

Mike read it and nodded once.

Joel added:

DOOR BLOCKED. PACKS TO WALL. WATCHES BY SIGHT. NO ANSWERING OUTSIDE.

The practical work steadied them because practical work asked less of language. They moved the table to brace the door but left a gap they could open if they had to. They dragged the broken chairs under the worst leak and set the black pot and collapsed jug to catch water. Mike cut a square from a ruined pack liner and wedged it with the rusted file over one roof gap, a repair too poor to trust and too useful to ignore. Will found a place along the wall where the floor held his weight and spread his damp sleeping pad there without inflating it fully. Joel hung wet socks and bandage wrappers from old nails because anything lifted off the floor felt like progress. The cabin did not become safe. It became arranged.

The work made noise. Table legs scraping. Pack buckles. The pot's dull clank. The soft thud of gear against wall. Each sound had to be matched to a visible source before it was allowed to pass into the background. Joel found himself doing it silently, automatically. Table: Mike. Pot: Will. Buckle: Joel. Mouse: wall. Drip: roof.

Near dusk, Will let Joel redo the bandage.

The cut had opened at the edge where damp skin had softened. It looked less serious than it smelled. Dirt had worked into the gauze. Joel cleaned it with the last of a sting packet and a squeeze from a water bottle. Will's fingers flexed against the table.

"Sorry," Joel almost said.

He stopped in time.

Will saw him stop. His mouth twitched once.

"Appreciated," he whispered.

Joel glared at him.

Will closed his eyes. "Written appreciated."

Joel wrote:

WILL WHISPERED APPRECIATED.

Will looked at the page and gave a silent little laugh through his nose. It was the saddest version of himself Joel had ever seen, still correcting the social record at the edge of whatever was hunting them.

They ate without cooking. Bars, the last of the cheese sweating oil into its wrapper, two handfuls of trail mix divided by sight. Mike gave Will the larger share without comment. Will noticed and did not comment either. Joel watched them not speak kindness because kindness had become another thing to ration.

The light left the clearing in layers. First the grass lost color. Then the old cans became dark mouths in the weeds. Then the road opening beyond the cabin thickened until it no longer looked like a way through. Dusk did not arrive all at once. It took useful distinctions one at a time.

They had the door mostly shut by then, table against it, a gap at the side wide enough for a blade of evening. The cabin smelled worse as it cooled. Wet wood. Mouse. Old ash. Human breath. The metal pot under the leak rang at longer intervals now as the roof emptied itself.

Mike sat nearest the door with Alex's bear spray across his knees. Will sat against the back wall, injured hand elevated on his pack because Joel had pointed until he did it. Joel sat where he could see both men and the window gap. The notebook rested open on his thigh.

Outside, something clicked.

A dry bag buckle. Once.

No one moved.

Again.

Mike's head tilted. Not much. Enough. Joel saw him sort the sound before he could stop sorting: buckle, pack, water, key, mistake. The old categories still fired first. Fear had not erased training. It had made training dangerous.

Joel wrote without looking down:

BUCKLE OUTSIDE. NO VISUAL.

He held the page toward Mike.

Mike did not look at it. His eyes were on the door gap.

From the same place outside, Will's voice said, "Mike?"

The living Will sat against the back wall, visible, his mouth closed, his face gone slack with fear. The false voice had used him softly. Not panic. Not argument. A simple call for help or attention, the way Will had said Mike's name a thousand times before beginning a correction, a complaint, a joke, a question about where the hell the filter had gone.

Mike looked at the living Will.

Good, Joel thought.

Then the false Will said, closer, "Mike?"

Mike stood.

Joel stood with him.

Mike did not reach for the table. He did not open the door. He only stood, bear spray in one hand, the other hand going to the pocket with the yellow pouch. But standing had changed the room. It had put motion into him. It had made a future in which the door opened.

Joel shook his head.

Mike whispered, "I know."

Outside, something brushed the wall under the broken window.

Will pressed his good hand over his own mouth. His eyes watered behind the glasses, and Joel could not tell if it was fear or fury at hearing himself reduced to bait.

The brush came again. Then a buckle click. Then Alex, from just beyond the wall, said, "Quit mothering me."

The words were almost playful.

Mike moved toward the window.

Not fast. That was the worst part. Not a lunge, not stupidity, not forgetting the rule. He moved like a man trying to confirm a threat at the only opening in the room. The wall had been touched. The window was a vulnerability. The thing had brought Alex into a place where no dead man should have a voice. Capable men moved toward problems they could define.

Joel understood then that the rule had a weakness inside it. Sight did not only save them. Sight demanded maintenance. A sound at the window made a man check the window. A sound at the pack made a man check the pack. Every confirmation cost attention somewhere else.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Mike pulled free.

His face when he turned was not angry now. It was stripped down to something Joel could not bear. "I can't just sit here."

Joel wanted to tell him that sitting here was the work. That not answering was work. That not fixing a thing could be the only way to survive it. He wanted to say that this was what the thing had learned about him: not his mistakes, not only his phrases, but the shape of his usefulness.

He wrote instead:

SITTING IS ACTION.

Mike read it.

For one second, maybe two, it held him. His shoulders dropped a fraction. The bear spray lowered until the black nozzle pointed at the floorboards instead of the wall.

Then from beyond the wall, farther away now, Mike's own voice said, "We're fine."

It was not loud. It did not need to be. It had his old impatience with fear, his old dismissal of hesitation. It came from outside the cabin, from the left side where the grass met the trees, and it sounded almost kind because certainty can resemble kindness when men are tired enough.

Mike's face changed.

Will made a sound behind his hand. Not a word. A broken little intake.

Joel wrote:

FALSE MIKE: WE'RE FINE.

He looked up.

Mike was at the door.

Not outside. Not yet. One hand on the table, shifting it just enough to make the legs scrape against the floor.

"Key," Mike said.

Joel shook his head.

"If something gets the pack--"

Joel pointed to Mike's PFD.

"I know it's on me." His whisper was harsh enough to scrape. "I know. I want to check the road edge. Ten feet."

Will shook his head hard.

Mike looked at him. "I'm not following a voice."

That was true in the narrowest possible way. It was not enough truth.

He was following the part of himself that had always gone first.

Joel stepped between Mike and the table. He held up the notebook.

NO OUTSIDE.

Mike looked past the page toward the door gap.

From outside, beyond the cabin, beyond the clearing's wet grass, Alex laughed.

Bright. Wrong. Perfect.

Then, from a different place, Will's voice said, "You guys hear that?"

The living Will flinched as if struck.

The room broke into directions. Alex at the road. Will near the back wall outside. Mike's old reassurance in the trees. A buckle at the threshold. Water in the pot. Mouse in the wall. Three living men breathing too hard in a room built by people who had left no help behind.

Mike shoved the table.

Joel grabbed for him and caught only the slick shoulder of the PFD. Mike twisted sideways, still controlled, still trying not to make it a fight. That restraint made him stronger. The table moved enough for the door to open a foot. Cold air entered. Wet grass smell. Spruce. Old ash from the clearing.

"Mike," Joel said.

Mike looked back.

For that moment Joel had him. The living Mike was in the gap, one boot inside, one boot on the soft threshold, bear spray in his hand, face white with the effort of being still. His left PFD pocket was zipped. The yellow pouch was still there. The whole road out of the wilderness was still the size of a small hard shape under wet orange fabric.

From the trees behind him, Mike's throat cleared.

Mike turned his head.

Only his head. Only a little.

Joel saw the rule fail by an inch.

He reached again, but Will was moving too, coming off the back wall too late, knocking his shoulder against the table, making the pot ring under the leak. For half a second Joel looked toward that sound because every sound demanded accounting now.

When he looked back, Mike was outside.

Still visible.

Three feet from the door. Then five.

Joel pushed through after him.

Will caught the back of Joel's jacket. Joel shook him off without meaning to. The clearing had gone blue with dusk. The cabin threw one crooked bar of shadow over the grass. Mike stood at the edge of it, facing the old road, bear spray raised but not extended. His PFD pocket was still zipped. The key pouch was still with him. His body was there. Seen. Seen. Seen.

"Mike," Joel said.

The real Mike did not answer.

From the old road, very softly, the false Mike said, "It opens up after this."

Mike took one step toward it.

Joel moved after him.

Behind Joel, Will said, "Visual."

It was the right word. Spoken too late. Spoken because speech was the fastest tool they had and the worst.

Mike stopped.

For one clean second all three living men were visible: Will in the doorway, Joel in the wet grass, Mike at the clearing edge. The cabin behind them useless and real. The old road ahead closed and dark. Daylight almost gone.

Then a dry bag buckle clicked inside the cabin.

Will turned.

Joel turned with him.

One glance. Less than a second. Long enough to see the dark doorway and the packs still in place. Long enough for the line between the three bodies to break.

When Joel looked back, Mike had moved into the trees.

Not far. Not gone. A darker body between dark spruce, still visible if Joel chose the right gap and did not blink.

Then the gap closed around him.

Joel understood too late that visual confirmation was not a rule you owned. It was an act, and the act had stopped.

He took one step toward the trees and stopped. Another step would put Will behind him. Staying would leave Mike ahead.

The page was wet in his hand. Mike was in the trees, still close enough to count and no longer close enough to hold.


# Chapter 17 - Mike's Authority

Mike died because he was almost right.

That was the part Joel kept returning to when he tried to put the week in an order that would hold. Mike did not run into the dark because a dead friend teased him. He did not forget the rule in a clean, stupid way. He moved ten yards into brush to confirm the things he had spent his life confirming: road, shape, pocket, key.

Joel understood the motion as it happened. Mike had made a life out of checking what other men hoped was fine. Buckles. Straps. Weather. Time. That kind of man was useful until usefulness could be made to answer.

For a few seconds after Mike crossed into the young spruce, no one moved.

The cabin door stood open behind Joel and Will, black in the wet wall. The table they had shoved against it sat crooked from Mike forcing his way out. Inside, the packs were still against the wall, buckles dulled by damp. The black pot under the leak rang once, then once again, thin and domestic.

Outside, dusk thickened the clearing. Every spruce at the edge seemed to stand in front of another spruce, and another behind that. The old road was no longer a road so much as a darker permission between trees.

Mike was still visible.

Not well. Not enough. A darker block between dark young trunks, the orange of his PFD turned brown by rain and shadow. But there. Joel held one hand up as if the gesture itself could keep him counted. Will stood at the cabin threshold with his injured hand pressed against his chest and his good hand on the doorframe. His mouth was open. He knew not to call. Knowing did not give him another tool.

From the old road, Mike's voice said, "It opens up after this."

The living Mike turned his head toward it.

Only his head. Only a little. The smallest possible failure of the rule.

Joel stepped into the grass.

"Mike."

The word left him before he could stop it. It hung below the wet spruce tips, useless and available.

Will made a small sound behind him. Not a word. A swallowed warning, hard enough to hurt.

Mike did not answer. He took another step forward.

Joel went after him.

He went because Mike was still visible. Because sight had to mean something or none of the rules meant anything. Because leaving a man ten yards into brush while his shoulders could still be counted was another kind of murder.

The grass was high and wet. It dragged against Joel's shins and laid cold water into his sandals. The old roadbed made a shallow raised strip through the clearing, but once it entered the trees it broke into suggestion: ditch, moss, alder, gravel under roots. It had been built for wheels and left long enough for the woods to answer.

Mike's shoulders moved ahead of him.

Close enough to see. Close enough to reach if Joel moved faster. Close enough to call if calling were not feeding.

Behind Joel, Will left the doorway.

Joel turned enough to see him and raised one hand hard, palm out.

Stay.

Will stopped at the edge of the cabin shadow. His glasses were smeared. The bandage on his hand had gone dark at the center. In the blue dusk he seemed held in place only by the habit of being seen.

From ahead came Mike's throat-clear.

Not the living one. Joel could see the side of Mike's face now, rain in his beard, jaw set, mouth closed. The sound came from farther up the roadbed, where the young spruce shut into a black wet seam.

Then Mike's voice, calm and low: "We're fine."

Mike stopped.

That was the worst part. He kept stopping. He kept almost understanding.

Joel stepped close enough to touch the back of Mike's PFD.

Mike did not turn around. "Don't grab me."

The sentence was real. Breath in it. Anger in it. A living man's impatience with being handled.

Joel let his fingers hover an inch from the strap. He wanted the notebook, the slow authority of graphite, the way a written word lost tone and gained edge. But the notebook was in his jacket pocket, and taking it out meant looking down.

"Back," Joel said.

Mike's shoulders lifted once with breath. "I'm looking at it."

"At what?"

Mike did not answer at first. Rain ticked off spruce needles. Water moved from one leaf to another in a soft run of drops. The woods ahead had no center.

"There's a gap," Mike said. "Road keeps going."

Joel looked past him. There was a gap because brush made gaps everywhere. A man could look at any dark place long enough and make a route from it. Joel saw two alder stems crossed like bones, a jack pine down with its roots up, and beyond that a pale strip that might have been moss, water, stone, or nothing useful.

"Not tonight."

Mike's head moved once. Not a nod. A refusal he had not yet put into words.

Behind them, Will whispered, "Joel."

Joel turned. Will pointed toward the cabin.

The dark doorway held the shape of their packs. Nothing had moved. Then a dry bag buckle clicked from inside.

Once.

Clean plastic. Familiar as a finger snap.

Mike turned his whole body.

Joel caught the strap.

Mike twisted free. "Key."

"On you."

"I know where it is."

"Then leave it."

The old argument almost found them there. At a landing, in another life, Alex would have made the line funny. Will would have added the technical distinction that annoyed both of them. Mike would have checked the pocket anyway.

Instead Mike pressed his left hand to the PFD pocket.

The zipper was still closed. Joel saw it. The little black pull lay flat against the orange nylon, wet and dark. The key was a hard shape beneath the fabric, no bigger than the last ordinary thing in the world.

"There," Joel said.

Mike looked down at the pocket as if his own body had become evidence entered by someone else. His face changed, not yielding, only losing one layer of certainty.

His thumb stayed on the zipper pull. He rubbed it once, hard, as if the teeth of it could report back.

From the cabin came Will's voice.

"Mike?"

The living Will stood fifteen yards behind them in the clearing, one hand still raised from pointing at the doorway. His mouth was closed.

Mike looked at him.

Good, Joel thought.

Then Will's voice came again from inside the cabin, soft and practical and wrong. "Mike?"

Mike stepped backward, toward the cabin this time.

The sound put duty in both directions. Forward meant false route. Back meant false Will. The only safe thing was stillness, and stillness had never been Mike's gift.

Joel gripped the back of the PFD.

Mike wheeled on him. "Let go."

Joel shook his head.

"Let go of me."

"Look at Will."

"I am."

"Keep looking."

Mike's eyes flicked past him to the living Will in the clearing. For two seconds the three of them made a line of sight: Will at the cabin shadow, Joel in the wet grass, Mike at the first trees. Each man seen by another, each body held in place by attention.

Then from the brush to Joel's right, Alex laughed.

Bright. Exact. Close enough that Joel's body turned before his mind could object.

There was no Alex. Alder. Spruce. Rain-dark leaves. A torn strip of birch bark hanging from a branch. No headlamp. No red jacket. No dead man, only the laugh emptied and used.

When Joel looked back, Mike had moved three steps deeper into the roadbed.

"No," Joel said.

Mike lifted Alex's bear spray.

His voice was lower now. "Come on, then."

The words were not brave in the way a story would make them brave. They were tired. Angry. Humiliated. A man addressing a problem because the alternative was to let it choose the terms.

Joel saw all of Mike in the line of his arm: the canoe dealer who could spot a warped hull from a driveway, the man who checked straps twice and complained about everyone else's knots, the man who had shown Joel how to read a faint current seam and then acted as if the lesson were obvious. Once, on a spring trip years back, Mike had walked knee-deep into black water to free a pinned boat and come back cursing them for letting him be the only adult. Obnoxious, useful, first in.

"Mike," Joel said.

Mike did not turn.

From farther up the old road, Mike's own voice said, "Just keep moving."

The phrase passed through the trees with the steadiness of a hand on the back. It was one of Mike's good sentences. Joel hated that. It had been useful on portages, in mud, when Will talked too much and Alex made complaint into theater. It had kept them from sitting down in bad places.

Now the woods wore it without earning it.

Mike took one step toward it.

Joel lunged and got one hand around the shoulder of the PFD.

Mike shoved him off hard enough that Joel slipped sideways into alder. A wet branch scraped his cheek. He tasted blood or rain and could not tell which.

"I said don't grab me."

"That's not you."

"No shit."

"Then stop."

Mike turned back. His face was red under mud and rain. He looked less like a leader than a man cornered by the failure of every tool he trusted.

"You think I don't know that?"

The words were too loud. Joel could feel them leave Mike and become part of the clearing.

Will made a broken gesture from the clearing. Down. Quiet. Please.

Mike saw him and lowered his voice, but the damage had already become air.

"I'm not following it," Mike said. "I'm checking the line."

"There is no line."

"If the road dies here," Mike said, "then I put us in a box."

The sentence came out stripped of command. It was the closest thing to fear Mike had given them.

"Then it dies here," Joel said.

Mike looked past him into the trees. "You don't know that."

He was right. That was the small knife in it. Joel had a rule and fear and eyes that could not see far enough. Mike had the same eyes and more route sense. On any other day, Joel would have trusted him past the first wall of spruce because Mike could read old cuts on stumps, water choosing grade, gravel under moss, human decisions left in dirt.

The thing had not invented Mike's authority. It had found the working part.

Joel pulled out the notebook at last. He kept his head up and wrote badly against his palm.

NO SCOUTING.

He held it out.

Mike glanced, then looked away.

Joel wrote another line beneath it.

SIGHT IS THE RULE.

Mike looked at that one longer.

Behind them, the dry bag buckle clicked again from the cabin.

Will flinched but did not turn. He held the rule better than either of them for that second, his whole face clenched around the effort.

Then from the trees to Mike's left, Will's voice said, "No, no, no."

The living Will made the same sound half a beat later, quieter, horrified at himself.

The two Wills overlapped and separated. One in the woods. One in the clearing. One stolen. One ashamed.

Mike turned left.

"Visual," Joel said. "You don't have him."

Mike stopped again.

Almost. Always almost.

The brush ahead opened without wind.

Not wide. Not like a door. The alder stems leaned apart in a shallow breathing motion, and behind them something pale moved low to the ground, or high and bent, or neither. Joel saw wet hide, or water on wood. He saw river mud on something that was not a bank. He saw a long blackness where an animal's shoulder might have been, then no shoulder, only spruce shadow closing over the shape it had offered. The smell came next: mud, old meat, wet fur, and the mineral cold of channel water after sunset.

Mike fired Alex's bear spray.

The orange mist burst into the brush and came back on the damp air. Joel's eyes stung at once. His throat closed. Will coughed from the clearing and bent over his injured hand. Mike shouted, not a word but a refusal, and drove another burst into the moving alder. The can hissed in his hand. For half a second he looked like exactly the man they needed: feet set, arm straight, doing the practical thing with the practical tool Alex had never used.

For one wild second the world became almost ordinary. Bear spray. Brush. Men coughing. An animal charge. A bad but known story.

Then Mike's voice came from behind them, at the cabin.

"We're exactly where we ought to be."

Mike looked back.

The impact took him sideways.

Joel did not see the body of the thing. He saw Mike's feet leave the ground. He saw the bear spray canister spin out of his hand and strike stone with a flat metallic crack. He saw alder whip and close. He heard Mike say, "Jesus," in a voice emptied of command, a voice that belonged to pain before pain had language.

Then the woods folded over him.

Will screamed his name.

The sound tore out once and stopped. He clapped his good hand over his mouth as if he could shove the name back inside. His eyes were huge behind his glasses, wet from the spray and from something that had arrived too fast to be grief yet.

Joel moved forward.

He moved two steps because Mike had been there. Because the PFD had been orange. Because a living man did not disappear from ten yards away while two other living men watched.

Something thrashed ahead, lower than where Mike's shoulders should have been. A branch snapped with the clean sound of bone, or a branch. Mike grunted once. Not a word. A wet drag followed, leaves dragged through leaves, weight through alder.

"Mike," Joel said.

Will made a strangled sound behind him.

From the brush, Mike answered, in Mike's exact anger, "Just keep moving."

Joel froze.

The words were too perfect. That was how he knew.

Not because a dying man could not have said them. Men reached for old sentences when new pain gave them no language. But this came from the wrong place, shoulder height, several yards left of the drag, clean through the brush with no blood in it and no breath broken by ribs or mud. It was Mike as instruction, not Mike as body.

Joel backed up.

Every cell in him chose backward. He hated himself while doing it and kept doing it.

Will met him halfway across the clearing with the hatchet in his good hand. Joel did not know when he had picked it up. The blade looked small and wet and useless.

"Where is he?"

Joel shook his head.

"Where is he?"

Joel put one hand on Will's chest and pushed him back.

Will resisted. Not much. Enough to make the hatchet wobble between them.

"That's Mike," Will said.

Joel shook his head again.

"You don't know."

Joel did know. Not enough for police. Not enough for a ranger with a clipboard. Not enough for Mike's sister, or whoever would be called when three men became one report. Enough for the body. Enough to know tone could be pulled over absence.

From the old road came Mike's throat-clear.

Will flinched.

Then Mike's voice, rougher now: "Joel."

It used his name.

Joel's hand tightened on Will's jacket.

There were many ways to be made helpless. One was not knowing. Another was knowing exactly what was being done to you and still feeling the hook enter. Mike had said Joel's name for years in annoyance, in argument, across camp, across water, from truck beds and kitchens and cheap motels. The sound had history in it.

"Joel," Mike said again from the brush.

Will whispered, "Don't."

Joel looked at him.

Will's lips were pressed white. He had given the warning to Joel, maybe to himself. For once, Will had the right word and no explanation after it.

The brush thrashed once more. Farther away this time. Then quiet.

Rain worked through the clearing. A drop gathered on Joel's chin and fell. His eyes burned from the spray drifting back. He wiped them with his sleeve and made the sting worse.

Inside the cabin, the buckle clicked again.

No one turned.

"We have to get him," Will said.

The sentence came out flat. Not argument. Obligation making one last attempt to sound like a thing men could still obey.

Joel took the notebook from his pocket. His hands shook enough that the pencil tore the damp page.

NO VISUAL.

Will stared at it.

"I know we don't have visual."

Joel wrote:

WE GO IN, IT GETS TWO.

Will's face twisted. For a moment Joel thought he would hit him. Maybe he should have. A hit would have belonged to the world of bodies. It would have been an answer a man could understand.

"You don't get to make that math," Will said.

Joel had no defense. The math had made itself. It stood in the clearing with them, ugly and correct.

From the brush, Mike's voice said, "Will?"

Will closed his eyes.

The thing had learned smallness from them too. Not tenderness. Volume.

"Will?" Mike said.

Will bent forward as if the name had struck him low in the stomach.

Joel grabbed his jacket with both hands.

Will did not fight him. That frightened Joel more than resistance would have. Will only stood there, hatchet hanging, his whole body leaned toward the trees.

"He'd come for us," Will said.

He meant the Mike who could poison an evening with certainty and still get up first in cold rain to fix someone else's bad knot. He meant all the years in which coming for each other had been assumed.

Joel wrote:

NOT HIM.

Will read it and made a sound like there was something caught in his throat.

"You don't know."

Joel underlined NOT HIM until the page began to shred.

They stood at the cabin doorway until the light went.

This was not waiting in any useful sense. It was failure given duration. They did not go inside because inside meant admitting they would not run into the brush. They did not go into the brush because going meant giving Mike's death company. They stood where the clearing narrowed into old road and listened to a man who was not there use what remained of a man who was.

Once, from far left, Mike said, "I'm okay."

He had never said it that way in life. That almost helped.

Once, very close to the road, Alex laughed, and Will covered his ears before lowering his hands again because covered ears meant not hearing what might matter.

Once, from inside the cabin, a tent zipper moved. There was no tent set up inside.

Joel kept writing because writing was an action and because the page could hold what his head could not.

MIKE TAKEN OLD ROAD EDGE.

ALEX SPRAY LOST.

FALSE MIKE USED: WE'RE FINE / JUST KEEP MOVING / JOEL / WILL.

REAL MIKE LAST SEEN AT ALDER.

He paused before the last line.

He did not want to write died. Alex had a covered body and a marked place. Mike had brush and sound and a hole where sight had been. But not writing it did not leave Mike living.

ASSUME MIKE DEAD.

Will saw the line and turned away.

Dark came by inches and then all at once. The clearing lost its low green. The cabin became a box of black corners. The old cans in the grass disappeared first, then the road crown, then the nearest spruce trunks. Only the pale ruin of the sky remained between treetops, and that too was closing.

"We call once," Will said.

Joel shook his head.

"Once."

Joel shook his head harder.

Will looked at him with a hatred so clean it almost looked like strength. "I can't sit here and not call once."

Joel wanted to say that calling was not mercy if the thing could wear mercy afterward. He wanted to say Mike had already answered too many things. He wanted to say every name in the group as if names could hold men in place.

Instead he wrote:

IF WE CALL, IT HAS THAT TOO.

Will took the pencil from him.

His bandaged hand fumbled, and the letters came out uneven.

THEN WE CALL BADLY.

Joel looked at him.

Will's mouth trembled once and stopped. "Together," he whispered. "Flat. No names."

It was a terrible idea. It was also the only mercy they could make small enough to survive.

Joel nodded.

They stood shoulder to shoulder at the clearing edge. Will held the hatchet. Joel held the notebook and the broken pencil. The cabin breathed cold behind them. The trees ahead gave them wet dark.

Together, in voices as low and flat as they could make them, they called, "Answer if seen."

The phrase sounded absurd. Bureaucratic. Inhuman. The kind of thing no friend should have to say in woods after dark.

No one answered.

They waited.

Nothing living answered.

Then from far up the old road, Mike's voice said, pleasantly, "Answer if seen."

Will folded at the waist.

Joel put a hand between his shoulder blades and felt the fine shaking there. He did not know whether it was grief or cold. The body did not separate them anymore. Will did not pull away.

Something dragged in the brush beyond the road. Not toward them. Not away in a straight line. Sideways through alder, heavy enough to move stems, slow enough to sound deliberate whether or not anything deliberate stood behind it. Joel listened for Mike in it and heard only weight.

After that, the woods went quiet.

They backed into the cabin before full dark took the threshold. Backed, because turning away from the clearing felt like giving it permission. Will went first, still facing out until his heel struck the table leg. Joel followed, one hand on the doorframe, eyes on the road gap.

They moved the table back against the door. Not fully. Enough. The door did not latch. The table did not make it secure. The act mattered because bodies needed tasks to keep from spending themselves all at once.

Inside smelled of wet wood, mouse, bear spray blown back on their clothes, and the sour salt of fear. The pot rang under the leak. The sound made Joel flinch every time, and every time he forced himself to see the drop fall before he let the sound exist.

Will sank against the back wall.

The hatchet slipped from his hand and struck the floor.

Joel looked at it. Hatchet. Will. Floor. Source confirmed. He picked it up and set it beside Will's knee, handle first, where Will could reach it without looking.

Will's face was wet. He was crying without sound, the tears running straight down into the dirt beside his mouth. He did not cover his face. Maybe he had run out of hands. Maybe he knew that any hand over his eyes was another way to lose sight.

Joel sat across from him and wrote by the last usable light from the window gap.

DOOR BLOCKED.

NO OUTSIDE.

MIKE DEAD VOICE ACTIVE.

KEY UNKNOWN.

The last line stopped him.

He looked at Will.

Will read it and closed his eyes.

The Highlander sat at North Road Bridge in Joel's mind with cruel clarity. Gray or green depending on the light. A cracked dashboard. Mike's canoe straps in the back. An old gas receipt jammed in the console. A vehicle so ordinary that it had become the shape of rescue.

A key made that shape usable. Without it, the Highlander was another cabin: real, human-made, locked against them.

"Maybe it stays on him," Will whispered.

Joel glared.

Will wiped his nose with his sleeve and nodded, ashamed and stubborn both. "I know."

Outside, at the threshold, something small struck wood.

Tick.

Not a buckle. Not a branch. A hard little sound, bright even through the door.

Neither of them moved.

The sound came again. A scrape this time, metal or plastic dragged half an inch over the sill.

Will's eyes went to Joel.

Joel shook his head.

From outside, Mike cleared his throat.

The sound was so close it might have been made by a man standing with his mouth against the door.

Then Mike said, "Key."

Will pressed both hands over his mouth.

Joel wrote:

BAIT.

The pencil point broke on the period he did not need.

The object at the threshold shifted again.

Tick.

Tick.

They waited. The rain softened. The pot rang. A mouse moved in the wall, then stopped.

Joel could not leave the key outside all night if it was the key.

That was the problem. Some bait was real and still bait.

Joel slid the table back an inch.

Will shook his head hard.

Joel held up one finger. Then pointed to his own eyes. Then to Will. Watch me.

Will took the hatchet again and stood. His legs shook. He moved to where he could see the gap beside the door, the room, and Joel's shoulders all at once.

Joel crouched. He moved the table only as far as his forearm needed. The door opened a hand's width. Cold air entered with the smell of wet grass and spruce. The opening made every part of him want to stand, push, run, make a whole action out of the half action. He stayed low.

The key lay just outside the threshold. Not in the room. Not far enough away to excuse leaving it. Close enough that reaching would put his wrist in the dark.

The Toyota fob was mud-slick and absurdly domestic, black plastic with worn buttons and a metal key folded into it. A torn strip of orange nylon clung to the ring. The zipper pull from Mike's left PFD pocket hung there too, bent open, its teeth still holding two threads of orange fabric.

Joel stared at it.

He had the insane thought that Mike would be furious about the zipper. Mike cared about gear even when gear deserved no care. He would complain about waterproof pockets that were not waterproof, about manufacturers making everything worse and charging more for it. The thought was so accurate that Joel almost made a sound.

He reached through the gap and took the key with two fingers.

Nothing grabbed him.

Nothing moved in the clearing that he could see.

That was worse than a grab would have been. The woods had placed the thing they needed at the line and let need do the work.

Joel shut the door and shoved the table back.

Will held out his good hand. Joel put the key in his palm for one second, long enough for both of them to confirm it by sight and touch. The fob left a wet black mark on Will's skin. Then Joel took it back and zipped it inside his inner jacket pocket.

He wrote:

KEY RECOVERED. MIKE LEFT PFD POCKET TORN. JOEL INNER JACKET ZIP.

Will read the line and began to cry harder, still without voice.

Joel wanted to join him. His body wanted it with a force that frightened him. Crying made sound. Grief made sound. Every human thing left in them wanted a mouth.

From beyond the clearing, Mike said, "We're fine."

Joel closed his hand around the key through his jacket until the plastic hurt him.

The voice came again, farther off now, receding into the old road or pretending to.

"It opens up after this."

Will folded down beside the wall, both hands over his ears now, rule or no rule. Joel let him. There were limits to what one body could be asked to hold upright.

Joel stayed by the door.

He watched the black line where the frame failed to meet the floor. Rain worked under it in a thin shine. The key pressed into his palm. The notebook lay open on his knee, the last written lines swelling in the damp.

He added one more line.

MIKE VOICE NOT PROOF.

Outside, Mike cleared his throat.

Not close this time. Somewhere past the cabin, past the road edge, past where sight could do any work.

"Just keep moving," Mike said.

Joel did not move.


# Chapter 18 - Two Men

They spent the night in the cabin because leaving in darkness would have been another way to die.

Shelter was too generous a word. Rain came through the roof in three places Joel could count and several he could only hear. Wind entered around the door. Mice worked inside the walls with the confidence of owners. The table they had pushed against the door sat crooked, one leg shorter than the others or sunk into a soft place in the floor. Every time the wind leaned on the door the table clicked once against the boards, a dry sound that tried all night to become a buckle, a latch, a fingernail.

Joel and Will sat with their backs to the least rotten wall and their feet braced against the table.

Neither slept.

Sleep came near them a few times. It came as heat behind Joel's eyes, as the slow loosening of his hands, as the small fall of his chin toward his chest. Each time something corrected him. A drop struck the pot. A mouse shifted insulation inside the wall. Will's breath caught and stopped being regular. Outside, beyond the door, beyond the clearing, a branch moved in rain. Joel returned all at once to the floorboards, to the table leg under his heel, to the weight zipped against his chest.

The key was real. He checked it without looking down. Black plastic. Metal folded inside. Torn orange nylon still on the ring, and the bent zipper pull from Mike's PFD pocket. It sat in his inner jacket pocket, zipped against his chest. He had wanted it so badly that needing it had felt like betrayal. Now that he had it, the want did not leave. It hardened into another thing he had to guard.

The Highlander was still miles away, if miles meant anything on a map that had already lied by omission. A key did not shorten a portage. It did not dry Will's hand or make the old road ahead passable. It proved only that some human system remained intact beyond the trees: a lock, an ignition, a vehicle waiting under spruce drip at North Road Bridge. That should have comforted him. Instead the hard little weight made his chest ache. The woods had not destroyed the thing they needed. It had given it back.

Will watched the door for most of the night. He had the hatchet in his good hand. His bandaged hand lay in his lap, palm up, fingers slightly curled, as if it had become an object he was required to carry but not use. The bandage had gone brown at the center and stiff at the edges. When he tried to flex the fingers, only the first two moved cleanly. He had stopped crying after the light went completely, or at least stopped letting Joel see it. Sometimes his shoulders moved once. Joel saw it and looked back at the door.

Outside, sometime after the rain thinned, Mike said, "We're fine."

Will pressed both hands over his mouth and held them there until his eyes watered.

The voice had come from the clearing, not far from the threshold. Low, familiar, managerial. A man making a hard thing smaller by saying it was small. It had the old irritation in it, the half-dismissal of other people's fear, the same tone Mike had used at landings and map stops and once in Will's kitchen when a strap had snapped and nearly taken Joel in the eye.

Joel wrote in the notebook because looking at the door gave the voice more space.

MIKE VOICE ACTIVE.

Then, after a moment:

NOT MIKE.

Will read the page by headlamp. He nodded once. It was not agreement so much as exhaustion moving his head.

The voice did not come again for a long time.

Long enough for the cabin to rebuild itself into ordinary misery. Wet wood. Mouse stink. Old ash in the stove they could not trust. The sour pepper of bear spray still trapped in their clothes from the clearing. Rain ticking through roof gaps and making little black circles in the dust. Joel's legs ached from holding the table in place. His left foot had fallen asleep and then come back wrong, bright with needles. Hunger came in dull waves and withdrew when fear rose.

He made himself inventory without moving, because counting was quieter than prayer and left less behind.

Two men.

One key.

One hatchet.

No useful bear spray in the cabin. Mike had carried Alex's full canister out toward the road, and it was gone with him. Mike's own near-spent canister was back with the cached gear, or useless even if it was not. One usable headlamp and Will's dim one with the battery already suspect. One notebook, damp at the lower edge. One pencil shortened and cracked, one pen that worked only if held at an angle. Food divided among packs. Wet socks. Damp sleeping bags. A cracked but repaired canoe at the cache. A better canoe hidden near it. Two paddles there, maybe three if nothing had shifted. One route with no Mike left to argue it into shape.

Two mouths.

That was part of the inventory too, though Joel did not write it yet. Two men meant two bodies, two witnesses, and two remaining supplies of sound. It meant every useful sentence had to be weighed against the possibility that usefulness itself was what the thing wanted.

The count did not save anything. It held the night in objects. Objects could still be looked at, and looking did not give the woods a voice.

Near morning, Mike said, "Just keep moving."

This time the voice came from farther away. Up the old trace, maybe, or from behind the cabin where there was no trace at all. It was not loud. It did not need to be. The sentence entered the cabin through the bad roof and the bad door and the gaps between boards. It sounded useful. That was the cruelty of it.

Will bent forward, hands still clamped over his mouth. For one second Joel thought he would vomit. Instead Will made a sound so small it might have been breath and might have been Mike's name stopped before it could form.

Joel reached across and touched Will's knee.

Will flinched.

Joel held up the notebook.

NO ANSWER.

Will stared at the two words. His eyes were wet again, but he did not look away from the page.

The page did one useful thing and failed at everything else. It removed tone. It took Mike's stolen steadiness out of the order, took Will's tremor out of the question, took Joel's need to make command sound like care. It left marks that could be pointed at and, if necessary, hated.

After a while he took the pencil with his bad hand. The grip hurt him; Joel saw it in the whitening around his mouth. Will wrote beneath Joel's line:

NO ANSWER EVER?

Joel read it and looked toward the door.

The old rule, the first rule, had been too simple. Do not answer. It had worked when voices came cleanly from places where no body stood, at the edge of camp, under rain, on water when sound crossed ahead of sight. But Mike had died inside a harder problem. A real man could be ten yards away and need help. A real key could lie at the threshold. False sounds were almost easy. They asked only to be refused. True things had a price.

He wrote:

UNLESS SEEN.

Will's mouth tightened. He tapped the page once with the pencil, then wrote:

SEEN HOW LONG?

Joel almost answered aloud. The habit of speech rose in him before thought. He felt the beginning of it in his throat and stopped so sharply it hurt.

He took the pencil back.

BODY IN SIGHT. SOURCE CONFIRMED.

He almost added TRUST, then did not. Sight did not deserve that much. Sight had lost Mike when Joel turned his head for half a second. Sight was not faith. It was work, and it had to be done again and again until the body failed.

Will read it and shut his eyes.

Then he opened them and wrote, slower than before:

YOU'RE MAKING RULES LIKE HIM.

Then Will added, smaller:

JUST QUIETER.

Joel looked at the sentences until the words lost their accusation and became only shapes. Will was not wrong. Joel had Mike's key in his pocket and Mike's habit in his hand: reduce, direct, make the next action smaller than the fear around it. The difference was supposed to be that Joel distrusted himself. He did not know whether that mattered. A man could mistrust his own authority and still spend it.

He wrote:

I KNOW.

Outside, something scraped the cabin wall.

Both men looked. The sound came from the left side, low, near the ground. Not a clawing. Not an animal working to enter. A single drag of something over old wood, then silence.

Joel held still until the next drop fell into the pot. Then another. The scrape did not repeat. The cabin returned to rain and mice and breath.

Will wrote:

IT CAN WAIT.

Joel nodded. That was fair. The scrape had not repeated. Whatever made it had no need to hurry.

At dawn, the window gaps grayed before the clearing did. The cabin did not brighten so much as lose its edges. Corners withdrew from black into wet brown. The old stove showed its rusted door. A shelf emerged above it, bare except for mouse droppings and a nail bent sideways. The floorboards were cupped with damp. Their packs sagged against the wall where they had left them. Will's face appeared by degrees, hollow under the eyes and bearded with the roughness of men who had stopped belonging to mornings.

Daylight did not make the place safe. It only made evidence possible again, and evidence had become another kind of labor.

Joel eased his feet off the table and waited for pain to travel up both legs. He moved one joint at a time. Knee. Ankle. Hip. Neck. His body had slept in no position and punished him for all of them.

Will did not move until Joel touched the table.

They listened.

Nothing outside but dripping spruce and the soft empty knock of rain falling from roof to grass.

Joel shifted the table back far enough to open the door a few inches. Cold air came in carrying wet leaves, mud, and the faint sourness from the clearing that might have been bear spray, might have been blood, might have been his own clothes. He put his eye to the crack.

The clearing was there.

Grass flattened by rain. The roadbed rising shallowly through it. The old cans near the brush. The trees at the far side. No Mike. No orange PFD. No bear spray canister. No moving shape low in the alder. Only the place where he had last seen Mike, and the place where sight had failed.

Joel opened the door wider.

Will lifted the hatchet.

Joel shook his head and pointed to the floor. Stay. Then he pointed to his own eyes and the clearing. Look.

Will stood because sitting had become impossible, but he stayed behind the table. His face had the stunned obedience of a man using the last instructions available not because he trusted them fully, but because without them he would run toward the worst thing.

Joel stepped onto the threshold.

The boards outside were slick. The key lay heavy in his pocket. He had an absurd urge to apologize to Mike for having it. He did not.

He scanned from left to right the way Mike would have scanned a landing: close ground first, brush line second, route beyond. Grass. Door sill. Mud. The old road's raised back. Alder stems wet and bowed. Spruce boughs dripping into themselves. The cabin wall to his left, where the scrape had sounded in the night.

In the mud below the wall was a mark.

Not a footprint. Not enough of one. A long pressure in the wet soil and two deeper points beside it, as if weight had been set down and lifted without the courtesy of an animal pattern. A strand of orange thread lay near the deepest point, or else a pine needle had gone bright with wet. Joel looked at the mark until it became several things at once. Bear. Root. Dragged branch. Knee. A fact made by weight, not by any story he could use.

He made himself look away before the shape taught him anything he could not use.

Back inside, he wrote:

MARK BY WALL. DO NOT STUDY.

Will read it, then looked at him with a flash of real anger. He wanted to study. He wanted evidence, category, contradiction. His eyes kept going to the wall, then back to Joel, then down to his injured hand. The part of him that corrected maps and knots and weather still leaned toward the mark. The rest of him knew what leaning cost.

Joel added:

STUDY AFTER EXIT. NOT NOW.

That was a lie. There would be no after for the mark. No return with a camera, no ranger kneeling in mud, no patient reconstruction of weight and angle. But the notebook made the lie less like comfort and more like triage, and Will let it stand.

They drank without cooking. Cold water from a bottle that tasted faintly of plastic and smoke. They split a crushed bar and a handful of trail mix. Will picked out raisins with the automatic disgust of a normal man, stared at them in his palm as if the old preference had come from someone else, and ate them anyway.

Joel almost smiled.

Will saw the almost and looked down.

That was the morning's first mercy: a joke too small to say.

Joel pointed at Will's hand.

Will shook his head.

Joel pointed again, then at the first-aid bag. The gesture came out sharper than he meant. Will's mouth tightened.
