For a second they were back in the old shape of themselves, one man overcorrecting another, both of them ready to mistake care for insult. Then Will set his bad hand on his knee and looked away.

Joel unwrapped only the outer layer. The cloth stuck where it had dried. Will's fingers curled against the pain, and Joel stopped until they straightened again. The cut had gone puffy at the edges. The skin around it looked too tight and pale, the knuckles reddened from cold and use. He cleaned what he could with two square inches of gauze and water that was too precious to waste and too little to help. He wrapped it flatter, not better.

Will flexed once. Only the first two fingers answered cleanly.

Joel wrote:

USE LESS.

Will looked at the words with a tired contempt that had almost become humor.

He wrote beneath them:

WITH WHAT THIRD HAND.

Joel gave him one raisin from the trail mix pile. Will stared at it, then put it in his mouth.

They inventoried properly in the gray light.

Will laid items on the floor in rows. Food. Filter. Lighter. Pot. Headlamps. Spare batteries, two good and one questionable. First-aid bag, lighter than it should have been. Map case damp at one corner. Compass. Joel's knife. Will's fishing pliers, useless except as pliers now. The hatchet. The Highlander key. Will's truck key, still sealed in the small blue dry pouch in his pack, a second vehicle on the wrong end of the route and therefore only a fact.

Mike's absence took up more floor than any object.

Will paused with Mike's small repair kit in his hand. He had found it in the side pocket of the pack Mike had left inside when he forced his way out. A coil of wire. Zip ties. A roll of black tape flattened from use. A little tube of epoxy. Mike had mocked expensive gear and kept every cheap fix that might hold for one more day.

Will set the kit down carefully.

Joel wrote:

TAKE.

Will nodded.

Then he wrote:

BODY?

The word sat between them.

Joel did not answer quickly. He looked out the open door. The clearing waited. The brush beyond the road was close enough that a man could throw a stone into it. Close enough that a living Mike, hurt and hidden, should have been able to crawl, call, curse, make some human sound they could trust.

But the woods had already used him cleanly.

Joel wrote:

NO VISUAL. NO RECOVERY.

Will's face closed.

He took the pencil.

WE LEFT ALEX.

The letters dug hard into the damp page. The X at the end of Alex tore the paper.

Joel looked at the word and saw the groundsheet at Second Camp, stones along the edge, Alex's hand not fully covered until Mike went back and fixed it. The note they had left. The absurd formality of writing suspected bear attack while the woods listened.

He wrote:

WE MARKED HIM.

Will struck one line through that. Not hard enough to erase it. Hard enough to reject the mercy.

Joel let him.

From outside, to the right of the door, Will's voice said, "No, no, no."

Will stopped moving.

The pencil rolled from his fingers and tapped the floor.

The living Will was inside the cabin, kneeling by the food row, mouth open and silent. The voice had come from outside near the wall, thin and panicked, exactly Will's panic and not his breath. It had the shape of him without the body. It had stolen even the way his fear doubled back on itself before reaching a sentence.

Joel lifted one hand. Slow.

Will nodded in tiny increments, eyes fixed on Joel.

The voice came again, outside and closer to the open door. "No, no, no."

Will put his good hand over his mouth.

Joel wrote without looking down:

NOT YOU.

The pencil snagged and made the T too long.

Will looked at the page. His chest hitched once. He shook his head, not in denial, but in a kind of insulted wonder. His own terror had been taken from him and returned like a tool.

Joel reached for the door.

Mike cleared his throat from the road.

Joel froze.

The throat-clear was worse in daylight. Night had given it room to be a sound among sounds. Morning made it administrative. A man about to make a decision. A man ready to turn uncertainty into instruction.

"Just keep moving," Mike said.

Will's eyes went to Joel's hand on the door.

Joel did not close it. He did not open it wider. The sentence hung in the wet air with all its old usefulness. On the first muddy portage it would have been correct. On the beaver dams it had kept Alex from making every log a debate. In rain and bad footing and fatigue, it had been a good rule. That was why it could kill them.

Joel took the notebook and wrote:

RIGHT WORDS CAN BE WRONG SOURCE.

Will stared at the line for a long time. Then he wrote underneath it:

RIGHT SOURCE CAN BE WRONG WORDS.

Joel looked at him.

For the first time since Mike disappeared, Will looked almost present. Ruined, wet-eyed, trembling, but present. His mind was still reaching. Still making distinctions. Still himself. The best part of a man did not separate cleanly from the worst.

Joel tapped Will's sentence once.

YES.

They closed the door long enough to pack.

Packing without speech made them slow and mean in small ways. Joel pointed at the food bag and Will thought he meant the filter. Will held up the map and Joel shook his head too hard because the road trace did not get another chance to lie. Will tried to strap Mike's repair kit to the outside of his pack one-handed and failed twice before Joel reached for it. Will jerked away, then held it out. Apology without apology.

The little economies of speech had held the group together for years. Move. Grab that. You got it? Quit blocking the path. Nice work, genius. Rudeness saved time and hid care. Without it, every offered hand looked for a second like control.

Joel wrote lists.

TAKE: FOOD / FILTER / MAP / COMPASS / KEYS / REPAIR / FIRST AID / HATCHET.

LEAVE: EXTRA POT / MIKE WET CLOTHES / BROKEN STRAP / CABIN JUNK.

Will wrote beside MIKE WET CLOTHES:

NOT JUNK.

Joel crossed out CABIN JUNK, leaving the clothes unmarked.

Will took Mike's knit cap from a nail by the stove. It was damp and smelled like smoke, sweat, and the sour old wool smell of trips. He held it once against his chest, then shoved it deep into his pack where Joel would not have to see it and where the woods could not see it either.

Joel did not stop him.

When Will fumbled the buckle afterward, Joel steadied the pack with two fingers instead of taking it. Will let him. The small permission passed between them without a word and left both men looking at the floor.

They needed to decide route.

Will wanted, by the look of him, to talk through every option. He even opened his mouth once and shut it hard, teeth clicking. Joel felt for him then, with a tiredness almost like love. Will's mind did not become still because the woods had made thinking dangerous. It only grew louder inside him.

Joel drew four crude lines on a page.

ROAD TRACE FORWARD.

STAY.

BACK TO CANOES.

CUT CROSS-COUNTRY.

Will took the pencil.

ROAD MAY HIT REAL ROAD.

Joel wrote:

OLD MAP. DARK SOON IF WRONG.

Will wrote:

IT IS MORNING.

Joel answered:

WE ARE SLOW.

Will looked at his bandaged hand.

Staying required no argument. Will crossed it out himself.

Cutting cross-country lasted longer. They both stared at it because it had the false cleanliness of a line on paper. A direct line from cabin to take-out, if a man ignored swamp, blowdown, water, distance, no trail, no exact bearing, injury, and the fact that the woods had already proven it could stand between sound and source. Joel wrote:

NO.

Will underlined it.

That left the road trace forward and back to the canoes.

The old road beyond the cabin might go somewhere human. It might become muskeg, vanish in second growth, cross private logging land, end at a washout, or loop toward a place no one had used in twenty years. Mike had needed it to mean exit. The map had needed nothing. It had carried an old mark forward until men mistook memory for promise.

The canoes were known. Damaged, awkward, frightening, but known. The cache had a location on ground their own feet had crossed. The water beyond it connected to the route. The planned route connected, eventually, to North Road Bridge and the Highlander key in Joel's pocket. The river did not become safe because it was mapped. It was only the exit whose dangers they had already touched.

Joel wrote:

BACK TO BOATS.

Will stared at the words.

Then:

MIKE SAID KEEP MOVING.

Joel understood the accusation. Mike had wanted movement. Mike had died moving. The woods had Mike's sentence now. Every practical act could sound like obedience to a dead man or to the thing wearing him.

Joel wrote:

WE CHOOSE DIRECTION BY SIGHT.

Will breathed through his nose. Once. Twice.

He took the pencil back and wrote:

THIS IS STILL YOU CHOOSING.

Joel almost crossed it out. The anger in him rose fast because it had nowhere else to go. There was no fair way to divide command now. Mike had died trying to lead. Will could barely close his injured hand around the pencil. Joel had the key. Someone had to point the next hour in a direction, and pointing looked like power even when it was only fear with work to do.

He wrote:

YES.

Then, because the word was too bare:

CHECK ME.

Will read that, then wrote:

HOW DO WE KNOW IT ONLY USES WHAT IT HEARD?

Joel almost wrote a clean answer. Because it repeated exact phrases. Because it misplaced context. Because it said "we're fine" when no one was fine, "map says there's a cabin" after the cabin had failed, "answer if seen" without knowing why the phrase mattered. Because if it could invent freely, they would already be dead.

Or maybe that was not evidence. Maybe it was only hope, pressed flat enough to look like a rule.

He looked at Will's question for a long time.

It was fair. It was Will. Even in ruin he wanted the terms exact.

Joel wrote:

WE DON'T.

Then he wrote what he had wanted to make true:

ONLY WHAT IT HEARD.

He stared at the line, then boxed it badly and added:

ASSUME. NOT PROOF.

Below that:

ACT LIKE IT.

Will read it. A sound came out of him before he could stop it. Almost a laugh. Almost not. It was ugly and small and human.

Joel looked toward the door.

No echo answered.

That felt like luck, and Joel did not trust any luck that noticed them.

While Will cinched the top of his pack, a strap creaked from the other side of the door.

Both men stopped.

The packs were in front of them. Will's was open between his knees. Joel's leaned against the stove with one shoulder strap twisted outward. Mike's remaining pack sat by the wall, flattened where they had taken the repair kit and food. Nothing outside should have had nylon under load. Nothing outside should have made the small complaint of webbing pulled through a buckle by a tired hand.

Joel waited for a voice to follow it.

No voice came. That was worse in its way. The thing did not need Mike for every order. It could use work. It could use men getting ready to leave.

They finished packing.

Before they left, Joel made one more page. The letters came out blocky and uneven, more sign than handwriting. He wrote large enough that Will could read without taking the notebook from him.

DO NOT ANSWER UNLESS SEEN.

NO NAMES.

NO HELP UNLESS BODY SEEN.

WRITE WHEN POSSIBLE.

IF VOICE WITHOUT BODY, STOP.

Will took the notebook and added:

NO THEORIES OUT LOUD.

Then, after a pause:

NO COMFORT OUT LOUD.

The second line hurt more. It sat there among the practical instructions like an exposed nerve.

Joel wanted to cross it out. He wanted to tell Will there were kinds of comfort the woods could not use. A hand on a shoulder. Food pushed toward him. A turn taken under a heavier pack. He wanted to say they were not empty just because they could not speak.

But the page was right. It removed what the woods could use.

He said nothing.

Will looked at him and understood enough. Joel put the page away before he could argue with it.

They moved the table aside.

The door opened on the clearing. Midmorning had come without warmth. Low cloud pressed the spruce tops. Rain had become mist and hung in the air rather than falling. The old roadbed ran away from the cabin in two directions: one toward the unknown forward trace where Mike had vanished, one back the way they had come, toward mud, blowdown, the cache, the canoes, water, all the work still required to be alive.

Joel stepped out first.

He kept the cabin at his back for only three seconds, then turned so Will could pass and so neither of them lost sight of the other. Will crossed the threshold with the hatchet in his left hand and his pack sitting wrong on one shoulder. He looked smaller under it. Or the pack looked larger now that there was no third man to divide the route.

Two men.

The phrase had seemed like inventory in the night. In daylight it became a condition. Every task took longer. One injured hand mattered. No one could scout. No one could watch both directions. No one could carry grief privately because the other man would see it bend him. But the group was not yet gone. A line still existed between bodies. Joel could look at Will and know one sound in the world had a source.

At the clearing edge, Will stopped and looked back.

The cabin stood as it had stood before they found it: useless, square, patient, almost human from the outside because men had made it and then left it for the weather. In daylight the open door looked less like shelter than a mouth that had failed to say anything helpful.

Joel touched Will's shoulder. A small thing. Not speech. Not comfort out loud.

Will whispered, "That's not how this should work."

Joel closed his eyes.

Will knew as soon as he said it. His face folded with self-disgust. He lifted his good hand to his mouth, too late.

For a moment there was only the mist. Water dripping from spruce. The soft complaint of Will's pack strap as he shifted under it. Joel could almost believe the sentence had gone no farther than the clearing.

Then from inside the cabin, in Will's own voice, came the same words, softer and cleaner.

"That's not how this should work."

Will went still.

Joel did not look into the doorway. He looked at Will. Body in sight. Source confirmed. The living Will stood in front of him, white around the mouth, breathing hard through his nose.

Joel opened the notebook and pointed to the first line.

DO NOT ANSWER UNLESS SEEN.

Will read it. He nodded once.

Behind them, from the cabin, Will's stolen voice repeated, patient as a lesson, "That's not how this should work."

They walked.


# Chapter 19 - Written Rules

Traveling silently made them bad at travel.

On water, men could point. On land, under packs, in brush, with footing bad and sightlines worse, speech was the rail between bodies. Step left. Duck. Wait. Loose rock. Your strap. Don't put your foot there. The words were not eloquent. They were not kind. They kept a man from putting his weight where another man had just learned not to.

Without speech they had hands, eyes, and the notebook. They had touch too, though touch had become strange. A hand on a sleeve could mean stop, help, wait, look at me, do not look there. It could also mean branch, strap, brush, something close enough to reach.

It should have been enough. Men had crossed worse country with less. Those men had practiced silence before it became punishment. Joel and Will had filled half their lives with correction, jokes, and small insults. They had built friendship out of interruption. Now interruption had teeth.

At the edge of the cabin clearing, before the trace closed around them again, Joel stopped Will with two fingers on his sleeve.

Will turned too fast, hatchet lifting in his good hand.

Joel shook his head and pointed down. Not danger. Stop.

Will understood on the second try. Understanding had become slower in him, not because he was stupid, but because every gesture had to compete with what his body wanted. Listen. Turn. Answer. Explain. His eyes kept trying to go past Joel into the trees, to the places where Mike's voice had been and might be again.

Joel took out the notebook.

The pages had swollen at the corners. Damp had made the pencil lines dark and slightly furred. The old grocery lists and route notes had become a different kind of document: record, rulebook, wall. Joel's thumb left a gray print beside Mike's name from the night before.

He wrote:

VISUAL BEFORE VOICE.

NO ANSWER FROM TREES.

NO NAMES.

POINT FIRST.

ONE TAP STOP.

TWO TAPS LOOK.

WRITE IF POSSIBLE.

SOURCE BEFORE MOVEMENT.

Will read the lines. His lips moved once, silently, on the words. Joel hated that, then hated himself for hating it. A man reading a page was not a man speaking to the woods. It was only a man trying to keep rules inside his head.

Will took the pencil.

NO THEORIES OUT LOUD.

He underlined it until the damp paper almost tore.

Joel nodded.

Will held up one finger and wrote again:

NO COMFORT OUT LOUD.

The line was already in the notebook from the cabin, but Will wrote it again as if the second copy could make it a law. He stared at the page afterward. The sentence embarrassed him. It embarrassed Joel too. Comfort had always come sideways: Alex making an ugly joke so no one had to admit the hill was brutal, Mike saying they were fine and making the lie useful for thirty seconds, Will explaining a knot no one had asked about because silence had gotten too honest.

Joel took the pencil back.

TOUCH OK.

Will read it. His face tightened. For a second Joel thought he would cross it out. Instead he gave one hard nod.

Joel added:

NOT FROM BEHIND WITHOUT SEEN.

Will stared at that. Then he nodded again, slower. The new rule ruined even the old grace of being guided by a hand between the shoulders. It made help announce itself like a stranger at a door.

Joel tore the page out carefully and handed it to him. Will folded the rule page into a small rectangle, put it in the chest pocket of his rain shell, under the zipper, then pressed the pocket flat. The gesture was nearly military. It would have made Alex say something. It would have made Mike call them idiots. It would have made Will, two days ago, ask whether the procedure card was waterproof.

The versions of them who could have made that joke were not available.

They moved.

The return trace looked clearer from the cabin clearing than it did inside the trees. That was one of the old map's small cruelties. From a distance, human work held its shape. Up close, the way back toward the canoe cache became roots, wet leaves, broken alder, stones slick under moss, and the low black claws of spruce catching straps and sleeves. The cabin was behind them now; the water route was somewhere below and ahead. Mike had read the line because Mike knew how men marked land. Joel had to read it by absence: younger trees, stones too flat to be accidental, the shallow trough of old wheels holding rain.

Will went first for the first twenty yards and then stopped, turned, and pointed at Joel with irritation.

Joel shook his head.

Will pointed at his own injured hand, then at Joel's pack, then at the hatchet.

Joel understood: if Will went first, he could not see Joel; if Joel went first, Will could use the hatchet and still be watched.

The old reflex rose in Joel. Fine. You go. Two words, small and practical. Maybe safe. But intention did not matter to sound.

Joel stepped ahead.

They settled into an order. Joel first, Will close enough that Joel could hear his breathing but not so close that one stumble would take them both down. Every fifteen steps Joel stopped and turned. Will had to be there. Not a voice. Not a cough. Not brush moving in his shape. A body, seen. Face, pack, hatchet, bandaged hand, the corner of the folded rule page making a pale square under his pocket flap. After every turn Joel felt for the Highlander key without taking his hand off the strap: still zipped, still hard, still not rescue.

After the third stop Will rolled his eyes.

Joel let him.

After the sixth stop Will stopped rolling his eyes.

The system failed first on a deadfall.

A spruce had come down across the trace at an angle, its top buried in alder and its trunk slick with wet bark. The way over was not hard if a man could say where to put his foot. Joel pointed to the root flare, then to the higher branch, then made a flat motion with his hand that meant low, duck, wait, maybe all three. Will chose duck. His pack caught on a broken limb and the strap jerked him backward. His injured hand flew out for balance and struck the trunk.

He did not cry out. That was the terrible part. His face went white and his mouth opened on nothing, the pain kept inside because even pain had become a sound to spend.

Joel climbed back over and reached for the pack strap. Will slapped his hand away, then looked ashamed before the slap had finished. Joel pointed to himself, to the strap, to Will's hand. Let me. Will closed his eyes once and turned enough for Joel to free the webbing.

The broken limb ticked against Will's pack as it came loose. A small clean sound, the kind of sound that should have belonged to the thing it touched. Both men looked into the brush beyond it.

Nothing answered.

They stood too long over a problem speech would have solved in two seconds, and the woods did not hurry them. That was almost worse.

The second failure came almost immediately, smaller and more dangerous because it looked like success. Joel tapped once on the back of Will's sleeve when a loose stone rolled under his own sandal. Stop. Will stopped. Then, because Joel had touched him from behind without showing his face, Will spun with the hatchet half-raised and terror stripped clean of thought.

Joel lifted both palms.

Will saw him and lowered the hatchet. His eyes went wet with fury, not at Joel only, but at the whole stupidity of needing a rule for the difference between help and ambush.

Joel opened the notebook against his thigh.

NO BACK TAP.

Will jabbed the pencil point under TOUCH OK until the paper dented.

Joel wrote:

ONLY SEEN.

Will looked at the two lines together: TOUCH OK. ONLY SEEN. Rules that corrected each other, weakened each other, made survival into footnotes. He gave the notebook back without writing.

The woods took them back without ceremony. The cabin disappeared behind brush and then behind the idea of itself. Rainwater held in the spruce boughs and let go when they brushed beneath. It fell cold down Joel's neck and into his collar. His socks were wet inside hiking sandals. Grit worked under the straps and rasped the skin raw at the sides of his feet. His hips hurt from the pack. His shoulders had a deep packed pain that no shifting fixed.

Daylight was better than night because they could see each other. It was worse because seeing proved how little there was to see.

Mike's voice followed after the first half hour, after the cabin had vanished behind enough wet spruce that turning back would already mean another decision.

"Just keep moving."

It came from behind them, not from the cabin exactly, but from the direction of the clearing. Low. Tired. Irritated with delay. It sounded like a man who had been given the wrong crew and was making the best of it.

Will stopped.

Joel turned and put two fingers to his own eyes, then pointed at Will. See me.

Will stared at him.

Mike said, closer this time, "Just keep moving."

Will's mouth folded inward. His good hand closed around the hatchet handle so hard the knuckles lost color.

Joel held the notebook up without opening it. The object itself had begun to mean the rule.

Will nodded once.

They moved.

The voice did not change when they failed to answer. That was part of its wrongness. No anger. No impatience. Only the same useful sentence, placed again where it might fit.

For a while Mike came only in fragments. A throat-clear from the low brush behind Joel's right shoulder. A small curse from the slope above them. The scuffing exhale Mike made when lifting a heavy pack. Once, from no clear direction, a zipper pull clicked against plastic, so much like Mike's torn PFD pocket that Joel's hand went to the Highlander key before he could stop himself.

The key was there. Hard against his ribs. Black fob. Bent metal. Torn orange nylon.

He left his hand over it for three steps, then made himself let go. If the woods had given the key back, it could make the key matter in his body every time he heard a zipper.

Will saw. He did not write anything. That was mercy.

The trace dropped into a shallow swale where water had gathered overnight. The mud was black and shining. Old leaves floated on it with their stems up like insect legs. Joel tested one side with his foot and sank to the ankle. Cold mud came in over the sandal strap. He pointed left. Will pointed farther left. Joel looked, saw the stones, and nodded.

They crossed one at a time.

On the far side, a sole scraped stone ahead.

Both men stopped.

The sound had been plain and close: rubber sole dragged over wet granite, a small correction of balance. Human. Ordinary. It came from thirty yards ahead where the trace bent around a knuckle of spruce and jack pine.

Joel looked back.

Will was behind him. Body in sight. Hatchet in hand. Breath pale in the cool air. Not moving.

The scrape came again.

Will lifted the hatchet slightly.

Joel shook his head.

He wrote on the open page:

SOLE SCRAPE AHEAD. NO BODY.

Will read it, then looked toward the bend.

The sound came again, but this time behind them.

Joel felt his shoulders rise, uselessly, as if he could make less of himself for the sound to hold. A scrape ahead meant someone in front. A scrape behind meant someone following. A scrape in both places meant the sound had come loose from walking.

Will wrote:

SOLE / NO BODY.

Then, under it:

SOUND DETACHED.

Joel understood the impulse. Will was trying to make a column out of terror. Gear, camp, voices, body. Things that belonged to things. Another time Joel would have mocked him for inventing categories before breakfast. Will would have said there was a difference between naming and taxonomy. Alex would have made the answer filthy. Mike would have told them to shut up and walk.

Joel wrote:

NO NEW SYSTEMS OUT LOUD.

Will's expression, for one second, was almost offended professional pride. Then it broke into something like grief and went flat again.

They went around the bend with the hatchet ready and the notebook open. Nothing waited there but stone, spruce roots, and water collecting in old tire ruts.

After that the sounds changed.

A pack shifted behind them when both packs were fixed on their backs. A dry bag buckle clicked from the brush where there was no dry bag. A branch cracked exactly when Joel looked down to place his foot. Twice a small twig snapped from just beyond Will's left shoulder, not loud enough to summon him, only loud enough to cost him attention. By then it did not need complete sentences. A half inch of turned head was enough.

Silence helped. Silence did not save them.

The woods still had the world's old language. Weight on moss. Water off leaves. Bark rubbed by wind. A bird going quiet too quickly. Something small moving in the litter. Real sounds and made sounds wore the same clothes. Joel had no tool for the difference except the slow animal work of looking.

Late morning thinned into noon without light changing much. The sky stayed low and pale. Mist caught in the trace and made distance uncertain. Every tree seemed to begin twice: once where it stood and once where the wet air repeated it.

They stopped beside a fallen spruce because Will's hand had bled through again.

Will tried to keep going. He made two short chopping gestures with the hatchet and then pointed ahead.

Joel pointed to the blood at his bandage.

Will looked at it as if it had betrayed him.

They chose the side of the spruce away from the open trace. Joel made Will sit with his back to the trunk and his face toward Joel. No one turned away. No one got privacy. Privacy had become another old luxury, like jokes, hot food, saying a friend's name because you missed him.

The bandage came loose wet and sticky. Will looked above Joel's head while Joel worked. The cut itself was not terrible by the old scale. A torn palm, swollen, dirty at the edges despite their rinsing. In another life it would have been urgent care, a tetanus booster, three jokes from Alex about Will finally using his hand for something, Mike calling it a princess wound, Joel telling Will to stop touching stuff.

Here, it was one more piece of the route trying to take time.

Joel rinsed it with clean water from the bottle and used more gauze than they could spare. The skin around the cut had puffed tight and shiny. Dirt held in the creases no matter how carefully he poured. Will breathed hard through his nose but did not make a sound. Pain had become safe if it stayed inside.

Joel tied the bandage with his fingers stiff from cold. He wanted to ask if it was too tight. The question rose as pressure in his mouth, the old practical kindness of checking work. He pressed the knot flat and looked at Will instead.

Will flexed two fingers, winced, and gave a small nod.

When Joel finished, Will took the notebook.

IT USES SEQUENCE.

Joel read it and looked up.

Will wrote beneath it:

COUGH THEN PHRASE.

BUCKLE THEN GEAR.

THROAT CLEAR THEN ORDER.

Then, after a pause:

LAUGH THEN ALEX.

His handwriting was worse with the injured hand, blockier, angled upward across the page.

Joel wrote:

MAYBE.

Will took the pencil back too quickly.

NOT MAYBE.

Then:

IT LEARNED ASSOCIATIONS.

Joel looked at the words, then at the trees. He could feel the shape of Will's thought. It was clean and dangerous. The thing did not merely repeat isolated sounds. It repeated neighbors. Mike's throat-clear came before certainty, and their bodies still prepared for an order. Buckles belonged to gear, food, movement. Will's cough belonged to explanation. Alex's laugh belonged to attention. Mike's reassurance belonged to the hard relief of letting another man decide.

Maybe it understood. Maybe it did not. Maybe association was not understanding.

Joel wrote:

ONLY WHAT IT HEARD.

He stared at the line after he wrote it. It was too certain.

He added:

WE THINK.

Will read both lines and looked at him for a long time.

Then he wrote:

IF IT LEARNS ASSOCIATION, WRITING IS DIFFERENT.

Joel waited.

Will added:

WRITING HAS NO TONE.

Joel nodded before he meant to. That was true. Written words were not safe because words were pure. They were safer because they arrived stripped. No throat-clear. No panic. No old affection curled around insult. No Mike making certainty feel like a hand on your back. No Alex turning risk into performance.

Writing removed the part of speech the thing used best. It also removed the part that made speech bearable.

Will wrote:

BUT WE MOVE AFTER WRITING.

Joel read the line twice.

Will added:

IT MAY LEARN THAT.

The page seemed colder in Joel's hands. He had wanted writing to be a sealed room. Will was saying the room had a door, and the door was their bodies. The danger might not be the marks. It might be what men did after making them: write stop, then stop; write down slow, then go down; write no answer, then hold their mouths shut.

Joel looked into the wet trees. No voice came from them. No buckle, no scrape, no cough. The absence did not answer the question. It only left them standing where a question had been asked.

Joel wrote:

GOOD.

Will took the pencil.

BAD ALSO.

Joel almost smiled, then did not. He tapped the line once.

YES.

Will closed his eyes for a moment. His face, dirty and drawn, looked younger with his eyes shut. Joel saw the boy from church basements and lock-ins, from bad carpet and orange drink, from Sunday mornings when adults talked about being called and Joel had already begun to suspect no one was answering. Will had always talked through discomfort. He had whispered jokes during prayer, corrected youth leaders on historical details no one cared about, coughed into his fist before saying something that made Joel shake with silent laughter.

Joel had loved him first as a voice.

That was the problem now.

Will opened his eyes and wrote:

IF IT CAN USE SEQUENCE, IT MAY UNDERSTAND MORE THAN REPEAT.

Joel wrote:

DON'T TEACH IT.

Will's anger came so fast it was almost a sound. His shoulders lifted. His mouth opened and closed. He pointed at the sentence, then at his own head, then at the trees. A furious little pant came through his nose.

Joel understood. Will was not trying to be reckless. He was trying to be useful in the only way that still felt like himself. Joel's rules were blunt instruments. Will wanted edges. He wanted to know whether they faced an animal, a system, a mind, or only a trap with inputs.

Joel wanted that too.

He wanted it less than he wanted Will alive.

He wrote:

THINK IT. DON'T SAY IT.

Will wrote, hard enough to splinter the pencil tip:

THEN HOW DO WE KNOW?

The broken point left a gray smear after the question mark. Will stared at it with a look of disgust so naked Joel almost reached for his wrist. How do we know. The question under every rule.

Joel took the pencil stub.

WE DON'T.

Then:

NO TEST UNLESS TEST SAVES US.

Will's mouth twisted. He wanted to argue the word saves. He wanted to ask whether knowing the thing's limit might save them later, whether ignorance was just another way to die. Joel could see the whole argument waiting behind his teeth.

Will did not let it out.

Will looked at the page. The anger did not leave, but it had nowhere to go. He handed back the pencil and let Joel help him stand.

They ate while walking. Half a crushed bar each, chewed without appetite. Joel's stomach took the sugar and stayed hollow. Will drank too fast and coughed once before he could stop himself.

The cough went out into the wet woods.

Both men froze.

For three seconds there was only the drip of spruce and their own breath.

Then Will's cough answered from ahead.

Not louder. Not exaggerated. Just the small dry correction of his throat, placed beyond the next bend where Will was not. The sound had none of his pain in it. That made it worse. Will with the inconvenience removed.

Will's eyes went wide with an insult so intimate Joel felt ashamed to see it.

Joel lifted the notebook.

Will shook his head once, hard, before Joel even opened it. I know.

The cough came again, ahead and slightly left.

Will pointed to himself, then to the bend, then made a little pulling motion with his fingers. Joel understood the shorthand: that sound was for him.

Joel nodded.

Then he pointed at Will's feet, then at his own eyes, then down the trace. Walk where I can see you.

Will swallowed. The motion looked painful. He fell in behind Joel.

For the next hour, the trace became an argument conducted entirely in muscle.

Will wanted to move faster. Joel slowed down whenever sightlines bent. Will wanted to test side openings, to see if the old trace had forked or if the water might be closer through the lower ground. Joel kept to the line they had walked yesterday unless he could see the way back to it. Will's frustration came through his hands: a sharp point, a flat palm, fingers spread in the universal sign for Are you serious. Joel's replies became equally spare: stop, wait, look, no.

They were worse without speech. Less kind. A spoken argument could soften itself with tone. A hand refusing another man's idea had no softness. It was only refusal.

Once, after Joel rejected a lower deer path that might have cut time, Will held the notebook against his knee and wrote:

MIKE WOULD CHECK.

Joel read it and felt the old heat rise behind his eyes.

He wrote:

MIKE DID.

Will stared at the words. Joel regretted them at once and did not cross them out. The sentence was cruel and true. Written words removed tone, but they did not remove damage. They could still strike exactly where a voice would have softened itself.

Will looked away first.

Joel added:

STAY ON TRACE UNTIL WATER.

Will nodded without looking at him.

The trace climbed. The ground became stone under thin soil, then dropped through a stand of young birch where yellow leaves had begun to turn early from stress. The leaves trembled although there was little wind. Joel kept waiting for the smell of the forced shore, the dark water below it, the bough-covered shape of the canoe cache. Boats meant danger, yes. Open water, fog, wind, hull knocks from nowhere. But boats were objects. They obeyed weight and balance. They moved if paddled and sat if dragged ashore. Even damaged, they belonged to weight and leverage.

Behind him, Will shifted under his pack. Mike's cap was inside it now, a soft wrong weight Joel could not see but kept remembering. The dead had become inventory too.

The trace gave them no water.

It gave them a raven instead, somewhere above the trees, its call cracking open the low sky. Once, twice. Ordinary enough. Then, after a pause, a third call came from ground level to Joel's right.

Will did not turn.

Joel did, half an inch, before he caught himself.

The raven call from the brush repeated. It was good. Too good for comfort. It had the rough wooden quality of the bird, the little downward break at the end. But it came from under the spruce, at the height of a man's chest.

Joel wrote:

BIRD SOUND WRONG HEIGHT.

Will read it, then surprised him by writing:

DON'T NEED TO CLASSIFY EVERY ONE.

Joel looked at him.

Will's mouth twitched, bitterly. He tapped Joel's earlier line: DON'T TEACH IT.

Fair, Joel thought.

He did not write fair.

They went on.

By midafternoon the day had narrowed to the next twenty feet. Joel stopped thinking in miles. He thought in visible ground. Rock to root. Root to mud. Mud to the dark gap between alders. Stop. Turn. Confirm Will. Move.

Fatigue made the rules harder, not easier. A tired body loved sound because sound was faster than sight. It could locate without turning. It could believe before checking. Every human habit favored the thing. When Will's pack strap slipped, Joel almost said, Strap. When his own foot slid on wet stone, his throat made the beginning of a warning before he swallowed it. The unsaid words collected inside him. Silence was not emptiness. It was a room filling with voices that wanted out.

Once, Will stumbled.

Joel heard the wet skid behind him and turned in time to see Will go to one knee. The hatchet hit stone with a flat metal tick. Will's injured hand landed in mud. His face went white.

Joel moved toward him.

From the trees behind Will, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will's head jerked.

Joel lunged and caught his shoulder before the turn became a step.

For one instant Will fought him. Not hard, not knowingly, but with the blind force of a body hearing a dead friend exactly where a dead friend should not be. Alex's voice had come with effort in it, that old half-laughing complaint he used when he wanted everybody to wait and watch him make an inconvenience entertaining.

"Hold up," Alex said again, farther back.

Will made a sound into his closed mouth. It broke there.

Joel turned him physically until Will faced him. He put both hands on Will's shoulders, then pointed at his own face. Here.

Will's eyes found him.

Joel pointed at Will's chest. Here.

Alex laughed once from the brush. A hard bark, too bright in the damp air.

Will's eyes filled. He shut them and opened them again.

Joel opened the notebook against Will's chest and wrote awkwardly, the pencil bucking over the damp page:

NOT ALEX.

Will nodded.

Joel added:

OLD HABIT.

Will looked at the line and put his muddy good hand over it for one second. Not to hide it. To hold it down.

After that, they stopped less often but looked more carefully. Their world had become two linked circles of sight. Joel's around the trace ahead. Will's around Joel's back. When one circle bent, the other tightened.

The old trace began to smell different near late afternoon.

At first Joel thought it was only wet cedar giving way to alder. Then came the mineral smell of standing water, cold and iron-dark. The ground softened. The old tire troughs filled and joined. Through the trees, low to the left, light changed. Not brightness exactly, but an opening. Sky reflected somewhere it should not have been if they were still high on the trace.

Water.

The word rose in Joel with such force that he almost said it.

He stopped instead and pointed.

Will came even with him, close enough that their sleeves touched. He saw the paler break through the trees and shut his eyes for one beat. Relief moved over his face before he could discipline it. Not happiness. Only the body understanding that one form of work might soon become another.

Joel wrote:

WATER CLOSE. CACHE MAYBE.

Will took the pencil.

OR WRONG WATER.

Joel nodded. Good. Painful, but good.

The trace forked badly there, or seemed to. One path continued down toward the low shine. Another held higher through spruce and might have been the line they had followed up from the cache. A third was not a path at all, only brush bent by weather, animal movement, or something making a suggestion.

Joel crouched and looked at the ground. Mud held too many marks and not enough. Their own tracks from yesterday should have been there, but rain had softened every edge. Mike's tread, Will's sandals, Joel's sandals: all of it had become pressure and water. He found one cut stem where Mike had cleared brush with the hatchet on the way in. He found a smear of gray mud on a stone at knee height where a pack might have rubbed. He found nothing that felt like proof.

Will pointed to the higher trace, then to the notebook.

Joel shook his head and pointed to the cut stem.

Will pointed down toward the shine. His hand trembled with fatigue. Water meant canoes, and canoes meant the planned route, but water also carried sound better than trees did. The low place ahead might be the cache. It might be a flooded slough a hundred yards off the route. It might be the same false logic the cabin had used: a true feature, the wrong salvation.

Joel wrote:

CUT STEM. PACK SMEAR. DOWN SLOW.

Will read it, then wrote:

IF VOICE SAYS DOWN?

Joel answered:

NO.

Will wrote:

IF VOICE SAYS UP?

Joel almost crossed that out. Instead he wrote:

NO.

Will tapped both answers with the pencil: the first useful thing the day had given them was not a direction, but the refusal to let sound vote.

Behind them, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

Will stopped so hard Joel heard his pack settle.

Joel did not move.

The words had come from behind and slightly uphill, on the trace they had just descended. Low, flat, practical. Not a scream. Not a plea. Not even fear. A small caution at a landing. A man stopping another man from shifting a canoe too soon. Joel knew the phrase before memory supplied the scene: South Access, gravel under the hull, Alex laughing, Mike impatient, Will bent over a strap, the truck still visible through black spruce. Day 1, before anything had yet become evidence. Joel's hand on the canoe, his own voice saying it once.

Wait. Just wait.

His voice sounded younger from the trees.

Not much younger. Only less used.

Will turned slowly toward Joel, not toward the sound.

That saved him.

Joel could see the calculation in Will's face. Body in sight. Source confirmed. The living Joel was in front of him, crouched by the fork with the notebook in one hand and the pencil in the other. The voice behind them was therefore not Joel.

The rule held. Joel did not.

Until that moment he had kept one private error alive: that he was mostly witness, mostly recorder, a man who had given the woods less. Alex had been grief. Mike had been authority. Will was panic and explanation. Joel was the one writing it down. He had mistaken quiet for withholding. The woods did not need a man to be loud. It needed him present.

Now his own voice stood behind him on the trace.

His hand began to shake.

The Highlander key pressed against his chest when he breathed. He had thought of it all morning as the human object, the real thing at the end of the line: truck, ignition, road, phone signal, names restored to official use. But the voice behind him reached back before the key, before rules, before Alex's death, before Mike's failed cabin. It reached to the put-in, to the ordinary threshold where Joel had believed danger had not yet learned them.

Will saw it.

Will took one step toward him, then stopped, asking permission without words.

Joel wanted to say no. He wanted to keep his fear private because privacy felt like the last place a voice could not enter.

He nodded.

Will put his good hand on Joel's shoulder. The pressure was clumsy, too hard, then lighter. Comfort without sound. It did not fix anything. It did not become language. For that reason Joel let it stay.

From uphill, in Joel's voice, came again, "Wait. Just wait."

The timing was wrong now. Too patient. A phrase made for one second stretched into a rule.

Joel looked at Will. Will looked back.

Neither answered.

Joel opened the notebook. The page blurred once and cleared.

He wrote with a hand that did not feel fully attached:

IT HEARD ME TOO.

Will read the line. He did not write comfort under it. He did not write NOT YOU or ONLY SOUND or anything meant to make the sentence smaller. That restraint was the kindest thing he could do.

Instead he touched two fingers to his own eyes, pointed at Joel, then pointed down toward the cut stem and the low shine of water.

See. Move.

Joel nodded.

He folded the page once and put it away. His own voice waited uphill with the patience of a tool left where a man had dropped it.

They went down toward the water by sight, not by sound. Joel kept the cut stem on his left, the gray smear on the stone ahead, Will's body in the corner of his eye. Behind them, his voice did not plead or rage. It only gave the same small order again, practical and useless.

"Wait. Just wait."


# Chapter 20 - His Own Voice

After Joel heard himself, the trip would not stay in order.

Until then, some private part of him had kept a boundary. The thing belonged to Alex's death, to Mike's mistake, to Will's noise, to the brush around Second Camp. It began where the world had broken badly enough to deserve it. A man could bear almost anything if he could draw one line through time and keep one side clean.

His voice erased the line.

It had been near from the beginning, or near enough. At the landing. At the first water. It had been present while they made ordinary mistakes and called them ordinary. It had heard a small caution from a quiet man at a gravel put-in, and it had kept it. The week did not begin with Alex stepping past the light. It reached back and put a hand on the first boat, the first strap, the first harmless correction.

Joel felt the theft in his mouth. Not in memory only. In the hinge of his jaw, in the place where a word gathered before it became air. He had believed, stupidly and without admitting it, that quietness left less of a man outside himself. Now even restraint had a sound. Even being careful could be stored.

Will kept looking at him as if Joel might explain what a man was supposed to do with the sound of himself.

Joel had no explanation. He had the notebook, the pencil, the Highlander key zipped against his chest, and mud drying in a hard crust along the backs of his hands.

They stayed crouched at the bad fork too long. The lower path shone with water through alder. The higher trace vanished and returned in pieces. Behind them, uphill, his own voice had gone quiet. That did not help. Silence after a voice was not absence anymore. It was only a place a voice might return from.

Will took the pencil carefully, the way a man might take a blade from a child. His injured hand made his grip awkward. He wanted to comfort; Joel could see it in the way he leaned toward the page before he had words. Then he wrote:

NOT YOU.

Joel looked at the words.

Will underlined them once, then added:

ONLY SOUND.

Joel felt something rise hard enough that it almost became speech. No. Don't. Stop making it smaller. Stop trying to make a lie safer by putting it in pencil.

His mouth opened.

Will saw it and went still.

Joel shut his mouth so sharply his teeth clicked. The sound seemed enormous, a hard little snap in the wet air. Both men listened after it, because even a tooth click had become information. It had come from him. That was not the same as safe.

He took the notebook back and wrote:

USEFUL SOUND.

Then, because that was not enough:

WORSE.

Will read it and did not argue. The mercy cost him. Joel could see it in the muscles at his jaw, in the way his eyes wanted to move past the page and make a theory out of everything. He wanted to say that sound was not self, that repetition was not presence, that a copy did not own the thing copied. He was probably right. It did not help.

Joel pointed to the lower path, then held up one hand. Wait by sight. He crouched and studied the mud.

The rain had taken most of yesterday. Their tracks had come down this way or not. Mike had led. Will had followed, talking then because they still allowed talking, because the cabin had still been a possible answer, because Alex had been dead but Mike alive. That had seemed like a different kind of world. Joel looked for Mike's boot tread and found only oblong pressures filled with brown water. He looked for the diagonal scratch of Will's sandals and found roots, leaf rot, and a black feather stuck to mud by its own wet barbs.

The feather made him remember the raven at the put-in, its call breaking off over the trees while Alex snapped a strap and said something ugly about wilderness ambiance. He hated how quickly memory began to revise itself. The old landing, the first portage, the joke about poems, the food barrel latch, the hollow knock they had blamed on rope: all of it rearranged itself under the pressure of his voice from the trees.

Nothing in the past had changed. Everything had.

Will touched Joel's sleeve and pointed.

On the left edge of the lower path, a cedar branch had been cut clean through at knee height. The cut was pale under rain, not fresh-white, not old. Hatchet work. Mike's work, probably. Yesterday, on the way in, he had been making the old trace more real by cutting what resisted it.

Joel nodded. Lower.

They moved toward the water.

The ground softened within twenty yards. Mud took their footprints and folded them into itself. Alder crowded the path and slapped water onto their sleeves. Joel went first, then stopped, turned, and confirmed Will by sight. Will had stopped rolling his eyes at the checks. He endured them now with a concentration that looked almost angry. Joel understood. To be watched every fifteen steps was to be reminded every fifteen steps that the living body had to keep proving itself.

Once, from behind them, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

It was placed well. Not too close. Not theatrical. The exact distance a man might stand behind another on a bad portage, asking him not to shift a canoe too soon.

Joel did not turn.

Will did. Not toward the voice, but toward Joel.

That was the rule doing its work. It should have comforted him. Instead it made him jealous for one stupid second. Will trusted the procedure faster than he trusted Joel's face. The page had become a third body between them, thinner than a man and colder, but harder to imitate.

Joel lifted two fingers to his eyes. Will nodded.

They kept going.

The water smell strengthened. It was not open lake, not wind and distance. It was narrow water, tannin and mud, old leaves soaking under alder roots. The kind of channel that took a canoe because it had to, not because it welcomed one. Through the trees came the dull shine of it, broken by black stems. No wind reached them there. Sound did not travel far so much as stay trapped.

Joel had imagined the canoe cache as a return to objects. Two boats, gear under spruce, the route reduced again to weight and balance. He had wanted it with a child's stupid faith: reach the canoes and one kind of terror would end. A canoe was not a voice. A pack did not ask to be believed. Rope held or failed in the hand.

But objects could be moved. They could say where a person had been without proving who had touched them. A knot could be evidence or bait or only a knot. Things were safer than speech because they resisted being borrowed, not because they refused.

The cache had not been destroyed. That would have been easier to understand.

For three seconds Joel thought they had missed it. Then he saw the split granite face, the three dead spruce in a row, the bent cedar Mike had marked on the map. He saw the low roof of boughs they had made over the gear. He saw the dark line where the better canoe's hull should have been hidden under spruce.

The line was wrong.

He stopped so abruptly Will bumped his pack.

Will's good hand came up, caught Joel's strap, and then released. He looked over Joel's shoulder.

The damaged canoe was still under spruce boughs near the high-water line, the aluminum patch on its thwart dull through the green. The boughs over it had been moved. Not torn off. Not scattered. Moved aside and laid in a fan on the mud, their cut ends roughly aligned. The order was crude enough to be accident and deliberate enough to hurt. It looked less like an animal's rummaging than a poor memory of how men arranged things.

The better canoe had been dragged six or seven feet from where they had left it. It sat half out of brush, angled toward the water, its keel resting in a fresh smear of mud. The painter rope ran from its front handle to an alder stem and looped twice around the trunk. The knot at the end was neither a bowline nor a trucker's hitch nor any of Will's tidy canoe knots. It was a hard, wet clot of rope, ugly and effective.

Joel could hear Will breathing through his nose.

The food barrel was where it had been, hung badly and too low from a spruce limb because none of them had cared about animal standards when the cabin had still existed as hope. Its blue side showed through brush. The black lid ring appeared closed.

One dry bag had been pulled partly from under the gear tarp and left in the mud. It was the long one with the tent bodies and some clothing, the one they had decided was too heavy to carry inland and not important enough to guard. Mud covered one end. Water had pooled around the roll-top. The bag had not been opened. That almost made it worse. Disturbance without appetite. Contact without need.

The map case lay near the food barrel with grit stuck along the seal, its clear plastic filmed brown at the edges. Joel could see the folded map inside, softened at one corner, its printed blue water darkened by damp.

The rod tube lay beside it.

Will stepped forward.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Will's face came around fast. For a moment Joel thought he would wrench free. Not because of the canoe. Because of the rods. They were a piece of Will from before: his hands working line through guides, his voice explaining why a lake with no visible structure might still hold fish along the downwind shore, his body made quiet by attention rather than fear. Fishing gave his talking a place to go. It made waiting active.

Joel had mocked him for it often enough. Not cruelly, or not only cruelly. Back at First Camp, with Alex still complaining about the food and Mike making the stove sound like a performance review, Will had stood ankle-deep at the granite edge and cast three times into dark water as if the lake had asked a question in a language he could almost read. Joel had thought then that Will looked most like himself when no one was demanding an answer.

Joel shook his head and pointed to the ground between them.

Stop. Look first.

Will swallowed. He nodded once, too hard.

They stood at the edge of the cache and inspected it like men approaching a bad landing in high water: slow, humble, expecting the ordinary thing to punish speed.

No tracks made sense. Their own prints from yesterday had been softened by rain and overrun by new disturbances: long smears in mud where the canoe had dragged; two round punched places that might have been the ends of branches; shallow half-moons from Will's sandals or Joel's or Mike's, all blurred into each other. Near the alder stem, the mud held a line of compressed grass as if someone had knelt, or as if the rope had been pulled low. Joel wanted a category. Bear, person, water, wind. The mud refused all of them and kept the marks. He did not write any of it down. A written fact still needed a fact under it.

Joel pointed to the better canoe. Then to Will. Then made a slow circling motion with one finger: inspect.

Will shook his head and pointed at Joel.

Joel understood. He was in back. He would steer. He needed to decide if the hull was trustworthy.

They approached together.

The rope knot tightened when Joel put weight on it. It had been tied by force, not skill. The painter had bitten into the alder bark. Two loops crossed each other, then a series of jammed half-turns had been pulled down until the wet rope hardened. A human hand could have done it in panic. Mike could have done it with one glove on and no patience. Alex could have made worse and called it fine. Will would never have left such a thing unless he wanted to make a point about other people's standards.

That was the worst part. It was not impossible enough. It sat inside the edge of things a frightened person might do badly.

Will crouched beside it, touched one finger to a crossing loop, and stopped. His mouth shaped, impossible to stop, Can it--

Joel put two fingers hard against Will's shoulder.

Will closed his mouth.

The question hung between them anyway.

Can it tie knots? Can it understand what tied means? Can a trap still be a trap if it only knows pressure, rope, and waiting?

Joel took the notebook out.

DOESN'T MATTER.

He added:

CUT IT.

Then, because Will was still looking at the knot as if knowledge might come out of it, Joel wrote one more line.

NO TESTS.

Will stared at the page a second longer than necessary. Then he took the hatchet from his belt.

Cutting instead of untying felt wasteful and correct. They had begun the trip as men who preserved rope, packed spare cord, debated knots, saved lengths that might be useful later. Now Joel held the painter taut against the alder while Will chopped carefully through the outer loop. The first strike thudded into wet fiber. The second opened white strands. The third severed the line and left the knot clamped to the tree like a fist.

Will looked at the cut end, then at the knot left behind. His face had the bright, sick look of a man refusing to ask a good question.

The loose rope fell into mud.

From the water, behind the reeds, came a hollow knock.

Both men froze.

The canoe was not loaded yet. The damaged canoe sat under boughs. The better canoe lay at their feet. No hull moved.

Another knock came from farther out, beyond the first bend of the channel where fog had collected in a low seam above the water.

It was the old sound. Hull on stone. Paddle against gunwale. Rope tapping aluminum. Ordinary enough to have a dozen causes until all the causes were in sight and still.

Will wrote with the pencil braced against his thigh:

BOAT SOUND / NO BOAT.

Joel nodded.

Then the food barrel latch clicked.

The barrel hung fifteen feet away, visible through spruce, black ring closed, blue body swaying not at all.

Click.

Will turned his head only far enough to see the true barrel. His discipline had become painful to watch.

Click.

The sound came now from behind the damaged canoe.

Joel wrote:

BARREL VISIBLE.

Will tapped the line once. Yes. I know. The tap left a crescent of mud on the paper.

They worked.

Work was the only answer that did not add material. Joel checked the better hull first. No crack he could see. No puncture. No new gouge deep enough to matter. He ran his hands along the outside while Will held the boat tilted. Cold water spilled from a shallow pocket near the seat and ran over Joel's knuckles. The caned front seat had one broken strand. The back seat was sound. The thwarts held. The shortened painter could be retied later, badly but by them.

He made himself check slowly. The urge was to hurry because every second at the cache felt like standing in a doorway with his back exposed. Hurry would miss the split seam, the loose seat bolt, the hairline crack that became a sinking boat a half mile from shore. Fear wanted speed. Water wanted accuracy.

The damaged canoe was worse. Mike's repair on the cracked thwart still held when Joel pressed it, but the aluminum splint had shifted and one wrap of cord had loosened. Rainwater had collected along the keel line. A small gray leaf had plastered itself over a dent in the aluminum, and for one stupid second Joel did not want to touch it. It would float. It might carry gear. It might fail under portage strain or current. Two men with two canoes made less sense than two men in one boat, but one boat meant all weight, all risk, all hope in a single hull.

Joel set his palm on the better canoe.

Will looked at him.

Joel held up one finger.

One boat.

Will looked at the damaged canoe, the gear, the fog, the channel. His face did not protest. His body did. He shifted once as if the old argument had entered his knees. Two boats gave redundancy. Two boats let one man survive if the other went wrong. Two boats also meant more loading, more rope, more distance between them, more turns of the head, more sounds made by things they could not see at the same time.

Will took the notebook.

ONE BOAT = IF LOSE IT WE DIE.

Joel wrote:

TWO BOATS = WE SEPARATE.

Will read it. His mouth went tight.

Joel added:

STAY VISIBLE.

Will nodded.

They made a pile of what would go. The food barrel first, because hunger was not optional and because leaving it would make every later mile imaginary. The cached food bags. Filter. Stove and one pot. First aid. Tarp. Sleeping bags, one still damp enough to weigh like an animal. Mike's repair kit. The hatchet. Two paddles and the cracked repaired paddle as spare. The map case went flat on top though grit had gotten into the seal and the paper inside felt damp at the edges. The notebook stayed inside Joel's jacket when he did not need it, buttoned under the rain shell. The Highlander key stayed in his inner pocket, zipped behind fabric, checked once by touch and then not touched again.

Every decision reduced four men to two. It was not sentimental math. It was volume and weight. One pot. One tarp. Two bags. One boat. The extra things had once meant comfort, redundancy, preference, personality. Now extra meant time at the shore.

They left the mud-soaked tent bag. Too heavy. Too wet. Too much time. They left extra clothes that were no longer extra in any meaningful sense. They left the extra cookware, a small sack of trash, the bent tent stakes, and a spoiled snarl of cord too short to matter. Joel found Alex's cracked headlamp in the small bag of remaining things and wrapped it in a shirt because light still made an argument for itself. The hot sauce bottle stayed with Alex's odds. So did the wrapper, the dead batteries, the useless pocket trash no one could make meaningful or throw away. The abandoned pile looked at once excessive and poor, the remains of four men measured against what two men could lift.

Will picked up the rod tube last.

Joel looked away at first, giving him privacy he knew was false. Privacy required a world where a man could step ten feet off and still remain himself. Still, Joel turned to the canoe and tightened a strap that did not need tightening.

Behind him, plastic scraped mud.

Will had opened the tube and taken out the two rods. One had a fine crack near the second guide. The other looked all right. He held them awkwardly with his bandaged hand, their tips trembling in the damp air. The reels had grit in them. Line clung in wet loops.

For one moment, with the rods in his hand, Will looked almost normal. Not better. Not safe. But placed in a shape Joel recognized. A man at a lake, making small decisions about weight and lure and angle. A man who would say, quietly and with too much precision, that fishing was not about catching fish, not exactly. It was about holding an instrument in the water and waiting without speech.

Will looked at the channel.

The channel gave them nothing but fog and the second hollow knock, softer now, from where no canoe moved.

Will closed the rod tube.

He did not ask.

He laid the rods under the damaged canoe they were abandoning and covered them with the same boughs that had been moved aside. His hand worked slowly because of the bandage. He put one spruce bough, then another, then corrected the first because a tip still showed. The care did more damage than a sob would have. Will could leave them, but he could not leave them exposed. Even now he wanted a thing done properly where proper no longer helped.

When he finished, he stayed crouched.

Joel waited.

Will took the notebook and wrote:

TELL ME THIS IS NOT STUPID.

The sentence sat there, too human and too dangerous even in pencil.

Joel wanted to write, It isn't. He wanted to write, You can buy new rods. He wanted to write some ordinary lie about objects and survival and later. But later had become a superstition, and the rods were not the point.

He wrote:

IT COSTS.

Will read it. His face changed once and closed.

He wrote:

YES.

He tore that small corner of page away, folded it once, and put it into the rod tube before he slid the tube under the canoe. Joel did not stop him. A note that said only yes could not help the rods. It could mark that someone had known what was being left.

They loaded the canoe low. Food barrel near the center. Heavy bag against it. Sleeping bags compressed badly under the thwart. Map case flat and reachable. Alex's cracked headlamp went in Joel's smaller pack, wrapped in the shirt. Mike's cap was already deep in Will's pack, unseen but present in the way kept things were. Hatchet tucked beside Will but not under anything. Spare paddle lashed where Joel could see it. The small blue dry bag stayed under the front seat with the emergency paddle inside it. Every buckle they closed sounded too loud. Every strap pull was a small confession. The thing had all these sounds already. That did not make giving them again feel harmless.

From the alder behind the cache, Mike cleared his throat.

Will did not look.

Joel did, because the sound went through him before the rule caught it. Only a quick glance. Brush. Wet black stems. No body. The glance cost nothing and everything. It proved again that his body still belonged to the old world first.

"We're fine," Mike said.

Joel's hand closed on the canoe gunwale.

The words had once been useful even when false. We're fine. Almost there. Weather's moving off. This will hold. Now the phrase had no weight behind it. It wore Mike's mouth and nothing else.

Will touched Joel's sleeve. Two fingers. Gentle.

Joel looked back at him.

Will pointed at the notebook.

Joel opened it, but Will took the pencil.

NOT MIKE.

Then, below that:

NOT JOEL.

Joel stared at the second line.

Will underlined nothing. He did not try to comfort him again. He had learned that distinction at least: the page could mark fact without pretending fact was repair.

Joel touched the second line with one muddy finger. NOT JOEL. The page did not absolve him. It only refused the voice a body.

Joel tapped both lines.

They carried the canoe to water.

The launch took too long. Everything silent took too long. Joel held the back while Will steadied the front with one good hand and one bandaged hand wrapped in plastic. The mud sucked at Joel's sandals. Cold water entered over the straps and took the heat from his feet at once. The canoe rocked under the weight. The food barrel shifted. Joel stopped it with his knee and almost said wait.

The word rose in his throat with the exact shape the woods already had.

He swallowed until his throat hurt.

Behind them, from the cache, his own voice said, "Wait. Just wait."

This time it sounded nearer. Not better. Nearer.

Will's eyes went to Joel's face. Not to the voice. The rule again, working and cold.

Joel shook his head once.

Together they pushed the canoe free.

A rope tapped somewhere in the fog. Then another tap, nearer the far bank. The shortened painter lay visible across the front pack, wet and muddy and theirs. The sound went on anyway, a patient little knock against nothing they could see.

Will climbed in first, awkward and careful, placing his knees and then sitting low in the front. Joel held the canoe while it tried to turn broadside. He looked once at the cache: damaged canoe under spruce, rod tube hidden, wet gear abandoned, the strange knot still clenched around the alder stem, the cut rope hanging dark and wet from it. Their decisions were already losing their shape.

The food barrel latch clicked from the trees.

Joel stepped in.

The canoe dipped, steadied, and took them.

For a moment they drifted without paddling. The channel ahead was narrow and dark. Fog took the far bank in pieces and gave it back as alder, then shadow, then nothing. Behind them the cache disappeared faster than it should have, not by magic, only by the ordinary fact of bends and brush and water accepting whatever men pushed onto it. The abandoned canoe, the hidden rods, the knot clenched on alder bark: all of it was gone before Joel stopped listening for the latch.

Joel lifted his paddle.

Will turned enough for Joel to see his face.

No words.

Joel set the blade in the water, and the canoe moved.


# Chapter 21 - Bad Boat

The bad boat was not bad enough to abandon and not good enough to trust.

For the first fifty yards after the cache, Joel kept waiting for the decision to prove itself wrong.

He sat in back with the Highlander key zipped inside his jacket and the shortened painter coiled where he could see it. Will sat in front, shoulders high, his injured hand wrapped in plastic and taped at the wrist. The bag had gone cloudy from sweat and seeped water. It flashed white whenever he lifted the paddle, a small surrender flag no one had asked for.

The canoe rode low.

Food barrel just ahead of center. Tarp and damp sleeping bags wedged under the middle thwart. Map case flat against the ribs, grit still caught in the seal. Stove, pot, filter, first-aid kit, Mike's repair kit. Hatchet by Will's right knee. Spare paddle lashed on Joel's side where his hand could find it without searching. The small blue dry bag stayed under the front seat, not yet important except as one more shape that had to remain accounted for. Every object had been chosen against another object. Everything they carried made the boat ride worse. Everything left behind made the future smaller.

The hull accepted the weight and settled into the tannin-dark channel.

Only three or four inches of aluminum showed above the waterline at midship. A split seam would have decided for them. A cracked seat, a hole drinking water from the first push, a thwart giving under Will's hand. Instead the canoe floated, heavy and reluctant. It did not consent. It only held.

Will looked back once.

Joel held one hand palm down.

Easy.

Will nodded and faced forward.

They had scratched a system into the notebook at the cache. One deliberate tap on the hull meant stop. Two meant look. A flat hand meant wait. A pointed finger meant direction or danger or that thing, not the other. A fist meant hold. Two fingers to the eyes meant confirm by sight. Hand to chest meant me. They had written the signs down, though both men knew the body had its own older language.

The old canoe language was gone. Draw. Back. Left. Right. Hold up. Watch that rock. Wait.

They had used those words for years without gratitude. Now each missing word made the boat harder to keep alive.

Silence made safety expensive. It removed the small corrections men used before danger became visible and left them with gestures broad enough to arrive late.

Joel dipped his paddle and corrected the back end. Will pulled once on the right. The front nosed toward a black stitch of open water between alder stems. Fog lay low over the channel, not hiding everything, only making pieces of it. Bank, then nothing. Spruce root, then water. A pale stick floating where a rock should have been.

Behind them, at the cache, the food barrel latch clicked.

Joel kept paddling.

Will's shoulders lifted, then settled. He did not turn. The blue barrel sat in the canoe in front of Joel, black ring closed, side pressed against a wet dry bag. Visible. Accounted for. Heavy as fact.

Click.

Joel watched the barrel instead of the brush. It did not move.

Click.

Will tapped the gunwale once with two fingers. Not the signal. Reflex. He caught himself and put his hand back on the shaft as if the hull had burned him.

The channel bent left. Alder brushed both sides. Branches stroked the gunwales with small dry sounds, though the leaves were wet and shining. The canoe moved slowly because speed made noise and noise forced decisions. Joel felt every scrape in his knees. Twice he looked down to see whether the bottom had flexed or only sounded as if it had. The boat was no longer only an object beneath them. It was a claim they had to keep proving.

He had trusted boats before this trip the way he trusted floors. They held a body above what would otherwise take it. Even when they rocked, even when they shipped water, even when Alex did some showy incompetent thing in front and laughed at his own balance, the boat had remained a boat. Shape, weight, skill, buoyancy.

Now it was one thin hull between two men and the cold fact beneath them. One boat. One route. One key in Joel's pocket. The arithmetic was simple and indecent.

The first knock came from ahead.

Not loud. A hollow tap of wood or aluminum, placed somewhere beyond the fogged bend.

Will stopped paddling.

Joel let the canoe glide. He lifted his blade and held it over the water until drops fell from the tip. Drip. Drip. Drip. Each drop made a small ring, then no evidence.

The knock came again, farther right.

There could have been another canoe beyond the bend. A submerged branch tapping stone. A loose paddle in a boat they had not seen. A fisherman who would look at them and understand none of it but still have a radio. Hope came now as a practical possibility, small enough to disguise itself as responsibility.

Joel touched two fingers to his eyes and pointed at Will.

Will looked back. Visible.

Joel pointed ahead, then held up his flat hand. Wait by sight.

They drifted until the current gave out against a mat of drowned grass. The bend opened. No canoe. No person. No bright hull pulled into reeds. Only a fallen spruce leaning from the far bank, root mass black and wet, and a crescent of foam caught against sticks.

The knock came once more from behind them.

Will's mouth tightened.

Joel shook his head.

They moved on.

In ordinary travel one word from the back corrected a bad angle before it became a problem. One curse made a submerged rock visible to the man in front. A laugh turned error into nothing. Without that, the canoe became stubborn and literal. It found every shallow, every hidden branch, every place where two bodies had to know the same thing at the same time and could not say it.

Will's hand cost them time. He could paddle with it, but he could not grab quickly. When the canoe slid toward a snag, his good hand crossed awkwardly over the bad, and the plastic bag squeaked against the shaft. Each time he looked down as if betrayed by a tool that had worked yesterday.

Joel knew that look. He had seen it in church basements when a model rocket failed to launch, in Will's first apartment when a chess game turned because he had missed an obvious fork, on lake trips when a knot held badly after he had told everyone else to learn the difference. Will did not hate failure most. He hated the instant the world proved he had been using the wrong rule.

At the next shallow, the canoe stopped.

Joel tapped once.

Will held still.

They had settled on gravel hidden under six inches of tea-colored water. Current pressed from the side, not enough to free them, enough to turn them if Joel gave it slack. He shifted his weight. The boat creaked.

Both men froze.

A loaded canoe flexing under tired men. Ordinary. But ordinary sounds no longer stayed where they were made.

Joel set his paddle across the gunwales and leaned over the side. Cold water covered his knuckles. The gravel under them was pale and small. No crack that he could see. No seam opening. Hung, not broken.

He tapped the hull once, pointed to himself, then over the side.

Will shook his head sharply. His eyes went to the bank, the fog, Joel's face.

Joel understood. If he got out, they were slower. If he got out, one man was in the boat and one in the water. If he got out, cold took more from him. If they stayed, the current could turn the hull broadside and pin it harder. There was no clean choice, only exposure chosen before exposure chose them.

Joel pointed to the shallow and made a pushing motion. He pointed to Will, then to the front rope.

Will's jaw moved once around a word he did not let out. He nodded.

Joel slid over the side into water just above his knees.

The cold bit cleanly. It took his calves first, then his thighs, then sent a narrow line upward through his body. His sandals sank into gravel and mud. He held the back handle with both hands and lifted enough to lighten the hull.

Will got out at the front more slowly, bad hand held high. With the other hand he took the painter and pulled. The canoe rasped over gravel. The sound ran forward through the fog, long and rough.

From downstream, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will stopped with the painter tight across his body.

Joel shook his head once. Hard.

Alex said it again, farther away. Same brisk annoyance. Same practical timing. A dead man asking not for belief, only delay.

Will's eyes shone in the gray light. Cold, exhaustion, the body's water. Grief, if the word still helped. He turned forward and pulled.

The boat came free.

They walked it another ten yards before the channel deepened. Joel climbed in first, because if the back swung loose they would lose the boat's direction. His wet legs dragged mud over the bottom. Will got in with a broken care, planting his good hand, folding the bad one close, settling low. For a few seconds neither man paddled. They listened to their own breathing and to the boat, which gave no new sound except water touching aluminum.

Neither man looked back.

For a while they made progress.

Progress no longer felt like distance. It was the continued absence of a worse fact: no water pouring through a seam, no hidden split opening under the food barrel, no voice close enough to make a body answer before thought could stop it. The canoe advanced in small refusals.

Water gathered in the ribs under Joel's heels. Not much. A shallow runnel from wet legs and dripping packs, he told himself, but the boat was too low for casual water. He took the pot from beside the stove, dipped twice, and poured the water over the side. The splash sounded too large in the narrow channel. Across the bend, a second pour answered after a delay, cleaner than an echo.

Will turned his head.

Joel held up the pot, then pointed at the bright water sliding along the canoe floor. Real. Here.

The answering splash came again from beyond the bend, where no one bailed anything.
