Will looked at the pot in Joel's hand, then at the fog. His face did not ask only whether the boat was leaking. It asked whether a false sound could hide a true one long enough to matter.

Joel set the pot where he could reach it and picked up the paddle.

The channel narrowed, widened, and narrowed again. Beaver work had changed it since any map had cared. Old sticks silvered under water. Chewed points rose from mud like sharpened stakes. A lodge humped under alder, its upper sticks black with rain. The place smelled of wet bark and old leaves. The beavers had made a world suited to themselves. Men entered it by dragging aluminum over the proof.

Twice they slid over submerged dams. Once Joel got out and felt mud take his sandal to the ankle. Once Will nearly lost the front rope when his bad hand closed late and the painter burned across the plastic. His face went white. He did not cry out. Even pain had become something to spend carefully.

Joel stopped the boat with his thigh against the gunwale. He pointed to Will's hand.

Will lifted it. The plastic had torn at one taped edge. Water had gotten in. The bandage beneath showed a spreading gray.

Joel made for shore.

They found a strip of mud under spruce roots, not a landing, only a place to hold the canoe while the water worried at the back. Joel kept one hand on the gunwale while Will knelt and unwrapped the bag. The wound across his palm looked swollen at the edges, the skin around it too pale. The cut had become more than a cut by demanding decisions from them. Not infected yet. Or not visibly enough for the word to help.

Will studied it with the offended attention he gave broken gear.

It was unfair that Will still looked most like himself when something failed. His face gathered around the problem. His fear went precise. The bad hand had become one more wrong system asking to be understood.

Joel took the notebook from inside his jacket. The cover had gone soft from damp. He wrote:

REWRAP. NO ARGUMENT.

Will read it and gave him a look that almost had humor in it.

He wrote beneath:

I WAS SAVING MY BIG SPEECH.

Joel almost smiled. The expression stopped before it reached his mouth. Humor had become another sound they could not afford to let out.

He took the first-aid kit from the top pack. They worked without speaking. Dry gauze, or what passed for dry. Tape. A new plastic bag from the food scraps. More tape at the wrist. Will flexed his fingers and winced. Mike's cap, buried somewhere in Will's pack, made a small hard shape against the fabric when Joel shifted it aside; one kept thing knocking softly against another.

Across the channel, hidden in spruce, Mike cleared his throat.

The sound arranged the air into expectation. Decision coming. Complaint coming. Some blunt pronouncement meant to reduce the number of possible worlds.

Will looked at Joel.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

Not loud. Not theatrical. Almost tired. Better because Mike had often sounded tired when he meant to sound certain.

Joel wrote:

NOT MIKE.

Will took the pencil.

IT KNOWS WHEN WE STOP.

Joel looked at the line.

He wanted to write that it knew sound, not time. He wanted to write that it could hear them stop. He wanted to write that they did not know what it knew. All of those were doors, and Will's mind would open them. Will did not want comfort from the page anymore. He wanted a lab.

Joel wrote:

MAYBE. MOVE ANYWAY.

Will read it. His eyes came up, sharp and resentful.

The look held. Not fear, not grief. Distrust. Will was beginning to see Joel's restraint as another authority, another man telling him what was useful, what counted, what could be risked. Do not answer. Do not test. Do not say names. Keep moving, but in Joel's handwriting.

Joel felt the resemblance and hated it.

He did not apologize. An apology might repair nothing and still give the woods the shape of repair.

They pushed off.

The fog thinned by degrees. The sky above it had a yellow-gray cast, late afternoon already waiting inside the day. Light came flat through spruce and made every wet thing show its edge. Joel checked the map when the channel widened enough to drift. The paper inside the case had dampened at the folds. He could still read the blue line, the faint bend, the small marsh sign, the next widening that might be a lake or only another lie told at map scale. North Road Bridge was still beyond too many inches of paper, and the paper had stopped feeling like a promise.

The Highlander key pressed against his ribs when he leaned forward.

A key was absurd in wilderness. A small cut piece of metal and plastic waiting to matter only if a man reached a machine on a road. Before that, it was useless weight. After that, it might be everything. Joel kept wanting to touch it. Zipper. Fabric. Fingernail against plastic. Even checking a fact had a sound. He forced his hand away and let the pressure against his ribs stand for proof.

The canoe bumped something under the surface.

A dull knock rose through the hull and into Joel's knees.

Will turned, eyes wide.

Joel pointed down. Here. Real.

Then another bump came from the left bank.

Not echo. Not the same sound carrying. A separate hollow knock from alder roots six feet away, where no hull moved.

Will's face changed. If it could make boat sounds from the bank, it could make damage where there was none. Or cover real damage with false evidence. Sound had become bad data exactly where they needed it most: under them.

Joel tapped once.

They held still in the current. The canoe turned a few degrees. Joel corrected with the smallest stroke he could manage. He leaned over and ran his fingers along the inside seam by his seat. Water in the bottom, but not rising. A few cups from wet feet and gear. Nothing pouring. Nothing breathing through the hull.

Will pointed ahead. Continue?

Joel nodded.

Will pointed at the boat, then the water, then made a flat falling motion with his hand.

If it fails.

Joel nodded again. Yes.

Will took the notebook and braced it on the food barrel. Writing in a canoe was awkward and slow. The pencil tore at the damp page.

IF BOAT FAILS WE LOSE EVERYTHING.

Joel read the line while the canoe drifted. He could hear water sliding along the hull, each small touch exact and useless.

He wrote:

BOAT FAILS HERE OR BACK. FORWARD HAS CAR.

Will stared at the word CAR.

It looked childish on the page. Not Highlander. Not take-out. Not North Road Bridge. Just CAR, reduced to doors, engine, heat, road.

Will wrote:

IF ROAD STILL THERE.

Joel remembered Mike's map spread on the hood before the trip, the bridge printed with the same confidence as lakes and portages and the cabin that had given them nothing. Human marks aged badly. Roads became brush. Cabins became mouse nests. Bridges washed out or did not, with no interest in men's plans.

He wrote:

IF NOT, STILL FORWARD.

Will did not answer.

The canoe nosed into a tongue of deeper water. Joel dipped the pot again before he paddled. Less water this time, or the same amount spread thinner. Not proof. Not comfort. He poured it out carefully, keeping the pot close to the hull so the splash would be small.

From the bank, something poured back.

Will's shoulders tightened.

Joel put the pot away.

The next bend showed them the print.

Will saw it first. Two taps on the hull, deliberate enough this time to mean look.

Joel looked right.

Below a wall of alder roots, in a small tongue of exposed mud, a depression held brown water. Broad at one end, long at another. Too soft at the edges to name and too clear to ignore. One side showed something like a heel. The other splayed into torn mud. Bear if you needed bear. Human if you needed worse. A kneeling place if you needed a third category and had no word for it.

Will pointed to the mark. Then to his eyes. Then lifted both hands slightly from his paddle.

Joel shook his head.

Will pointed again, harder.

Evidence.

Joel pointed at the channel ahead, then at the sinking light.

The old Will would have said there was a difference between not knowing and refusing to know. The old Joel would have told him to save it for camp and meant later, a time when speech was only speech. Their old arguments had depended on later.

No time. No answer.

The canoe drifted past the print.

Joel looked until the bend took it. He wanted it to become one thing before it vanished. It did not. The mud held its shape without confessing.

Late afternoon made the water look stiller than it was. The channel opened into a slow reach between black spruce, no wider than a narrow road, with a gravel bar shouldering out from the left bank. The bar rose only a foot above the water. A driftwood snag lay across it, silver and smooth where old floods had rolled it. Behind it, spruce grew too tight for tents. Beyond the spruce, nothing Joel could see promised higher ground, and the next bend took the water into darker alder.

They needed a camp and did not want one.

Camps were where human routine collected. Tarp lines. Food. Zippers. Fire. Bodies turning in sleep. Names nearly spoken. A camp made a pattern.

Joel pointed to the bar.

Will looked at it, then downstream, then back.

Neither man liked the decision. That made it likely.

They landed with care that took too long. Joel stepped into ankle-deep water and held the canoe while Will climbed out stiffly. Gravel shifted under both of them with small clicking sounds. They unloaded only what could not stay in the boat overnight or what they needed within arm's reach. Food barrel. Tarp. Sleeping bags. Filter. Notebook. Hatchet. The rest stayed lashed. If they had to move fast, the boat had to be more than a pile of choices.

Joel pulled the canoe up until the front rested on gravel and the back still touched water. Then higher. He hated the scrape and pulled anyway. He tied the shortened painter around the driftwood snag, then around his own wrist with a loose loop he could free if he had to.

Will watched him.

Joel wrote:

IF BOAT MOVES, I WAKE.

Will took the pencil.

IF YOU SLEEP.

The line had no accusation in handwriting, which meant it could hold all of them. Neither of them had slept properly since before Mike died. Their faces had become unfamiliar from fatigue, stripped to bone and wet beard. Sleep was starting to look less like rest than another place a man could be separated from himself.

Joel wrote:

IF EITHER SLEEPS, GOOD.

Will gave a silent breath that might once have been a laugh.

They made the tarp low against the driftwood and two spruce roots, more cover from dew than shelter. No fire. Smoke would not matter much, but flame made a center, and centers drew old habits: faces turned inward, hands warmed, someone finally saying what the day had cost. Food stayed beside them instead of hung. Animal risk had not disappeared, but climbing into brush near dark asked them to leave the boat and each other. Joel set the hatchet where both could see it and neither could reach for it by accident.

They filtered water at the edge. The pump made a soft, regular squeak.

After ten strokes Joel stopped.

The same squeak continued across the channel.

Will's head came up.

The filter in Joel's hands was still.

Squeak.

Squeak.

Then water dripping from a lifted paddle. Slow drops. Exact drops.

Their paddles lay on the gravel beside the canoe.

Will closed his eyes for one second. When he opened them, his face had hardened into something almost calm.

He looked at the filter, then at the opposite bank. His good hand lifted half an inch. Pump again. Stop. Count the delay. Move the filter. See whether the sound moved with it. Joel could read the experiment in the pause between Will's fingers and the handle.

Joel set the filter down and put his hand flat over it.

Will looked at Joel's hand. His mouth made no word, but Joel could read the sentence anyway. You are stopping me. Not saving me. Stopping me.

They finished filtering. Joel hated each pump. The sound left his hands and no longer belonged to him.

Dinner was cold food eaten with fingers. Tortillas damp at the edges. Peanut butter from a pouch. Trail mix with grit in it. Will sorted raisins out without seeming to know he was doing it, then stopped and shoved the whole handful into his mouth.

Alex would have said something about that. Joel knew the shape of the joke without hearing words. He looked at the water until the impulse passed.

The light lowered. Fog returned from the channel in strips. The canoe sat where they had left it, ugly and necessary, its aluminum side dull, its shortened rope running from the handle to the snag to Joel's wrist.

Bad boat. Good boat. Only boat.

Will took the notebook.

He wrote slowly because his hand hurt.

IF IT ONLY REPEATS OLD THINGS, IT CAN'T REPORT NEW FACTS.

Joel read the sentence twice.

Will wrote under it:

WE CAN TEST.

The bar seemed to narrow around them. The channel made the sentence feel overheard even before anything answered it.

Joel took the pencil.

NO.

Will did not look at him. He kept looking at the page, as if the page were the less frightening face.

He wrote:

TESTING IS NOT ANSWERING.

Joel wrote:

YES IT IS.

Will's mouth opened and closed. He pressed the pencil so hard the point broke. The snap was tiny and violent. Both men looked toward the opposite bank before they looked at the page.

Nothing answered the break.

That was worse than an answer. It left Will alone with the small proof of his own pressure.

Joel found the spare pencil stub in the notebook pocket and handed it to him.

Will wrote:

WE NEED LIMITS.

Joel almost wrote, It does not understand. But that was faith disguised as caution. They had survived by acting on assumptions and calling them rules because rules were easier to obey than guesses.

He wrote:

LIMITS COST SOUND.

Will read it and shook his head once.

He wrote:

WRITING COSTS NOTHING.

Joel looked at the dark line of the opposite bank. The spruce there stood close enough together that dusk gathered between trunks before it gathered on the water. The channel made a narrow mirror of the sky, then broke it around stones and drowned sticks.

Writing removed tone. That was why it had saved them. It made grief blunt and orders plain. It let Will argue without feeding the trees his voice. But writing still made marks. The pencil scratched. Pages turned. Hands moved. A note changed what a man did next. The page had begun to feel like shelter, and Joel no longer trusted anything that called itself shelter.

He wrote:

WE DON'T KNOW THAT.

Will read it.

His face changed, not toward fear exactly. Toward insult. The last room had lost its lock.

He wrote:

THEN WE HAVE NOTHING.

Joel wanted to deny it. The want came with the old physical urgency of speech. He could see Will at sixteen in a church basement, explaining to a youth pastor why the shepherd metaphor did not work if anyone cared about actual sheep. He could see him at twenty-six, drunk in Joel's first apartment, correcting Mike about a canoe neither of them yet owned. He could see him before the trip, making the layoff into a joke about becoming a full-time lure consultant. He could see him two mornings ago, line in hand, trying to believe fish still existed as fish. All that language had been Will's way of staying present in the world. Now presence itself had become a liability.

Joel wrote:

WE HAVE SIGHT.

Then:

WE HAVE BOAT.

Then, after a pause:

WE HAVE EACH OTHER IF WE DON'T GIVE THAT AWAY.

Will looked at the last line for a long time.

Across the channel, in Will's own voice, someone said, "There is a difference."

Will shut his eyes.

The phrase had been his once, ordinary as a cough, said when distinctions still helped men feel clever. It arrived now in the exact place Will's argument had made for it: not as an answer, only as pressure applied to the bruise.

Joel put two fingers to his eyes and waited until Will opened his.

Visible.

Will nodded. His eyes were wet now, unmistakably, and furious about it.

Joel wrote:

IT HEARD THAT BEFORE.

Will took the pencil.

IT USED IT CORRECTLY.

Joel looked at the line and felt the cold of the channel return inside his clothes.

He wrote:

IT USED IT NEAR YOU.

Will read it. His hand stayed on the page.

That was the narrow difference Joel could give him. Maybe not understanding. Placement. Will plus correction. Will plus argument. A sound set beside the wound that had taught it where to go.

Will wrote nothing after that. The restraint looked less like agreement than injury.

From behind the tarp, a rope tapped aluminum.

Joel looked at the canoe.

The painter ran visibly from its front handle to the snag to his wrist. The hull did not move.

Tap.

Will turned only enough to confirm the boat by sight.

Tap.

The sound came again from behind the tarp, though the canoe sat in front of them.

Joel wrote:

NOT BOAT.

Will touched the rope where it crossed Joel's wrist. Not affection. Confirmation. The line was real. The boat was there. Joel was there. Will's fingers were cold through the plastic. The touch lasted half a second too long, then ended before either of them had to name it.

They lay down under the tarp before full dark because darkness would come whether they prepared for it or not. Joel kept his wrist outside the sleeping bag so the painter's loop could tug him awake if the canoe shifted. Gravel pressed into his hip. The tarp breathed damp air against his face. The food barrel sat within reach, black ring closed. The hatchet lay where both men could see its dull edge. Will lay close enough that Joel could feel each controlled movement, each withheld cough, each time his body almost turned toward a sound and stopped.

Across the channel, Mike cleared his throat once.

Neither man moved.

Farther downstream, Alex laughed under his breath.

Neither man moved.

Then, from somewhere in the fog where no boat touched shore, came the hollow knock of a paddle against a gunwale. Once. Then silence long enough to become another kind of pressure.

Joel kept his eyes open on the canoe's dim shape. Bad boat. Only boat. The thing that might fail them and the thing they had to trust until it did. Beside him Will breathed through his nose, still awake, still thinking.

The rope around his wrist held.

It was not safety. It was contact. For that night, before sleep or whatever passed for it, contact was the whole world.


# Chapter 22 - The Channel

Morning came with frost along the tarp edge and no sleep worth having.

Joel knew he had gone under once or twice because each time he came back the world had shifted without moving. The fog had thickened. The canoe had become a darker shape. Will had changed from one curled outline under the tarp to another, closer to the food barrel, his bad hand held against his chest as if sleep had required him to guard it too. But none of it had the depth of rest. It was more like being dropped through a hole and jerked back by the rope on his wrist.

The rope was still there.

He lifted his hand slowly. The shortened painter ran from his wrist to the driftwood snag, then to the canoe's front handle. Frost had roughened the line where it crossed the gravel. The canoe sat where they had left it, half gray, half black with dew, its side dull in the light before sunrise.

For a while Joel did nothing but look at it.

The boat had not moved. The rope had not been cut. The knot had not slipped. The food barrel stood under the tarp between them, black ring closed. Will was visible. Joel was visible to himself in pieces: hand, sleeve, wet pant leg stiff with cold, dark crescents of mud under his nails. The painter had bitten a shallow red groove around his wrist. It looked like proof and like the price of needing proof.

He pressed two fingers to the inside of his jacket and felt the hard nub of the Highlander key without opening the zipper.

Then he made himself do the rest in order.

Notebook inside jacket, swollen but present. Hatchet in the gravel beside Will's knee, head turned inward so no one would cut a hand reaching blind. Blue dry bag still under the front seat of the canoe, its color dulled by frost and shadow, emergency paddle inside it by weight and shape. Food barrel closed. Map case flat under the thwart. Filter bag tucked where he had left it. Will's body curled under the tarp, breath showing once, then not showing until the cold gave it back.

Each fact held only while he looked at it. That was the part no rule could soften. Sight did not keep a thing true. Sight only caught it in the act of being true, then ended.

Sight first. Then thought, if thought still helped.

Across the channel, something small moved in the alder and stopped.

Joel did not turn his head fast. That had become one of the new rules too, though they had not written it. Fast movement made the body choose before the eyes had finished. He moved only his eyes, then his chin, and saw nothing but alder stems and the pale thread of mist between them.

Will opened his eyes.

Joel put two fingers to his own eyes and pointed at him.

Will nodded once. Visible.

They ate cold food from the bag. Tortillas gone stiff at the edges. Peanut butter too cold to spread, squeezed straight from the pouch onto whatever could hold it. A handful of trail mix passed from Joel to Will, then back when Will could not get the plastic open cleanly with his swollen hand.

Will hated that. Joel saw it in the set of his mouth.

Will accepted the opened packet anyway.

The old Will would have made a remark about assistive technology, or about how the human hand was an overrated interface, or about Mike buying medical tape with the adhesive integrity of wet lasagna. Any one of those would have made the morning human. Instead Will pinched the packet between two fingers and ate without looking up.

Joel remembered Mara at their kitchen counter in Madison, opening a stubborn jar for him because he had nicked his thumb on a paint scraper and was pretending the cut did not matter. She had made a face, handed it back, and said, "Heroic." He had laughed because the room had allowed laughter. A kitchen could hold a joke and not turn it into a tool.

The channel could not. It had already made a tool of less.

The water steamed where first light touched it. Not a true river here, not enough width or authority for that, but a channel under alder, moving because somewhere upstream more water insisted. It slid black and slow around the gravel bar, carrying leaves, foam, a sliver of bark turned pale-side up. The fog came off it in low torn strips. Where the sun found the surface, the mist rose and vanished. Where the spruce leaned over, it stayed close.

Joel pulled the notebook from inside his jacket.

The pages had swollen. Corners curled. Pencil marks from the night before had blurred where damp had gotten in, but the words still held their blunt shapes.

WE HAVE SIGHT.

WE HAVE BOAT.

WE HAVE EACH OTHER IF WE DON'T GIVE THAT AWAY.

Will watched him read it. His face was drawn so tight that his eyes looked too large.

He reached for the pencil.

Joel hesitated, then gave it to him.

Will wrote slowly:

IF I AM RIGHT, WRONG CONTEXT EXPOSES IT.

The sentence sat there, technical and miserable. Will's handwriting had always leaned forward as if impatient with the hand that made it. Now it cramped into block letters. The pencil shook at the end of CONTEXT and tore a small dark fleck from the page.

Joel read the line twice.

He wrote:

SURVIVAL EXPOSES IT ENOUGH.

Will's eyes moved over the words. He did not answer on paper.

That was answer enough.

They packed without speech. The tarp came down stiff and wet. Each fold made small crisp sounds where frost had settled along the edge. Joel hated all of them. Will held the food barrel steady while Joel tightened the strap. Joel held the canoe while Will lowered the first dry bag in with his good hand and knee. Every task took longer because every noise needed a witness.

Work still required trust.

Joel had to turn his back while Will lifted the hatchet and stowed it by his knee. Will had to look away while Joel checked the key by pressing two fingers to the inside of his jacket and not opening the zipper. The rules demanded sight, but no human task could survive total sight. You could not pack a boat, tie a knot, piss, rewrap a wound, or sleep with another man's eyes fixed on you forever.

By morning, both of them were already in debt to what they could not verify.

At the water's edge, Will held up one finger and pointed to his hand.

Joel nodded.

They unwrapped the plastic enough to check the wound. The palm had puffed overnight. The cut looked more purple than red now, and the skin around it had the waxy look of something held too long in cold water. The edges were not clean anymore. They had softened and lifted. Will flexed his fingers. Two moved badly. He did not make a sound, but his jaw went hard and his eyes watered.

Joel wrote on the notebook cover because opening it again with wet fingers seemed foolish.

LESS FRONT PULL.

Will read it and made a face. Then he tapped his chest, pointed to the canoe, and made a paddling motion.

Me. Still paddle.

Joel shrugged once. Yes, but less.

Will seemed almost offended by the concession.

They pushed off.

The channel ahead bent through alder so tight the canoe entered it like a thought no one had room to finish. Branches leaned from both sides and touched above them in places, making a low ribbed tunnel. The water under the branches was black with leaves and tannin. Old beaver sticks lay half-sunk along the banks, stripped white at the ends. Grass mats floated loose in the eddies. Gravel showed through wherever the current thinned.

Joel sat in back, paddle ready across his knees more often than in the water. Will sat in front, shoulders rounded, his good hand doing too much. The food barrel pressed between them. Tarp, wet sleeping bags, stove, filter, first-aid kit, map case, Mike's repair kit, hatchet, spare paddle, and the small blue dry bag with the emergency paddle under the front seat all rode low inside the hull.

The canoe moved with the burdened reluctance of something asked to do too much and answer for it.

The blue bag stayed wedged where it had ridden since the cache. Joel had no use for it yet except as a fact. Blue. Front. Under. A child's description of an object, plain enough that fear could not dress it up.

Joel made the first correction with a small draw. Will saw it and stopped paddling before the front hit the alder. Good. The boat slid by with only a brush of leaves along the side.

They had become quicker with gestures. Two fingers to the eyes. Flat hand. Point. Fist. A palm turned down to slow. A touch to the food barrel to mean load. A point to the rope to mean watch the painter. Not language exactly, but close enough that Joel distrusted them. Anything repeated became pattern. Anything useful became a thing the world might learn.

But the woods could not see a hand sign, he thought, and corrected himself before the thought could settle.

They did not know what counted as seeing.

They did not know whether a pattern needed sound to become useful.

The first obstruction came before the sun cleared the spruce.

A beaver dam crossed the channel at an angle, not large, but enough to stop them. Water slid through the sticks in small glassy tongues. Mud had collected on the downstream side. On the upstream side, the level was a foot higher and flat as a held breath.

Will lifted his paddle, looked back.

Joel pointed left. There was a low place where the dam had sagged. They might shove over without unloading.

Will pointed at his hand.

Joel nodded. He knew.

They slid into the sag. The canoe's front climbed, stuck, tilted sideways. Water touched the outer lip and rocked back. Joel shifted his weight, too much, then corrected. Will braced with his good hand and hissed air through his teeth before he could stop it.

The sound was barely a sound.

It still went out.

Both men froze.

The channel held them. Water worked through the sticks below. A kingfisher rattled once downstream, then stopped as if ashamed of its own voice.

Nothing answered.

Joel tapped the hull once. Stop.

He got out first.

Cold water came over his sandals and into the legs of his pants. Mud took his feet with a soft suction. He kept one hand on the canoe and one on the dam. The sticks were slick with algae and older than they looked. One snapped under his palm with a small pistol crack.

Will closed his eyes.

Joel raised one hand. Wait.

They listened.

The alder dripped. Water worked through the dam. Somewhere ahead, not close, there was the sound of a paddle knocking once against a gunwale.

Will opened his eyes.

Joel shook his head.

No boat was visible. No person. No bright dry bag through the brush. Only the narrow thread of channel beyond the dam and fog lifting in tatters from it.

The knock came again.

Same distance. Same hollow body.

The sound was so ordinary that Joel had to fight the old impulse to place it properly. Another party. A canoe around the bend. Help. Witnesses. Men with a satellite device clipped to a shoulder strap because they were better prepared, or luckier, or simply not them.

He did not believe it.

Not believing did not stop hope from making a small room inside him and lighting it.

Will pointed ahead with the question in his face.

Joel touched two fingers to his eyes and pointed through the alder. See first.

Will nodded, but his mouth had tightened. The same argument lived in both of them now. If they ignored a real human sound, they might paddle past help. If they answered a false one, the proof might kill them.

There was no way to be innocent in the choice. Caution could become abandonment. Hope could become bait. A responsible man might die because another responsible man had learned too well not to turn his head.

Joel leaned his weight into the canoe.

The hull rasped over the dam. Too loud. Everything was too loud. Aluminum on wet sticks, packs shifting, the food barrel thudding once against the thwart, Will's breath catching, water pouring off the front as it tipped over. The channel took the sound and carried it ahead.

From beyond the bend, Will's voice called, "You guys hear that?"

Will was in front of Joel, visible from the back: shoulders hunched, wrapped hand held high, good hand clenched on the painter.

The false voice had his exact upward hook. Not panic. Not command. Invitation. The phrase that made other men stop and give him their attention.

Joel's body answered before his mind did. Every muscle paused toward listening.

He hated that. He hated Will for having given the thing a question so useful. He hated himself more for still preparing to answer it.

Will did not turn at first. His real body stood shin-deep in black water, still as a stump. Then, slowly, he looked back at Joel.

Joel put two fingers to his eyes and pointed at him.

Visible.

Will's face was gray. He nodded once.

The voice came again from ahead.

"You guys hear that?"

This time it sounded almost annoyed. Still Will. Still living. Still wrong.

The sentence did not ask only for an answer. It asked for the old pause: heads up, paddles still, each man part of a small listening group. It was not a lure shaped like terror. It was shaped like manners.

Will pointed to himself, then ahead.

Joel nodded. Yes. Here was the cleanest fact yet: a living voice, in full daylight, from a place the living body was not. Not grief. Not rain and night and the mind after death. Daylight did not clean it. Visibility only gave the lie something to contradict.

Will looked toward the bend again, not with belief now, but with attention sharpened past caution. Joel knew the look from harmless years: Will at a bad route junction, Will under a leaking sink, Will with a reel taken apart on a motel towel. The thing that was wrong had become interesting to him. Terror had not killed that habit. It had fed it better material.

They got the canoe over the dam. Joel climbed in with mud to his knees. Will followed awkwardly, lowering himself with his good hand and almost losing balance when the front shifted. Joel steadied the boat with his paddle. Their eyes met for one sharp second. Anger passed between them because fear had no place to go.

Then they moved.

After that, the channel became work without space.

Gravel shallows. Beaver cuts. Grass mats. Alder stems slapping shoulders. Once the canoe caught on a submerged root and spun half-sideways before Joel corrected it with two hard strokes he should not have made so loudly. Once a drowned log rose under the hull and lifted them both as if a back had arched beneath the boat. Will turned to ask without speaking. Joel pointed down. Real. Here. Then, from the right bank, the same dull bump came out of the roots.

Will's eyes widened.

Joel shook his head and kept them moving.

He could feel Will's thought forming ahead of him. Boat sounds at boat moments. Voices at decision moments. Mike at movement, Alex at hesitation, Will at questions. Not random, Will would write if Joel let him. Pattern.

Maybe it was understanding. Maybe it was only placement. The difference mattered less while both could kill.

The difference mattered to Will. Joel could see that too. Will was starting to need the distinction the way a cold man needs fire: not because it changes the weather, but because it gives him somewhere to put his hands.

At the next shallow they had to get out again.

The water was colder now because shade held this bend. Joel stepped into it and felt the shock climb from ankle to knee to groin. His wet waistband from the first slip had never dried; now cold found that line and widened it. He gripped the back handle and pushed. Will took the painter and pulled with his good hand.

The canoe slid six inches and stopped.

Joel pushed harder. His sandals sank deeper. Gravel shifted under mud. The boat groaned against something hidden.

Will braced and pulled.

His bad hand dropped by reflex to help. The plastic bag scraped the rope. He jerked it back with his mouth open in a silent curse.

From behind them, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will froze.

The painter went tight from his hand to the canoe. The water moved around his legs. He stood with his head turned a little, not enough to have answered, enough to have heard.

Alex said it again, farther back along the channel.

"Hold up."

Same brisk annoyance. Same practical timing. Not the big dead laugh. Not the campfire performer. A working Alex from before the bad world, the one who had called from a portage or a dam because his foot was stuck or a strap had snagged, the one who could make inconvenience into an event and still be genuinely asking for help.

Joel wanted to see him.

The wanting was immediate and physical. It arrived before belief. A body hears a friend ask for delay and prepares to delay. A body hears a dead man use a living errand and prepares to forgive the impossibility because the errand sounds ordinary.

Joel lifted his hand slowly, palm flat.

Not to Will. To himself.

Wait.

Then he shook his head.

Will's shoulders trembled once. He turned forward and pulled.

The canoe came loose.

They walked it another twenty yards before the water deepened. Joel climbed in first. Will handed him the painter with shaking fingers, then got in and sat low. His face had gone remote. Not calm. Removed from the expression it should have had.

Joel wanted to write something.

Not Alex.

Good.

Stay with me.

All of those were useless or dangerous or both. He put the notebook back in his jacket and paddled.

By late morning the fog had lifted. The sky showed itself in a dull lid of cloud, high enough to flatten the light but not high enough to promise weather. The channel ran north by the compass and the wet map, though in the body it ran nowhere but bend to bend. Every turn seemed to return them to some earlier version of the same alder, the same black water, the same torn mats of grass, the same low lodge half-swallowed by brush.

The map case was still gritty at the seal. Joel opened it only when they could drift in a wider reach and Will could keep them from touching either bank. The paper inside had softened at the folds. Blue water lines, portage dashes, marsh marks, old campsite dots. Human symbols holding themselves together under plastic and damp.

The channel ran into a narrow lake if the map still told the truth. Beyond that, a portage tomorrow. Beyond that, water again. North Road Bridge beyond too many small decisions to count.

Joel touched the place inside his jacket where the Highlander key rested.

Will saw the motion.

He pointed at Joel's chest, then at the map, then made the flat little rectangle of a car with his hands.

Joel nodded.

Car.

The word had no sound between them and still seemed childish. A child's drawing of rescue. Four wheels, doors, heat, a road. Mike's old Highlander with its bad smell, cracked console, and little St. Louis Cardinals keychain he had mocked but never removed. Joel could see it too clearly: dirty windshield, map folded in the back seat, spare sweatshirt Mike had probably left there because he left things in vehicles the way other men left messages.

Will lifted one finger and traced on the damp plastic of the map case, not marking, only moving through the film of water:

IF IT IS THERE.

Joel knew the words without seeing them.

He nodded again.

If it was there. If the bridge was there. If the road was passable. If the Highlander started. If the key still worked. If a human-made thing did not turn out like the cabin, real and useless.

The channel narrowed again before noon.

They came to a place where three threads of water separated around a low island of alder and dead grass. The left looked deeper but disappeared under brush. The middle moved fastest over stones. The right was almost still, choked with floating leaves. The map did not care about the distinction. It showed one line.

The water cared. The boat cared. Will's hand cared. Every mark the map had simplified now required payment from muscle and skin.

Will looked back.

Joel held up a flat hand. Wait.

The old version of this moment had been simple. Mike would have decided loudly. Will would have argued the hydrology or the map scale. Alex would have complained that all three choices looked like a place to ruin shoes. Joel would have picked the least bad water if asked, or followed the boat ahead and adjusted from back.

Now the decision sat in silence.

Will pointed left, then lifted his hand palm up.

Joel studied it. The left channel had depth but no sightline. Alder leaned over it so low the canoe would have to be ducked through blind. The middle had sound. Water quick over rocks, too shallow maybe, but visible through most of the run. The right looked like a trap for the hull.

Joel pointed middle.

Will shook his head, pointed to the rocks, then to the loaded canoe.

Bad boat.

Joel pointed to his eyes, then middle.

Visible water.

Will looked at him for a long moment. His expression had changed from disagreement into something more private. Joel knew the look because he had worn a version of it at Mike: the route had narrowed until one man's fear could call itself judgment.

Joel took the notebook.

He wrote:

MIDDLE IS VISIBLE. LEFT MAY BE BETTER BUT BLIND.

Will read it.

He wrote:

THAT IS NOT A RULE. THAT IS A PREFERENCE.

Joel stared at the sentence. It was so Will that for one terrible second he almost heard it aloud. Preference. Rule. Evidence. Assumption. The old machinery of him still turning under frost and grief.

It was also true.

That was what made Joel angry. Not that Will was being difficult. That he could still find the exact weakness in a thing they needed to obey.

Joel wrote:

YES.

Will's face tightened.

Joel added:

I CHOOSE VISIBLE.

Will did not like it. He nodded anyway.

That was the agreement now: not trust, not consent, only two men choosing the same bad thing because separate bad things were worse.

From the left channel, the blind one, came a faint dry scratch.

Not a voice. Not a knock. The tiny scrape of pencil against wet paper, or a twig worrying bark, or a claw on a hollow stem. It lasted less than a second. Will heard it too. Joel saw the knowledge cross his face before either man moved.

The scratch had no source, and because it had no source it entered every source at once. Page. Bark. Bone. The sound was small enough to deny and exact enough to remember.

Joel closed the notebook.

The middle run scraped them twice and almost pinned them once. Joel had to get out and ease the back around a stone while Will held the painter from the front. Water climbed over Joel's knees. His legs went numb in patches. The canoe made a thin shriek against rock that seemed to travel downstream and wait there for them.

At the end of the run, Mike cleared his throat from the left channel they had not taken.

The sound came from under the alder canopy, exactly where a man might have gone if he had chosen better water and wanted the others to follow. It was Mike's throat-clear before practical certainty. Not grief. Not panic. Management.

Will flinched so hard the painter jerked in his hand.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

The phrase moved through the brush with tired authority.

Joel felt it hit Will first. Then himself. Because the words were what Joel had been saying without saying. Move anyway. Forward. Morning. Car. Mike's dead voice and Joel's living rule had found the same shape.

Will looked at him.

There it was again. Not accusation exactly. Recognition.

Joel took the notebook with wet fingers and nearly tore the page getting it open.

NOT MIKE.

Will read it.

He took the pencil.

NOT ONLY MIKE.

Joel looked at the line and understood why Will had written it.

The woods had not only stolen Mike's voice. It had found the part of Mike that still lived in Joel's decisions. Keep moving. Do not stop. Survive now, know later.

He wanted to write, I know.

He wrote nothing. The pencil already made too much of him.

They went on.

Afternoon thinned the day without warming it. The cloud lid brightened and then began to lose light at the edges. They ate while drifting in a wider stretch, each taking food from the same open bag without letting the canoe turn broadside. Will's hand shook worse after the cold water. Joel made him stop twice to flex it. The fingers moved, but the swelling had made the knuckles blunt. When Will closed them around the paddle shaft, his grip looked borrowed.

From the right bank, Will's dry cough sounded in three soft pieces.

Will looked at the bank, then deliberately coughed once into his own shoulder.

The living cough was rougher. Wetter. Smaller.

Joel lifted two fingers to his eyes.

Will nodded. He was visible. His cough was not proof by itself. Only his body made it his.

At the next bend the channel opened suddenly into a pond-shaped widening. Wind touched the surface there. Not much, but enough to make small scales of light where the clouds broke thinly. Dead trees stood from the water near the far end, gray trunks with no bark, each one reflected below itself in a darker line. A beaver lodge rose on the eastern bank. Fresh sticks lay over old sticks. Mud packed the gaps. A slide ran down to the water.

For one minute there was space.

Joel let the canoe drift.

The relief was dangerous. Open water made the body imagine options. A person could paddle hard here. A person could cross. A person could gain time. The trees drew back, and the sounds moved with them, no longer beside his ear but no more trustworthy for the distance.

Then something slapped the water near the lodge.

Will jerked.

It could have been a beaver. It probably was a beaver. A real animal's flat tail warning the pond against them. The sound cracked across the widening, hard and wet, and echoed once from the far bank.

Joel watched the water rings spread.

The rings were a mercy. Real sound left marks when water was kind enough to receive it. Real slap, real widening circles, real beaver somewhere under the lodge or already gone from it. For three seconds the world performed evidence in the old way.

A second slap came from behind them, where no lodge stood.

Then a third from the left, dry and wrong, too much like a hand striking a canoe hull.

Will looked back at Joel, eyes sharp with the hunger to make a pattern of it.

Joel shook his head before Will could reach for the notebook.

Will's eyes narrowed. He understood the refusal and hated it.

He wanted to count. Joel could see the count beginning in him: first with rings, second without lodge, third too dry. If there was a sequence, there might be a law. If there was a law, the terror could be moved from the body into the mind, where Will had always been strongest and where the rest of him was starting to fail.

They paddled across the widening. No more slaps came. That made it worse in a way Joel had no use for. Repetition could become evidence. Silence after wrongness could become bait. He kept his eyes on the dead standing timber, the outlet, then Will's shoulders as the channel took them again.

The final beaver obstruction was higher.

It crossed the outlet in a packed brown wall of sticks, mud, grass, and cut saplings. Water spilled over in three places. The drop on the downstream side looked close to two feet. No loaded shove-over would work. They would have to unload enough weight to lift, or line the canoe around the left edge through muck and brush.

Joel pointed to a small mud shelf on the left.

Will nodded.

They landed in pieces. Food barrel first, because it was heavy and central. Then the wet bags. Then the stove bag, map case, repair kit. Joel kept the Highlander key on his body. Will kept the hatchet in sight on the mud shelf, not because it would save them but because losing it would be another failure with a handle.

The blue dry bag stayed in the canoe under the front seat with the emergency paddle. Joel checked it by sight and left it there. Moving everything made a camp. Moving only what they needed made a pause.

The mud shelf stank of wet leaves, old wood, and the mineral cold of disturbed bottom. Their sandals sank past the straps. Mosquitoes lifted weakly from the grass despite the chill. Joel's hands slipped on the canoe handle. Aluminum numbed his palms.

They lifted together.

The empty canoe was still awkward because the place gave them nowhere to stand. Will's bad hand failed halfway up. He caught the weight against his forearm and made a sound through his nose.

From the downstream brush, in Joel's own low voice, someone said, "Wait. Just wait."

Joel nearly dropped the boat.

Will saw it. That was the worst part. He saw Joel's hands open a fraction. He saw the body answer itself.

The voice did not repeat. It did not need to. It had used him rarely enough that every use felt less like bait than like trespass.

Joel tightened his grip.

He and Will lowered the canoe carefully onto the dam.

Joel stood with his palms on the gunwale and his head down. Water slid over sticks below him. His breathing had gone too fast. He counted it down without words. Four in. Four out. Not because counting saved anyone, but because it gave the body a task that did not involve listening.

Will touched his sleeve.

Joel looked up.

Will pointed two fingers to his eyes, then at Joel.

Visible.

Joel nodded.

Will took the notebook from Joel's jacket pocket himself, slowly, showing each movement. He wrote with the pencil braced against the canoe:

IT HAS YOU TOO.

Joel read it.

The line should not have hurt. They had already known it. The voice had proved it near the cache. But knowledge changed when another person wrote it down. The page took the damage out of Joel's private body and put it between them.

Joel took the pencil.

ONLY A LITTLE.

Will read it and, despite everything, gave him a look that almost became an old look. Dry. Skeptical. You absolute idiot.

Then the expression passed.

They got the canoe over.

The downstream side was worse. Mud to mid-shin. A tangle of submerged sticks. Water too shallow for the canoe, too deep for clean footing. They reloaded in stages and lined the boat fifty yards before they could climb in. By then Joel's legs had gone from cold to burning to a numbness that made each step uncertain. Will's lips had a pale edge.

Joel wanted to stop.

Stopping had become suspect too.

They continued.

The channel lengthened into a straighter reach by late afternoon. Alder gave way to black spruce and tamarack. The banks rose a little. Gravel appeared in tongues along the inside bends. The water deepened enough that the canoe moved more cleanly. Wind came at them from ahead, small but steady, carrying the smell of open water.

The map said the channel would join a longer lake. Narrow, but a lake. If they made enough distance today, tomorrow might hold the final portage. North Road Bridge beyond that. The Highlander beyond that, if road, bridge, and machine had kept their promises.

The word tomorrow had become almost obscene. It assumed a night could be crossed like a portage: miserable, finite, capable of being put behind them by effort. Joel no longer believed in that kind of time. He believed in the next bend, the next visible object, the next place where Will's hand might fail and force them to choose between speed and keeping him whole.

Will's strokes began to fail before the lake.

At first he only shortened them. Then the paddle dipped late. Twice the blade skimmed the surface without bite. The third time he missed the water completely and stared at the paddle as if it had changed its agreement with him.

Joel tapped once.

Will shook his head.

Joel tapped again, harder.

Will held still.

Joel pointed to a gravel shelf on the left bank, just before the channel opened. It was no campsite, only a shoulder of stone and mud with spruce roots behind it and a strip of level ground perhaps wide enough for two bodies if they did not mind being half in the trees. But it was above the water. It had sightline to the canoe. It was not brush on all sides.

Will looked at the shelf, then at the widening water beyond.

He wanted the lake. Of course he did. Another hour, another mile, another visible payment toward the car.

Joel pointed to Will's hand.

Will lifted it as if surprised to find it still attached. The plastic was cloudy with moisture inside. Blood or water had darkened the gauze at the palm. His fingers would not close all the way.

The look he gave Joel was almost pleading and almost furious.

Joel pointed to the gravel shelf again.

They landed.

At camp, if the gravel shelf could be called camp, they unloaded less than seemed wise and more than seemed safe. Food barrel. Tarp. Sleeping bags. Filter. Notebook. First-aid kit. Hatchet. They left everything else lashed in the canoe in case the night made the decision for them. Joel pulled the boat high enough that it could not drift on its own, then tied the shortened painter around a spruce root and left the loose end where he could loop it to his wrist again after the tarp was set.

He checked the blue dry bag under the front seat before he stepped away. Still wedged there. Still blue under mud and shadow. Emergency paddle inside by shape, by the slight hard clack when the canoe settled, by the fact that nothing else in the bag had that length. He did not open it. Opening made sound. Opening made a new fact for the world to overhear.

Will sat on the gravel with his paddle across his knees. He did not move for several minutes.

Joel rewrapped the hand.

The wound was worse. Not dramatically. Nothing useful like a clear line between safe and dangerous. Only swelling, heat, stiffness, a grayness around the wet places. Will watched Joel clean it with filtered water gone cold and a sting of antiseptic from the kit. He trembled once but did not pull away.

Joel wrote:

NO MORE FRONT PULLING IF POSSIBLE.

Will took the pencil.

THEN WE ARE SLOWER.

Joel wrote:

WE ARE SLOWER IF YOU LOSE THE HAND.

Will read it.

He wrote:

I HAVE ANOTHER ONE.

Joel looked at him.

Will gave a small shrug with one shoulder. It was the ghost of a joke, so thin it hardly counted as one. Joel let it be.

There had been a time when Joel would have fed it. One line back, something dry enough to give Will a place to stand. He could feel the old answer in his mouth and let it dissolve there. The cost of silence was not only fear. It was all the small mercies that needed sound.

They filtered water at the edge of the shelf. The pump squeaked. Joel kept the strokes slow, then stopped every few pumps to listen. Once the same squeak came from across the channel and continued after his hands stopped. Will stared at the filter. Joel held it up so both men could see it motionless.

Squeak.

Squeak.

Then the food barrel latch clicked behind them.

Both men looked.

The barrel sat between the tarp roll and the first-aid kit, black ring shut. Visible.

Click.

The sound came from farther behind the spruce roots, where no barrel had ever been.

Will closed his eyes. When he opened them, the anger had come back. It steadied him more than fear did.

They finished filtering.

The light slid out of the channel by degrees. Not sunset yet, but the first withdrawal. The spruce darkened from the trunks outward. The water lost its brown surface and became a narrow piece of sky laid between banks. The lake beyond the opening showed small wind marks, silver and then dull.

Joel did not want to sleep here.

He did not want to continue.

Those two refusals sat in him with equal weight. The body wanted a third thing the world did not contain.

Will took the notebook.

His first line came hard, the pencil digging into the softened paper:

CAN IT LEARN FROM WRITING?

Joel looked at the question and felt a fear different from voices.

He had treated writing as safer because the thing was sound. The page had become shelter, law, apology, argument, command. It had let grief pass between their hands without entering the air. It had also let Joel pretend that a rule written down became different from a rule imposed.

But assumptions had killed better men than him.

The entity could not read minds. They believed that. It could mimic sounds it had heard. They believed that too. Writing made only small noises: pencil on softened paper, page scrape, breath, sleeve against damp cover. It removed tone. It removed the tremor from a plea and the old warmth from an insult. It took friendship down to block letters and made the mutilation feel like safety.

None of that proved the page was private.

The thing did not have to read the words to learn the posture around them: two bodies bent over one object, the long pause after a question, the way Will's anger steadied when the pencil was in his good hand, the way Joel took the page back when fear needed to look like leadership. A written question still made a shape in the world. The scratch from the left channel returned to Joel now. Twig or pencil. Animal or answer. Nothing he could use. Nothing he could dismiss.

And if it could not learn from the page, it could learn from what the page made them do.

Joel wrote:

NO PROOF. DON'T TEACH IT.

Will took the pencil back too quickly.

IF WE NEVER TEST, WE NEVER KNOW.

Joel wrote:

KNOWING IS NOT THE SAME AS LEAVING.

The woods beyond the gravel shelf gave Will's dry cough.

Will turned his head toward the sound before he could stop himself.

Joel tapped once on the notebook.

Will turned back.

Ashamed. Angry. Alive.

From the same darkening trees, in Will's voice, very close to the living one, came the question again.

"You guys hear that?"

Will stared at the page.

Joel put two fingers to his eyes and waited until Will looked up.

Visible.

Will nodded.

Joel wrote, pressing so lightly the pencil barely marked and made almost no sound:

I HEAR YOU HERE.

Will read it. His face changed. For a moment all the argument went out of him, leaving only exhaustion and the old person Joel had known before jobs, before trips, before the group's jokes had become ammunition. Not safer. Only still there.

Will touched two fingers to his own eyes, then pointed at Joel.

I see you too.

The channel moved beside them. The false cough did not come again. The false question did not need to. It had done what questions did. It had made two men prove where they were.

Joel closed the notebook.

They still had to make the tarp low. They still had to decide what counted as food safety, what counted as sleep, what counted as watch. They still had to pass through the night with less speech than breathing wanted.

The canoe rested on the gravel, tied and visible. The lake waited beyond the mouth of the channel. The road waited beyond the map or did not.

For now, the only answer they owned was the one they did not give.


# Chapter 23 - No, No, No

They did not build a fire.

The reason was practical enough. Smoke would not matter much in the open mouth of the channel. Light might. So would the work of getting one going. A flame would give their hands work and their eyes a center. It would also give the dark a sequence to keep: match scrape, stove click, twig snap, the small domestic sounds by which men told themselves a place had been made theirs.

Nothing here was theirs.

The last line in the notebook still sat between them.

I HEAR YOU HERE.

Joel had written it for living Will, not for the trees, but the page had no way to say who the marks were for.

The gravel shelf was barely large enough to hold them. Behind it the spruce rose in a dark rank, roots lifted and braided where water had eaten the bank. In front, the channel moved toward the lake mouth, a narrow black strip losing the last of the sky. Beyond that, the longer lake lay somewhere in the half-light, and beyond the lake the final portage waited as an idea too heavy to lift tonight.

The canoe lay pulled high on stone, angled against the wind. The painter ran from its handle to a spruce root, then to Joel's wrist while he worked. He had stopped trusting knots as facts. A knot stayed true only until something put a hand on the rope.

Will sat on the gravel with the food barrel between his knees and watched the trees as if he could keep them honest by looking hard enough.

Joel left the barrel visible. He wanted it under cover. He wanted everything under cover. But the barrel had become one of the sounds the woods liked. Better to keep the black ring and latch where both men could see them.

They made the tarp low and ugly. Joel tied one corner to the spruce root above the canoe rope, another to a bent alder stem that did not deserve the trust. Will held the third line with his good hand while Joel wedged a paddle under the edge as a poor ridge. The tarp sagged in the middle. If rain came, it would collect there and bow the whole thing into their faces. It would hold their breath, the smell of wet clothes, the sour plastic smell of the food barrel. Cover, not shelter.

Will pointed at the canoe, then at Joel's wrist.

Joel nodded.

He had already checked the Highlander key twice since landing. It sat zipped inside his jacket, under the damp fleece and the chill weight of his shirt. He did not take it out. He pressed two fingers to the pocket through the fabric and felt the hard small shape answer him.

Key. Car. Bridge. Road.

Four words, all human-made. Four words with too much weather between them.

They ate without cooking. Tortilla, peanut butter, the last broken pieces of jerky, trail mix gone damp from too many cold hands. Will could not open the jerky bag. He tried with his teeth, then stopped because teeth on plastic made a small tearing squeal, and both men looked at the trees.

Joel took the bag and opened it with his knife, slowly, blade tucked against his thumb. He handed Will half without looking at him.

Will accepted it. The bandage around his hand had gone gray at the edges. His fingers looked thick and stupid, not like his fingers at all.

Joel wrote:

HAND?

Will held it up and gave him the same face he would have used in a garage, a kitchen, a parking lot outside a wedding reception. The face meant: what, this old thing?

Then he looked at the hand himself and the face failed.

He wrote:

HURTS. WORKS ENOUGH.

Joel wrote:

NO WATCH IF YOU SLEEP.

Will read it, frowned, and took the pencil.

NO WATCH IF YOU SLEEP EITHER.

That was fair. Worse than fair. They had both moved past tired into a state where thought came in strips. Joel could still make decisions, but he did not trust the blank places between them. He checked the rope, then checked it again. He stared at the closed barrel ring until the shape blurred. He looked at Will, found him present, and looked again.

He wrote:

SHORT WATCHES. TOUCH TO WAKE. NO VOICE.

Will stared at the last two words.

Joel knew the look. Will was not objecting to the rule. He was measuring what it took. No voice meant no comfort when the dark came hard. No names for the men who were not here. No old method of being friends: insult, correction, complaint, joke, apology hidden inside a practical sentence. Grief would have to sit in the body with nowhere decent to go.

Will took the pencil.

IF I WAKE SCARED I MAY TALK.

Joel read it. The honesty of the line was worse than an argument.

He wrote:

THEN I STOP YOU.

Will's eyes went up from the page.

Joel did not look away.

The channel moved beside them. A small wind came off the lake and entered the tarp from the open side, cold along the gravel. The last light had gone thin and colorless. In it, Will's face looked older than forty. Older than grief, even. As if the night had already started deciding what to keep of him.

Will wrote:

DO IT FAST.

They crawled under the tarp before full dark.

The space beneath it was just high enough for shoulders and elbows. They lay side by side, feet toward the canoe, heads toward the spruce roots, food barrel at Joel's knees where he could put a hand on it without reaching. The canoe remained visible if he lifted the tarp edge two inches. Boat. Barrel. Will. Key. Notebook. Hatchet. Rope. The world had become a series of objects to verify.

The blue dry bag under the front seat was visible only as a darker block under the hull's lip. Joel made himself confirm it once and then stop looking. If he lifted the tarp for every fear, the tarp itself would become another kind of answer.

The cold came first through the ground. Gravel was not earth. It held no warmth from the day. It pressed up through the sleeping pads in small hard points, finding hip, knee, rib. The damp in Joel's pants cooled again where his body had nearly warmed it. His toes stayed wet inside the hiking sandals. He had dry socks somewhere, or socks that had once been dry, but getting them meant unpacking in the dark and making the small human noises the dark collected.

Will's shoulder touched his. Not by sentiment. There was no room. The contact should have been reassuring. Instead it made Joel responsible for every tremor, every change in breath, every small sound Will might make before he could stop it. It also kept Will human in a way sight could not. Heat, weight, the small involuntary corrections of a living body on hard ground.

The world narrowed to sound.

Water moving over small stones. Tarp ticking in the wind. The canoe hull giving one faint cooling tick as the temperature dropped. Will's breath catching when he almost slept. Joel's own pulse in his ears. Farther off, or closer, a branch breaking under no visible weight. A bead of water falling from the tarp seam to the gravel, patient as a clock.

Will stiffened.

Joel put a hand out in the dark and found his sleeve.

Not voice. Sleeve. Pressure. Here.

Will's breath slowed one count, then another.

The branch did not break again.

For a while nothing happened except the night becoming itself. A faint line of sky remained at the tarp's open edge, then no line. The channel had a different sound in darkness. In daylight the water had moved through channel, around stone, under alder. Now it seemed to move around the shelter itself.

Will shifted beside him.

Joel touched his sleeve again.

Will did not settle. His breath hitched once, twice. Then he whispered, "I'm sorry."

The words were almost not words. Barely air shaped against teeth.

Joel's body moved before thought. He put his hand over Will's mouth.

Not hard.

Hard enough.

Will went rigid beneath it.

For one second they were not two men under a tarp in the north woods. They were old friends in a stupid scuffle made serious by the wrong audience. Joel felt Will's jaw clench against his palm, the beard stubble, the wet heat of breath. Will's good hand clamped around Joel's wrist.

There was hatred in him. Joel felt it through the hand as clearly as pressure.

Joel kept his palm there anyway.

That was the part he would have to live with if they lived. Not the rule. The hand.

Then Will's grip loosened.

Understanding came back. Not forgiveness. A colder thing. Will nodded once against Joel's palm.

Joel took his hand away.

No sound answered from the trees.

That was not comfort. It only meant the night had held what it heard.

Joel took the notebook from inside his jacket. He did it slowly, each motion shown to Will, because surprise had become another kind of sound. The red lamp was almost dead. He cupped it under the jacket and clicked it once. The tarp flushed red for a second, then dulled to a weak circle on the page.

Will wrote first, awkwardly, pencil gripped between fingers that did not want to close.

MIKE IS DEAD BECAUSE WE DID NOT GO AFTER HIM.

Joel read it in the red dim. The sentence brought back the cabin door against their backs, Mike's voice outside wearing command after command, the torn orange PFD fabric and the bent zipper pull, the key in Joel's hand. He thought of where Mike had stood and where Mike had stopped being visible. The distance had been small. Small distances had done most of the killing.

Joel wrote:

MIKE IS DEAD BECAUSE IT TOOK HIM.

Will read it. His face tightened, not into disagreement but into the refusal of relief.

He wrote:

BOTH CAN BE TRUE.

Joel had no answer that improved anything.

He looked at Will's line until the words loosened from grammar and became marks. Alex had stepped away, and something had killed him. Mike had gone out of sight, and they had failed to get him back. Joel had kept them alive by refusing voices. Refusal still had the shape of abandonment.

Will took the pencil again.

ALEX WAS CLOSER THAN MIKE.

Joel closed his eyes.

Will wrote underneath, harder:

WE HEARD HIM.

The line sat on the page with more heat than any spoken accusation would have had. Joel wanted to take the notebook from him. Not because the words were wrong. Because they were too close to speech. Will's grief had found the page and made a mouth.

The pencil itself had a sound now. A small dry animal scratch in the red dark. Joel heard it and thought of the channel, of the little scrape from the blind water, of his own note from the night before: NO PROOF. DON'T TEACH IT.

He wrote:

WE HEARD ALEX. THEN WE HEARD OTHER THINGS.

Will stared at him.

The red light showed his eyes too bright and too wet.

He wrote:

DO NOT LAWYER THIS.

Joel almost laughed. The body had the old reflex before the mind could stop it. Will accusing him of lawyering anything belonged to a living room, a driveway, a table with beers on it, a church basement when they were fifteen and arguing over a movie neither of them had understood. The laugh rose and stopped at the back of his throat.

He swallowed it.

Will saw. That made it worse.

Outside, something stepped on gravel.

Both men went still.

Not branch. Not brush. Gravel. A small shift of weight on stone near the canoe, then a pause. The exact sound Joel had made twenty times setting camp. Sandal on loose rock. Heel careful. Weight transferred.

Joel turned off the red lamp.

Darkness took the page.

The step did not repeat.

Joel lifted the tarp edge less than an inch.

The canoe lay where it had been, a low dark shape against darker water. The rope line ran from hull to root to his wrist. The blue dry bag was still wedged under the front seat, a dark block in a darker boat. The food barrel was inside by his knees. Will was against his shoulder. The hatchet lay between them, handle toward Joel, blade wrapped in cloth.

Nothing visible.

He lowered the tarp.

From beyond the canoe, Alex said, clear and cheerful, "I'm taking a piss. Nobody panic."

Will's whole body jerked.

Joel caught him with both arms.

For one wild second Will fought him. Not against Joel, not exactly. Toward the voice. Toward the old joking sentence that had once meant Alex was just outside the light being Alex, making a performance out of a bodily errand. Toward the impossible mercy of a friend who could still be located by sound.

Joel clamped a hand over Will's mouth harder this time.

Will made a sound against Joel's palm. It was not a word. It was worse because it wanted to become one.

Alex laughed outside.

Not the big camp laugh. Smaller. Nearer. The bark he gave when he had gotten exactly the reaction he wanted. It did not sound dead. It sounded social.

Then Mike cleared his throat from the spruce roots behind their heads.

Joel felt his skin tighten.

Mike said, "We're fine."

The phrase did not reassure. It managed. It set itself over the night like a hand flattening a map.

The food barrel latch clicked under Joel's hand.

His fingers were on the barrel. The black ring was closed. The latch had not moved.

Click.

This time the sound came from the trees beyond the spruce root.

Will shook beneath Joel's hand. He was crying now, though he made almost no sound. Tears ran sideways into his hair. His breath blew hot against Joel's palm and came back on his own face.

Then Will's voice, from out by the canoe, said, "No, no, no."

Joel had heard the real version after Alex, the words breaking out of Will as if his body were trying to refuse the event before the mind could see it. No, no, no. Not language then. A door shoved closed again and again after what had already entered.

This was not that.

It was not panic. That was what made it unbearable. The words were even, copied from fear and emptied of it, spaced like drops from the tarp edge.

"No, no, no."

From the channel mouth, the same voice answered.

"No, no, no."

Then from behind the spruce.

"No, no, no."

Three Wills around them. One by the canoe. One at the channel mouth. One behind the spruce. One under Joel's hand, real by heat and breath because the dark had taken sight away.

The voices did not overlap. They took turns.

"No, no, no."

"No, no, no."

"No, no, no."

Will stopped fighting.

That frightened Joel more than the struggle had. Will lay rigid, mouth covered, eyes open in the dark. Joel felt the tears continue. He felt the jaw muscles work once, trying to shape something he did not let become sound.

Joel held him because holding was the only answer left that did not feed the thing.

The night went on repeating him.

At first the voices stayed in Will's exact register: high with shock, scraped thin, breathless. Then the copies changed by almost nothing. One came too calm. One came with the small upward hook of Will correcting a detail. One came low, almost Mike's shape wearing Will's words. None of the changes made meaning. They were only ways to carry the sound.

It had found a useful sound. A sound that stopped men. A sound that made the body turn toward damage. It did not need to understand refusal. It only had to repeat the noise refusal made.

"No, no, no."

Will's good hand found Joel's wrist again. Not to pull it away. To hold it there.

The grip hurt.

Joel let it.

After a long time, the voices stopped.

Not faded.

Stopped.

The absence rang.

Joel waited until waiting itself became another sound inside his skull. Then he removed his hand from Will's mouth.

Will did not speak.

They lay shoulder to shoulder under the tarp, listening to the river and to the space the voices had left. Joel's palm was wet with Will's breath and tears. He wiped it once against his pants and hated the practicality of it.

Will reached for the notebook.

Joel clicked the red lamp under his jacket again. The glow trembled because his hand did. Will took the pencil. It took him three tries to set the point where he wanted it.

IT DOES NOT KNOW WHY THAT HURTS.

Joel read it.

Will wrote beneath:

THAT MATTERS.

Joel stared at the page.

He knew at once what Will meant. That was the problem. The words outside had been wrong not in sound but in relation. They had the fear without the wound. They had Will's panic without Alex's body, without Mike gone in the clearing, without the human knowledge of what no, no, no had failed to stop. The thing could hold tone. Cause was somewhere else.

Maybe that mattered. Maybe it was the first clean edge they had found. Maybe thinking it through in the dark was already a kind of answer.

Joel took the pencil.

NOT TONIGHT.

Will read it and closed his eyes.

Joel wrote:

MORNING. MOVE.

Will opened his eyes again. In the red light, his face went flat with anger.

He wrote:

YOU SOUND LIKE MIKE.

Joel felt the sentence land because it was true enough. Morning. Move. No answer. Stay visible. He had hated Mike's authority when it rounded danger down into route. Now his own rules had begun to take the same shape from the outside. Short. Final. Practical.

Being right did not keep a rule clean.

Joel wrote:

YES.

Will looked at the word for a long time. Then he gave one breath through his nose that might have been a laugh if the world had allowed it.

The red lamp dimmed further. Joel turned it off to save what little it had.

Darkness returned without depth.

They tried the watch plan. Joel stayed awake first because he did not believe he could sleep. Will lay on his back with his injured hand on his chest and his good hand closed around Joel's sleeve. That was not part of the written system, but Joel let it stand.

The night worked the camp in pieces.

A sleeping pad squeaked from beyond the canoe. Their pads were under them, compressed and still.

A paddle knocked once against a hull from the lake mouth. Their paddles lay inside the tarp, one under the ridge, one beside Joel's thigh.

Someone coughed in Will's dry way across the channel. Will did not move. Joel touched his shoulder anyway. Present.

Mike cleared his throat under the spruce and did not speak.

That was almost worse. The habit without the phrase. Authority gathering itself.

Joel looked at nothing until his eyes invented color in the dark. When he blinked, he saw the red lamp circle. When he closed his eyes, he saw Alex's cracked headlamp in mud, Mike's bent zipper pull in his palm, Will's handwriting cramped around IT DOES NOT KNOW WHY THAT HURTS.

He thought of Mara once, not as voice. He made himself think of her hands on the kitchen counter, the way she pressed one thumbnail into a grocery list when she was deciding what could wait. If he let memory speak, it would use her. If he kept it to image, maybe it would remain his.

Sleep pressed down in shallow waves. He jerked awake from each one before it became dream. The rope on his wrist tugged when he moved and told him the boat was there, or told him only that a rope was tied to him.

At some point Will touched Joel's arm twice.

His watch.

Joel did not want to give it to him. Will was exhausted. Will was hurt. Will was full of theory and grief and the bright, dangerous thought that the thing could be exposed. But Joel's own mind had begun to smear the sounds together. He could not tell whether the last branch crack had been outside or inside a dream.

He touched two fingers to his eyes, then pointed at Will.

Will nodded.

Joel held up the notebook where Will could see the page in the faintest gray from the tarp edge. He did not turn on the lamp. The written lines were invisible, but the object itself had become a command.

No voice.

Will nodded again.

Joel tried to sleep.

Sleep did not come whole. It came as falling. A drop through black water, a rope tightening, Will's hand on his sleeve, the canoe shifting, Alex laughing from a place under the gravel. Each time Joel surfaced, Will was still beside him, upright on one elbow or propped against the food barrel, eyes fixed toward the open edge of the tarp. The notebook lay closed against Will's thigh. Even closed, it looked usable.

Once Joel woke to Will leaning forward.

Not far. Only a few inches. Enough.

From outside the tarp, so close it seemed to stand where a man's face would be if he crouched at the opening, Alex said softly, "Quit mothering me."

Will's mouth opened.

Joel hit him.

It was not a punch. Not quite a slap. His palm struck Will's chest hard enough to knock the breath out before breath could become Alex's name. Will folded over the sound, bad hand jerking against his ribs. Joel grabbed him by the jacket and pulled him back under the tarp, hand already over his mouth.

Will stared at him.

Hatred came again, hotter this time. It had reason. Joel had hurt him. Joel had put a hand on his mouth like Will was a piece of gear to be managed. Joel had done it because the alternative was worse. The reason did not make the act less ugly.

Outside, Alex laughed once.

Then, in Joel's own voice from the waterline, low and practical, someone said, "Wait. Just wait."

Joel's grip tightened on Will's jacket.

Will's hatred changed.

He heard it too. He understood the gift Joel had not asked to give the thing: not only their dead, not only Mike's authority, not only Will's panic and theory, but Joel's restraint. Joel's own command had come back from the waterline.

Will nodded under Joel's hand.

Joel kept the hand there a second longer than he needed to.

He let go.

Will put both hands, good and bad, flat on the gravel between them. A gesture almost like surrender. Almost like prayer. His injured palm must have hurt against the stones. He kept it there anyway.

The tent zipper began after that.

It started far enough away to be memory. A small metal tooth catching, then freeing. A slow downward pull. The sound of a tent being opened by someone trying not to wake camp.

They had not pitched a tent.

The zipper moved again.

Closer.

Will closed his eyes. Joel could see the lids pale in the weak pre-dawn that had begun to gather without announcing itself. Not morning. Not yet. Only the dark losing a little of its hold on shapes.

The zipper came from behind them now, inside the spruce roots.

Zip.

Pause.

Zip.

Pause.

It was too slow to be a mistake. Too patient to be animal. It opened the old ordinary sound one tooth at a time.

Will reached for the notebook.

Joel caught his wrist.

Will looked at him.

Joel shook his head.

Not because writing was certainly unsafe. Because in that moment writing would become answering. The pencil would move because the zipper asked it to. Joel did not have proof. He had only the practical knowledge that a tool could become a habit, and a habit could be used.

Will understood. Or was too tired to fight.

The zipper stopped.

For a while nothing moved except the channel.

By the time the first gray entered the tarp, Will was sitting with his back against the food barrel, eyes open, injured hand held against his chest. He had not slept. Joel had slept in cuts so small they only made waking worse.

The canoe was still tied.

The food barrel was still closed.

The key was still in Joel's jacket.

The blue bag was still under the front seat.

Will was still beside him.

These facts did not feel like victory. They felt like items counted after a fire.
