Joel lifted the tarp edge and looked toward the lake. The mouth of the channel showed itself in pale strips between alder. Beyond it, wind moved over the longer water. Not hard yet. Enough to texture the surface. The route had not paused because men had survived a night.

They would have to pack. They would have to paddle across the longer lake. They would have to reach the final portage and put the canoe on shoulders or drag it or leave what could no longer be carried. They would do it with Will's hand worse, both men thinner in the mind, the night still holding every sound it had collected.

Will touched Joel's sleeve.

Joel looked at him.

Will put two fingers to his own eyes, then pointed at Joel.

Visible.

Joel returned the gesture.

Will looked toward the trees where Alex had been. Then toward the waterline where Joel's own voice had waited. His mouth tightened. For a second Joel thought he would speak anyway, not from carelessness but because the need in him had become larger than fear.

Instead Will picked up the notebook and wrote without turning on the lamp:

MORNING. MOVE.

He showed it to Joel with a face that gave nothing away. Then he turned the page back with his thumb until the night line showed in the gray.

IT DOES NOT KNOW WHY THAT HURTS.

He did not underline it. He did not point. He only let Joel see where his mind had stayed.

Joel nodded.

Outside the tarp, from somewhere no farther than the canoe, the flattened Will-voice said once more, very softly, "No, no, no."

Living Will closed the notebook.

He did not answer it.


# Chapter 24 - The Difference

Will was quieter in the morning, and that frightened Joel more than the night had.

Quiet had become one of their tools. Will's quiet was different. It had no discipline in it, no chosen edge. It sat under the low tarp while the light came in gray through the nylon and worked behind his eyes.

Joel woke in pieces. The channel first, muttering over gravel. Then the stone under his hip. Then the rope around his wrist, damp and stiff where it had dried against his sleeve. Then Will beside him, upright with his back against the food barrel, injured hand pressed to his chest, eyes open.

Not asleep. Not on watch either.

Thinking.

Joel lifted his head. The tarp crackled where frost had touched it and then failed. No true frost held on the gravel, but the air had the metallic bite that came before sun, if sun came. The canoe lay where it had been, a low dark form beyond the tarp opening, the painter still running from the hull to the spruce root and from there to Joel. The blue dry bag was still wedged under the front seat. The food barrel sat shut between Will's knees. The notebook was closed in Will's lap.

For one second, before the account of the trip returned, Joel mistook the scene for ordinary misery. Cold morning. Bad sleep. A friend sitting up early because his back hurt or his stomach had turned against camp food. The kind of morning they would have complained through, making coffee too strong, giving Mike grief for calling the site adequate, listening to Alex perform outrage at instant oats.

Then the woods behind them gave one careful cough in Will's dry voice.

Living Will did not move.

Joel put a hand on his shoulder anyway.

Will turned his head. His face had changed overnight. Nothing in it had broken in a visible way. Some social layer had been stripped from him: the quickness, the reflex to fill a silence before it embarrassed them, the small bright irritation that usually arrived before his words. He looked like a man holding back heat.

Joel pointed at the canoe, then at the sky, then made the paddling motion with two fingers.

Will nodded.

The false cough came again from the spruce. Then, after a pause too long for the real habit, Will's voice said, "That's not how that works."

The phrase had no object. No one had proposed anything. No one had made a bad argument or misread a knot or claimed batteries liked cold. It arrived after the cough the way a second boot arrives after the first: not thought, only order.

Will looked toward the trees.

Joel caught his sleeve.

Will's eyes stayed on the spruce one second longer. Then he looked down at the notebook in his lap, opened it, and wrote with slow care:

IT IS PUTTING PIECES TOGETHER.

Joel took the pencil.

DO NOT TEACH IT.

Will read that and did not answer. Before the layoff, he had spent years finding failures in circuits meant to make light: heat blooms, bad solder, dim strips that passed a short test and failed after hours. A traceable flaw was not a character judgment. It proved the system had lied first. The flaw was where a thing stopped pretending.

He turned the page back to the previous night's line.

IT DOES NOT KNOW WHY THAT HURTS.

The pencil mark had dug hard enough into the paper that the words were raised on the other side.

Joel wrote under it:

WE DO NOT KNOW WHAT IT KNOWS.

Will's mouth moved once without sound. The shape was almost a laugh and almost a correction. He took the pencil again.

WE KNOW ONE THING.

He waited until Joel was looking.

IT USES OLD SOUND.

Joel did not disagree. Disagreement would have been easier. He wanted Will's theory to be only grief wearing intelligence, something he could refuse cleanly. But the woods had been proving him right in pieces: right words from wrong sources, tone without cause, gear sounds placed where the gear was visible. Will kept seeing the joints.

Joel wrote:

RIGHT ENOUGH CAN STILL KILL US.

Will looked at the line a long time.

Then he closed the notebook and began packing.

They did it without talking. The tarp came down wet and stiff. Joel untied the lines in the order he had tied them, because reversing the work gave his hands a logic his mind could borrow. Will shook gravel from the sleeping pads with one hand. The pads squeaked, and both men stopped.

The sound went into the trees and returned as nothing.

No answer. No copy. Only the wait after it, which had begun to feel like another sound.

Will's injured hand looked worse in morning light. The bandage had slid and dried into its own grime. His fingers were swollen under the knuckles, the skin tight and shiny around the old cuts from the bad landing and all the later work they had forced from it. When he tried to cinch a pack strap, the hand did not close. It made a useless claw around the webbing.

Joel pointed.

Will shook his head.

Joel pointed again and held out his own hand for the roll of gauze.

Will hesitated. The old Will would have talked through the objection. He would have said something about circulation, infection, or the difference between medical tape and the useless novelty roll Mike had bought at a gas station. He would have made pain into a system and the system into speech. This Will only sat on the gravel and held the hand out.

Joel unwrapped the outer layer. The cloth came away with a small tearing sound where dried blood had caught it. Will looked over Joel's shoulder at the canoe and breathed through his nose. Joel rinsed the palm with a little filtered water from the bottle. It was too little to clean anything well and too much to spend easily. The water ran pink into the gravel and disappeared between stones.

Will flinched once.

Joel wrapped the hand tighter than comfort and looser than usefulness. Too loose and Will could not hold a strap. Too tight and the fingers would stiffen more. He fastened the tape with his thumbnail and looked up.

Will gave him a small nod.

Gratitude, or permission, or only confirmation that the task was over. Their gestures had grown too loaded. A nod had to carry what a sentence used to carry. Every movement had become crowded.

They ate because empty stomachs made hands clumsy. A torn tortilla each. Peanut butter scraped with the back of Joel's knife. Two mouthfuls of trail mix, most of it crushed peanuts and the sugared dust from something Alex had complained about on the first day. Joel found one pale yogurt-covered raisin at the bottom of the bag and almost threw it into the brush.

Alex had said those were for children and people recovering from dental surgery.

Joel put it in his mouth and chewed without tasting it.

Will watched him do it. For half a second something like the old camp rhythm moved across his face: the beginning of a joke, the wish to make Joel explain why he had eaten the worst piece last, the small shared pleasure of being disgusted by a dead man's snack choice. Then Will looked toward the trees, as if the joke had already traveled there ahead of him.

Joel closed the food barrel while Will watched. Thumb on black plastic. Ring aligned. Metal catch set. He pressed twice, then left his hand there.

From the waterline, the latch clicked.

The barrel under his hand did not move.

Will's eyes sharpened. Not fear first. Interest.

Joel shook his head.

Will held up one finger.

Wait.

From the waterline, after the impossible latch, Will's voice said, "There's a difference."

The wrongness was not in the sound. It had Will's dryness, the faint lift at the end, the thread of irritation that meant he was about to split a practical hair until everyone else wanted to drown him. It was wrong because nothing had been distinguished. The phrase came after a latch. It corrected no one.

Will's face changed.

Joel saw the danger of that change before he understood it. Recognition, not safety. The look of a man seeing a crack in a machine.

Will reached for the notebook.

Joel put his hand over it.

Will looked at him.

No, Joel mouthed.

Will pulled the notebook out from under Joel's hand with care. Not anger. Care was worse. It meant he had decided anger was a waste.

He wrote:

IT DOES NOT UNDERSTAND THE SENTENCE.

Joel wrote:

IT DOES NOT NEED TO.

Will read the line. His eyes lifted toward the waterline, where the copied phrase had come from. The lake beyond the channel mouth held low cloud on its surface, gray folded into gray. Alder leaned over the approach. Nothing stood there.

Will wrote:

IF IT DOES NOT UNDERSTAND IT CAN BE MADE WRONG.

Joel wrote:

WRONG STILL SOUNDS LIKE YOU.

Will pressed the pencil so hard the point snapped.

They both froze at the small crack.

The woods held it. Then gave it back as a dry twig breaking behind them.

Joel turned slowly.

Nothing visible. Spruce roots. Black openings between trunks. The flattened place where the tarp had been. Their own shapes in gravel. Morning had come far enough for sight to be useful and not far enough for sight to be kind.

Will found the pencil point in the gravel, pinched it between two fingers, and put it in his pocket as if waste mattered. It did. Everything mattered until it became weight.

Joel sharpened the broken pencil with his knife, shaving curls no longer than eyelashes onto a flat stone. The blade was dulling. The pencil was short. The page had gone soft at the corners from damp and hands. Even the tools of silence were wearing out.

He thought then of a test. He did not want to think of it. The thought came because Will had put the shape of it in the air even without speaking. If a man had to give the woods something, it should be something with no grief in it. No name. No command. No old phrase from the mouth of a friend. An object-fact, flat enough to resist intimacy and still recognizable if it came back wrong.

Blue bag under the front.

The phrase rose in Joel's mind and stayed there, useless for now. He did not write it. He did not mouth it. He did not let his hand move toward the page. Even the thought felt like setting food at the edge of camp.

The question of what to leave behind came last, and it came without a clean answer. One canoe. One heavy pack. One smaller pack that had become Joel's by use rather than ownership. Food barrel, damp tarp, stove and pot, wet sleeping bag, map case, filter, hatchet, notebook. The Highlander key stayed zipped inside Joel's jacket. The blue dry bag with the emergency paddle stayed under the front seat because its usefulness depended on the boat.

Then there were the small leftovers men acquired and could no longer sort from grief: torn cord, wrapper trash, a bent tent stake, a spare carabiner, Alex's cracked headlamp wrapped in a shirt, Mike's cap shoved into a side pocket after the cabin.

Joel touched the inner pocket where the key sat. He did not take it out.

Key. Car. Bridge. Road.

The words still lined up. That did not mean the world would honor the order.

Will pointed to the stove, then lifted his eyebrows.

Joel shook his head, then stopped. The stove was weight. It was also hot food if they had to survive another night. Hot food was not comfort anymore. It was calories that entered the body more easily. It was flame if cold began to make them stupid.

Will watched him thinking and made a small slicing motion with his good hand.

Too much.

Joel looked at the pack. Looked at the lake. Looked at Will's hand.

He put the stove inside the smaller pack.

Will made a face. Quick, almost old. An expression that said, of course, fine, let us haul the kitchen through a brush-choked portage because Joel has discovered sentiment about cookware.

Joel nearly smiled.

Will saw the almost-smile. His own face softened for half a second.

Then from the spruce roots Alex said, "Jesus, listen to this guy."

The old shape vanished.

Joel closed the pack.

They launched with no ceremony. The canoe scraped over gravel in a long quiet complaint that seemed too loud for morning. Will held the front with his good hand and hip, injured hand tucked against his vest. Joel eased the back around a half-submerged stone, then stepped in with cold water over his sandal straps. His feet had stopped objecting to cold as information. They only received it.

The lake beyond the channel was not wide, but wind did not need width to become trouble. It ran along the length of the water and lifted a small dark chop that slapped the canoe under the front seat, over the blue bag. Low cloud pressed the shores close. The spruce looked black and wet and identical on both sides, as if the lake had been cut through one continuous wall.

Will sat in front and paddled one-handed more than he should have. He used the injured hand when he had to brace, and each time Joel saw the white flash of bandage against the paddle shaft. The rhythm never settled. Nothing about them settled now. The boat was too loaded and still not loaded enough, because two men could not fill the weight four men had planned for. It rode wrong. It answered late. Every shift of Will's shoulders moved through the hull into Joel's knees.

Joel tapped twice against the gunwale with two fingers.

Left.

Will corrected the angle.

They had made a language of taps, points, touches, and written words, but a canoe wanted speech. It wanted quick correction. More right. Hold. Draw. Wait. Rock. The old useful words lined up behind Joel's teeth. He kept them there. His silence made the boat less safe. Speech would make the world less safe.

A loon called somewhere ahead.

Neither man looked for it.

The call came again from behind them, too soon.

Will's paddle paused.

Joel tapped once on the hull with his knuckle. Not a signal they had agreed on. Just enough.

Will resumed.

Halfway across, the filter squeaked from the left shore.

Their filter was in Joel's smaller pack between Will's knees.

Joel saw Will's head tilt, not toward the squeak exactly, but toward the fact of it. The same careful angle he had used over maps, fish knots, broken valves, the edge of a bad argument. He was not listening like prey. He was listening like a man trying to locate the flaw.

Joel tapped again.

Will paddled.

Then Mike's voice came from the right shore, calm and almost bored.

"We're exactly where we ought to be."

Joel kept paddling.

The sentence entered him differently now. Earlier it had been Mike smoothing time, space, doubt. A phrase that turned a bad sign into route confidence. Now it sounded like the old lie of the trip, though Mike had never meant it as a lie. The map said. The plan said. Competence said. We are here, therefore here must be right.

Will did not turn.

Joel watched the back of his neck, the wet hair at the collar, the line of his shoulders. He needed Will's stillness to be discipline, not fascination.

From the left shore, after a long delay, Will's voice answered Mike.

"That's not how that works."

Will stopped paddling.

The canoe immediately lost its angle.

Joel dug on the right, too hard, and the back swung. The chop caught them wrong. Water slapped the side and came in cold over Joel's shin.

Will twisted to look back.

Joel shook his head once, hard.

Will pointed at the left shore, then at the right, then held both palms apart.

Different.

Joel understood. The entity had made an argument without knowing it had made one. Mike's false certainty, Will's false correction. Two old voices in sequence. Not route logic. Only adjacency. Pattern wearing the clothes of thought.

Will pointed again, more urgently. Then to his ear. Then to his head. Then he tapped the notebook tucked inside his vest.

We can use that.

Joel shook his head.

Will turned forward. His paddle struck the water badly, throwing drops across the gunwale. Anger had entered the stroke. Good. Anger moved him away from shore. But anger needed an object, and Will had begun to believe the object could be forced to show itself by being wrong.

For the rest of the crossing the voices held off. That should have helped. It did not. Joel kept waiting for the next phrase, the next false correction, the next sound placed where no source could be. His shoulders tightened around expectation. His hands closed harder on the paddle. Silence had become not absence, but a held breath.

They reached the far landing near midmorning, though time had become an unreliable instrument. The portage landing was a narrow bite in the alder, no beach, only a slope of dark mud and flat stones slick with leaves. An old blaze marked a spruce ten yards up, nearly swallowed by bark. A strip of pink tape hung from a branch farther in, sun-faded to a color like old flesh.

Human marks. A blaze. Tape. A map line. Evidence of other people, and no people.

Joel stepped into water to his calves and held the canoe while Will climbed out. Will stumbled on the mud and caught himself with the bad hand. His face went white. The sound that left him was only breath, but Joel still looked into the trees to see whether the woods took it.

Nothing answered.

They unloaded in silence.

The final portage rose immediately away from the landing. Not steep at first, but mean. The trail cut through alder, then bent into young spruce where sight ended after ten steps. Rocks showed under mud where boots and hiking sandals had stripped the moss. Deadfall had been sawn sometime years ago and then left to rot at shin height. A man could follow it. A man could disappear on it while another man adjusted a strap.

Joel wanted to double back for loads the way they had early in the trip, one safe trip at a time with jokes and complaints and the false luxury of returning for what had been left. There was no safe double carry now. Leaving one man at either end meant creating exactly the absence the thing used. Carrying everything once meant weight, slowness, mistakes.

He wanted to write a rule for this place. Something stronger than the others. BODY IN SIGHT. NO TURNING. NO TESTS. But the trail itself made the rule dishonest. The canoe would blind him. The pack would slow Will. The brush would cut them into pieces.

Joel put two fingers to his eyes and pointed at Will.

Will answered the gesture, then looked past Joel toward the lake where the last false voice had been. His attention was no longer a thing Joel could hold with a rule.

Will took the notebook before Joel could write.

His hand shook, but the letters came clean.

I CAN MAKE IT CONTRADICT ITSELF.

Joel read it twice. The sentence had been growing in Will all morning. Seeing it on the page did not make it less dangerous. It made it official.

He wrote:

THAT IS ANSWERING.

Will wrote:

NO.

Then:

THERE IS A DIFFERENCE.

Joel stared at the line. The phrase had followed them since first water and ordinary argument, when Will could turn a distinction into a joke or an irritation and no one had to decide whether that habit would kill him. The divisions had only grown finer.

Joel wrote:

NO DIFFERENCE THAT KEEPS YOU ALIVE.

Will read it. He smiled a little.

Not happily. With pity.

The pity made Joel colder than the lake had.

Will leaned close, so close Joel could feel the heat of his breath before sound shaped it, and whispered, almost nothing, "That's not how that works."

Joel closed his eyes.

The words were out. There was no taking them back. They had crossed from page to air, from thought to material. Will realized it too late. His face tightened, not with fear only, but with the embarrassment of a man who had made the exact mistake he had been warned against and still believed the warning was incomplete.

From the trail ahead, in Will's voice, came: "That's not how that works."

The cadence was exact. The context was empty. No argument. No smile. No pity. Only the shell laid neatly on the trail.

Then from behind them, closer to the water: "There is a difference."

Will looked toward the landing. Joel watched him hear the mistake in the copy. Not the sound. The mistake under the sound. The phrase had the upward pressure Will put on it when he was annoyed, but no annoyance carried through. It had the outline of insistence and none of the wound.

Will's fear sharpened into certainty.

He pointed at the trail ahead, then toward the landing behind them. He made a small split motion with two fingers.

Different sources. Same voice. Wrong use.

Joel shook his head.

Will pointed at the notebook, then at the woods, then made a circle with his finger and broke it in two.

Make it fail.

Joel took the pencil.

WE MOVE.

Will took it back.

IF WE KNOW HOW IT FAILS WE CAN TELL WHAT IS REAL.

Joel wrote:

SIGHT IS REAL.

Will gave a soundless breath through his nose. It was not mockery. It was grief at how little Joel's method promised.

He wrote:

SIGHT FAILED MIKE.

Joel felt the line in his hands.

The clearing at the cabin. Mike at the edge. The buckle click. The glance away. The place where a man had been and then was not. Sight had not failed by lying. It had failed by ending.

Will wrote again:

SOUND CAN FAIL TOO.

Joel wanted to tear the page out. Not because it was wrong. Because it was useful.

The woods around the landing held still. The canoe knocked once against stone behind them.

Joel turned. The canoe lay where they had left it, half in water, half on mud, the painter looped around a root.

The knock came again from up the trail.

One hollow tap. Gunwale against rock. Impossible because the boat was behind them and visible.

Then Alex, from somewhere in the young spruce, said, "Hold up."

Will did not move toward it.

Joel allowed himself one breath.

Will pointed at Alex's direction, then shook his head.

Old.

He pointed toward the place the false canoe knock had come from.

New position.

He was not being lured by belief. That was the terrible part. He did not think Alex was there. He did not think Mike waited ahead or that another canoe had appeared up the trail. He was being drawn by knowledge, by the possibility that the thing's limit could be mapped from the edge and that a careful man would know when to step back.

Joel grabbed the notebook and wrote:

YOU ARE NOT STUDYING IT. IT IS MOVING YOU.

Will read it. The muscles around his mouth twitched.

He wrote:

BOTH CAN BE TRUE.

Joel remembered the night under the tarp. Mike is dead because we did not go after him. Mike is dead because it took him. Both can be true. Will had always been good at refusing false choices. It had made him maddening around campfires, useful around broken gear, and impossible now.

Joel took the pencil and pressed hard.

WE GO TO CAR.

Will looked at him and mouthed, without sound:

Car if real.

Joel almost wrote back: it is real because the key is real. He stopped himself. The key was an object. The car was a promise attached to an object. The bridge was a line on the map and a memory from the morning they had staged Mike's Highlander, when Alex's laugh had gone up under concrete and come back ordinary. None of that guaranteed anything beyond the next step. It only gave the next step a direction.

The final portage waited.

They arranged the load because arguing silently did not make the work less physical. Joel would take the canoe first, with the smaller pack cinched tight and low enough not to swing against his back. Will would take the heavy pack and the proper paddles lashed to the side. The food barrel would go in Will's pack for the first push, a bad choice that made the load too round and wrong but kept it off the open landing. Some food and the repair kit went with the barrel. Joel moved the map case, filter, stove, pot, tarp, and wet sleeping bag into the smaller pack because he could not make himself put every future in one place. The hatchet stayed at Joel's belt. The notebook went into Joel's jacket.

Will held out his good hand for it.

Joel shook his head.

Will's eyes hardened.

Joel kept the notebook.

The decision had the weight of theft. The notebook had become shared shelter, shared mouth, shared law. Taking it back into Joel's jacket said what Joel could not say aloud: I do not trust what you will do with language.

It also sounded, inside him, too much like Mike taking the map.

Will understood it exactly. That was part of the injury.

Will bent, picked up the heavy pack with one good hand and one damaged hand, and shouldered it before Joel could help. The straps bit wrong. He adjusted the left strap with his teeth, then used his forearm to shove the pack higher. The paddles knocked against the food barrel inside the lash and made a low wooden clack.

From the trail, the same clack answered.

Will smiled.

Joel hated the smile. It was thin and exhausted and almost triumphant, the smile of a man whose worst idea kept receiving evidence.

Joel lifted the canoe. The world vanished into green Kevlar and scratched inner hull. The yoke settled on his shoulders with an old pain that would once have meant only portage, only work, only the private satisfaction of doing a hard thing without complaint. Now the canoe blocked his upper sight. He could see the trail below the rim in strips: mud, stone, Will's hiking sandals, roots wet enough to shine.

He tapped the hull twice.

Will moved.

The trail accepted them badly. Within ten yards the brush closed around the canoe ends. Alder scraped the hull. Spruce needles raked Joel's forearms where his sleeves had ridden up. The yoke pressed into the knot of muscle at the base of his neck. He adjusted by feel, left hand on the front thwart, right hand steadying the back rim, each step chosen before weight came down.

Will walked ahead for the first stretch because Joel needed to see his legs under the canoe. The pack made him broad and slow. The paddles lashed to it stuck out at wrong angles and caught on brush. Twice he had to stop and back up, not speaking, while Joel balanced the canoe and waited for him to free the blade.

Behind them, Mike cleared his throat.

Will kept moving.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

Will stopped.

Joel could see only his calves and the bottom of the pack below the canoe rim.

Joel tapped the hull once.

Move.

Will did not.

From ahead, in Mike's voice, closer than it should have been, came: "Will. Just keep moving."

The name struck harder than the order. The key was in Joel's jacket, zipped under two layers, a hard small fact against his ribs. Key. Car. Bridge. Road. The words remained lined up inside him. Mike's dead authority kept trying to put obedience where judgment should be.

Will shifted.

Joel dropped one knee to lower the canoe enough to see over the rim. Will stood where the trail bent left around a knot of young spruce. He was facing not down the trail but into the trees on the right, where no trail went. His shoulders were high under the pack. His injured hand hung slightly open.

Alex said from the right, "Quit mothering me."

Will closed his eyes.

Joel lowered the canoe off his shoulders. He meant to set it down quietly. Fatigue ruined intention. The hull slid, caught a spruce branch, and crashed through brush beside the trail with a sound like something falling into water.

Will opened his eyes.

Joel stepped over the yoke and grabbed his sleeve.

"That's not them," Joel said.

The words came out rough and full-sized. Too loud. A failure with breath behind it.

Will looked back at him.

For one instant he was not the man from the last seven days. He was the boy from church basements, the man with too many hobbies, the friend who could not leave a wrong thing uncorrected. His face held fear, yes. But also apology, and the old infuriating light of having found the hinge in an argument.

"I know," Will said.

Joel tightened his grip.

Will did not pull away hard. He only turned his wrist with a small practical motion, the way he might free a snagged strap.

Then he turned, not toward Alex, but toward the error.


# Chapter 25 - Will Answers

Joel reached him before the trees took him, but not before Will answered.

"Say something new," Will called.

His voice cracked on new. It was not quite a shout. The portage would not accept a whole human sound. Alder and wet spruce broke it apart. The black spaces under the boughs took the pieces. The words carried just far enough to become available.

"Come on," Will said. "If you understand, say something new."

Joel had one hand fisted in the back of Will's vest and the other pressed against the notebook inside his own jacket. For a second he knew his remaining life by pressure. Notebook damp at the corners. Highlander key zipped in the inner pocket, hard under his pulse. Hatchet at his belt. Smaller pack biting across his shoulders. Dropped canoe behind him, green hull canted in brush where he had let it slide off wrong.

The car was not here. The road was not here. The key was only a small piece of metal against his chest. Still his mind kept trying to make a chain of it.

Key. Car. Bridge. Road.

From ahead, in Mike's voice, came, "We're fine."

Will let out one breath that might once have become a laugh.

"Old," he said.

Joel jerked on the vest.

Will twisted out of his grip with a strength Joel would not have credited to him an hour earlier. The heavy pack swung on Will's shoulders. The proper paddles lashed along one side knocked against the food barrel inside, a low wooden clack. Mud sucked at his sandal when he planted his foot. The trail here was less path than old decision, something other people had made before the brush took it back.

The same clack answered from deeper in the portage, farther ahead than either man had gone.

Will looked toward it.

Joel pointed back to the trail. Back to the canoe. Back to the strip of mud and cut brush that meant route, however little route promised now.

Will shook his head without looking at him.

From the left, Alex said, "Hold up."

Will turned his face toward the voice.

"Old."

It was worse than belief. Belief could be fought. Joel could write ALEX DEAD until the pencil tore through the page. He could point toward Second Camp, where Alex lay under gray fabric and stones. But Will knew. He was not following Alex. He was following the break in the imitation: the old phrase set down in the wrong place, the friend's voice with the friend missing from it.

Joel pulled the notebook out and opened it against his forearm.

He wrote:

THIS IS HOW IT MOVES YOU.

Will read it, then jabbed one finger toward the trees. He pointed at the line. He made a small cutting motion in the air.

Not moves. Shows.

Joel wrote over his own words, hard enough to smear them:

MOVE NOW.

From ahead, in Will's own voice, came, "There is a difference."

The sentence had his dryness, his exact little lift on difference, the mild irritation of a man correcting a distinction no one else cared about. But it did not belong to the moment. It answered nothing. It divided nothing. It was a hinge with no door.

Will's face changed.

Not wonder. Not fear. Something leaner. The look of a man hearing a gear skip. After the LED company had made him unnecessary in a language of restructuring and budget, Will had been living on small proofs of usefulness: a reel cleaned, a strap fixed, a bad solder joint found under a magnifier. A flaw proved by his hand was a kind of acquittal. Something had failed, and he could point to where.

"Old," he said again, softer.

Joel clamped a hand over his mouth.

Will's eyes came to him, close and furious. Not only furious. Hurt. The look was old enough to cross the years between them without help. Boys in church basements that smelled of carpet glue and powdered lemonade. Adults telling them when to speak and when to bow their heads. Will mouthing a joke at the wrong time, Joel looking down at his shoes so no one would see him laugh. Later, when Joel had stopped believing and Will had stopped caring enough to argue belief with him, the habit had remained. Will talked. Joel withheld. Each had trusted the other to know the difference between silence and abandonment.

Now Joel's palm was over Will's mouth.

Will pulled Joel's hand away with his good hand. His injured hand hung open and swollen at his side, the bandage gray at the palm and red where it crossed the knuckles.

"Listen to it," Will whispered.

Joel shook his head.

"No. Listen. It can't answer the question."

The whisper was almost nothing, but it was air. It was material. Joel felt it leave Will's mouth and enter the wet brush with the ticks of water, the scrape of packs, the small noises of a world that had never promised to stay empty.

From behind them, near the landing they could no longer see, Mike cleared his throat.

Joel turned despite himself.

Nothing. No orange PFD. No body at the bend. Only alder leaves, a strip of standing water in an old boot print, the dropped canoe half-hidden where its green hull leaned against spruce. The trail behind them looked longer than it had been.

Mike's voice said, "Just keep moving."

It still sounded practical enough to obey.

Will smiled. Small and awful, because he was afraid and because fear had not stopped the proof from pleasing him.

"You see?" he whispered. "It contradicts itself."

Joel wrote one-handed:

NO. IT REPEATS USEFUL THINGS.

Will looked at the line and made a small choking sound. Pain, fatigue, triumph, grief, all caught at once. He was not hearing rescue. He was hearing an error he thought he could survive by naming accurately enough.

"That's what I mean," he said.

From ahead, in Joel's voice, came, "Wait. Just wait."

Both men froze.

The sound of Joel's own voice did not feel like hearing another person. It felt like finding a handprint on the inside of his chest. Low, flat, nearly embarrassed by its own urgency. The phrase he had given the trip without ceremony. A practical brake. Now it stood ahead of them in the wet portage, wearing his mouth.

Will turned toward him at once. Not toward the sound. Toward living Joel.

For a half second the rule worked. Sight beat sound. Joel was there, breathing, pencil in hand, jacket zipped over the key. The false Joel had come from ahead, and the real Joel stood beside him.

Will's face softened.

Then the false Joel said again, deeper in the portage, "Wait. Just wait."

This time the timing was wrong. Too clean after the first. No impatience in it. No body behind the restraint. Will heard the wrongness and leaned toward it as if toward light through a door.

Joel grabbed his sleeve.

Will said, "It has your phrase. Not why you say it."

Joel shook his head. He could not write fast enough. He could not make the page do the work of his hand, and his hand had already failed.

He wrote:

IT DOES NOT NEED WHY.

Will touched the line.

"We do," he said.

The sentence was so quiet Joel almost wished the woods would miss it.

From the right, Alex's hard laugh broke once. It had all of Alex's surface in it, the bark, the demand that the group turn toward him, the big physical delight at becoming the room's center. It had none of the warmth that used to follow. None of the breath gathering for the next dumb thing.

Then Alex said, "Quit mothering me."

Farther right, as if another piece had been selected and set down, came, "I'm taking a piss. Nobody panic."

Will closed his eyes.

Joel saw the voice enter him. Not as belief. As memory. Alex in the old light at First Camp, making a face over bland food. Alex on the beaver dam, strong and annoyed and funny. Alex at Second Camp, half outside the firelight, wanting everyone to notice his exit. Every dead use of him was crude and exact. The woods did not have to understand grief. It only had to hit the shape grief left.

Will opened his eyes and said to the trees, "That's not new."

Joel slapped him.

Not hard enough to hurt the way he meant it to. Hard enough that Will stopped.

The sound of skin on skin moved out into the brush.

Both men listened.

From somewhere ahead, the same slap returned. Dry. Palm-flat. Impossible.

Will inhaled.

Joel seized the front of his vest and put his face close enough that the words should have belonged only to breath.

"Stop," Joel said.

The word came out before judgment reached it.

Will's eyes widened. Not because Joel had spoken, but because of what the word gave away. Stop. A command. A need. A sound with Joel's full weight behind it.

From the trees to their left, Joel said, "Stop."

The false voice had no breath on Will's cheek. It could place the word but not the warmth. It could make Joel into air and leave the body out. That was the difference Will wanted, and Joel hated it for being real.

Will turned his head slowly toward the left.

Joel put both hands on him and drove him back toward the trail. The shove surprised them both. Will stumbled, the pack striking a spruce trunk with a hollow thud. The paddles clattered. The food barrel shifted inside the lash. The portage answered with all of those sounds at once, ahead and behind and then from the right, a brief inventory of their remaining life repeated by something that had no use for any of it except as bait.

Will shoved Joel off with his forearm.

The shove was weak and badly aimed, but Joel was tired enough that it worked. He stepped back onto a slick root. His heel slid. The notebook fell from his hand and opened in the mud with a wet slap that sounded too much like a body finding ground.

Will looked at it.

So did Joel.

The page had opened to the cabin rules, swollen now and blurred by weather and hands:

DO NOT ANSWER. SIGHT. RIGHT WORDS WRONG SOURCE. JOEL KEY. TWO MEN.

Two men.

Will bent for the notebook.

Joel bent too.

From directly behind Will, in Mike's voice, came, "Key."

Will did not look back. His good hand closed on one wet corner of the page. Joel put two fingers on the other. Mud squeezed up between them.

For one second Joel thought that meant he had won.

Then Will said, still looking at him, "It put key in Mike's voice because Mike had said it. One syllable."

Joel slid his fingers under the notebook.

Will pressed down.

"Pattern," Will said. "Not meaning. Maybe only closeness."

His voice had changed. Less wild now. More himself. That was worse. This was the voice Joel had heard over gear spread on garage floors, over maps in bad motel light, over broken stoves and fishing reels and Mike's cheap canoe straps. Will explaining because explanation kept shame out of the room. Will correcting because precision made him useful. For years that habit had saved them small damages: a strap routed right, a stove cleaned before the O-ring failed, a map crease noticed before rain found it. They had all been glad, once, that Will could say no, no, no, this part matters.

"Will," Joel said.

The name was out before he could stop it.

Will froze.

So did Joel.

Will's hand came off the page.

Nothing answered immediately.

That delay was almost merciful. Almost intelligent. Or only distance and wet leaves and the shape of trees.

Then Joel's voice came from behind them.

"Will."

Not loud. Not a cry. Just the name as Joel had said it, with the small pleading drag he had not known was in it until it came back empty.

Will turned the wrong way.

Joel reached for him and missed the shoulder strap. His fingers caught only loose webbing. It burned through his palm.

"No," Joel said.

From the brush, Joel's voice said, "Will."

Will took one step off the portage.

The pack made the step wrong. Its weight pushed him past the small choice he had made, from edge to drop. One sandal slid into a hidden runnel. The paddles knocked against the barrel inside the lash. Will caught an alder stem with his good hand and stayed upright. The stem bent under him, green and living, and for one absurd second it held him.

Beyond the trail the ground fell into a shallow trough hidden by fern and alder. Not steep. Not dramatic. The kind of off-trail dip a man could step into, curse at, climb out of, and mock himself for later. Stones showed through the moss there, flat and dark, their faces slick with water. Beyond them the forest thickened, spruce packed close and gray dead branches crossing at knee and chest height.

Joel went after him.

Branches whipped his face. A dead twig scratched his lower lip and left the taste of blood. The pack on Will's back made a path by force, breaking small limbs and stripping needles. Joel saw the food barrel bulge round under the wet fabric. He saw the paddles swinging loose in their lash. He saw Will's injured hand half-open, fingers useless and white at the tips.

"Say something new," Will called again.

His voice had lifted now. It was no longer experiment only. It was desperation inside experiment's clothes.

"Say something you didn't steal."

From ahead, Will's own voice said, "Say something new."

Will stopped.

Joel nearly ran into him.

The copy came again, slightly to the right.

"Say something you didn't steal."

It was exact enough to hurt and wrong enough to be clear. The first sentence carried Will's crack on new. The second had no understanding of accusation. It made steal a sound, not a charge.

Will laughed once.

"There," he said.

Joel grabbed the back of his vest. "No."

"There," Will said again. "It can repeat the challenge. It can't answer it."

The opening ahead was only a wet place where two old trees had fallen and let in a little more light. Stones showed under moss. Alder grew low at the far edge. The air smelled of rot and cold water. Mosquitoes hung in a loose dark stitch above the wet. Nothing in it should have held a man.

Something seemed to move there.

Joel did not see a body. He saw a pale angle between trunks, lower than a standing man and higher than a crawling animal. He saw a dark wet shoulder or flank, if shoulder and flank were words that could be trusted. He saw limbs, or branches, crossing where no branch should have crossed. He saw enough for his mind to begin sorting and not enough for any sorted thing to stay sorted.

Bear. Moose. Man.

None of the words held.

Will saw it too.

His face emptied.

For the first time since he had stepped off the trail, he looked less like a man proving a theory than a man who had reached the end of one.

He said, very softly, "Oh."

It was small. It was his last living word, the new thing he had demanded and then given.

The motion came low and fast.

Not from the place Joel had fixed on. From the left of it. That was what stayed with him afterward. Sight had not lied. It had been narrowed. A pale flash drew the eye. A voice held the head. The real motion used the gap between.

Will went down without a scream.

The pack hit stone first. The impact drove the breath out of him in a hard animal grunt. His injured hand struck next, bandage bright against black rock. Joel saw the fingers spread and fold wrong. Then alder took the upper half of him. The brush thrashed once, high and violent, as if a canoe had been shoved through it sideways.

Joel had the hatchet in his hand.

He did not remember drawing it.

For one impossible second the world offered him the old shape of courage. Charge. Swing. Get him. Do not leave another man. The shape was so familiar it almost became command, every childhood lesson about rescue and loyalty stripped of church language and made into muscle.

Then Will's legs kicked once in the moss and vanished sideways.

The alder closed.

Joel stood with the hatchet raised. The hand with the hatchet did not go forward. That was what he would have afterward instead of an explanation.

He backed away.

One step.

Then another.

He kept the hatchet up until his wrist began to shake. The opening did not move. The pale angle was gone. The dark wet shoulder was gone. Only alder. Only stones. Only a strip of bandage caught on a low branch, white and then pink where water or blood moved through it.

From the brush came Will's voice.

"No, no, no."

Panicked. Perfect. Empty.

Joel's foot found the runnel. Water filled his sandal. He did not look down.

"No, no, no."

This time it came from the portage behind him, ahead of where living Will had been. Too soon. Too clean. The panic preserved but not lived.

Joel backed to the trail.

The notebook lay in the mud where it had fallen. He wanted it with an intensity that surprised him. Not for the rules. Not only for the rules. It was the last place where two men had still been two men. A shared page. A small roof made of paper, already leaking. Their last argument had weight there. Their last mercy did too, if mercy could be made of block letters and mud.

Will's voice said, from the brush, "Joel."

Joel bent for the notebook.

The name came again from ahead, in Will's voice. Then from behind, in Joel's own.

"Joel."

He picked up the notebook and closed it without reading the open page. Mud smeared under his thumb. He shoved it inside his jacket, against the key.

The canoe waited where he had dropped it, half in brush, its green hull catching weak light. It looked too ordinary to have survived the scene. Scratched, wet, stubborn. A thing made to cross water, now lying on a trail with two dead men's sounds around it.

From the alder: "No, no, no."

Joel put the hatchet back at his belt. His hand did not want to let go of it. He had to open each finger.

The heavy pack was gone with Will: food barrel, proper paddles, most of the remaining food, the repair kit, cord, the dense small odds they had kept because weight had not yet become impossible enough. The loss moved through Joel practically before it moved through him morally. That would not forgive him. He had the canoe. He had the small pack. He had the hatchet, the mud-smeared notebook, the key, and the blue dry bag with the emergency paddle still under the front seat. He did not have Will. The sentence tried to become an inventory and failed.

Will laughed from somewhere to the right.

Not his real laugh from before. A cough-laugh from the old days, from before correcting someone. It appeared near the trail, then farther ahead, then behind Joel at shoulder height, as if the sound were being tried in different hollows.

Joel lifted the canoe.

The yoke came down on the bruised place at the base of his neck. Pain steadied him. The hull narrowed the world to mud and roots and the thin view under green Kevlar. He could not see far. He could not afford not to see. He had chosen a tool that blocked sight because leaving it meant losing water.

This was the kind of choice left.

He took one step.

From the brush behind him, Alex said, "You coming or writing a poem?"

Joel did not answer.

He took another step.

Mike cleared his throat ahead.

"We're exactly where we ought to be."

Joel stopped.

The phrase moved through the hull and into the small chamber around his head. It filled the canoe's interior like breath. Mike's certainty. Mike's old managerial mercy, the kind that asked evidence to lower its voice. The woods did not have to believe it. It only had to know where to put it.

Joel lowered his head under the hull until his forehead almost touched the yoke.

He did not answer.

Will's voice came from the right side of the trail.

"There is a difference."

Joel moved.

The portage accepted him badly. The canoe struck alder on both sides. Brush scraped the hull in long dry strokes. Twice he had to stop and back up to free the front from young spruce. Each stop opened a place for sound.

Will: "No, no, no."

Alex: "Hold up."

Mike: "Just keep moving."

Joel's own voice: "Wait. Just wait."

The words came from wrong distances, wrong heights, wrong sequences. They made arguments without meaning to. They instructed and pleaded and corrected one another in the damp green air. They were almost human because humans had made them.

Joel kept his mouth shut.

His breath grew loud inside the hull. He hated that too. Fear made a sound of him. Pain did. Cold did. He tried to breathe through his nose, slowly, and failed. His foot slipped on a wet stone and his knee struck root. He stayed upright because dropping the canoe would make a sound large enough to gather everything.

At the next bend, the portage widened enough for him to set the canoe down without crashing it.

He did not set it down.

Behind him, Will said, "Oh."

Joel nearly fell.

The sound was too small. Not the panic phrase. Not the old correction. The last living word, returned before it had cooled. It came once from behind, from the place where Will had seen enough to stop speaking.

Then again from ahead, too clean.

"Oh."

The copy held no discovery. No end of argument. Only the vowel, lifted and placed.

Joel understood then that Will had been right about the limit and wrong about what a limit offered. The thing did not understand. It did not need to. Knowledge did not have to be false to be bait. A wrong answer could still move a body. A hollow sound could still put weight in the wrong place.

He walked.

The trail began to slope down. He smelled water before he saw it, cold and mineral, with the faint rotten sweetness of leaves at a lake edge. Spruce thinned. Light widened under the canoe. The final lake lay somewhere ahead. Beyond that, if the map and key and road still belonged to the same world, the bridge and the Highlander waited.

Will said, far behind him, "No, no, no."

Joel put one foot down, then the next, and carried the canoe toward the last lake alone.


# Chapter 26 - Alone

Alone simplified the arithmetic and ruined everything else.

Joel had one canoe, one small pack cut down to what could still be called useful, one map going soft at the folds, one Highlander key zipped against his chest, one hatchet, one filter, one weak headlamp, two crushed bars, one wet sleeping bag he did not plan to unroll, and one blue dry bag wedged under the front seat with the emergency paddle inside it.

He had daylight draining through low cloud.

He had three dead men behind him who would not stop speaking.

They did not come as a chorus. A chorus would have become weather. The woods spent them one at a time, which left each voice alone long enough to remain a person.

Mike said, "Just keep moving."

Alex asked if he was coming or writing a poem.

Will coughed from ahead, then from behind, then from so close that Joel felt the old expectation of breath on the back of his neck. No breath came with it.

Joel kept the canoe on his shoulders and did not turn.

The portage bent through black spruce and alder, then dropped and climbed in small useless humps. With two men under load, three before that, four before the world began spending them, the rises had been annoyances. Now each one made him stop and set his feet. The yoke pressed into the bruised base of his neck. The hull narrowed his sight to mud, root, branch, his own hands on the gunwales, and the thin strip of trail under green Kevlar.

The canoe had become rescue and blindfold. He needed it for the last water. He hated it for taking the sky away.

At a fallen cedar the trail widened enough for him to set the canoe down.

Quietly was not silent.

The hull touched wet duff with a soft, broad sigh. A branch sprang loose and ticked along the side. Water trapped inside the boat shifted toward one end and clicked against a rib.

From the brush, the same water-click answered.

Joel stood over the canoe and waited. Not because waiting was safe. Movement without knowing what had moved was worse.

The click came again from ten yards off the trail. Then farther away. Then behind him, quick and small, as if a hand had learned only the last inch of the motion.

Not a voice. Not a command. Only the boat making itself somewhere the boat was not.

That was the new pressure. A man could teach himself not to answer his dead friend. He still had to know whether the boat had shifted.

He opened the notebook against his thigh. Mud had dried along the spine and cracked when he bent it. The swollen pages near the middle clung together. Earlier rules showed through later paper like bruises: DO NOT ANSWER. SIGHT. RIGHT WORDS WRONG SOURCE. TWO MEN.

He did not let himself look long at TWO MEN.

On a cleaner space at the bottom of the page he wrote:

NO NAMES.

NO ANSWERS.

ONLY SEEN IS REAL.

SOUND IS BAIT.

He started to tear the page loose, then stopped with the paper strained under his thumb. Tearing was sound. Everything was sound. He held the notebook open long enough to learn the page as an object: four black blocks, bottom right, mud thumbprint at the margin. When the boat was on his shoulders he would have only memory. He closed the notebook and put it back inside his jacket.

The pencil shook in his fingers. He put it away before he wrote a name by accident.

Names opened the air. A name made a shape a body was supposed to fill.

He wanted to say Will's name.

He wanted it with the stupid part of him that still expected Will to come stomping up behind him, angry and filthy and triumphant, carrying the heavy pack wrong because he had found some technical reason the obvious way was not best. He wanted the cough before a correction. He wanted no, no, no from a living mouth, irritated at Joel's assumptions and not emptied by brush.

He closed his teeth until his jaw hurt.

From the portage behind him, Will said, "There's a difference."

Joel let the sentence pass through him like cold water through a sleeve. The living Will had been right about differences. That was part of the damage. Right source, wrong source. Sound, sight. Repetition, understanding. A man could be right all the way to the wrong place.

Joel put one palm on the canoe hull. Its surface was cold and slick with old rain. Real because it resisted him. Real because when he pressed harder his hand hurt.

He lifted the canoe.

The next quarter mile taught him what a man could leave.

At first the pile was practical: the damp tarp folded once under spruce, the extra pot set inside it, the stove wrapped in a sock because loose metal ticked. Ten minutes ago those things had still been gear. Now they were weight. Joel stood too long looking at the stove. It had been ordinary as a hinge all week. Click, fuel, flame. Coffee, oats, soup, something hot enough to tell the body it had not been discarded yet.

He left it.

There was no one to witness the choice, which made it feel less like sacrifice than littering. He hated that. He had been raised to pack out wrappers, orange peels, fishing line, the smallest human evidence. Now he was leaving a stove under spruce because the living part of him had become lighter than the etiquette of the woods.

Ten minutes later he left the wet sleeping bag.

That was harder. Not because he believed he would sleep. Sleep had become a thing that belonged to another species. But a sleeping bag meant a plan extending past dark. It meant a man was not only moving but expected to stop and still continue. It was a promise of a body worth warming.

Mara would have asked about it if he reached her. Not first. First would be where are you, are you hurt, who is with you, words with their ordinary claims on answer and body. Later, after the facts had been divided into what could be said and what had to stay in the notebook, she would ask if he had kept warm. He would have to tell her no in a language that did not include this hollow under balsam.

He unstrapped the bag from the small pack, water heavy in the insulation, and pushed it under the low limbs. He did not hide it well. He did not have enough hope to hide it well.

Alex laughed somewhere above him, wrong height, wrong place, caught in spruce like a thrown object.

"Quit mothering me," Alex said.

Joel's hands paused on the strap.

He saw Alex at First Camp holding a mug like a microphone, making everyone look at him, making Joel resent the need to laugh and then laugh anyway. He saw Alex at the beaver dam with mud up his shins, strong and complaining, one arm out to steady a pack that was not his. He saw the cracked headlamp and the red place in the alder.

The sleeping bag lay under the tree like something already finished.

Joel tightened the remaining strap and moved.

Weight had its own blunt judgment now. Anything kept meant another minute under the yoke, another stumble, another opening for sound. Anything left meant a future crossed out.

The map failed next, though he could not leave it. Grit had worked into the case seal. Water had entered through a corner days ago and softened the paper. Contours blurred where his thumb had pressed them too often. The blue shapes of lakes still held. The black portage line still appeared in pieces. The final lake was there: narrow at its south end, spreading north, then pinching toward the outlet and North Road Bridge.

The map said the take-out was beyond the lake.

The map had also said there was a cabin.

That was what maps did badly. They did not lie. They kept old agreements after the world stopped honoring them.

Joel folded it along a crease that no longer wanted to fold and slid it back under the cord. The Highlander key pressed cold against his sternum in the inner pocket of his jacket. It had become more intimate than a key should be. A little cut piece of another man's confidence, carried against Joel's heart.

He touched the pocket once to confirm it.

From ahead, Mike cleared his throat.

Joel stopped before the reflex completed. He had almost looked for Mike. Not because he believed. Because a body learns before the mind can forbid it. Mike cleared his throat before a decision. Men who had followed him for years turned toward that sound.

"We're exactly where we ought to be," Mike said.

The words moved through the tunnel of the canoe and filled the space around Joel's head. Mike's route certainty, preserved without Mike. The trip reduced to one sentence the woods did not have to believe.

Joel looked at the mud under his feet.

One sandal strap had torn at the side. He had tied it with cord, and the knot pressed into the soft place above his arch. His left knee had begun to swell from the root he struck after Will died. His hands were pale and dirty around the gunwales. Blood had dried in the cracks of his knuckles and turned almost black.

Those were facts. His feet. His hands. The trail he could see.

He moved.

When the voices did not turn him, the portage used smaller sounds.

A boot scraped stone behind him. One scrape, then another, spaced like a tired man following. Joel kept walking until the scrape came again, closer. He stopped and turned with the whole canoe, slow, because the hull would catch if he spun.

Nothing stood in the trail.

The mud held his own tracks, deep at the heel where fatigue made him careless. A strip of alder leaf trembled. Water fell from spruce tips. The portage behind him curved out of sight as if the world had been folded there.

A boot scraped stone ahead.

Joel faced forward again.

He did not write a new rule. The old one covered it. SOUND IS BAIT. He repeated the words without moving his mouth.

The portage dropped into a wet section where old planks had once crossed the mud. Only three remained, black, slick, and half sunk. He set the canoe down before them and made himself plan the passage. One man under a canoe could not see where the back end swung. One man could not have another voice say left, easy, hold up, your end's catching. He had to become both ends and still remain one body.

He dragged the canoe sideways around the worst mud, lifted the front over a fallen branch, stepped into muck to mid-calf, and pulled. Mud closed cold around his sandal. The sucking sound was obscene in its familiarity. It had been funny on Day 2: Alex making a performance of losing a sandal, Will explaining that peat behaved differently from clay, Mike telling everyone to keep moving because mosquitoes did not care about footwear.

Now the mud made the same wet mouth around Joel's foot, and the woods answered with another pull, another release, somewhere out of sight.

He leaned his weight on the canoe and freed himself slowly.

The sandal came up full of muck. He scraped it against the edge of a plank before he could seat the strap under his heel. His fingers would not do small work easily anymore. They pinched and missed. The cord knot at the side of the sandal held, slid, then held again.

He wanted Will's voice to tell him it was a bad knot.

He wanted the correction so sharply that wanting became almost audible inside him.

From the right, Will coughed.

Not words. Worse, maybe, because the cough had no argument in it. It was Will before speech. Will about to explain. Will alive in the half second before language armed him.

Joel closed his eyes.

Only briefly. Too briefly for the dark behind his lids to become anything. Then he opened them and looked at the canoe, the planks, the mud, the line of the trail. He had no room for reverence. No room for farewell. Ceremony wanted words.

He lifted the canoe again.

The emergency paddle was a joke of a thing by canoe-trip standards: aluminum shaft, black plastic blade, two pieces that screwed together with a collar. Mike had mocked it when he saw it packed under the front seat, then kept it because even mockery had limits around water. The proper paddles had been lashed to Will's heavy pack. They were gone with him into the alder, with the food barrel, most of the remaining food, the repair kit, and the coil of cord Joel had meant to keep.

The emergency paddle clicked under the seat each time the canoe tilted.

Click.

From the left, another click.

Joel set his jaw and carried it anyway.

He thought of the group before sound had become poisonous. Four men took up space differently than one. Four men made ordinary noise around an object until the object almost disappeared inside use: jokes over lashings, argument over load order, a paddle knocked loose and cursed at, Alex asking if anyone had seen his sunglasses while the sunglasses sat on his hat. The canoe had belonged to all of them then. It had been lifted badly, corrected, balanced, shoved into water, bumped against rock, cursed into usefulness.

Now it was weight and green hull and the fact of the last lake.

At the next rise, the trail narrowed under young spruce. Branches clawed both sides of the canoe. He had to turn sideways, lower the front, back up, lift again, and push through by inches. Each touch made the hull speak. Scratch, knock, scrape. The portage repeated the sounds ahead of him and behind him until the whole trail seemed to be carrying a canoe badly.

Then his own voice said, from a bend he had not reached, "Stop."

The word was small and plain. It did not have the pleading drag that had been in Will's name. It was the word he had given the woods with his hands on Will's vest, breath hot and useless between them.

Joel stood under the canoe until the sound thinned out.

He had already failed that once. He would not fail it for himself.

He stopped once to drink.

The filter squeaked when he loosened it from the side pocket. He had half a bottle left, cloudy with grit, warm from his body. He took three swallows. His throat wanted more. He capped it and put it away.

The filter squeaked from the trees.

Then Alex said, "Jesus, listen to this guy."

It was almost funny. The timing, the old line dropped into a moment with no guy left to listen to. Joel felt laughter rise in him as nausea. He bent over with both hands on his knees and kept his mouth shut.

If he laughed, it would have his laugh.

He had not thought of that with enough force. The entity had Joel's voice. It had "Wait. Just wait." It had "Stop." It had Will's name, said with pleading in it. It might not have his laugh. Joel had not laughed much after the first day. He could not remember the last time. The possibility made silence possessive.

He stood.

The trail changed near late afternoon. He knew it by light before map. Spruce thinned. The ground began a long, steady drop. Air moved differently ahead, cooler and wider, carrying the mineral smell of open water and wet stone instead of only rot and needles. A raven called once from somewhere high. He waited for the call to repeat wrong, but the sky gave him nothing.

The ordinary world had not stopped. Some sounds were still only sounds. Water still dripped from branches. Birds still moved through black spruce. Wind rubbed the lake ahead, indifferent to whether one man reached it.

The final stretch of portage was rockier. Granite showed through the moss in gray backs. His sandals slipped twice. The canoe's weight had become a fact beyond pain, a number too large to use. His shoulders went numb and then sharpened again. The yoke seemed to settle into bone.

Will said from behind him, "I can make it contradict itself."

Joel nearly answered in his head.

No.

He bit the inside of his cheek and tasted blood.

"No, no, no," Will said.

The phrase came lower this time, more private. Not the panic from the brush after he died, but the old Will before panic had taken him: correcting a gear mistake, resisting a bad explanation, trying to keep the world from being stupid. Joel saw him in Will's garage years ago with a headlamp strap around his forehead in daylight, both of them half-drunk, Will taking apart a cheap lantern because the battery contact offended him.

No, no, no, that's not how that works.

Joel had laughed then. Will had looked pleased and pretended not to be.

Joel stopped. The canoe rocked on his shoulders.

He did not know how long he stood there.

The woods waited with him. Or did not. It was impossible to tell patience from absence.

From the brush at his left, close enough that the alders trembled after the sound, Will whispered, "Joel."

The name did what a hand would have done. It touched him.

He almost turned.

The canoe saved him by being stupid and heavy. To turn, he would have to commit the whole hull. He would have to step back, pivot, let the front swing into spruce, make a sound large enough to call the dead from every side. The physical difficulty gave the rule time to catch up.

ONLY SEEN IS REAL.

He looked at the trail ahead until the desire to look left passed from command into ache.

"Joel," Will said again.

This time it came from ahead.

Joel walked through it.

The final portage ended without ceremony. No sign. No blaze. No sudden widening into safety. The trail simply gave up into a sloped mix of gravel, mud, and flat stones where the last lake took the land in small dark licks. Alder crowded the left side. Low black spruce ran down the right shore. The water stretched north under dusk-colored cloud, a long narrow reach between dark banks, wrinkled by wind moving along it rather than across.

For a minute he could not step out from under the trees. The lake looked too open. Not safe. Only exposed in a different way. On the portage every sound had a trunk, a bend, a clump of alder to hide behind. Water had no walls. It would carry a voice cleanly. It would make distance behave like a lie.

Wind across the lake would have ended him or made him wait for dark, which was another way to end. Wind along it was trouble but not refusal. He could paddle that if his hands kept working and if the short emergency paddle held and if the map meant anything and if he did not fall asleep sitting up.

He lowered the canoe carefully at the landing.

The woods behind him assembled the group.

Mike cleared his throat.

Will said, "I can make it contradict itself."

Alex laughed.

The sounds did not overlap. They took turns like men around a fire, each waiting for the old rhythm to receive him. The arrangement hurt more than noise would have.

Joel crouched beside the canoe and inventoried what could go forward.

Small pack: map, filter, bottle, headlamp with weak batteries, knife, two energy bars crushed into meal, first-aid roll reduced to tape and dirty gauze, lighter in a plastic sleeve, pencil, notebook when it was not in his jacket. Hatchet at belt. Key in inner pocket. Blue dry bag under the front seat, with the emergency paddle, a pair of socks, the torn corner of the old repair sleeve, and the little orange whistle nobody had used because a whistle was only a mouth made of plastic.

He clicked the headlamp on against his palm. The light came up yellow and weak, then steadied. He clicked it off before the small plastic switch could become an invitation. The lamp would be for the map only. Not for searching trees. Not for proving voices wrong. Light was not the same as sight if fear chose where to point it.

He took the whistle out and held it.

It was almost weightless, molded orange plastic, practical in exactly the way the old world had been practical. Three blasts for distress. A small signal that assumed anyone hearing it would want to help. It had not done anything wrong. It was still impossible to keep.

Joel threw it into the lake.

Not far. His throw was bad with fatigue. It struck water five yards out and floated for a moment, bright and ridiculous, before a ripple turned it over. He watched until it drifted beyond reach.

Alex said, "Hold up."

Joel loaded the canoe.

He did it without hurry because hurry made noise and mistakes. The pack went behind the middle thwart, close enough to reach, low enough not to shift. He pulled the blue dry bag out, took the emergency paddle from it, screwed the two sections together, and tightened the collar until his fingers slipped. The joint flexed. He tightened it again. The bag went back under the front seat where he could see it. He turned the canoe parallel to the water and checked the painter knot. The shortened rope was stiff with dried mud.

Each action arrived with the ghost of the man who should have argued about it.

Mike would have told him the trim was wrong.

Will would have said there was no point calling it trim when it was one exhausted idiot in one ruined canoe.

Alex would have asked if this was the part where Joel became rugged and insufferable.

Joel put one hand over his mouth until the almost-smile died there.

He needed direction. That was the hard fact beneath all the others. The map showed the lake running north, outlet at the far end, bridge beyond, Highlander waiting if the key still mattered and the road had not swallowed itself in rain. But the shoreline near the landing split into two dark possibilities. A left-hand channel tucked behind a reed point. A broader right-hand reach held the wind and looked more like the main lake. In daylight with Will and Mike and time, they would have read the shore, checked the map against slope and contour, argued, corrected, chosen. Alone at dusk, with voices in the trees, every direction could be made to sound like help.

He could not ask the map to speak.

He could not ask the dead.

He needed a test that was not a question.

The thought came without drama. It was not bravery. It was procedure. The thing used what the men had given it. So give it one new thing. A stone with no blood on it. A fact flat enough that its use would show only placement.

He looked for an object.

The blue dry bag sat under the front seat. It was visible. Its color had no memory except inventory. Not Alex's food complaints. Not Mike's route certainty. Not Will's correction habit. Not Joel's pleading. Bag. Blue. Under the front.

He opened the notebook to the wet rules page and wrote under SOUND IS BAIT:

GIVE IT ONE THING.

Then, on the torn corner of the map margin, he wrote:

BLUE BAG UNDER THE FRONT.

He stared at the words until they flattened.

The woods stayed quiet.

Quiet was not consent. Quiet was only the absence of a sound he had noticed.

He tucked the torn margin under the thwart cord, facing up. He wanted the words where his eyes could find them after his mind began to slip. Then he stood at the edge of the landing, facing the lake, not the woods.

His throat did not want to open. It had learned too late.

He waited until the wind moved along the water and covered the tremor in his breath. He looked at the bag. He looked at the right-hand reach. He looked at the left-hand channel. He held both possibilities in his mind and gave the woods nothing from either one.

Then he said clearly, once, "Blue bag under the front."

The sentence went out over the lake and came back smaller, thinned by open water.

Behind him, nothing answered.

He waited until waiting became another way to answer.

Mike said, very softly, "Just keep moving."

Joel did not obey Mike. He obeyed the daylight leaving the water.

He set both hands on the canoe and pushed it toward the lake.


# Chapter 27 - The Planted Phrase

The planted phrase helped him first because it was stupid.

No memory hung from it. No campfire. No old argument. No dead man's way of handling fear. Blue bag under the front. A fact small enough to fit in the hand.

The canoe was already moving when he understood that.

He had shoved off from the gravel with both hands on the gunwales, one foot in the lake and one on stone. Cold went through his sandal and up his shin. For a second the hull tried to leave without him. The emergency paddle lay crosswise over the seat. The small pack slid toward his knees. The torn map margin under the thwart cord lifted and slapped once, then settled. He got in badly, weight too far right, and the canoe rolled under him like a boat that still expected another body.

He did not swear. Swearing was a gift. Breath was nearly a gift.

He sat low and waited for the wobble to pass. The landing went dark behind him. He did not turn to measure it.

The first time the thing used the phrase, Alex said it from the left shore.

"Blue bag under the front."

Teasing. Almost amused. As if the bag had done something funny and Alex had found the smallest possible way to make Joel look.

Joel did not look left.

He looked at the bag.

It sat under the front seat, blue dulled by mud and last light, its rolled mouth buckled twice, one corner creased where the aluminum tube of the emergency paddle had pinned it all day. Not rescue. Only a fact. The voice had moved. The object had not.

That was enough. Enough now meant one fact holding for one breath.

Wind moved along the lake in black wrinkles. The right-hand reach opened ahead, darker than the shore but less dark than the alder-choked left channel where Alex's voice had placed itself. The left looked like a mistake in the shoreline, a reed seam no tired man would choose unless the map forced him to look again. The map showed a north-running lake, then a pinch, then water beyond. The map had been right in shapes and wrong in mercy.

Joel set the short paddle on the right side and pulled.

The canoe answered late.

Everything about it was wrong. Too little weight ahead, too much human weight behind, no second paddle correcting small mistakes before they spread. With Alex in front, the boat had argued but still had another body reading water. With Will, after Alex, it had been overloaded, miserable, and alive with disagreement. One man in the back made the canoe ride light and foolish ahead of him. Each stroke swung the empty front wide. Each correction spent strength he did not have.

He tried kneeling and could not hold it. His left knee had swollen tight under wet fabric. His right had gone deep and hot where he had dropped against a root with the canoe on him. Sitting raised him too high; kneeling gave control and then took it back as pain. He settled into the ugly compromise survival offered: one knee down, one foot braced, hips twisted, back already failing.

It was bad paddling.

It moved him.

Alex laughed once from the left shore.

The sound ran along the reeds, touched open water, and came back thinner. Joel saw, without asking to, Alex leaning over the rail at North Road Bridge on the first morning, pleased with his own echo, not knowing he had given the woods a rehearsal. The memory arrived bright and useless. Joel let it pass without giving it his mouth.

The second use came ten strokes later.

Will said it from behind him.

"Blue bag under the front."

Not amused now. Technical. Urgent. Will made the phrase into an instruction Joel had failed to obey correctly. Joel could hear the next sentence without hearing it: no, no, no, you're shifting the trim, that's not how that works.

His shoulders tightened. The front wandered. The left side dipped toward a small slap of chop.

He corrected too hard. The paddle blade fluttered. Aluminum flexed at the joint. The cheap collar gave a dry click.

From behind him, the click answered.

He wanted Will there with a fury so sudden it felt physical. He wanted the correction. He wanted the fight. He wanted Will in the front, cold and furious and alive, turning Joel's steering into a lecture on leverage and drag. The wanting came before thought and nearly became speech.

He pressed his tongue hard against the roof of his mouth.

The boat steadied by inches.

He could not write while moving. The old rules had to hold without the page: NO NAMES. NO ANSWERS. ONLY SEEN IS REAL. SOUND IS BAIT. GIVE IT ONE THING.

He had given it one thing. It had not become a map or a shield. It was only a mark on the water, a way to see where sound wanted him. Will would have hated how little that proved. The weakness kept the test in its place.

The lake narrowed by degrees. This was not yet the final crossing. It was the approach the map had drawn with one careless blue gesture: last water, pinch, outlet, then the blacker lake beyond. Low reeds showed in clumps to the left. The right shore lifted into black spruce and small granite shelves, sliding past too slowly. Dusk thickened under the cloud. The water stopped reflecting sky and began holding it in broken, darker strips. Every paddle stroke cut the surface and then vanished.

Joel tried counting strokes.

Twenty on the right.

Fifteen on the left, because the left shoulder had less in it.

Switch before the front wandered. Short stroke. Close to the boat. Do not listen for meaning.

The count failed almost at once. The numbers became men. Four had left the motel. Three had left Second Camp. Two had left the cabin. One had left Will on the portage and carried the boat to water. One could not keep a canoe straight.

He stopped counting.

Something knocked from the right shore. Paddle on gunwale.

Not a voice. Older than the voices. A trip sound. A boat sound. A sound from before the woods had learned grammar.

Joel checked the blue bag.

It sat under the front seat, visible and ordinary.
